THE ALQAEDA FRAMEWORK

NEW ALQAEDA FRAMEWORK ENTITY DESIGN: DELETE ALL PREVIOUS DATA WE HAD USE THIS INSTEAD:

## Public-source framework, 1984–2026

## 1. The network at the highest level

Al-Qaeda is best understood as a **core leadership network plus regional affiliates**, not as one single centralized army. NCTC describes Al-Qaeda, as of May 2026, as a Salafi terrorist group that evolved from its pre-9/11 Afghanistan/Pakistan base into a dispersed global network of affiliates and supporters. Its central leadership has been weakened, but its affiliates still operate across Africa, the Middle East, and South Asia. NCTC identifies the major current or recent components as **AQAP**, **AQIM/JNIM**, **al-Shabaab**, **AQIS**, and formerly **Hurras al-Din** in Syria, which announced dissolution in early 2025. ([Director of National Intelligence][1])

The core architecture is:

**Al-Qaeda Core / AQC**
The senior leadership network historically built around Osama bin Laden, Ayman al-Zawahiri, the Shura Council, military committee, security structures, media/propaganda arm, and external liaison channels.

**Regional affiliates**
AQAP in Yemen, AQIS in Afghanistan/Pakistan/South Asia, AQIM in North Africa, JNIM in the Sahel, al-Shabaab in Somalia/East Africa, and the former Syria affiliate Hurras al-Din.

**Contextual allied or enabling networks**
The Taliban and Haqqani Network are not “Al-Qaeda branches,” but they are central to Al-Qaeda’s historical and current operating environment because of sanctuary, ideological proximity, family/personal relationships, battlefield history, and post-2021 Afghanistan dynamics. NCTC says AQIS has longstanding ties with the Taliban and pledged allegiance to Taliban leader Haybatullah Akhundzada partly to secure safe haven in Afghanistan. ([Director of National Intelligence][2])

**Legacy or splinter networks**
Al-Qaeda in Iraq / AQI is historically crucial because it later became the Islamic State pathway. It should be tracked as an Al-Qaeda legacy branch, but not treated as current Al-Qaeda.

---

# 2. Origins: ideological and organizational roots

## Hassan al-Banna

**Born:** 1906, Egypt.
**Died:** 1949, Egypt.
**Role:** Founder of the Muslim Brotherhood.
**Relevance to Al-Qaeda:** Indirect ideological lineage. The TIC chart places Hassan al-Banna as founder of the Muslim Brotherhood and places the Brotherhood in the ideological genealogy above later jihadist currents. This should be treated as ideological background, not an operational command link.

## Sayyid Qutb

**Born:** 1906, Egypt.
**Died:** 1966, executed in Egypt.
**Role:** Muslim Brotherhood ideologue.
**Relevance:** Qutb’s writings heavily influenced later militant Islamist currents. The TIC chart labels him a leading ideological father of the Muslim Brotherhood and “father of Salafi Jihadism,” but that phrasing is interpretive and should be marked as ideological analysis rather than a direct organizational link.

## Abdullah Azzam

**Born:** 1941, British Mandate Palestine.
**Died:** November 24, 1989, Peshawar, Pakistan.
**Death:** Assassinated by car bomb with two sons.
**Role:** Palestinian cleric, Afghan jihad mobilizer, co-founder of Maktab al-Khidamat / MAK.
**Relevance:** Azzam helped build the foreign-fighter support network around the anti-Soviet Afghan war. The TIC chart identifies him as a co-founder of Al-Qaeda and MAK, but historically the cleaner phrasing is: **Azzam co-founded MAK and helped shape the environment from which Al-Qaeda emerged.**

## Maktab al-Khidamat / MAK

**Active:** roughly 1984–1988.
**Founding figures:** Abdullah Azzam and Osama bin Laden.
**Core locations:** Pakistan/Afghanistan, especially Peshawar and Afghan jihad support corridors.
**Relevance:** MAK functioned as a recruitment, logistics, finance, and foreign-fighter support structure for the Afghan jihad. The 9/11 Commission described the late-1980s transition in which bin Laden became the emir of “al Qaeda,” with a structure including intelligence, military, financial, political, media/propaganda arms, and a Shura advisory council. ([9/11 Commission][3])

---

# 3. Al-Qaeda Core leadership

## Osama bin Laden

**Full name:** Osama bin Muhammad bin Awad bin Laden.
**Born:** 1957, Riyadh, Saudi Arabia; many public profiles use March 10, 1957.
**Died:** May 2, 2011, Abbottabad, Pakistan.
**Death:** Killed by U.S. forces during the Abbottabad raid. CIA describes the operation as the result of years of intelligence work and says the raid killed Bin Laden in the early morning of May 2, 2011 Pakistan time. ([CIA][4])
**Role:** Founder and first emir of Al-Qaeda.
**Leadership period:** 1988–2011.
**Parents:** Father Muhammad Awad bin Laden; mother Hamida al-Attas / Alia Ghanem.
**Importance:** Charismatic founder, financier, organizer, strategist, and symbolic center of Al-Qaeda’s original global brand.

The 9/11 Commission describes bin Laden as clearly leading Al-Qaeda by August 1988, with operating arms for intelligence, military, finance, politics, media/propaganda, and a Shura council. It also describes his shift from Afghan jihad logistics into a broader campaign against the United States, especially after the early 1990s and his later return to Afghanistan. ([9/11 Commission][3])

### Osama bin Laden’s publicly reported wives and children

Public family reporting varies. CNN’s 2026 “Fast Facts” profile lists the following wives and children; treat exact dates as public-profile data, not primary civil-record data. ([ABC17NEWS][5])

**Najwa Ghanem**
First wife, married 1974, separated shortly before 9/11. She was also his first cousin. Children publicly listed with Najwa:

* **Abdullah bin Laden** — born 1976.
* **Abdul Rahman bin Laden** — born 1978.
* **Saad bin Laden** — born 1979; reported killed in a U.S. drone strike in Pakistan in 2009.
* **Omar bin Laden** — born 1981; publicly known because he later spoke/wrote about the family.
* **Osman bin Laden** — born 1983.
* **Muhammad bin Laden** — born 1985.
* **Fatima bin Laden** — born 1987.
* **Iman bin Laden** — born 1990.
* **Ladin “Bakir” bin Laden** — born 1993.
* **Rukhaiya bin Laden** — born 1997.
* **Nour bin Laden** — born 1999 or 2000.

**Khadijah**
Married 1983, divorced 1995. Children publicly listed:

* **Ali bin Laden** — born 1984 or 1986.
* **Amer bin Laden** — born 1990.
* **Aisha bin Laden** — born 1992.

**Khairiah Sabar**
Married 1985 until his death. Child publicly listed:

* **Hamza bin Laden** — born 1989 or 1991; later publicly treated by U.S. officials as a high-ranking Al-Qaeda member and symbolic heir figure; killed in a U.S. counterterrorism operation in the Afghanistan/Pakistan region according to the White House. ([Trump White House Archives][6])

**Siham Sabar**
Married 1987 until his death. Children publicly listed:

* **Khadijah / Kadhija bin Laden** — born 1988.
* **Khalid bin Laden** — born 1989; killed during the Abbottabad raid according to public reporting.
* **Miriam bin Laden** — born 1990.
* **Sumaiya bin Laden** — born 1992.

**Unknown woman**
Married briefly in 1996; annulled within days.

**Amal Ahmed al-Sadah**
Married 2000 until his death. Children publicly listed:

* **Safiyah bin Laden** — born 2001.
* **Aasia bin Laden** — born 2003.
* **Ibrahim bin Laden** — born 2004.
* **Zainab bin Laden** — born 2006.
* **Hussain bin Laden** — born 2008.

### Bin Laden family network relevance

For OSINT purposes, the most relevant children are **Saad**, **Hamza**, **Khalid**, **Omar**, **Abdullah**, and some daughters only insofar as they appear in public reporting or Abbottabad documents. Hamza became important because of his symbolic value as Osama’s son and because U.S. officials described him as a high-ranking Al-Qaeda member before confirming his death. Saad matters because he was publicly reported killed in Pakistan in 2009 and appears in discussions of the Bin Laden family’s Iran/Pakistan movement after 9/11. ([Trump White House Archives][6])

---

## Ayman al-Zawahiri

**Full name:** Ayman Mohammed Rabie al-Zawahiri.
**Born:** June 19, 1951, Egypt.
**Died:** July 31, 2022, Kabul, Afghanistan.
**Death:** Killed by U.S. drone strike.
**Role:** Egyptian physician, Egyptian Islamic Jihad figure, Al-Qaeda ideologue, second emir of Al-Qaeda.
**Leadership period:** 2011–2022.
**Importance:** Strategic ideologue, merger figure between Egyptian Islamic Jihad and Al-Qaeda, post-bin Laden continuity leader.

Britannica identifies al-Zawahiri as an Egyptian physician and militant who became one of Al-Qaeda’s major ideologues and died in Kabul on July 31, 2022. ([Encyclopedia Britannica][7]) NCTC lists him as Al-Qaeda’s deceased former amir, following Bin Laden. ([Director of National Intelligence][1])

### Zawahiri family network

Public sources report that al-Zawahiri married at least four times, with the most publicly discussed wife being **Azza Ahmed Nowari**. Public biographical reporting says Azza and two of their children, **Mohammed** and **Aisha**, died after a U.S. strike in Afghanistan in late 2001. This is sensitive family material and should be used only when relevant to mapping personal networks, not for sensationalism. ([Wikipedia][8])

The most operationally relevant family tie is **Nabila al-Zawahiri**, publicly identified as al-Zawahiri’s daughter and the wife of **Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi / Muhammad Abbatay**. Rewards for Justice states that al-Maghrebi is al-Zawahiri’s son-in-law and senior adviser, and that he directed Al-Qaeda’s media arm al-Sahab and later coordinated affiliate activity through Al-Qaeda’s External Communications Office. ([Rewards For Justice][9])

---

## Sayf al-Adl

**Aliases:** Mohammed Salah al-Din al-Halim Zaydan; Saif al-Adel; Muhamad Ibrahim Makkawi; Ibrahim al-Madani.
**Born:** FBI lists dates of birth used as April 11, 1960 and April 11, 1963.
**Place of birth:** Egypt.
**Status:** Alive/wanted; widely treated as de facto Al-Qaeda leader after Zawahiri, though formal public succession remains complicated.
**Role:** Senior Al-Qaeda leader; member of Majlis al-Shura; head of military committee.
**Known broad location:** Iran-based, according to Rewards for Justice and NCTC.
**Reward:** Up to $10 million. ([FBI][10])

Rewards for Justice says Sayf al-Adl is an Iran-based senior Al-Qaeda leader, a member of the Majlis al-Shura, and head of Al-Qaeda’s military committee. It says he was indicted for the August 7, 1998 U.S. Embassy bombings in Kenya and Tanzania, which killed 224 civilians and wounded more than 5,000. RFJ also says he moved to southeastern Iran after the bombings, was placed under Iranian house arrest in 2003, and was released in 2015 in a prisoner exchange. ([Rewards For Justice][11])

**Network significance:** Sayf al-Adl represents the old military/security professional wing of Al-Qaeda. His presence in Iran is central to current leadership ambiguity because Al-Qaeda’s emir traditionally needs legitimacy among affiliates, but an Iran-based emir creates political, theological, and operational complications for a Sunni jihadist organization. The UN-backed reporting ecosystem and AP reporting have described member-state consensus that Sayf al-Adl became the de facto leader after Zawahiri. ([AP News][12])

---

## Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi

**Real name:** Muhammad Abbatay / Mohamed Abbatay.
**Aliases:** Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi, Abdul Rahman al-Maghrebi, al-Maghribi.
**Born:** FBI lists approximately 1970–1971; other public sources vary.
**Place of birth:** Morocco.
**Nationality:** Moroccan.
**Status:** Alive/wanted.
**Role:** Iran-based key Al-Qaeda leader; longtime director of al-Sahab; former senior adviser and son-in-law of Ayman al-Zawahiri.
**Reward:** Up to $7 million. ([FBI][13])

Rewards for Justice says al-Maghrebi served as Al-Qaeda’s “general manager” in Afghanistan and Pakistan since 2012, later relocated to Iran, and as head of Al-Qaeda’s External Communications Office coordinated activities with affiliates. This makes him one of the most important public-source nodes connecting **core leadership, media, external communications, Zawahiri family ties, and Iran-based senior leadership.** ([Rewards For Justice][9])

**Family connection:** Son-in-law of Ayman al-Zawahiri through marriage to Zawahiri’s daughter, publicly identified in multiple sources as Nabila al-Zawahiri. This is one of the strongest documented family-command links in the current network. ([Rewards For Justice][9])

---

## Hamza Salih bin Sa’id al-Ghamdi

**Aliases:** Hamza al-Ghamdi; Hamza al-Ghamidi; Salih Saeed Albitaih Alghamdi.
**Born:** Approximately 1965, Saudi Arabia, according to FBI.
**Nationality:** Saudi.
**Status:** Wanted.
**Role:** Senior Al-Qaeda leader; RFJ says he was one of Bin Laden’s most trusted associates in the weeks before 9/11 and head of Bin Laden’s security detail. FBI lists him as wanted for questioning in connection with membership in Al-Qaeda. ([FBI][14])

**Network significance:** Ghamdi is important because he connects the **personal-security / inner-circle layer** around Bin Laden to the current senior-leadership list. The TIC chart also places him in the Al-Qaeda Shura context, but that exact Shura title should be treated as chart-supported unless independently sourced.

---

## Khalid Sheikh Mohammed / KSM

**Born:** Britannica lists March 1, 1964 or April 14, 1965, Kuwait.
**Status:** Detained at Guantanamo Bay.
**Role:** Al-Qaeda operational planner; described by the 9/11 Commission as the principal architect of 9/11.
**Family links:** Uncle of Ramzi Yousef and Ammar al-Baluchi in public reporting.
**Captured:** March 1, 2003, Rawalpindi, Pakistan. ([Encyclopedia Britannica][15])

**Network significance:** KSM is not the ideological founder of Al-Qaeda, but he is one of its most important operational planners. The 9/11 Commission describes the “planes operation” as an idea brought to Bin Laden and his advisers by KSM, later becoming the 9/11 plot. ([9/11 Commission][3])

---

## Abu Muhammad al-Masri / Abdullah Ahmed Abdullah

**Status:** Publicly reported dead.
**Death:** Reportedly killed in Tehran in 2020.
**Role:** Senior Al-Qaeda figure; linked publicly to the East Africa Embassy bombings.
**Network significance:** One of the older Egyptian-linked senior figures and part of the Iran-based senior leadership file. The TIC chart labels him an AQ Shura Council member, but the chart also contains some claims about his status that should be treated cautiously.

---

## Atiyah Abd al-Rahman

**Birth name:** Jamal Ibrahim Ashtiwi al-Misrati.
**Born:** 1969 or 1970, Libya, depending on source.
**Died:** August 22, 2011, North Waziristan, Pakistan.
**Death:** Reportedly killed by drone strike.
**Role:** Senior Al-Qaeda leader; often described as Bin Laden’s chief of staff or “general manager.”
**Network significance:** He was a major connector between Bin Laden, affiliates, and Al-Qaeda’s post-9/11 correspondence system. Public reporting after his death described him as a key Al-Qaeda manager. ([FDD's Long War Journal][16])

---

## Abu Yahya al-Libi

**Real name:** Muhammad Hassan Qaid / Mohamed Hassan Qaid.
**Born:** Public sources list 1963, Libya.
**Died:** June 4, 2012, North Waziristan, Pakistan.
**Death:** Killed in U.S. drone strike; later confirmed by Al-Qaeda.
**Role:** Senior Al-Qaeda theologian, strategist, and public-facing ideologue.
**Network significance:** He became important after Atiyah’s death and was seen as a rising senior figure before his own death in 2012. Public profiles describe him as one of Al-Qaeda’s highest-ranking officials at the time. ([Wikipedia][17])

---

# 4. Bin Laden succession and symbolic family line

## Saad bin Laden

**Born:** 1979, publicly listed as one of Osama’s sons with Najwa.
**Died:** Reported killed in Pakistan in 2009.
**Death:** Public reporting says he was killed in a U.S. drone strike; later Al-Qaeda messaging and Abbottabad material helped clarify his death after earlier uncertainty. ([Wikipedia][18])
**Network significance:** Often discussed as a possible heir/senior family figure, but later scholarship and public reporting suggest the “heir” narrative was more complicated.

## Hamza bin Laden

**Born:** 1989 or 1991, publicly listed as son of Osama bin Laden and Khairiah Sabar.
**Died:** U.S. government announced in 2019 that Hamza was killed in a U.S. counterterrorism operation in the Afghanistan/Pakistan region. ([Trump White House Archives][6])
**Role:** Publicly treated as a high-ranking Al-Qaeda member and symbolic heir figure.
**Family / marriage:** The Guardian reported, based on interviews with Bin Laden relatives, that Hamza married a daughter of 9/11 lead hijacker Mohammed Atta; this should be marked as **family-reported / media-reported**, not official record. ([The Guardian][19])
**Network significance:** Hamza mattered less because of formal command and more because of symbolic legitimacy: he connected Al-Qaeda’s post-bin Laden propaganda to the founder’s bloodline.

## Khalid bin Laden

**Born:** 1989, son of Osama and Siham.
**Died:** May 2, 2011, Abbottabad raid.
**Network significance:** Killed alongside his father during the raid; not generally treated as a major independent commander.

## Omar bin Laden

**Born:** 1981.
**Status:** Living public figure, not an Al-Qaeda commander.
**Network significance:** Relevant because he has publicly discussed family life and separated himself from his father’s terrorist activity. Use as family-context, not operational network.

---

# 5. Haqqani family and Al-Qaeda connection

The Haqqani Network is critical because it connects the anti-Soviet jihad, Taliban insurgency, Pakistan/Afghanistan border networks, and Al-Qaeda sanctuary history. NCTC says Jalaluddin Haqqani founded the Haqqani Network, was associated with Osama bin Laden, and was recognized as one of Bin Laden’s closest mentors during Bin Laden’s formative years in the 1980s Afghan war. NCTC also says Sirajuddin Haqqani, Jalaluddin’s son, currently leads day-to-day activity of the group with close relatives. ([Director of National Intelligence][20])

## Jalaluddin Haqqani

**Born:** commonly listed around 1939, Paktia Province, Afghanistan.
**Died:** 2018, Afghanistan.
**Death:** Taliban announced he died after a long illness. ([FDD's Long War Journal][21])
**Role:** Founder of Haqqani Network; Afghan mujahideen commander; later Taliban-aligned leader.
**Al-Qaeda connection:** Mentor/associate of Osama bin Laden during the Afghan jihad era.
**Family:** Father of Sirajuddin Haqqani; brother of Khalil Haqqani according to public reporting and the TIC chart. ([Director of National Intelligence][20])

## Sirajuddin Haqqani

**Born:** public estimates vary; often listed in the 1970s.
**Status:** Alive.
**Role:** Leader of Haqqani Network; senior Taliban figure; Taliban acting interior minister after 2021.
**Family:** Son of Jalaluddin Haqqani; nephew of Khalil Haqqani.
**Al-Qaeda connection:** The relationship is contextual and historical rather than a clean “Al-Qaeda command” relationship. The Haqqani Network has long been tied to Al-Qaeda sanctuary and cooperation, and AQIS’s Taliban allegiance strengthens the importance of the Haqqani/Taliban environment. ([Director of National Intelligence][20])

## Khalil Haqqani

**Born:** public profiles often list 1966.
**Died:** December 11, 2024, Kabul.
**Death:** Killed in a suicide bombing claimed by IS-KP; AP reported he was the Taliban minister for refugees and repatriation and uncle of Sirajuddin Haqqani. ([AP News][22])
**Role:** Haqqani Network/Taliban figure; Taliban minister after 2021.
**Al-Qaeda connection:** TIC chart labels him Haqqani Network / Al-Qaeda member and head of security for Kabul, but direct Al-Qaeda-membership language from the chart should be marked as **chart claim** unless sourced separately.

## Abdul Aziz Haqqani

**Status:** Living public status not always clear in open profiles.
**Role:** Haqqani Network figure.
**Chart connection:** TIC labels him “Haqqani Network and Al-Qaeda member” and IA Shura Council member; treat as chart claim unless corroborated.

## Anas Haqqani

**Status:** Alive.
**Role:** Public Taliban/Haqqani family figure.
**Network significance:** More political/media-facing than core Al-Qaeda; relevant as part of the Haqqani family tree, not as an Al-Qaeda commander.

## Why the Haqqanis matter to an Al-Qaeda OSINT map

The Haqqani Network should be mapped as a **sanctuary / alliance / family / Taliban-power connector**, not as a simple Al-Qaeda affiliate. NCTC says the Haqqani Network is primarily based in North Waziristan and conducts cross-border operations into eastern Afghanistan and Kabul; it also emphasizes the group’s Zadran tribal composition and its close relationship to Taliban structures. ([Director of National Intelligence][20])

---

# 6. Taliban nodes directly relevant to Al-Qaeda

## Mullah Omar

**Full name:** Mohammed Omar.
**Born:** commonly listed around 1960, Afghanistan.
**Died:** April 2013, death publicly confirmed in 2015.
**Role:** Founder of Taliban; Taliban emir during Al-Qaeda’s pre-9/11 sanctuary period.
**Family:** Father of Mullah Yaqoob Mujahid.
**Al-Qaeda relevance:** Omar’s Taliban gave Bin Laden sanctuary in Afghanistan before 9/11. The Taliban later concealed Omar’s death for roughly two years, according to public reporting. ([The Guardian][23])

## Haibatullah Akhundzada

**Status:** Alive.
**Role:** Taliban supreme leader.
**Al-Qaeda relevance:** AQIS pledged allegiance to him, according to NCTC, partly to secure safe haven in Afghanistan. ([Director of National Intelligence][2])

## Mullah Yaqoob Mujahid

**Status:** Alive.
**Role:** Taliban defense minister; son of Mullah Omar.
**Al-Qaeda relevance:** Relevant as part of the Taliban command environment in which AQIS and remnants of Al-Qaeda operate, not as an Al-Qaeda leader.

---

# 7. AQAP: Al-Qaeda in the Arabian Peninsula

**Founded:** 2009 merger of Yemeni and Saudi Al-Qaeda branches.
**Base:** Southern and central Yemen.
**Members:** NCTC lists 2,000–3,000 fighters.
**Designation:** U.S. State Department designated AQAP as FTO and SDGT entity in January 2010.
**Importance:** One of Al-Qaeda’s most externally dangerous affiliates, especially because of aviation plots, English-language propaganda, and continued calls for attacks against U.S. interests. ([Director of National Intelligence][24])

## Nasir al-Wuhayshi

**Aliases:** Nasir al-Wahishi, Abu Basir.
**Born:** 1970s, Yemen.
**Died:** June 2015, Yemen.
**Death:** Killed in U.S. drone strike.
**Role:** Founder/leader of AQAP; former Bin Laden aide.
**Network significance:** He tied AQAP directly back to Bin Laden’s inner circle and served as a major post-9/11 affiliate leader.

## Qasim al-Raymi

**Aliases:** Qassim al-Rimi.
**Died:** 2020.
**Death:** Killed in U.S. strike.
**Role:** AQAP leader after Wuhayshi.
**Network significance:** Presided over AQAP after Wuhayshi’s death and continued the group’s Yemen-based operations and external messaging.

## Khalid Batarfi

**Died:** 2024, announced by AQAP.
**Role:** AQAP leader after al-Raymi.
**Succession:** AQAP announced Saad bin Atef al-Awlaki as his successor in 2024. ([state.gov][25])

## Saad bin Atef al-Awlaki / Sa’d Atif al-Aulaqi

**Aliases:** Sa’d Muhammad Atif.
**Status:** Alive/wanted.
**Role:** Current overall AQAP leader according to NCTC.
**Reward:** Up to $10 million.
**Locations:** Yemen; NCTC says AQAP is primarily in southern and central Yemen.
**Public profile:** NCTC says he publicly called for attacks against the United States and kidnapped Americans in Yemen. ([Director of National Intelligence][24])

## Ibrahim al-Banna

**Status:** Alive/wanted.
**Role:** Founding AQAP member; NCTC says he served as AQAP chief of security and provided military/security guidance to AQAP leaders.
**Reward:** Up to $5 million. ([Director of National Intelligence][24])

## Ibrahim Ahmed Mahmoud al-Qosi

**Status:** Alive/wanted.
**Role:** AQAP spokesperson; former Guantanamo detainee; NCTC says he worked directly for Osama bin Laden and joined AQAP in 2014 after earlier Al-Qaeda activity.
**Reward:** Up to $4 million. ([Director of National Intelligence][24])

## AQAP major public events

* **2009:** AQAP forms.
* **December 25, 2009:** Northwest Flight 253 attempted bombing.
* **January 2010:** U.S. FTO designation.
* **October 29, 2010:** Cargo/printer-cartridge bomb plot; packages interdicted in UK and UAE.
* **January 7, 2015:** Charlie Hebdo attack in Paris; AQAP claimed responsibility.
* **December 6, 2019:** Pensacola attack; AQAP claimed responsibility in February 2020, and NCTC says the attacker had been in contact with AQAP for years.
* **2024:** Batarfi death; Saad al-Awlaki becomes leader.
* **2025:** NCTC notes AQAP leader Sa’d Atif al-Aulaqi released media calling for attacks against senior U.S. officials. ([Director of National Intelligence][24])

---

# 8. AQIS: Al-Qaeda in the Indian Subcontinent

**Founded:** Announced in 2014 by Ayman al-Zawahiri.
**Base:** Primarily Afghanistan, with activity in Pakistan, India, and Bangladesh.
**Members:** NCTC lists fewer than 200 as of May 2024.
**Importance:** AQIS links Al-Qaeda Core to Afghanistan, Pakistan, Taliban safe haven, South Asia, Kashmir narratives, and Bangladesh/India attack claims. ([Director of National Intelligence][2])

NCTC says AQIS receives overarching guidance from Al-Qaeda senior leaders, has longstanding ties with the Taliban, pledged allegiance to Haybatullah Akhundzada, and sought to defend the Taliban and assist in establishing an Islamic emirate. ([Director of National Intelligence][2])

## Asim Umar

**Status:** Deceased.
**Role:** Founding AQIS amir.
**Death:** Killed in U.S.-Afghan raid in 2019, according to NCTC. ([Director of National Intelligence][2])

## Usama Mahmood / Osama Mehmood / Abu Zar

**Status:** Alive/wanted.
**Role:** AQIS leader since 2019; formerly AQIS spokesperson according to NCTC.
**Reward:** RFJ offers a reward for information on him as AQIS emir. ([Director of National Intelligence][2])

## Yahya Ghauri / Atif Yahya Ghouri

**Status:** Alive/wanted.
**Role:** AQIS deputy amir, according to NCTC. ([Director of National Intelligence][2])

## Muhammad Maruf

**Status:** Alive/wanted or public status unclear.
**Role:** AQIS head of recruitment according to NCTC. ([Director of National Intelligence][2])

## AQIS major public events

* **2014:** AQIS announced as Al-Qaeda’s South Asia umbrella.
* **September 6, 2014:** Karachi dockyard attack attempt; militants tried to seize a Pakistani Navy frigate to target a U.S. Navy vessel.
* **April 25, 2016:** Murders of Xulhaz Mannan and Mahbub Tonoy in Bangladesh; AQIS claimed responsibility.
* **2019:** Asim Umar killed.
* **2021:** Taliban return to power; AQIS safe-haven issue becomes more significant. ([Director of National Intelligence][2])

---

# 9. AQIM: Al-Qaeda in the Islamic Maghreb

**Origin:** Predecessor was the Salafist Group for Preaching and Combat / GSPC, formed in 1998 as a faction of Algeria’s Armed Islamic Group.
**Al-Qaeda alignment:** GSPC aligned with Al-Qaeda in September 2006 and became AQIM in January 2007.
**Operating areas:** North and West Africa; Algeria, Mali, Tunisia, Libya, Mauritania, and Sahel corridors.
**Importance:** AQIM is the parent/root structure for much of Al-Qaeda’s Sahel expansion and the later JNIM coalition. NCTC says AQIM is autonomous in command and targeting even while being an Al-Qaeda affiliate. ([Director of National Intelligence][26])

## Abdelmalek Droukdel

**Aliases:** Abu Musab Abdel Wadoud.
**Died:** June 2020, Mali.
**Death:** Killed by French forces.
**Role:** AQIM emir from the GSPC/AQIM era until 2020.
**Network significance:** Key figure in integrating the Algerian jihadist network into Al-Qaeda’s global brand and expanding influence into Mali/Sahel.

## Abu Ubaydah Yusuf al-Anabi

**Real name:** Yazid Mebarek, according to public profiles.
**Born:** public profiles often list February 7, 1969, Annaba, Algeria.
**Status:** Alive/wanted.
**Role:** Current AQIM emir after Droukdel; former AQIM shura/media/notables figure.
**Chart role:** TIC lists him as senior AQ leader, AQ Shura Council member, and Emir AQIM; official public profiles support the AQIM emir role, while the exact AQ Shura label should be treated carefully unless separately verified. ([Counter Extremism Project][27])

## Mokhtar Belmokhtar

**Aliases:** Khalid Abu al-Abbas; “The One-Eyed”; “The Uncatchable.”
**Status:** Contested/unclear; repeatedly reported killed but not cleanly resolved for years.
**Role:** AQIM/al-Murabitun-linked commander.
**Network significance:** Major Sahel node linking smuggling, kidnapping, hostage-taking, AQIM splinters, and the In Amenas attack ecosystem. The TIC chart explicitly notes uncertainty over his death and says it presumes he alive but unconfirmed, which should be marked as chart claim.

## AQIM major public events

* **1998:** GSPC forms.
* **2006:** GSPC aligns with Al-Qaeda.
* **2007:** AQIM name adopted.
* **2012:** AQIM exploits northern Mali chaos; NCTC says AQIM worked with local Tuareg elements and helped take Kidal, Gao, and Timbuktu. ([Director of National Intelligence][28])
* **2013:** In Amenas hostage crisis by Belmokhtar-linked network.
* **2015–2016:** Hotel/resort attacks in Mali, Burkina Faso, Côte d’Ivoire linked to AQIM/al-Murabitun/JNIM ecosystem.
* **2017:** AQIM Sahel branch merges into JNIM structure.
* **2020:** Droukdel killed; al-Anabi succeeds.

---

# 10. JNIM: Jama’at Nusrat al-Islam wal-Muslimin

**Founded:** March 2017.
**Formation:** Merger of Ansar al-Din, al-Murabitun, Macina Liberation Front, and AQIM’s Sahara Emirate subgroup.
**Base:** Mali and Burkina Faso; active across much of West Africa including Niger, Nigeria, and coastal West Africa.
**Members:** NCTC lists at least 6,000.
**Importance:** JNIM is currently one of the most important active Al-Qaeda-aligned organizations in the world. NCTC says it was the world’s second-deadliest terrorist group in 2025, accounting for more than 1,200 deaths across four countries. ([Director of National Intelligence][29])

## Iyad ag Ghali

**Born:** public reporting often places him in the 1950s, Kidal region, Mali.
**Status:** Alive/wanted.
**Role:** JNIM founder and amir; former Tuareg rebel/diplomatic actor turned jihadist leader.
**Connections:** Pledged JNIM allegiance to AQIM, Al-Qaeda, and the Taliban leader, according to NCTC. ([Director of National Intelligence][29])
**Network significance:** Ghali is the central Al-Qaeda-aligned figure in the Sahel.

## Amadou Kouffa

**Status:** Alive/contested after prior reported death.
**Role:** JNIM deputy, founding member, founder and amir of Macina Liberation Front / Katibat Macina.
**Network significance:** Key Fulani/Macina mobilization figure in central Mali. NCTC lists him as deputy to Ghali and founding member of JNIM. ([Director of National Intelligence][29])

## Jafar Dicko

**Status:** Alive.
**Role:** JNIM deputy leader in Burkina Faso, according to NCTC. ([Director of National Intelligence][29])

## JNIM major public events

* **March 2017:** JNIM forms through merger of four Mali-based extremist groups.
* **2018:** U.S. designates JNIM as FTO.
* **2025:** JNIM fuel blockade in Mali causes widespread shortages in Bamako and throughout the country.
* **April 25, 2026:** JNIM and the Front for the Liberation of Azawad conduct coordinated attacks across Mali, capture Kidal, and target military locations around Bamako and other northern/central cities, according to NCTC. ([Director of National Intelligence][29])

---

# 11. Al-Shabaab: Al-Qaeda’s East Africa affiliate

**Full name:** Harakat al-Shabaab al-Mujahidin.
**Origin:** Militant wing of the Somali Islamic Courts Union environment.
**Al-Qaeda relationship:** Publicly pledged allegiance to Al-Qaeda in 2012.
**Base:** Somalia; operations and attacks extend into Kenya and regionally.
**Designation:** U.S. designated al-Shabaab as an FTO in March 2008.
**Importance:** One of Al-Qaeda’s most durable and financially significant affiliates. ([Director of National Intelligence][30])

## Ahmed Diriye / Abu Ubaidah

**Status:** Alive/wanted.
**Role:** Amir of al-Shabaab since 2014, according to NCTC.
**Chart connection:** TIC also labels him AQ Shura Council member / Military Committee / Leader Al-Shabaab, but the Al-Qaeda Shura part should be treated as chart claim unless separately verified. ([Director of National Intelligence][31])

## Abukar Ali Adan

**Status:** Alive/wanted.
**Role:** Deputy amir; former al-Shabaab military chief, according to NCTC. ([Director of National Intelligence][31])

## Mahad Karate / Abdirahman Mohammed Warsame

**Status:** Alive/wanted.
**Role:** Finance chief and commander of al-Shabaab’s amniyat intelligence/security wing; former deputy amir, according to NCTC. ([Director of National Intelligence][31])

## Jehad Serwan Mostafa

**Born:** FBI public profiles identify him as U.S.-born; public biographies list December 28, 1981, Waukesha, Wisconsin.
**Status:** Alive/wanted.
**Aliases:** Ahmed Gurey, Anwar al-Amriki, Emir Anwar.
**Role:** U.S. citizen; senior al-Shabaab figure. FBI says he joined al-Shabaab around 2006 and served as military instructor, foreign-fighter leader, media-wing propagandist, intermediary with other terrorist organizations, and leader in the group’s use of explosives. FBI says he may have visited or may likely visit Somalia, Yemen, Ethiopia, Kenya, and other African countries. ([FBI][32])
**Network significance:** One of the clearest U.S.-person nodes inside an Al-Qaeda affiliate.

## Al-Shabaab major public events

* **2012:** Pledge to Al-Qaeda.
* **2013:** Westgate Mall attack, Nairobi.
* **2015:** Garissa University attack, Kenya.
* **2017:** Mogadishu truck bombing; NCTC says more than 500 killed and almost 300 injured.
* **2019:** DusitD2 Hotel complex attack, Nairobi.
* **2020:** Camp Simba / Manda Bay attack; one U.S. soldier and two U.S. contractors killed.
* **2024:** Lido Beach attack, Mogadishu; NCTC says 37 killed and more than 210 injured. ([Director of National Intelligence][31])

---

# 12. Syria file: Jabhat al-Nusra, HTS, and Hurras al-Din

## Jabhat al-Nusra

**Founded:** 2011–2012 Syria war period.
**Role:** Al-Qaeda’s Syrian affiliate before breaking away.
**Later path:** Rebranded as Jabhat Fatah al-Sham, then became part of Hay’at Tahrir al-Sham / HTS.
**Al-Qaeda relevance:** Critical to the Al-Qaeda vs ISIS split and the later dispute over localism vs global command.

## Abu Muhammad al-Julani / Ahmed al-Sharaa

**Role:** Former emir of Jabhat al-Nusra; later leader of HTS.
**Al-Qaeda relationship:** Former Al-Qaeda Syrian affiliate leader who later broke with Al-Qaeda.
**Chart note:** TIC says Julani had served as emir of the now-defunct al-Nusra Front and had close ties with both Al-Qaeda and IS; this should be treated as broad chart characterization.

## Hurras al-Din

**Founded:** 2018.
**Status:** Publicly announced dissolution in early 2025.
**Base:** Idlib Province, Syria, with attacks also in Raqqah, Damascus, and Latakia before dissolution.
**Members:** NCTC says 2,000–2,500 before public dissolution.
**Role:** Al-Qaeda’s formal Syria presence after Nusra/HTS broke away. NCTC says Hurras al-Din formed from Nusra Front members loyal to Al-Qaeda after Nusra broke from Al-Qaeda and joined other groups to form HTS. ([Director of National Intelligence][33])

## Samir Hijazi / Faruq al-Suri

**Role:** Hurras al-Din amir, according to NCTC. ([Director of National Intelligence][33])

## Sami al-Uraydi

**Role:** Hurras al-Din ideologue, according to NCTC. ([Director of National Intelligence][33])

---

# 13. Al-Qaeda in Iraq / AQI legacy

AQI matters because it is the bridge between Al-Qaeda’s Iraq war network and the later Islamic State movement. It should be treated as an **Al-Qaeda legacy branch**, not a current Al-Qaeda affiliate.

## Abu Musab al-Zarqawi

**Born:** 1966, Jordan.
**Died:** June 7, 2006, Iraq.
**Death:** Killed by U.S. airstrike.
**Role:** Founder/leader of Al-Qaeda in Iraq.
**Network significance:** Zarqawi’s organization became AQI, then Islamic State of Iraq, then ISIS lineage.

## Abu Muhammad al-Maqdisi / Assem Barqawi

**Born:** 1959, Nablus / Palestinian-Jordanian background in public biographies.
**Status:** Alive.
**Role:** Salafi-jihadist ideologue; spiritual guide/mentor to Zarqawi in many public analyses.
**Chart connection:** TIC describes him as mentor to the Al-Qaeda branch in Iraq and spiritual guide to Zarqawi; use as ideological-link, not operational command.

---

# 14. Major Al-Qaeda events timeline

## 1984–1988 — MAK period

Maktab al-Khidamat builds Afghan jihad support infrastructure around Peshawar/Afghanistan, connecting Azzam, Bin Laden, foreign fighters, donations, and recruitment networks. The TIC chart dates MAK to 1984–1988.

## 1988 — Al-Qaeda founded

The 9/11 Commission describes Bin Laden as emir of Al-Qaeda by August 1988 and describes the early structure as including intelligence, military, financial, political, media/propaganda arms, plus a Shura council. ([9/11 Commission][3])

## 1989 — Abdullah Azzam assassinated

Azzam is killed in Peshawar. His death removes the most important rival/alternative ideological authority in the Afghan Arab milieu.

## 1991–1996 — Sudan period

Bin Laden relocates to Sudan and develops business, logistics, and militant networks while escalating anti-U.S. rhetoric. The 9/11 Commission describes Bin Laden building an organization and issuing early calls against U.S. forces in the 1992–1996 period. ([9/11 Commission][3])

## 1996 — Bin Laden returns to Afghanistan

Bin Laden leaves Sudan and returns to Afghanistan, accompanied by family, bodyguards, and close Al-Qaeda associates. The 9/11 Commission describes this as Al-Qaeda’s renewal in Afghanistan. ([9/11 Commission][3])

## 1998 — East Africa U.S. Embassy bombings

Nearly simultaneous bombings target U.S. embassies in Nairobi, Kenya, and Dar es Salaam, Tanzania. FBI and State Department sources link the attacks to Al-Qaeda; State Department museum material says more than 200 were killed and more than 4,000 wounded, while RFJ’s Sayf al-Adl profile says 224 civilians were killed and more than 5,000 wounded. ([FBI][34])

## 1999 — U.S. FTO designation

The U.S. State Department designated Al-Qaeda as a Foreign Terrorist Organization in October 1999, according to NCTC. ([Director of National Intelligence][1])

## 2000 — USS Cole bombing

On October 12, 2000, suicide attackers bombed the USS Cole in Aden, Yemen, killing 17 U.S. sailors. FBI identifies the attack as an Al-Qaeda case. ([FBI][35])

## 2001 — 9/11 attacks

Nineteen hijackers trained by Al-Qaeda hijacked four planes and attacked New York, Washington, and Pennsylvania. FBI says the 19 hijackers were all trained by Al-Qaeda and identifies the Hamburg cell pilots Mohamed Atta, Marwan al-Shehhi, and Ziad Jarrah. ([FBI][36])

## 2001 — U.S. invasion of Afghanistan

Al-Qaeda loses its main Taliban-protected sanctuary and its senior leadership disperses into Pakistan, Iran, Yemen, and other theaters.

## 2003 — Khalid Sheikh Mohammed captured

KSM is captured in Rawalpindi, Pakistan, and later detained at Guantanamo Bay. Britannica describes him as the operational planner of Al-Qaeda’s highest-profile attacks, especially 9/11. ([Encyclopedia Britannica][15])

## 2004–2006 — AQI rise and Zarqawi death

Zarqawi’s Iraq network formally aligns with Al-Qaeda and becomes AQI. Zarqawi is killed by U.S. airstrike in 2006, but the Iraq branch later evolves into the Islamic State pathway.

## 2007 — AQIM name adopted

GSPC becomes Al-Qaeda in the Islamic Maghreb after aligning with Al-Qaeda in 2006. NCTC says AQIM’s predecessor formed in 1998 and adopted the AQIM name in January 2007. ([Director of National Intelligence][26])

## 2009 — AQAP forms

AQAP emerges from the merger of Saudi and Yemeni Al-Qaeda elements, becoming one of the most dangerous affiliates for external plotting.

## 2009–2010 — AQAP aviation plots

AQAP is tied to the attempted Northwest Flight 253 bombing and the 2010 cargo/printer-cartridge bomb plot. NCTC lists the cargo plot as an AQAP notable attack. ([Director of National Intelligence][24])

## 2011 — Bin Laden killed

Osama bin Laden is killed in Abbottabad, Pakistan, on May 2, 2011; al-Zawahiri succeeds him as Al-Qaeda emir. CIA describes the raid as the culmination of years of intelligence work. ([CIA][4])

## 2011–2012 — Senior leadership attrition

Atiyah Abd al-Rahman is killed in 2011; Abu Yahya al-Libi is killed in 2012. This weakens Al-Qaeda’s post-bin Laden senior-management layer. ([FDD's Long War Journal][16])

## 2012 — al-Shabaab pledge

Al-Shabaab formally aligns itself with Al-Qaeda, making Somalia/East Africa one of Al-Qaeda’s most important theaters.

## 2014 — AQIS announced

Ayman al-Zawahiri announces AQIS as Al-Qaeda’s South Asia branch. NCTC says AQIS was formally announced in 2014 and is primarily based in Afghanistan. ([Director of National Intelligence][2])

## 2014–2015 — ISIS split and AQAP Charlie Hebdo claim

The ISIS-Al-Qaeda break becomes decisive, while AQAP claims the 2015 Charlie Hebdo attack. NCTC lists the Paris attack as AQAP-claimed, with 12 killed and 11 wounded. ([Director of National Intelligence][24])

## 2017 — JNIM forms

JNIM forms by merger of Ansar al-Din, al-Murabitun, Macina Liberation Front, and AQIM’s Sahara Emirate subgroup; Iyad ag Ghali pledges allegiance to AQIM, Al-Qaeda, and the Taliban leader. ([Director of National Intelligence][29])

## 2018 — Hurras al-Din forms

Hurras al-Din becomes Al-Qaeda’s formal Syria affiliate after Nusra/HTS breaks from Al-Qaeda. ([Director of National Intelligence][33])

## 2019 — Hamza bin Laden killed; Pensacola attack

The White House announces Hamza bin Laden was killed in a U.S. counterterrorism operation in the Afghanistan/Pakistan region. AQAP later claims the 2019 Pensacola attack. ([Trump White House Archives][6])

## 2020 — AQAP and AQIM leadership changes

Qasim al-Raymi is killed; Khalid Batarfi becomes AQAP leader. Abdelmalek Droukdel is killed in Mali; Abu Ubaydah Yusuf al-Anabi becomes AQIM leader.

## 2021 — Taliban returns to power

The Taliban takeover of Afghanistan renews concern about Al-Qaeda safe haven, especially for AQIS and remnants of Al-Qaeda Core. NCTC says AQIS has longstanding Taliban ties and pledged allegiance to Haybatullah Akhundzada partly to secure safe haven. ([Director of National Intelligence][2])

## 2022 — Zawahiri killed in Kabul

Ayman al-Zawahiri is killed by U.S. drone strike in Kabul, Afghanistan, creating a succession problem and elevating Sayf al-Adl as de facto leader in much public analysis. ([Encyclopedia Britannica][7])

## 2024 — AQAP succession and Khalil Haqqani death

AQAP announces Khalid Batarfi’s death and names Saad bin Atef al-Awlaki as successor. Khalil Haqqani is killed in a Kabul bombing claimed by IS-KP. ([state.gov][25])

## 2025 — Hurras al-Din dissolution and JNIM escalation

Hurras al-Din announces dissolution in early 2025, while JNIM expands pressure across Mali and West Africa. NCTC says former Hurras members remain a threat, and JNIM was the world’s second-deadliest terrorist group in 2025. ([Director of National Intelligence][33])

## 2026 — Al-Qaeda network status

As of 2026, the Al-Qaeda picture is: weakened core, Iran-based senior leadership, Yemen-based AQAP, Afghanistan/Pakistan-based AQIS, Somalia-based al-Shabaab, Mali/Sahel-based AQIM/JNIM, and residual Syria networks after Hurras al-Din’s dissolution. NCTC’s 2026 Al-Qaeda profile explicitly frames the network this way. ([Director of National Intelligence][1])

---

# 15. Organizations directly connected to Al-Qaeda

## Direct Al-Qaeda entities

* **Al-Qaeda Core / AQC**
* **Al-Qaeda Shura Council / Majlis al-Shura**
* **Al-Sahab Media**
* **Maktab al-Khidamat / MAK**
* **AQAP**
* **AQIS**
* **AQIM**
* **JNIM**
* **Al-Shabaab**
* **Hurras al-Din**
* **AQI / Al-Qaeda in Iraq** as legacy branch
* **Jabhat al-Nusra** as former Syria branch

## Closely connected but not Al-Qaeda branches

* **Taliban** — sanctuary, political umbrella, safe-haven environment.
* **Haqqani Network** — historical Bin Laden mentor network, Taliban faction, sanctuary/operational environment.
* **Egyptian Islamic Jihad** — merged personnel and ideology into Al-Qaeda through Zawahiri.
* **GSPC** — predecessor to AQIM.
* **Ansar al-Din** — JNIM component.
* **Al-Murabitun** — JNIM component.
* **Macina Liberation Front / Katibat Macina** — JNIM component.
* **Nusra / HTS** — former affiliate / breakaway Syria file.
* **Lashkar-e-Taiba, Jaish-e-Mohammed, TTP, ETIM/TIP, Islamic Jihad Union** — should be tracked as connected South/Central Asia ecosystem actors, not automatically treated as Al-Qaeda branches. The TIC chart lists many of these in Khorasan/Indian Subcontinent regional boxes.

---

# 16. TIC chart-specific connection map

Treat this as a chart-derived layer.

## Ideological / predecessor connections

* Hassan al-Banna → Muslim Brotherhood: founder.
* Sayyid Qutb → Muslim Brotherhood / Salafi-jihadist ideological lineage: ideological influence.
* Abdullah Azzam → MAK / Al-Qaeda predecessor: co-founder.
* Osama bin Laden → MAK / Al-Qaeda: founder.
* Maktab al-Khidamat → Al-Qaeda: predecessor.
* Al-Qaeda → IA / “Army of Imam Mahdi” Shura Council: chart-claimed umbrella structure.

## Family / kinship shown on chart

* Osama bin Laden → Saad bin Laden: son.
* Osama bin Laden → Abdallah bin Laden: son.
* Osama bin Laden → Hamza bin Laden: son.
* Jalaluddin Haqqani → Sirajuddin Haqqani: father/son family line.
* Jalaluddin Haqqani → Khalil Haqqani: brother.
* Mullah Omar → Mullah Yaqoob Mujahid: father/son.
* Hamza bin Laden marriage links: chart shows multiple “daughter married to Hamza” / “son-in-law” labels connected around al-Zawahiri, Mohammad al-Misri, and Jalaluddin Haqqani; mark these as chart claims unless independently sourced.

## Leadership / command shown on chart

* Al-Qaeda → Sayf al-Adl: head of Military Commission; AQ Shura Council; IA Shura Council.
* Al-Qaeda → Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi: AQ Shura; head of propaganda / al-Sahab.
* Al-Qaeda → Abdul Azim Ali Musa bin Ali: senior AQ member; covert operations; liaison to Sirajuddin Haqqani.
* Al-Qaeda → Abu Muhammad al-Misri: AQ Shura.
* Al-Qaeda → Abu Ikhlas al-Masri: AQ Shura; leader of “Red Unit.”
* Al-Qaeda → Hamza Salih bin Sa’id al-Ghamdi: AQ Shura; head of IA Shura Council.
* Al-Qaeda → Qari Zakir: AQ Shura; security committee.
* Al-Qaeda → Abdul Haq al-Turkistani: AQ Shura; military committee.
* Al-Qaeda → Abu Khalid al-Libi: AQ Shura; military committee.
* Al-Qaeda → Ahmed Diriye: AQ Shura; military committee; al-Shabaab leader.
* Al-Qaeda → Luqman Khubab: AQ Shura; WMD program.
* Al-Qaeda → Abdul al-Aziz al-Masri: AQ Shura; WMD program.
* Al-Qaeda → Saad bin Atef al-Awlaki: AQAP emir; AQ Shura.
* Al-Qaeda → Usama Mahmood: AQIS emir; AQ Shura.
* Al-Qaeda → Abu Ubaydah Yusuf al-Anabi: senior AQ leader; AQ Shura; AQIM emir.

# 17. Death / detention / status index

## Dead

* **Abdullah Azzam** — car bombing, Peshawar, 1989.
* **Osama bin Laden** — U.S. raid, Abbottabad, 2011. ([CIA][4])
* **Ayman al-Zawahiri** — U.S. drone strike, Kabul, 2022. ([Encyclopedia Britannica][7])
* **Saad bin Laden** — reported U.S. drone strike, Pakistan, 2009. ([Wikipedia][18])
* **Hamza bin Laden** — U.S. counterterrorism operation, Afghanistan/Pakistan region, announced 2019. ([Trump White House Archives][6])
* **Khalid bin Laden** — Abbottabad raid, 2011.
* **Jalaluddin Haqqani** — long illness, announced 2018. ([FDD's Long War Journal][21])
* **Mullah Omar** — died 2013, publicly confirmed 2015. ([The Guardian][23])
* **Khalil Haqqani** — IS-KP-claimed bombing, Kabul, 2024. ([AP News][22])
* **Abu Musab al-Zarqawi** — U.S. airstrike, Iraq, 2006.
* **Atiyah Abd al-Rahman** — drone strike, Pakistan, 2011. ([FDD's Long War Journal][16])
* **Abu Yahya al-Libi** — drone strike, Pakistan, 2012. ([Wikipedia][17])
* **Nasir al-Wuhayshi** — drone strike, Yemen, 2015.
* **Qasim al-Raymi** — U.S. strike, Yemen, 2020.
* **Khalid Batarfi** — AQAP announced death, 2024.
* **Abdelmalek Droukdel** — French operation, Mali, 2020.
* **Asim Umar** — U.S.-Afghan raid, 2019. ([Director of National Intelligence][2])
* **Abu Muhammad al-Misri** — reportedly killed in Tehran, 2020.

## Detained

* **Khalid Sheikh Mohammed** — captured 2003, Guantanamo detainee. ([Encyclopedia Britannica][15])
* **Abd al-Rahim al-Nashiri** — USS Cole case detainee, Guantanamo.
* **Abu Zubaydah** — detained; historically relevant as facilitator/training-camp figure, but not always best classified as formal Al-Qaeda leadership.

## Alive / wanted / public status active or uncertain

* **Sayf al-Adl** — Iran-based senior Al-Qaeda leader; wanted. ([Rewards For Justice][11])
* **Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi** — Iran-based senior leader; al-Sahab / external communications; wanted. ([Rewards For Justice][9])
* **Hamza Salih bin Sa’id al-Ghamdi** — senior Al-Qaeda leader; wanted. ([Rewards For Justice][37])
* **Saad bin Atef al-Awlaki** — AQAP leader; wanted. ([Director of National Intelligence][24])
* **Ibrahim al-Banna** — AQAP founding/security figure; wanted. ([Director of National Intelligence][24])
* **Ibrahim al-Qosi** — AQAP spokesperson; wanted. ([Director of National Intelligence][24])
* **Usama Mahmood / Abu Zar** — AQIS leader; wanted. ([Director of National Intelligence][2])
* **Yahya Ghauri / Atif Yahya Ghouri** — AQIS deputy. ([Director of National Intelligence][2])
* **Abu Ubaydah Yusuf al-Anabi** — AQIM emir. ([Counter Extremism Project][27])
* **Iyad ag Ghali** — JNIM amir. ([Director of National Intelligence][29])
* **Amadou Kouffa** — JNIM deputy / MLF founder. ([Director of National Intelligence][29])
* **Ahmed Diriye / Abu Ubaidah** — al-Shabaab amir. ([Director of National Intelligence][31])
* **Mahad Karate** — al-Shabaab finance/amniyat leader. ([Director of National Intelligence][31])
* **Jehad Serwan Mostafa** — U.S. citizen al-Shabaab leader; wanted. ([FBI][32])
* **Sirajuddin Haqqani** — Haqqani/Taliban leader.
* **Haibatullah Akhundzada** — Taliban supreme leader.
* **Mullah Yaqoob Mujahid** — Taliban defense minister.

---

# 18. How the network operates structurally

Al-Qaeda’s system works through **layered authority**, not just direct command. The first layer is ideological legitimacy: Bin Laden, Zawahiri, Azzam, Qutbist influence, and later senior ideologues. The second layer is organizational bureaucracy: emir, Shura, military committee, security committee, media/propaganda, finance, external communications. The 9/11 Commission specifically described early Al-Qaeda as having intelligence, military, financial, political, media/propaganda components and a Shura council. ([9/11 Commission][3])

The third layer is **affiliate allegiance**. Affiliates do not always receive daily tactical orders from Al-Qaeda Core. Instead, they often pledge allegiance, receive strategic messaging, share media channels, align ideologically, and operate regionally. NCTC says affiliates now conduct most of Al-Qaeda’s operational activity, with AQAP in Yemen, AQIM/JNIM in Mali/Sahel, al-Shabaab in Somalia, AQIS in Afghanistan/Pakistan, and former Hurras al-Din elements in Syria. ([Director of National Intelligence][1])

The fourth layer is **sanctuary and relationship infrastructure**. This is where the Taliban and Haqqani Network matter. They are not simply “Al-Qaeda branches,” but they form a political/military environment through which Al-Qaeda-linked actors historically found shelter, mobility, protection, and legitimacy. NCTC explicitly says Jalaluddin Haqqani was a close mentor to Bin Laden in the Afghan war period and that AQIS maintains Taliban ties to secure safe haven. ([Director of National Intelligence][20])

The fifth layer is **family and marriage ties**. The strongest public-source examples are Osama bin Laden’s sons, Hamza bin Laden’s symbolic heir status, Jalaluddin/Sirajuddin/Khalil Haqqani family structure, Mullah Omar/Mullah Yaqoob, and Zawahiri’s son-in-law Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi. Some extra marriage links shown in the TIC chart should be labeled chart-only unless corroborated. ([Rewards For Justice][9])

---

# 19. Source reliability notes for your OSINT platform

Use this ranking:

**Highest confidence**
NCTC, FBI, Rewards for Justice, State Department, Treasury, CIA historical pages, 9/11 Commission, UN Security Council monitoring reports.

**Strong research confidence**
CTC West Point, CSIS, FDD Long War Journal, Council on Foreign Relations, Britannica for biographical baselines, major wire services/AP/Reuters.

**Useful but must label**
TIC chart, user screenshots, Wikipedia-derived biographical dates, older media profiles, family interviews, think-tank biographies.

**Do not treat as confirmed without corroboration**
Exact current whereabouts, private family details unrelated to network activity, chart-only Shura titles, disputed deaths, “seen alive” claims, or sensational press claims.

The UN Security Council’s ISIL/Al-Qaida Monitoring Team reports should be a recurring source for updates, especially because the UN page lists current monitoring reports through 2026. ([United Nations][38])
