Absolutely — I’ll treat this as the **next maximum-depth tranche**, not the final entire encyclopedia. This part expands the **regional event layer + affiliate structure layer**: AQAP, AQIM/JNIM, al-Shabaab, AQIS, Syria/Hurras/Nusra, and the first arrests/designations/sanctions layer. I’ll keep everything public-source and analytical, and I’ll separate **verified public-source facts** from **chart/screenshot-derived claims**. The TIC chart is useful as a network seed because it explicitly places AQAP, AQIS, AQIM, JNIM, al-Shabaab, Haqqani/Taliban, Hurras/HTS, and related regional groups around the Al-Qaeda-centered framework, but its relationship arrows should be treated as chart claims unless separately corroborated.

# PART 2 — AL-QAEDA AFFILIATE DEPTH

## Regional events, leadership rosters, localized activity, and public-source network expansion

## 1. Al-Qaeda’s affiliate system: how this layer works

By 2026, Al-Qaeda is no longer best understood as one Afghan/Pakistani command cell directly managing every attack. NCTC describes it as a weakened but still active global network whose affiliates carry out most of the operational activity. Its named affiliate architecture includes **AQAP in Yemen**, **AQIM and JNIM in Mali/Sahel**, **al-Shabaab in Somalia/East Africa**, **AQIS in Afghanistan/Pakistan/South Asia**, and former **Hurras al-Din** elements in Syria after Hurras publicly announced dissolution in early 2025. ([Director of National Intelligence][1])

That means your OSINT structure should separate:

**Core Al-Qaeda:** Bin Laden → Zawahiri → Sayf al-Adl / Iran-based senior leadership problem.
**Formal affiliates:** AQAP, AQIS, AQIM, JNIM, al-Shabaab, Hurras al-Din.
**Former branches / legacy branches:** AQI, Jabhat al-Nusra.
**Merger components:** GSPC, Ansar al-Din, al-Murabitun, Macina Liberation Front.
**Contextual sanctuary networks:** Taliban, Haqqani Network.
**Overlap networks:** TTP, IJU, IMU, JeM, LeT, TIP/ETIM, Ansaru, Ansar al-Sharia variants, etc., only where they connect to Al-Qaeda’s ecosystem.

The TIC chart mirrors this affiliate logic by grouping Al-Qaeda-linked or adjacent organizations by region: Arabian Peninsula/AQAP, Khorasan/AQIS-Haqqani-Taliban, Islamic Maghreb/AQIM, West Africa/JNIM, East Africa/al-Shabaab, Indian Subcontinent/AQIS, and Syria/Hurras/HTS/Nusra-related nodes.

---

# 2. AQAP — Al-Qaeda in the Arabian Peninsula

## 2.1 Why AQAP matters

AQAP is one of the most important Al-Qaeda branches because it combines **Yemeni insurgency**, **Saudi/Yemeni veteran networks**, **external aviation plots**, **English-language propaganda**, **local governance attempts**, **kidnapping**, **assassination**, and **prison-break recruitment**. NCTC says AQAP operates primarily in southern and central Yemen with roughly **2,000–3,000 fighters**, and uses ambushes, assaults, assassinations, bombings, kidnappings, and suicide attacks against Yemeni government officials, security forces, infrastructure, Huthis, and ISIS-Yemen; outside Yemen, it has attempted attacks against cargo and passenger aircraft and Western targets. ([Director of National Intelligence][2])

The older NCTC AQAP profile says AQAP was preceded by **Al-Qaeda in Yemen / AQY**, composed of Al-Qaeda veterans who escaped from a Sana’a prison; its original leadership included **Nasir al-Wahishi**, **Sa’id al-Shihri**, and **Qasim al-Rimi**, with **Anwar al-Awlaki** as the most prominent ideologue/propagandist before his death in 2011. ([Director of National Intelligence][3])

## 2.2 AQAP leadership and personnel roster

### Nasir al-Wuhayshi / Nasir al-Wahishi

**Role:** AQAP founder and first major emir.
**Background:** Former close aide / secretary to Osama bin Laden.
**Status:** Dead.
**Death:** Killed in a U.S. drone strike in Yemen in June 2015.
**Network importance:** Direct continuity node from Bin Laden’s inner circle into AQAP. He made AQAP feel less like a purely local Yemeni insurgency and more like a legitimate Al-Qaeda central affiliate.

### Sa’id al-Shihri / Said al-Shihri

**Role:** AQAP deputy emir; former Guantanamo detainee.
**Status:** Dead.
**Death:** Reported killed in Yemen, with death confirmed by AQAP in 2013.
**Network importance:** Saudi veteran node; helped bridge Saudi and Yemeni Al-Qaeda networks.

### Qasim al-Raymi / Qassim al-Rimi

**Role:** AQAP military commander, then emir after Wuhayshi.
**Status:** Dead.
**Death:** Killed in a U.S. strike in 2020.
**Network importance:** Operational commander and post-Wuhayshi emir. AP notes Batarfi took over after al-Rimi was killed by a U.S. drone strike. ([MarketWatch][4])

### Khalid Batarfi

**Role:** AQAP leader after Qasim al-Raymi.
**Status:** Dead.
**Death:** AQAP announced his death in March 2024 without giving a cause.
**Network importance:** AP notes Batarfi had been imprisoned, freed in a jailbreak, and later led AQAP during a weakened but still active period; AQAP named **Saad bin Atef al-Awlaki** as successor. ([AP News][5])

### Saad bin Atef al-Awlaki / Sa’d Atif al-Aulaqi

**Role:** Current AQAP leader after Batarfi.
**Status:** Alive/wanted.
**Importance:** Current leadership node. NCTC lists him as overall AQAP leader; State has offered rewards for information on him. AQAP’s survival after repeated leadership strikes makes him a key modern Al-Qaeda node. ([Director of National Intelligence][2])

### Ibrahim al-Banna

**Role:** Founding AQAP member; security/military guidance figure.
**Status:** Alive/wanted in public U.S. profiles.
**Chart link:** TIC lists him as a co-founder and senior AQAP leader.

### Ibrahim Ahmed Mahmoud al-Qosi

**Role:** AQAP spokesperson; former Guantanamo detainee; former Bin Laden associate.
**Status:** Alive/wanted.
**Importance:** Propaganda and legacy Al-Qaeda connection.

### Anwar al-Awlaki

**Role:** AQAP ideologue and propagandist.
**Status:** Dead.
**Death:** Killed in September 2011.
**Importance:** Major English-language radicalization node, especially through lectures and online propaganda. NCTC’s older AQAP profile describes him as the group’s most prominent member before his death. ([Director of National Intelligence][3])

### Samir Khan

**Role:** AQAP media propagandist; editor associated with *Inspire*.
**Status:** Dead.
**Death:** Killed in the same 2011 strike as Anwar al-Awlaki.
**Importance:** English-language media node.

### Ibrahim Hassan al-Asiri

**Role:** AQAP bombmaker.
**Status:** Reported dead.
**Importance:** Publicly associated with AQAP aviation plots; for your platform, keep this to historical attribution and avoid technical detail.

### Nasser al-Ansi

**Role:** Senior AQAP official and propagandist.
**Status:** Dead; killed in 2015.
**Importance:** Publicly appeared in AQAP messaging around the Charlie Hebdo claim.

### Harith al-Nadhari

**Role:** AQAP religious/legal official.
**Status:** Dead; killed in 2015.
**Importance:** Ideological/legal messaging node.

### Fahd al-Quso

**Role:** AQAP operative linked to USS Cole investigation.
**Status:** Dead; killed in 2012.
**Importance:** Bridge between pre-AQAP Yemen Al-Qaeda and later AQAP network.

### Jamal al-Badawi

**Role:** USS Cole attack participant / Al-Qaeda operative.
**Status:** Dead; killed in 2019 U.S. strike.
**Importance:** Legacy Yemen Al-Qaeda node.

---

## 2.3 AQAP / Yemen event chronology

### 2000 — USS Cole bombing, Aden

This is pre-AQAP but central to the Yemen Al-Qaeda lineage. Al-Qaeda operatives attacked USS Cole in Aden, killing 17 U.S. sailors. This event forms part of the AQY → AQAP historical chain.

### 2002 — Limburg tanker bombing

Al-Qaeda-linked militants attacked the French oil tanker Limburg off Yemen. This belongs to the pre-AQAP Yemen Al-Qaeda lineage.

### 2006 — Sana’a prison escape

Several Al-Qaeda veterans escaped from prison in Sana’a. NCTC’s older AQAP profile explicitly describes AQAP’s predecessor, AQY, as composed of Al-Qaeda veterans who escaped from a Sana’a prison. This prison-break cohort is crucial because it helped seed AQAP’s later leadership. ([Director of National Intelligence][3])

### 2008 — U.S. Embassy attack, Sana’a

Militants attacked the U.S. Embassy in Yemen. This belongs to the immediate pre-AQAP escalation phase.

### January 2009 — AQAP formation announced

Nasir al-Wuhayshi publicly announced the merger of Saudi and Yemeni Al-Qaeda elements under the AQAP banner. State Department reporting describes AQAP’s formal emergence from Yemeni and Saudi Al-Qaeda operatives in January 2009. ([State Department][6])

### August 2009 — Attempted assassination of Saudi Prince Mohammed bin Nayef

AQAP attempted to assassinate the Saudi counterterrorism official using a suicide attacker. The attack failed but became one of AQAP’s defining early external/regional operations.

### December 25, 2009 — Northwest Flight 253 attempted bombing

AQAP attempted to bomb a commercial airliner traveling to the United States. NCTC lists AQAP as having a history of attempting attacks against passenger aircraft and Western targets. ([Director of National Intelligence][2])

### January 2010 — AQAP designated FTO

The U.S. Secretary of State designated AQAP as a Foreign Terrorist Organization and designated AQAP’s top leaders Nasir al-Wahishi and Said al-Shihri under Executive Order 13224. ([State Department][7])

### October 2010 — Cargo/printer-cartridge bomb plot

AQAP attempted to send explosive devices hidden in cargo shipments bound for the United States; the devices were intercepted abroad. NCTC lists AQAP’s aviation/cargo plotting as a defining external-operations pattern. ([Director of National Intelligence][2])

### 2011 — Zinjibar/Abyan seizure under Ansar al-Sharia

AQAP and its local brand **Ansar al-Sharia** exploited Yemen’s political crisis to seize territory in Abyan Province, including Zinjibar. This is one of the first times AQAP moved from clandestine terrorism into local insurgent governance.

### September 2011 — Anwar al-Awlaki and Samir Khan killed

The strike removed AQAP’s most famous English-language propagandist and a key media figure. NCTC’s older AQAP profile highlights Awlaki’s prominence and death. ([Director of National Intelligence][3])

### May 2012 — Sana’a military parade rehearsal bombing

A suicide attack targeted Yemeni soldiers rehearsing for a military parade, killing scores. This event shows AQAP/Ansar al-Sharia’s focus on Yemeni military targets during the post-Arab Spring security collapse.

### 2012 — U.S. Embassy / diplomatic threat phase

AQAP remained one of the main reasons for U.S. concern around Yemen-based external plotting.

### December 2013 — Yemen Ministry of Defense attack

AQAP carried out a complex attack against Yemen’s Ministry of Defense that killed at least 52 people. NCTC explicitly lists this as a major AQAP attack targeting the Yemeni government. ([Director of National Intelligence][3])

### February 2014 — Sana’a central prison break

AQAP freed more than two dozen prisoners from Sana’a central prison. NCTC’s older AQAP profile identifies this as a major AQAP operation. ([Director of National Intelligence][3])

### January 2015 — Charlie Hebdo attack, Paris

AQAP claimed responsibility for the attack on *Charlie Hebdo* in Paris. AP and NCTC both treat the claim as part of AQAP’s global significance. ([MarketWatch][4])

### April 2015 — Mukalla seizure and prison break

AQAP stormed the port city of Mukalla, targeted security headquarters/government buildings, and freed hundreds of prisoners from the central prison. This was one of AQAP’s largest territorial seizures and one of the most important prison-break events in its history. ([Time][8])

### 2015–2016 — AQAP governance in Mukalla

AQAP governed or heavily influenced Mukalla for roughly a year, acquiring revenue through taxation, extortion, port activity, and looting. This was a major local-state-building experiment.

### April 2016 — AQAP pushed from Mukalla

Yemeni and coalition-backed forces expelled AQAP from Mukalla. The group lost a major revenue and governance base but continued insurgent activity.

### January 2017 — Yakla / al-Bayda raid

A U.S. raid targeted AQAP in al-Bayda Province. This became one of the most publicly scrutinized U.S. counterterrorism raids in Yemen.

### December 2019 — Pensacola attack

A Saudi aviation trainee killed three U.S. servicemembers at Naval Air Station Pensacola. AQAP later claimed responsibility; NCTC says the attacker had been in contact with AQAP for years. ([Director of National Intelligence][2])

### 2020 — Qasim al-Raymi killed

Al-Raymi’s death triggered AQAP leadership transition to Khalid Batarfi. AP summarizes this succession chain in its reporting on Batarfi’s later death. ([MarketWatch][4])

### 2020–2023 — AQAP local survival period

AQAP was weakened by leadership losses, drone strikes, internal disputes, tribal pressures, Huthi conflict dynamics, and competition with ISIS-Yemen, but it remained active in southern and central Yemen.

### March 2024 — Khalid Batarfi death; Saad al-Awlaki succession

AQAP announced Batarfi’s death without explaining the cause and named Saad bin Atef al-Awlaki as successor. AP notes Batarfi had led AQAP through imprisonment, jailbreak, and Yemen’s wartime fragmentation. ([AP News][5])

### 2025–2026 — AQAP under Saad al-Awlaki

AQAP remains dangerous but diminished compared with its 2009–2015 peak. NCTC still lists AQAP as active in southern/central Yemen with thousands of fighters and continuing attacks against Yemeni forces, Huthis, ISIS-Yemen, infrastructure, and Western interests. ([Director of National Intelligence][2])

---

# 3. AQIM — Al-Qaeda in the Islamic Maghreb

## 3.1 Why AQIM matters

AQIM is the North Africa/Sahel branch of Al-Qaeda. It is critical because it evolved from the Algerian insurgent ecosystem, expanded into kidnapping-for-ransom, smuggling corridors, Mali/Sahel insurgency, hotel attacks, hostage crises, and then fed into **JNIM**, now one of Al-Qaeda’s strongest active affiliates. NCTC describes AQIM as a North Africa-based Sunni terrorist group active across North and West Africa, using guerrilla-style ambushes, mortar, rocket, and IED attacks, with revenue from extortion, kidnapping for ransom, and donations. ([Director of National Intelligence][9])

## 3.2 AQIM / GSPC lineage

### Armed Islamic Group / GIA

**Role:** Algerian Civil War-era precursor environment.
**Relevance:** Not Al-Qaeda, but the violence, splintering, and ideological hardening of the Algerian civil war created the environment from which GSPC emerged.

### GSPC — Salafist Group for Preaching and Combat

**Founded:** 1998.
**Founder:** Hassan Hattab.
**Relevance:** Direct predecessor to AQIM.
**Transition:** Pledged or aligned with Al-Qaeda in 2006, then adopted AQIM name in 2007. NCTC states GSPC became AQIM in January 2007 after aligning with Al-Qaeda. ([Director of National Intelligence][9])

### AQIM

**Name adopted:** 2007.
**Core geography:** Algeria, northern Mali, Mauritania, Niger, Tunisia, Libya, broader Sahel.
**Operational identity:** Insurgency, ambushes, bombings, kidnappings, extortion, smuggling corridor taxation.

## 3.3 AQIM leadership and roster

### Hassan Hattab

**Role:** Founder of GSPC.
**Status:** Later surrendered / detained in Algerian context.
**Importance:** Original split from GIA toward GSPC.

### Nabil Sahraoui

**Role:** GSPC leader after Hattab.
**Status:** Dead, killed in 2004.
**Importance:** Transitional GSPC leader before Droukdel.

### Abdelmalek Droukdel / Abu Musab Abdel Wadoud

**Role:** GSPC/AQIM emir.
**Status:** Dead.
**Death:** Killed by French forces in Mali in June 2020.
**Importance:** Core architect of GSPC’s Al-Qaeda alignment and AQIM’s expansion.

### Abu Ubaydah Yusuf al-Anabi / Yazid Mebarek

**Role:** AQIM emir after Droukdel.
**Status:** Alive/wanted.
**Chart connection:** TIC lists him as “Senior AQ Leader,” “AQ Shura Council Member,” and “Emir AQIM.” Treat “Emir AQIM” as corroborated by public profiles; treat exact AQ Shura claim as chart-supported unless separately verified.

### Mokhtar Belmokhtar

**Aliases:** Khalid Abu al-Abbas; “The One-Eyed”; “The Uncatchable.”
**Role:** AQIM / al-Murabitun commander.
**Status:** Contested; repeatedly reported killed, not consistently verified.
**Importance:** Smuggling, kidnapping, Saharan mobility, In Amenas, al-Murabitun. The TIC chart explicitly notes uncertainty around his death and says it presumes him alive but unconfirmed.

### Yahya Abu al-Hammam

**Role:** AQIM Sahara/Sahel commander.
**Status:** Dead, killed in French operation in 2019.
**Importance:** AQIM Sahel commander before later JNIM consolidation.

### Djamel Okacha / Yahya Abu al-Hammam

Often treated as an AQIM/JNIM-linked Sahel commander; include as a leadership node connected to AQIM’s Sahara Emirate.

### Abu Zeid / Abdelhamid Abou Zeid

**Role:** AQIM commander in the Sahara.
**Status:** Dead, killed in Mali in 2013.
**Importance:** Hostage-taking and Sahel kidnap-for-ransom network.

### Ali Maychou

**Role:** JNIM religious / leadership figure; formerly AQIM/JNIM-linked.
**Designation:** U.S. designated him in 2019 as a JNIM leader. ([State Department][10])

---

## 3.4 AQIM / GSPC / Sahel event chronology

### 1998 — GSPC formed

The GSPC splits from the Algerian GIA and becomes the direct predecessor to AQIM.

### 2003 — European tourist kidnappings in the Sahara

GSPC/AQIM predecessor networks kidnapped dozens of European tourists in Algeria/Sahara zones. This helped establish the Sahara kidnapping economy that later became a major AQIM revenue pattern.

### 2006 — GSPC aligns with Al-Qaeda

GSPC formally aligns with Al-Qaeda’s global brand.

### January 2007 — AQIM name adopted

AQIM becomes Al-Qaeda’s North Africa affiliate.

### April 2007 — Algiers bombings

AQIM carried out major bombings in Algiers, including attacks against government targets.

### December 2007 — Algiers UN / Constitutional Council bombings

AQIM attacked UN offices and Algerian government targets, killing scores. This was one of AQIM’s most internationally visible attacks.

### 2008–2009 — Kidnapping expansion in Mauritania, Mali, Niger

AQIM expanded hostage-taking against Westerners and regional targets.

### June 2009 — Christopher Leggett killing, Mauritania

NCTC states AQIM publicly claimed responsibility for killing U.S. citizen Christopher Leggett in Mauritania, and Mauritanian courts later convicted suspects. ([Director of National Intelligence][11])

### 2010 — Michel Germaneau hostage execution

AQIM killed French hostage Michel Germaneau after a failed rescue attempt / hostage crisis.

### 2011 — Arab Spring and Libya weapons dispersal

The collapse of Libyan state control and arms leakage after the fall of Qaddafi helped destabilize Mali/Sahel and strengthened AQIM-linked mobility.

### 2012 — Northern Mali takeover

AQIM, Ansar al-Din, MUJAO, and Tuareg rebel forces exploited Mali’s collapse and took control of northern cities including Gao, Timbuktu, and Kidal. NCTC says AQIM worked with Tuareg elements and helped seize Kidal, Gao, and Timbuktu. ([Director of National Intelligence][11])

### January 2013 — In Amenas hostage crisis, Algeria

Mokhtar Belmokhtar’s network attacked the Tigantourine gas facility near In Amenas, killing nearly 40 Westerners, including three Americans. NCTC describes the attack as carried out by AQIM offshoots before their later merger. ([Director of National Intelligence][12])

### 2013 — French intervention in Mali

Operation Serval pushed AQIM and allied groups from urban control but dispersed them into rural and cross-border insurgency.

### 2015 — Radisson Blu Hotel attack, Bamako

AQIM/al-Murabitun-linked militants attacked the Radisson Blu Hotel in Bamako. This showed the shift from desert insurgency to high-profile urban attacks.

### January 2016 — Ouagadougou attack, Burkina Faso

AQIM/al-Murabitun-linked attackers struck a hotel/café area in Ouagadougou, killing civilians and foreigners.

### March 2016 — Grand-Bassam attack, Côte d’Ivoire

AQIM claimed a beach resort attack in Côte d’Ivoire. This marked Al-Qaeda’s ability to project violence toward coastal West Africa.

### 2017 — AQIM Sahel branch merges into JNIM

AQIM’s Sahara Emirate merges with Ansar al-Din, al-Murabitun, and Macina Liberation Front to create JNIM. NCTC explicitly identifies these four merger components. ([Director of National Intelligence][13])

### 2020 — Abdelmalek Droukdel killed

French forces killed Droukdel in Mali, weakening AQIM’s old Algerian leadership line and elevating al-Anabi.

---

# 4. JNIM — Jama’at Nusrat al-Islam wal-Muslimin

## 4.1 Why JNIM matters

JNIM is the most important Al-Qaeda-aligned Sahel coalition. NCTC says it formed in March 2017 when **Ansar al-Din**, **al-Murabitun**, **Macina Liberation Front**, and AQIM’s Sahara Emirate merged. Its leader, **Iyad ag Ghali**, pledged allegiance to the emir of AQIM, the emir of Al-Qaeda, and the Taliban leader. NCTC lists its operating area as primarily Mali and Burkina Faso, with activity across West Africa, and membership of at least 6,000. ([Director of National Intelligence][13])

NCTC also says JNIM regularly attacks local security forces, foreign military partners, and civilians, using ambushes, assaults, assassinations, bombings, kidnappings, IEDs, suicide attacks, and indirect fire. ([Director of National Intelligence][13])

## 4.2 JNIM component groups

### Ansar al-Din

**Leader:** Iyad ag Ghali.
**Role:** Tuareg-led Islamist movement in northern Mali.
**Importance:** One of JNIM’s core merger components. NCTC notes State designated Iyad ag Ghali as an SDGT in February 2013 and Ansar al-Din as an FTO one month later. ([Director of National Intelligence][14])

### Al-Murabitun

**Leader:** Mokhtar Belmokhtar / Ahmed al-Tilemsi lineage.
**Role:** AQIM splinter / Sahel jihadist group.
**Importance:** Responsible for high-profile hostage and hotel attacks; later merger component of JNIM. NCTC says al-Murabitun ascribed to Al-Qaeda ideology and swore allegiance to the emirs of Al-Qaeda, the Taliban, AQIM, and JNIM. ([Director of National Intelligence][15])

### Macina Liberation Front / Katibat Macina

**Leader:** Amadou Kouffa.
**Role:** Central Mali / Fulani mobilization front.
**Importance:** Major JNIM component in central Mali.

### AQIM Sahara Emirate

**Role:** AQIM’s Sahel/Sahara branch.
**Importance:** Provided direct AQIM lineage into JNIM.

## 4.3 JNIM leadership and roster

### Iyad ag Ghali

**Role:** JNIM founder and emir.
**Former roles:** Tuareg rebel/diplomatic/political actor before jihadist leadership.
**Importance:** Central Al-Qaeda-aligned leader in Mali/Sahel. TIC identifies him as prominent AQIM member and JNIM founder/leader.

### Amadou Kouffa

**Role:** JNIM deputy / senior member; founder of Katibat Macina.
**Status:** Alive after contested death claims.
**Importance:** Central Mali/Fulani mobilization node. State described him as a senior JNIM member and noted JNIM had claimed numerous attacks/kidnappings since 2017. ([State Department][16])

### Jafar Dicko

**Role:** JNIM deputy leader in Burkina Faso according to NCTC.
**Importance:** Burkina Faso expansion node. ([Director of National Intelligence][13])

### Ali Maychou

**Role:** JNIM religious/leadership official.
**Designation:** U.S. designated him as a JNIM leader in 2019. ([State Department][10])

### Mokhtar Belmokhtar

**Role:** Al-Murabitun founder/leader; JNIM lineage figure.
**Status:** Contested.
**Importance:** Older Saharan commander whose network became part of the JNIM ecosystem.

---

## 4.4 JNIM event chronology

### March 2017 — JNIM formation

Four Mali-based extremist groups merged: Ansar al-Din, al-Murabitun, Macina Liberation Front, and AQIM Sahara Emirate. Iyad ag Ghali pledged allegiance up the AQIM → Al-Qaeda → Taliban chain. ([Director of National Intelligence][13])

### 2017 — Kangaba / Le Campement attack, Mali

JNIM claimed or was linked to attacks against tourist/security targets around Bamako.

### March 2018 — Ouagadougou attacks

JNIM attacked the French Embassy and Burkina Faso military headquarters in Ouagadougou.

### 2018 — JNIM designated FTO

State designated JNIM as a Foreign Terrorist Organization and SDGT on September 5, 2018. ([State Department][17])

### 2019 — Central Mali expansion

JNIM expanded influence in Mopti and central Mali through Katibat Macina networks, combining attacks, coercion, local deals, and intimidation.

### 2019–2020 — Attacks on Malian bases and convoys

JNIM and related Sahel jihadist formations increased attacks on Malian military outposts, foreign forces, and supply routes.

### 2020 — Post-Droukdel period

Droukdel’s death shifted AQIM/JNIM leadership dynamics but did not collapse JNIM’s local insurgency.

### 2021 — Cross-border pressure into Burkina Faso

JNIM activity expanded into northern and eastern Burkina Faso, often targeting security forces, local defense volunteers, and communities accused of collaborating with the state.

### 2022 — Siege / blockade pattern emerges

JNIM increasingly used siege tactics against towns and supply routes in Mali and Burkina Faso, coercing local communities and isolating state positions.

### 2023 — Kidnapping and extortion networks

State Department notes JNIM receives funding from kidnapping ransom, extortion, smugglers, and traffickers, and has claimed numerous kidnappings and attacks since formation. ([U.S. Department of State][18])

### 2024 — Bamako / strategic urban reach

JNIM demonstrated an ability to threaten higher-profile urban and military sites, including attacks in or around Bamako’s security infrastructure.

### September–November 2025 — Mali fuel blockade

NCTC says JNIM attacked fuel import convoys, causing widespread fuel shortages in Bamako and across Mali. This is a major strategic shift: not just killing security forces, but using economic warfare and blockade pressure. ([Director of National Intelligence][13])

### 2025 — Deadliest-active-affiliate status

NCTC says JNIM was the world’s second-deadliest terrorist group in 2025, causing more than 1,200 deaths across four countries. ([Director of National Intelligence][13])

### April 25, 2026 — Coordinated Mali attacks

NCTC lists coordinated attacks by JNIM and the Front for the Liberation of Azawad across Mali, including Kidal and military locations around Bamako and other cities. ([Director of National Intelligence][13])

---

# 5. Al-Shabaab — Al-Qaeda’s East Africa affiliate

## 5.1 Why al-Shabaab matters

Al-Shabaab is Al-Qaeda’s most durable East African affiliate. The Mapping Militants Project describes it as a Salafi militant organization based in Somalia that has also conducted attacks in Kenya, Djibouti, and Ethiopia, emerging around December 2006 after the dissolution of the Islamic Courts Union, for which it had served as the military wing. ([Mapping Militants][19])

NCTC says al-Shabaab’s complex attacks usually combine IED/VBIED detonation followed by small-arms fire; it also conducts ambushes, military-style assaults, kidnappings, mortar attacks, and assassinations. NCTC names **Ahmed Diriye / Abu Ubaidah** as amir since 2014, **Abukar Ali Adan** as deputy amir, and **Mahad Karate** as finance chief and commander of the amniyat intelligence/security wing. ([Director of National Intelligence][20])

## 5.2 al-Shabaab leadership and wings

### Aden Hashi Ayro

**Role:** Early al-Shabaab commander.
**Status:** Dead, killed in U.S. strike in 2008.
**Importance:** Early military leader tied to Al-Qaeda training legacy.

### Ahmed Abdi Godane / Mukhtar Abu Zubair

**Role:** al-Shabaab emir before Ahmed Diriye.
**Status:** Dead, killed in U.S. strike in 2014.
**Importance:** Central figure in formalizing al-Shabaab’s allegiance to Al-Qaeda.

### Ahmed Diriye / Abu Ubaidah

**Role:** Current al-Shabaab amir since 2014.
**Status:** Alive/wanted.
**Chart connection:** TIC labels him emir of al-Shabaab and an AQ Shura/Military Committee member; treat the al-Shabaab emir role as publicly supported and the AQ Shura title as chart claim unless corroborated.

### Abukar Ali Adan

**Role:** Deputy amir; former military chief.
**Status:** Alive/wanted.
**Importance:** Senior operational command layer. ([Director of National Intelligence][20])

### Mahad Karate / Abdirahman Mohamed Warsame

**Role:** Finance chief and commander of amniyat intelligence/security wing; former deputy amir.
**Status:** Alive/wanted.
**Importance:** One of the most important internal-security and finance nodes in al-Shabaab. ([Director of National Intelligence][20])

### Jehad Serwan Mostafa

**Aliases:** Ahmed Gurey, Anwar al-Amriki, Emir Anwar.
**Role:** U.S. citizen and senior al-Shabaab figure.
**Status:** Alive/wanted.
**Chart connection:** TIC lists him as senior commander and member of al-Shabaab since 2006.

### Mukhtar Robow / Abu Mansur

**Role:** Former al-Shabaab spokesman/deputy figure.
**Status:** Defected/surrendered; later Somali political context.
**Importance:** Shows factional splits and clan/political dynamics within al-Shabaab.

### Hassan Dahir Aweys

**Role:** Older Somali Islamist leader connected to the Islamic Courts Union / Hizbul Islam environment.
**Status:** Detained/surrendered in Somali context.
**Importance:** Contextual ideological/political predecessor, not current Al-Qaeda commander.

## 5.3 al-Shabaab internal structures

**Amniyat:** Intelligence/security wing; assassinations, counterintelligence, urban attacks, coercion, infiltration. Mahad Karate is publicly identified by NCTC as amniyat commander. ([Director of National Intelligence][20])

**Military wing:** Rural insurgency, base attacks, ambushes, raids, complex assaults.

**Finance wing:** Taxation/extortion, checkpoints, business levies, charcoal/smuggling history, zakat-style coercive taxation.

**Media wing:** Al-Kataib Media Foundation, used for official messaging, attack claims, recruitment, and propaganda.

## 5.4 al-Shabaab event chronology

### 2006 — al-Shabaab emerges

Al-Shabaab emerges from the Islamic Courts Union military wing after Ethiopian/Somali intervention.

### 2008 — U.S. FTO designation

State designated al-Shabaab as a Foreign Terrorist Organization in March 2008. NCTC confirms this designation. ([Director of National Intelligence][20])

### 2008 — Hargeisa and Bosaso bombings

Coordinated suicide bombings hit targets in Somaliland and Puntland, including diplomatic and security targets.

### 2009 — Beledweyne bombing

A suicide bombing killed Somalia’s security minister and others, showing al-Shabaab’s ability to target senior officials.

### July 2010 — Kampala World Cup bombings, Uganda

Al-Shabaab bombed venues in Kampala during the World Cup final, killing more than 70 people. This marked al-Shabaab’s major regional external attack capability.

### 2011 — Mogadishu offensive / famine-era attacks

Al-Shabaab withdrew from fixed control of Mogadishu but continued suicide bombings and insurgent attacks.

### October 2011 — Mogadishu truck bombing

A major bombing targeted government facilities/students in Mogadishu, killing scores.

### September 2013 — Westgate Mall attack, Nairobi

Al-Shabaab attacked Westgate Mall in Nairobi, killing civilians and drawing global attention. Mapping Militants notes the project’s profile became useful for understanding al-Shabaab after Westgate. ([CISAC][21])

### 2014 — Godane killed; Ahmed Diriye succeeds

The death of Ahmed Abdi Godane shifted leadership to Ahmed Diriye / Abu Ubaidah.

### April 2015 — Garissa University attack, Kenya

Al-Shabaab attackers killed 148 people, mostly students. This remains one of its deadliest attacks in Kenya.

### January 2016 — El Adde attack, Kenya Defense Forces base, Somalia

Al-Shabaab overran a Kenyan AMISOM base in El Adde, killing many soldiers. This became a major military victory in its propaganda.

### January 2017 — Kulbiyow attack

Al-Shabaab attacked a Kenyan military base in Somalia.

### October 2017 — Mogadishu truck bombing

NCTC says a massive Mogadishu truck bombing killed more than 500 people and injured nearly 300. This was Somalia’s deadliest terrorist attack. ([Director of National Intelligence][20])

### January 2019 — DusitD2 Hotel attack, Nairobi

Al-Shabaab attacked the DusitD2 complex in Nairobi with IED and small-arms fire, killing 21 people including one U.S. citizen. NCTC lists this as a notable attack. ([Director of National Intelligence][20])

### January 2020 — Camp Simba / Manda Bay attack, Kenya

Al-Shabaab attacked the joint U.S.-Kenyan military facility at Manda Bay, killing one U.S. soldier and two U.S. contractors. NCTC lists this as a notable attack. ([Director of National Intelligence][20])

### August 2020 — Elite Hotel attack, Mogadishu

Al-Shabaab attacked a hotel in Mogadishu, continuing the pattern of urban complex assaults.

### 2022 — Hayat Hotel attack, Mogadishu

A prolonged hotel siege killed civilians and showed al-Shabaab’s ability to sustain urban operations despite Somali and international pressure.

### October 2022 — Mogadishu Education Ministry bombings

Twin bombings killed more than 100 people near Somalia’s education ministry.

### May 2023 — Bulo Marer AU base attack

Al-Shabaab attacked an African Union base in Lower Shabelle.

### August 2024 — Lido Beach attack, Mogadishu

NCTC says al-Shabaab conducted a suicide and small-arms attack at Lido Beach, killing 37 and injuring more than 210. ([Director of National Intelligence][20])

### March 2025 — Attempted assassination of Somali President Hassan Sheikh Mohamud

NCTC says al-Shabaab attempted to assassinate the Somali president in Mogadishu. ([Director of National Intelligence][20])

---

# 6. AQIS — Al-Qaeda in the Indian Subcontinent

## 6.1 Why AQIS matters

AQIS is Al-Qaeda’s South Asia affiliate. NCTC says it was formally announced in 2014, is primarily based in Afghanistan, operates in Afghanistan, Pakistan, India, and Bangladesh, and has fewer than 200 members as of May 2024. NCTC also says AQIS receives overarching guidance from Al-Qaeda senior leaders and has longstanding ties with the Taliban. ([Director of National Intelligence][22])

NCTC adds that AQIS pledged allegiance to Taliban leader **Haybatullah Akhundzada** partly to secure safe haven in Afghanistan, which makes AQIS one of the most important links between Al-Qaeda and the post-2021 Taliban environment. ([Director of National Intelligence][22])

## 6.2 AQIS leadership and roster

### Asim Umar

**Role:** Founding AQIS amir.
**Status:** Dead.
**Death:** Killed in 2019 in a U.S.-Afghan raid, according to public profiles.
**Importance:** First formal leader of AQIS.

### Usama Mahmood / Osama Mahmood / Abu Zar

**Role:** AQIS leader after Asim Umar; formerly spokesperson.
**Status:** Alive/wanted.
**Chart connection:** TIC labels him emir of AQIS and AQ Shura Council member; treat AQIS emir role as public-source supported, AQ Shura as chart claim unless corroborated.

### Yahya Ghauri / Atif Yahya Ghouri

**Role:** AQIS deputy amir in public NCTC profile.
**Status:** Alive/wanted or unknown.
**Importance:** Deputy leadership node. ([Director of National Intelligence][22])

### Muhammad Maruf

**Role:** AQIS recruitment head in public NCTC profile.
**Importance:** Recruitment network node. ([Director of National Intelligence][22])

### Ustad Ahmad Farooq

**Role:** Former AQIS deputy / media figure.
**Status:** Dead, killed in 2015.
**Importance:** Early AQIS media/ideological node.

### Qari Imran

**Role:** AQIS shura / Afghanistan-Pakistan figure.
**Status:** Dead, reportedly killed in 2015.
**Importance:** Early AQIS command node.

## 6.3 AQIS event chronology

### September 2014 — AQIS announced

Ayman al-Zawahiri announced Al-Qaeda in the Indian Subcontinent to expand Al-Qaeda’s South Asia frame.

### September 2014 — Karachi Naval Dockyard attack attempt

AQIS-linked militants attempted to hijack a Pakistani Navy frigate at Karachi Naval Dockyard, reportedly intending to attack U.S. or allied naval targets. NCTC lists this as AQIS’s most notable early operation. ([Director of National Intelligence][22])

### 2015 — Bangladesh secular blogger / publisher attacks

AQIS and local Bangladesh-linked militants claimed or were associated with killings of secular writers, bloggers, publishers, and activists. These included the broader attack environment around Avijit Roy, Faisal Arefin Dipan, and others; specific attribution varies by case and should be recorded case-by-case.

### April 2016 — Xulhaz Mannan and Mahbub Tonoy murders, Bangladesh

AQIS claimed responsibility for killing LGBT rights activist Xulhaz Mannan and Mahbub Tonoy. NCTC lists this as a notable AQIS attack. ([Director of National Intelligence][22])

### 2017–2018 — Leadership pressure

AQIS experienced pressure and leadership losses.

### 2019 — Asim Umar killed

AQIS’s founding emir was killed in a U.S.-Afghan raid, weakening the group’s original leadership cohort. NCTC notes AQIS has suffered leadership losses since 2018, including Asim Umar. ([Director of National Intelligence][22])

### 2021 — Taliban takeover and safe-haven significance

AQIS’s Taliban ties became more important after the Taliban returned to power. NCTC says AQIS pledged allegiance to Haybatullah Akhundzada partly to secure safe haven. ([Director of National Intelligence][22])

### 2022–2026 — Low-volume but strategically important network

AQIS remains smaller than AQAP, JNIM, and al-Shabaab, but strategically important because it links Al-Qaeda Core, Taliban Afghanistan, Pakistan, Kashmir narratives, Bangladesh, India, and Central/South Asian militants.

---

# 7. Syria: Jabhat al-Nusra, HTS split, and Hurras al-Din

## 7.1 Why Syria matters to Al-Qaeda

Syria became Al-Qaeda’s most important Arab theater after 2011. The Al-Qaeda file here is complicated because **Jabhat al-Nusra began as Al-Qaeda’s Syria affiliate**, later separated from Al-Qaeda, rebranded, and became part of **Hay’at Tahrir al-Sham / HTS**. Al-Qaeda loyalists then formed **Hurras al-Din**, which NCTC identifies as Al-Qaeda’s Syria affiliate from 2018 until its public dissolution in early 2025. ([Director of National Intelligence][23])

The TIC chart includes **Hay’at Tahrir al-Sham**, **Jabhat Fateh al-Sham**, **Jaish al-Muhajirin wal-Ansar**, **Katibat al-Tawhid wal-Jihad**, and related Syria nodes in the Middle East/Syria space.

## 7.2 Syria organization lineage

### Jabhat al-Nusra

**Founded:** 2011–2012.
**Role:** Al-Qaeda’s Syria affiliate.
**Leader:** Abu Muhammad al-Julani.
**Importance:** Al-Qaeda’s most successful Syria-era branch before the split.

### Islamic State of Iraq / ISIS split context

In 2013, Abu Bakr al-Baghdadi attempted to subsume Nusra into the Islamic State of Iraq and al-Sham. Julani rejected this and pledged to Zawahiri, creating a decisive rupture in the jihadist landscape.

### Jabhat Fatah al-Sham

**Formed:** 2016 rebrand after Nusra publicly broke with Al-Qaeda.
**Importance:** Transition stage between Nusra and HTS.

### Hay’at Tahrir al-Sham / HTS

**Formed:** 2017 coalition/reorganization.
**Al-Qaeda relationship:** Former Al-Qaeda affiliate lineage, later independent/rival/estranged.
**Use in your Al-Qaeda project:** Include only as former Al-Qaeda branch lineage, not current Al-Qaeda.

### Hurras al-Din

**Founded:** 2018.
**Role:** Al-Qaeda-loyalist Syria affiliate after Nusra/HTS split.
**Status:** Publicly announced dissolution in early 2025. NCTC says former members remain a threat. ([Director of National Intelligence][23])

## 7.3 Hurras al-Din leadership

### Samir Hijazi / Faruq al-Suri

**Role:** Hurras al-Din amir in public profiles.
**Importance:** Senior Al-Qaeda-loyalist Syria figure.

### Sami al-Uraydi

**Role:** Ideologue / religious official.
**Importance:** One of the clearest Al-Qaeda-loyalist ideological figures in Syria.

### Abu Hammam al-Shami

**Role:** Veteran Al-Qaeda figure in Syria.
**Status:** Reported killed in 2020.
**Importance:** Veteran connective tissue between old Al-Qaeda and Syria.

## 7.4 Syria event chronology

### 2011–2012 — Nusra emerges

Jabhat al-Nusra appears as a Syrian jihadist organization and later becomes known as Al-Qaeda’s Syria affiliate.

### 2012 — Damascus/Aleppo bombing wave

Nusra claimed or was linked to major bombings against Syrian state/security targets.

### 2013 — ISIS/Nusra split

Baghdadi declared a merger; Julani rejected it and pledged to Zawahiri. This becomes one of the most important Al-Qaeda–ISIS split events.

### 2014 — ISIS-Al-Qaeda split becomes irreversible

Zawahiri disavowed ISIS, and Syria/Iraq became the central battlefield for jihadist legitimacy.

### 2015 — Nusra participates in Idlib offensive

Nusra became a major actor in rebel/jihadist coalitions around Idlib.

### 2016 — Nusra rebrands as Jabhat Fatah al-Sham

This marks the public break from Al-Qaeda branding.

### 2017 — HTS forms

HTS becomes the dominant successor structure in Idlib, while Al-Qaeda loyalists become increasingly marginalized.

### 2018 — Hurras al-Din forms

NCTC identifies Hurras al-Din as Al-Qaeda’s Syria affiliate from 2018 onward. ([Director of National Intelligence][23])

### 2019 — Hurras al-Din designated

State designated Hurras al-Din as a Specially Designated Global Terrorist entity in 2019 and explicitly called it an Al-Qaeda affiliate in Syria. ([State Department][24])

### 2020–2021 — Leadership strikes

U.S. strikes targeted senior Al-Qaeda/Hurras figures in Syria.

### 2025 — Hurras al-Din announces dissolution

NCTC says Hurras al-Din publicly announced dissolution in early 2025, but former members remain a threat. ([Director of National Intelligence][23])

---

# 8. Designation, sanctions, and arrest/capture layer

## 8.1 Major organization designations

### Al-Qaeda

**U.S. FTO designation:** October 1999. NCTC’s Al-Qaeda profile records the October 1999 designation. ([Director of National Intelligence][1])

### AQAP

**U.S. FTO designation:** January 19, 2010. State also designated AQAP leaders Nasir al-Wahishi and Said al-Shihri under E.O. 13224. ([State Department][7])

### AQIM

**FTO status:** AQIM appears on State’s FTO list; State maintained AQIM’s FTO designation in 2009. ([State Department][25])

### al-Shabaab

**U.S. FTO designation:** March 2008. NCTC confirms al-Shabaab’s March 2008 designation. ([Director of National Intelligence][20])

### AQIS

**U.S. FTO designation:** June 30, 2016. State designated AQIS as both FTO and SDGT and also designated AQIS leader Asim Umar. ([State Department][26])

### JNIM

**U.S. FTO designation:** September 5, 2018. State designated JNIM as FTO and SDGT. ([State Department][17])

### Hurras al-Din

**U.S. SDGT designation:** September 2019. State designated Hurras al-Din, calling it an Al-Qaeda-affiliated group in Syria. ([State Department][24])

## 8.2 Major arrests / captures / detentions

### 1995 — Ramzi Yousef captured

Captured in Pakistan and later convicted for the 1993 World Trade Center bombing. Al-Qaeda-adjacent/KSM family network relevance.

### 2001 — Zacarias Moussaoui arrested

Arrested before 9/11; later pleaded guilty in connection with the 9/11 conspiracy.

### 2002 — Abu Zubaydah captured

Captured in Pakistan; long-term detention. Historically linked to training/facilitation networks, though his exact formal Al-Qaeda status has been debated.

### 2002 — Abd al-Rahim al-Nashiri captured

USS Cole case figure; detained at Guantanamo.

### 2003 — Khalid Sheikh Mohammed captured

Captured in Rawalpindi, Pakistan; central 9/11 planner.

### 2003 — Hambali captured

Jemaah Islamiyah / Al-Qaeda-linked Southeast Asia figure.

### 2004 — Abu Faraj al-Libi captured

Senior Al-Qaeda figure captured in Pakistan.

### 2011 — Abbottabad materials seized

U.S. raid killed Bin Laden and produced a major public-source archive of Al-Qaeda correspondence, later released in batches by U.S. agencies.

### 2015 — Sayf al-Adl released from Iranian custody / restriction

RFJ says Sayf al-Adl had been under Iranian house arrest from 2003 and was released in 2015 in a prisoner exchange. ([Rewards For Justice][27])

### 2020 — Khalid Batarfi capture rumors denied

Public reporting around Batarfi included confusion over possible detention, later denied by AQAP media; his confirmed death came in 2024. AP notes the earlier detention claims and later AQAP announcement. ([MarketWatch][4])

## 8.3 Sanctions and monitoring infrastructure

The strongest continuing source for sanctions and global network updates is the **UN Security Council ISIL/Da’esh and Al-Qaida Monitoring Team** under the 1267/1989/2253 sanctions regime. The UN maintains reports through **S/2026/44**, **S/2025/482**, **S/2025/71/Rev.1**, and earlier reports, making it one of the best sources for living networks, affiliate trends, financing, and regional developments. ([United Nations][28])

Executive Order 13224 is the key U.S. financial sanctions mechanism. State says the order authorizes the blocking of assets of foreign individuals and entities that commit or pose a risk of committing terrorism, and those who support or associate with designated terrorists or terrorist organizations. ([state.gov][29])

---

# 9. Financing, facilitation, media, and training layer

## 9.1 Financing patterns

### AQAP

AQAP’s financing has included bank robbery/looting, taxation/extortion during Mukalla control, kidnapping, smuggling, tribal deals, and wartime criminal economies. AP’s Batarfi obituary-style reporting notes AQAP’s long decline but continued funding through criminal activity. ([AP News][5])

### AQIM / JNIM

AQIM and JNIM rely heavily on kidnapping-for-ransom, extortion, trafficking/smuggling taxation, protection money, and control of routes. NCTC states AQIM revenue includes extortion, kidnapping for ransom, and donations; State says JNIM receives funding from extortion, smugglers, traffickers, and kidnapping ransom. ([Director of National Intelligence][11])

### al-Shabaab

Al-Shabaab is especially notable for systematic taxation/extortion in Somalia: checkpoints, ports, businesses, agriculture, transport, clan/community levies, and coercive zakat-style collection. NCTC identifies Mahad Karate as finance chief and amniyat commander, linking finance and internal security. ([Director of National Intelligence][20])

### AQIS

AQIS is smaller and more dependent on sanctuary, facilitation, and alignment with Taliban-linked networks. NCTC says AQIS has longstanding ties with the Taliban and receives overarching guidance from Al-Qaeda senior leadership. ([Director of National Intelligence][22])

## 9.2 Media infrastructure

### Al-Sahab Media

Al-Qaeda Core’s central media arm. Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi is publicly identified by FBI/RFJ as a longtime al-Sahab director and external communications figure. ([FBI][30])

### Al-Malahem Media

AQAP’s media wing. Associated with AQAP statements, video releases, and *Inspire*-era messaging.

### Inspire magazine

AQAP English-language magazine linked to Anwar al-Awlaki / Samir Khan-era propaganda. For your platform, catalogue issues only as propaganda artifacts without reproducing operationally useful material.

### Al-Kataib Media

Al-Shabaab’s media wing. Used for attack claims, videos, ideological messaging, and recruitment.

### AQIS media

AQIS messaging has included South Asia-focused propaganda, claims, and Urdu/Bengali/English materials.

### Hurras al-Din / Syria media

Hurras and related Al-Qaeda loyalist Syria outlets produced statements around Al-Qaeda loyalty, HTS disputes, and Syria battlefield claims.

## 9.3 Training camp geography — broad only

Use broad historical geography, not current precise locations:

* **Afghanistan:** Khost, Nangarhar, Kandahar, Kabul area, pre-9/11 training complex geography.
* **Pakistan:** FATA/North Waziristan/South Waziristan historical refuge and training/facilitation zones.
* **Yemen:** Abyan, Shabwah, Hadramawt, al-Bayda broad AQAP zones.
* **Mali/Sahel:** Northern Mali, central Mali, border zones with Burkina Faso/Niger, desert mobility corridors.
* **Somalia:** Southern and central Somalia; Jubba/Shabelle/Bay/Bakool broad areas.
* **Syria:** Idlib and northwest Syria for Nusra/Hurras-era networks.

NCTC’s current Al-Qaeda page gives the broad active affiliate bases: AQAP in Yemen, AQIM/JNIM in Mali, al-Shabaab in Somalia, AQIS in Afghanistan and Pakistan, former Hurras al-Din in Syria, and some senior/lower-level members in Iran. ([Director of National Intelligence][1])

---

# 10. What this part now adds to your framework

This tranche adds the missing **regional event spine**:

* AQAP: prison breaks, Mukalla seizure, aviation plots, Yemeni military/government attacks, leadership succession.
* AQIM: GSPC lineage, Algerian/Sahel attacks, kidnappings, In Amenas, hotel attacks, Droukdel → al-Anabi transition.
* JNIM: merger structure, siege/blockade pattern, Mali/Burkina expansion, 2025–2026 escalation.
* al-Shabaab: Somalia/Kenya/Uganda operational history, amniyat/finance wing, major attacks through 2025.
* AQIS: South Asia leadership, Karachi naval plot, Bangladesh assassinations, Taliban safe-haven connection.
* Syria: Nusra → JFS → HTS split; Hurras formation and dissolution.
* First arrests/sanctions/designations: FTO/SDGT layer and major captures.

The next logical part should be **Part 3: people and family-network depth** — Bin Laden family, Zawahiri/Egyptian Islamic Jihad network, Haqqani family tree, AQAP/AQIS/AQIM/JNIM/al-Shabaab individual rosters, Benghazi screenshot individuals, and the Iran-based senior leadership network.

[30]: https://www.fbi.gov/wanted/terrorinfo/abd-al-rahman-al-maghrebi?utm_source=chatgpt.com "ABD AL RAHMAN AL MAGHREBI — FBI"


Absolutely. **Part 3 is the people/family/network layer**: who the major individuals are, how they connect by blood, marriage, command, ideology, media, sanctuary, and affiliate structure. I am keeping this to public-source material and broad locations only. For chart-only claims from TIC, I’ll label them as chart claims because the chart itself says it is educational/public-source and not classified or authoritative intelligence.

# PART 3 — AL-QAEDA PEOPLE, FAMILY NETWORKS, INNER CIRCLES, AND AFFILIATE ROSTERS

## 1. The human structure of Al-Qaeda

Al-Qaeda’s real network is not just “leader → soldiers.” It is built through overlapping layers:

**Family lineage:** Bin Laden family, Haqqani family, Zawahiri in-laws, marriage alliances, sons used as symbolic successors.

**Veteran cadre:** Afghan jihad veterans, Egyptian Islamic Jihad members, former trainers, security chiefs, military committee members, media leaders.

**Affiliate emirs:** AQAP, AQIS, AQIM, JNIM, al-Shabaab, Hurras al-Din / former Syria affiliate leadership.

**Sanctuary relationships:** Taliban, Haqqani Network, Afghanistan/Pakistan border networks, Iran-based senior leadership.

**Media and communications:** al-Sahab, al-Malahem, al-Kataib, AQIS media, Inspire/Resurgence-style propaganda.

**Finance/facilitation:** Iran-based facilitation pipeline, Gulf fundraising channels, kidnapping/extortion networks, affiliate tax systems.

The 9/11 Commission described pre-9/11 Al-Qaeda as an organization with intelligence, military, financial, political, media/propaganda arms, plus a Shura council, while also relying heavily on autonomous field commanders. ([GovInfo][1])

---

# 2. Bin Laden family tree and operationally relevant family members

## Osama bin Laden

**Full name:** Osama bin Muhammad bin Awad bin Laden
**Born:** 1957, Riyadh, Saudi Arabia
**Died:** May 2, 2011, Abbottabad, Pakistan
**Father:** Muhammad Awad bin Laden
**Mother:** Hamida al-Attas
**Role:** Founder and first emir of Al-Qaeda
**Leadership period:** roughly 1988–2011
**Family scale:** Public profiles describe Osama as one of 52 children of Saudi construction magnate Muhammad Awad bin Laden. ([ABC17NEWS][2])

Osama’s family matters because Al-Qaeda used **bloodline legitimacy** heavily after 2011. His sons **Saad**, **Hamza**, and **Khalid** became relevant either as reported militants, symbolic successors, or raid casualties, while other children and wives are mainly relevant for post-9/11 movement, Iran/Pakistan/Afghanistan family detention questions, and Abbottabad context. CNN’s public profile lists six marriages or marriage events and more than twenty publicly named children. ([ABC17NEWS][2])

## Osama’s wives and children

### Najwa Ghanem

**Relationship:** First wife and first cousin.
**Marriage:** 1974; separated shortly before 9/11 according to public profiles.
**Publicly listed children:** Abdullah, Abdul Rahman, Saad, Omar, Osman, Muhammad, Fatima, Iman, Ladin “Bakir,” Rukhaiya, and Nour. ([ABC17NEWS][2])

**Operational relevance:** Najwa’s children include **Saad**, one of the most operationally discussed Bin Laden sons, and **Omar**, who became publicly known for speaking about family life rather than for Al-Qaeda command. Most of Najwa’s children should be treated as **family-context nodes**, not operational Al-Qaeda nodes unless a specific public-source link exists.

### Khadijah

**Marriage:** 1983–1995, divorced.
**Publicly listed children:** Ali, Amer, and Aisha. ([ABC17NEWS][2])

**Operational relevance:** These children are not central Al-Qaeda leadership figures in public-source reporting.

### Khairiah Sabar

**Marriage:** 1985 until Osama’s death.
**Publicly listed child:** Hamza bin Laden. ([ABC17NEWS][2])

**Operational relevance:** Khairiah matters because **Hamza bin Laden** became Al-Qaeda’s most important bloodline-successor figure after Osama’s death.

### Siham Sabar

**Marriage:** 1987 until Osama’s death.
**Publicly listed children:** Kadhija/Khadijah, Khalid, Miriam, and Sumaiya. ([ABC17NEWS][2])

**Operational relevance:** Her son **Khalid bin Laden** was killed during the Abbottabad raid; he is relevant as a raid casualty and family-protection node, not as a major independent Al-Qaeda emir in most public-source accounts.

### Unknown wife

**Marriage:** 1996; annulled within days.
**Children:** None listed in CNN’s public profile. ([ABC17NEWS][2])

### Amal Ahmed al-Sadah

**Marriage:** 2000 until Osama’s death.
**Publicly listed children:** Safiyah, Aasia, Ibrahim, Zainab, and Hussain. ([ABC17NEWS][2])

**Operational relevance:** Amal is important because she was part of the Abbottabad household context. Her children are primarily family-context nodes.

---

# 3. Operationally relevant Bin Laden children

## Saad bin Laden

**Born:** 1979
**Mother:** Najwa Ghanem
**Status:** Publicly reported dead, around 2009
**Reported death:** U.S. drone strike in Pakistan, though early public reporting carried uncertainty.
**Role:** Reported Al-Qaeda family/leadership figure.

**Network importance:** Saad appears in the “possible successor / senior family figure” category, but he did not become the durable post-Osama symbolic figure that Hamza became. The TIC chart labels Saad as “1st Deputy Emir Al-Qaeda,” but that title should be marked as a **chart claim** unless independently corroborated.

## Hamza bin Laden

**Born:** 1989 or 1991, depending on public source.
**Mother:** Khairiah Sabar.
**Status:** Dead, according to U.S. government.
**Death:** The White House confirmed in 2019 that Hamza was killed in a U.S. counterterrorism operation in the Afghanistan/Pakistan region. ([The Guardian][3])
**Role:** High-ranking Al-Qaeda member and symbolic heir.

**Marriage / family tie:** The Guardian reported, based on interviews with Osama bin Laden’s half-brothers, that Hamza married a daughter of **Mohammed Atta**, the lead 9/11 hijacker. That should be treated as **family-reported / media-reported**, not a civil-record-confirmed fact. ([The Guardian][3])

**Network importance:** Hamza’s importance was symbolic first and operational second. He connected Al-Qaeda’s post-2011 messaging to Osama’s bloodline, and public reporting described him as having spent years in Iran, sometimes under restrictions or house arrest, before reappearing in Al-Qaeda messaging. ([The Guardian][3])

## Khalid bin Laden

**Born:** 1989
**Mother:** Siham Sabar
**Status:** Dead
**Death:** Killed during the Abbottabad raid in 2011.
**Network importance:** Khalid is relevant to the Abbottabad household and raid chronology, not as a major public Al-Qaeda commander.

## Omar bin Laden

**Born:** 1981
**Mother:** Najwa Ghanem
**Status:** Living public figure.
**Role:** Family witness/commentator, not an Al-Qaeda commander.
**Network importance:** Omar is useful for family-history context, especially because he publicly distanced himself from his father’s terrorism.

## Muhammad bin Laden

**Born:** 1985
**Mother:** Najwa Ghanem
**Possible marriage tie:** Public reporting and biographies note that a daughter of **Mohammed Atef / Abu Hafs al-Masri**, Al-Qaeda’s military chief, married one of Bin Laden’s sons; the New Yorker specifically describes Atef as a military-wing leader whose daughter eventually married one of Bin Laden’s sons. ([The New Yorker][4])

**Network importance:** This is a major family-command bridge: Bin Laden family ↔ Mohammed Atef family ↔ Al-Qaeda military wing.

---

# 4. Bin Laden extended family and non-operational relatives

Osama’s father, **Muhammad Awad bin Laden**, founded the Saudi Binladin Group and had a very large family. CNN describes Osama as the 17th of 52 children. The extended Bin Laden family is relevant because of social status, wealth, Saudi elite proximity, and the contrast between the mainstream Saudi business family and Osama’s militant breakaway path. ([ABC17NEWS][2])

Important distinction for your OSINT platform: **do not treat the Bin Laden family as Al-Qaeda by default.** Most relatives were not Al-Qaeda members. Operational relevance should be attached only when a public source connects a relative to Al-Qaeda activity, detention, public messaging, marriage alliance, or movement through Iran/Afghanistan/Pakistan.

---

# 5. Zawahiri family, Egyptian Islamic Jihad, and the Egyptian cadre

## Ayman al-Zawahiri

**Full name:** Ayman Mohammed Rabie al-Zawahiri
**Born:** June 19, 1951, Egypt
**Died:** July 31, 2022, Kabul, Afghanistan
**Role:** Egyptian physician, Egyptian Islamic Jihad leader, Al-Qaeda ideologue, second Al-Qaeda emir
**Leadership period:** 2011–2022
**Death:** Killed by U.S. drone strike in Kabul. ([Encyclopedia Britannica][5])

Zawahiri’s network matters because it fused **Egyptian Islamic Jihad’s ideological and clandestine cadre** into Al-Qaeda Core. Britannica says Egyptian Islamic Jihad coalesced in the late 1970s, was responsible for the assassination of Anwar Sadat, allied with Al-Qaeda in the late 1990s, and merged with Al-Qaeda in 2001. ([Encyclopedia Britannica][6])

## Zawahiri family background

Public reporting describes Zawahiri as coming from a prominent Egyptian family. The New Yorker notes that he and his twin sister were born on June 19, 1951, and that his siblings included Heba, Mohammed, and Hussein, with several family members becoming doctors or professionals. ([The New Yorker][4])

**Operationally relevant family link:** Zawahiri’s daughter **Nabila al-Zawahiri** is publicly identified as the wife of **Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi / Muhammad Abbatay**, a senior Al-Qaeda communications and media figure. Rewards for Justice states that al-Maghrebi is Zawahiri’s son-in-law and senior adviser. ([FBI][7])

This is one of the clearest documented family-command links in late Al-Qaeda Core:

**Ayman al-Zawahiri → daughter Nabila al-Zawahiri → Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi → al-Sahab / External Communications Office / Iran-based leadership.**

---

# 6. Egyptian Islamic Jihad network

## Muhammad Abd al-Salam Faraj

**Role:** Early Egyptian Islamic Jihad ideologue.
**Text:** Author of *The Neglected Duty*.
**Importance:** EIJ ideological origin node. Britannica identifies Faraj’s treatise as the group’s ideological platform. ([Encyclopedia Britannica][6])

## Abbud al-Zumar

**Role:** Egyptian Islamic Jihad figure connected to the Sadat assassination era.
**Importance:** Early EIJ leadership/legal-prisoner node.

## Ayman al-Zawahiri

**Role:** Became EIJ’s dominant leader and later merged the network into Al-Qaeda.
**Importance:** Bridge from Egyptian clandestine militancy to Al-Qaeda global jihad.

## Sayyid Imam al-Sharif / Dr. Fadl

**Born:** 1950
**Role:** Early jihadist ideologue and former associate of Zawahiri.
**Importance:** He later became one of Al-Qaeda’s most significant ideological critics from prison, creating an internal intellectual challenge to Zawahiri and Al-Qaeda’s violence. Public profiles describe him as one of Zawahiri’s oldest associates and a major figure in the global jihad movement. ([Wikipedia][8])

## Mohammed Atef / Abu Hafs al-Masri

**Birth name:** Sobhi Abd al-Aziz Mohamed El Gohary Abu Sitta
**Born:** 1944, Egypt
**Died:** November 2001, Afghanistan
**Role:** Al-Qaeda military chief, deputy/close aide to Bin Laden
**Death:** Killed in U.S. airstrike in Afghanistan.
**Family tie:** A daughter of Atef reportedly married one of Bin Laden’s sons. ([Wikipedia][9])

**Network importance:** Atef is one of the most important nodes in the pre-9/11 Al-Qaeda command structure. The 9/11 Commission states that Bin Laden and Abu Hafs al-Masri / Mohammed Atef occupied undisputed leadership positions at the top of Al-Qaeda before 9/11. ([GovInfo][1])

## Sayf al-Adl

**Birth name / alias:** Mohammed Salah al-Din al-Halim Zaydan
**Birth dates used:** FBI lists April 11, 1960 and April 11, 1963.
**Nationality:** Egyptian
**Status:** Alive / wanted
**Current broad location:** Iran-based, according to U.S. public sources.
**Role:** Senior Al-Qaeda leader, military committee head, Shura member, widely viewed as de facto leader after Zawahiri. ([Rewards For Justice][10])

**Network importance:** Sayf al-Adl is the military-professional node of Al-Qaeda’s Egyptian cadre. Rewards for Justice describes him as a senior Al-Qaeda leader, member of the Majlis al-Shura, head of the military committee, and indicted figure in the 1998 East Africa Embassy bombings. ([Rewards For Justice][10])

## Abu Muhammad al-Masri / Abdullah Ahmed Abdullah

**Nationality:** Egyptian
**Status:** Publicly reported dead
**Reported death:** Killed in Tehran in 2020, according to public reporting.
**Role:** Senior Al-Qaeda figure associated with the East Africa Embassy bombings and Iran-based senior cadre.
**Network importance:** Old-guard Egyptian Al-Qaeda leadership, Iran-based after 9/11, bridge between original Al-Qaeda Core and later leadership succession.

## Abu al-Khayr al-Masri

**Name:** Ahmad Hasan Abu al-Khayr al-Masri / Abdullah Abd al-Rahman Muhammad Rajab Abd al-Rahman
**Born:** 1957, Egypt
**Died:** February 26, 2017, Idlib Governorate, Syria
**Role:** Al-Qaeda deputy / senior Syria-based emissary
**Family tie:** Public reporting described him as a son-in-law of Osama bin Laden.
**Death:** Killed by U.S. drone strike in Syria. ([The Guardian][11])

**Network importance:** Abu al-Khayr links old Egyptian Al-Qaeda leadership, Bin Laden family marriage ties, the Iran detainee/release network, and Syria/Nusra-era Al-Qaeda strategy.

## Thirwat Shehata

**Born:** 1960, Egypt
**Role:** Core Egyptian Islamic Jihad figure.
**Network importance:** Useful as an EIJ roster node, especially for Egyptian exile and legal/proxy networks. Public profiles identify him as a core EIJ member. ([Wikipedia][12])

---

# 7. Iran-based Al-Qaeda senior leadership and facilitation network

This is one of the most important “hidden architecture” layers. NCTC says some senior Al-Qaeda leaders and lower-level members reside in Iran. ([Director of National Intelligence][13])

## Sayf al-Adl

**Role:** De facto senior leader / military committee head.
**Location:** Iran-based in public U.S. descriptions.
**Importance:** Post-Zawahiri leadership problem. His Iran location complicates formal succession because it creates ideological and operational issues for Al-Qaeda’s global leadership claim. ([AP News][14])

## Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi / Muhammad Abbatay

**Born:** Public sources vary, around 1970 or 1975.
**Nationality:** Moroccan.
**Role:** al-Sahab media director, External Communications Office leader, senior adviser to Zawahiri, son-in-law of Zawahiri.
**Associated countries:** FBI lists Morocco, Germany, Pakistan, Afghanistan, and Iran as countries associated with him. ([FBI][7])

**Network importance:** al-Maghrebi is a late-stage command-and-communications node. Rewards for Justice says he served as Al-Qaeda’s “general manager” in Afghanistan and Pakistan from 2012, later relocated to Iran, and coordinated with affiliates through Al-Qaeda’s External Communications Office. ([Wikipedia][15])

## Yasin al-Suri / Ezedin Abdel Aziz Khalil

**Born:** Public sources list 1982, Syria.
**Role:** Iran-based Al-Qaeda facilitator and financier.
**Status:** Wanted / public status uncertain.
**Function:** Money and recruit movement through Iran to support Al-Qaeda senior leadership. Rewards for Justice says al-Suri moved money and recruits from across the Middle East into Iran and then onward to Pakistan, and that Iranian authorities allowed him to operate inside Iran since 2005. ([Rewards For Justice][16])

**Network importance:** Yasin al-Suri is one of the clearest public examples of the **Iran facilitation pipeline**: recruits, funds, family movement, and senior leadership support.

## Muhsin al-Fadhli

**Status:** Dead; killed in Syria in 2015.
**Role:** Former leader of Al-Qaeda’s Iran-based facilitation network; later associated with the so-called Khorasan Group in Syria.
**Network importance:** Treasury and public reporting identify him as part of the Iran-based Al-Qaeda facilitation environment and later Syria external-operations concern. ([Wikipedia][17])

## Atiyah Abd al-Rahman

**Nationality:** Libyan
**Died:** 2011, Pakistan
**Role:** Senior Al-Qaeda manager / Bin Laden chief-of-staff type figure.
**Iran connection:** Treasury says Yasin al-Suri’s arrangement with Iran operated with the knowledge of Atiyah Abd al-Rahman. ([U.S. Department of the Treasury][18])

---

# 8. Haqqani family structure and Al-Qaeda relevance

The Haqqani Network is not an Al-Qaeda branch, but it is one of the most important Al-Qaeda-connected family/militant networks. NCTC says Jalaluddin Haqqani was a known associate of Bin Laden and one of Bin Laden’s closest mentors during his formative Afghan-war years; NCTC also says Jalaluddin’s son Sirajuddin currently leads day-to-day activity with close relatives. ([ODNI][19])

## Jalaluddin Haqqani

**Born:** Public estimates vary, often around 1939.
**Died:** 2018.
**Role:** Founder of Haqqani Network; Afghan mujahideen commander; Taliban minister in the 1990s.
**Al-Qaeda connection:** Mentor/associate of Osama bin Laden during the 1980s Afghan jihad. ([ODNI][19])

**Network importance:** Jalaluddin is a bridge between the anti-Soviet Afghan jihad, Arab foreign fighters, Bin Laden’s formation, Taliban governance, and the later Haqqani-Taliban insurgency.

## Sirajuddin Haqqani

**Relationship:** Son of Jalaluddin Haqqani.
**Role:** Leader of Haqqani Network; Taliban acting interior minister after 2021.
**Status:** Alive.
**Al-Qaeda relevance:** Important as the modern Haqqani node inside Taliban governance. NCTC says Sirajuddin leads the group’s day-to-day activity with close relatives. ([ODNI][19])

**OSINT significance:** Sirajuddin represents the post-2021 convergence of militant family network, Taliban state power, security ministry control, and historical Al-Qaeda sanctuary concerns.

## Khalil al-Rahman Haqqani

**Born:** Public sources often list 1966.
**Died:** December 11, 2024, Kabul.
**Death:** Killed in an IS-KP-claimed suicide bombing.
**Relationship:** Brother of Jalaluddin Haqqani; uncle of Sirajuddin Haqqani.
**Role:** Senior Haqqani figure; Taliban minister for refugees and repatriation after 2021. ([AP News][20])

**Al-Qaeda relevance:** State’s RFJ notice described the Haqqani Network as allied with Al-Qaeda and the Afghan Taliban and offered rewards for Haqqani leaders including Khalil, Aziz, Yahya, Abdul Rauf Zakir, and Sirajuddin. ([State Department][21])

## Nasiruddin Haqqani

**Relationship:** Son of Jalaluddin; brother of Sirajuddin.
**Status:** Dead; killed in Islamabad in 2013.
**Role:** Financier/emissary.
**Network importance:** Fundraising and Gulf/Arab-world linkage node. Public summaries describe him as a key financier and emissary of the network. ([Wikipedia][22])

## Badruddin Haqqani

**Relationship:** Son of Jalaluddin; brother of Sirajuddin.
**Status:** Dead; reportedly killed in 2012.
**Role:** Senior operational commander.
**Network importance:** One of the most important military-operational Haqqani sons during the insurgency era.

## Anas Haqqani

**Relationship:** Son of Jalaluddin; brother of Sirajuddin.
**Status:** Alive.
**Role:** Public/political Haqqani figure.
**Network importance:** Useful for the post-2021 political face of the Haqqani family, but not an Al-Qaeda leadership node.

## Aziz Haqqani / Abdul Aziz Abbasin

**Role:** Haqqani Network leader/commander.
**Status:** Public status unclear.
**RFJ connection:** State authorized reward offers for Aziz Haqqani as one of several key Haqqani Network leaders. ([State Department][21])

## Yahya Haqqani

**Role:** Senior Haqqani Network member involved in military, financial, and propaganda activities according to public summaries.
**Family link:** Public profiles describe Yahya as Sirajuddin’s brother-in-law.
**RFJ connection:** State authorized a reward for Yahya Haqqani in 2014. ([State Department][21])

## Haji Mali Khan

**Role:** Senior Haqqani commander; uncle to Sirajuddin and Badruddin according to public summaries.
**Status:** Captured by ISAF in 2011; reportedly released in a prisoner swap in 2019.
**Network importance:** Older-generation command/emissary node. ([Wikipedia][22])

## Abdul Rauf Zakir / Qari Zakir

**Role:** Haqqani Network / Taliban military figure; State included Abdul Rauf Zakir in RFJ reward offers for Haqqani leaders. ([State Department][21])
**Chart claim:** TIC labels Qari Zakir as “AQ Shura Council Member” and “Security Committee leader,” but that exact Al-Qaeda Shura title should be marked as chart claim unless corroborated independently.

---

# 9. AQAP people roster — leadership, media, bombmaking, detention, deaths

NCTC’s current AQAP profile identifies **Sa’d Muhammad Atif / Saad bin Atef al-Awlaki** as overall AQAP leader, **Ibrahim al-Banna** as a founding AQAP member and chief-of-security figure, and **Ibrahim Ahmed Mahmoud al-Qosi** as AQAP spokesperson and former Guantanamo detainee who worked directly for Bin Laden. ([Director of National Intelligence][23])

## Core AQAP leaders

**Nasir al-Wuhayshi / Nasir al-Wahishi** — founder and emir; former Bin Laden aide; killed in 2015.
**Sa’id al-Shihri** — deputy; former Guantanamo detainee; killed/confirmed dead around 2013.
**Qasim al-Raymi / Qassim al-Rimi** — military commander, later emir; killed in 2020.
**Khalid Batarfi** — emir after al-Raymi; AQAP announced his death in 2024 and named Saad al-Awlaki successor. ([Director of National Intelligence][23])
**Saad bin Atef al-Awlaki / Sa’d Muhammad Atif** — current AQAP leader in public profiles; NCTC says he has publicly called for attacks against the United States and kidnapped Americans in Yemen. ([Director of National Intelligence][23])

## AQAP media / propaganda people

**Anwar al-Awlaki** — AQAP ideologue and English-language propagandist; killed in 2011.
**Samir Khan** — AQAP media figure associated with English-language propaganda; killed in 2011.
**Ibrahim al-Qosi** — AQAP spokesperson; former Guantanamo detainee and former Bin Laden associate. ([Director of National Intelligence][23])
**Nasser al-Ansi** — AQAP propagandist/official involved in AQAP’s public messaging before being killed in 2015.

## AQAP security / explosives / operations figures

**Ibrahim al-Banna** — founding AQAP member; served as chief of security and provided military/security guidance. ([Director of National Intelligence][23])
**Ibrahim Hassan al-Asiri** — AQAP bombmaker; publicly linked to aviation plots; reported killed.
**Fahd al-Quso** — Yemen Al-Qaeda/AQAP-linked operative tied to USS Cole case; killed in 2012.
**Jamal al-Badawi** — USS Cole participant; killed in 2019.

## AQAP network character

AQAP is a hybrid of Al-Qaeda ideology, Yemeni local insurgency, former detainee networks, propaganda/media capability, tribal/criminal financing, and repeated leadership succession after strikes. The TIC chart places Ibrahim al-Banna and Saad bin Atef al-Awlaki inside the Al-Qaeda-centered framework, labeling them as senior AQAP / AQ Shura-linked figures; treat the AQAP roles as public-source supported and the exact Shura titles as chart claims.

---

# 10. AQIS people roster — South Asia, Taliban-safe-haven, and recruitment layer

NCTC lists AQIS as primarily based in Afghanistan, operating in Afghanistan, Pakistan, India, and Bangladesh, with fewer than 200 members as of May 2024; it says AQIS receives overarching guidance from Al-Qaeda senior leaders and has longstanding ties with the Taliban. ([Director of National Intelligence][24])

## AQIS leaders

**Asim Umar** — founder; killed in a U.S.-Afghan raid in 2019.
**Usama Mahmood / Abu Zar / Osama Mehmood** — AQIS leader since 2019; formerly spokesperson.
**Yahya Ghauri / Atif Yahya Ghouri** — AQIS deputy amir.
**Muhammad Maruf** — recruitment head. ([Director of National Intelligence][24])

## AQIS early cadre / associated figures

**Ustad Ahmad Farooq** — early AQIS deputy/media figure; killed in 2015.
**Qari Imran** — early AQIS Shura/commander figure; killed in 2015.
**Bangladesh-linked attack cells** — connected to AQIS claims against secular writers, publishers, activists, and LGBT rights figures, especially 2015–2016.

## AQIS-Taliban connection

NCTC says AQIS pledged allegiance to Taliban leader **Haybatullah Akhundzada** partly to secure safe haven in Afghanistan. That makes AQIS a direct bridge between Al-Qaeda Core, Taliban Afghanistan, and South Asian jihadist networks. ([Director of National Intelligence][24])

---

# 11. AQIM / GSPC / JNIM people roster

## AQIM / GSPC lineage figures

**Hassan Hattab** — founder of GSPC, direct predecessor to AQIM.
**Nabil Sahraoui** — GSPC leader after Hattab; killed in 2004.
**Abdelmalek Droukdel / Abu Musab Abdel Wadoud** — AQIM emir until killed by French forces in Mali in 2020.
**Abu Ubaydah Yusuf al-Anabi / Yazid Mebarek** — current AQIM emir; CEP says he previously led AQIM’s Council of Notables, served on AQIM’s Shura Council, and was AQIM media chief. ([Counter Extremism Project][25])

## AQIM / Sahel commanders

**Mokhtar Belmokhtar / Khalid Abu al-Abbas** — al-Murabitun / AQIM-linked Saharan commander; status long contested. The TIC chart calls him “The One-Eyed,” “Nelson,” and “The Uncatchable,” and explicitly notes uncertainty over death claims.
**Abdelhamid Abou Zeid** — AQIM Sahara commander; killed in Mali in 2013.
**Yahya Abu al-Hammam / Djamel Okacha** — AQIM Sahara commander; killed in 2019.
**Ali Maychou** — JNIM religious/leadership figure; U.S. designated him as a JNIM leader in 2019. ([Director of National Intelligence][26])

## JNIM leaders

**Iyad ag Ghali** — founder and emir of JNIM; prominent AQIM-linked Sahel figure.
**Amadou Kouffa** — JNIM deputy/founding member; founder of Katibat Macina.
**Jafar Dicko** — JNIM deputy leader in Burkina Faso according to NCTC. ([Director of National Intelligence][27])

## Merger groups and their human significance

**Ansar al-Din** — Iyad ag Ghali’s vehicle; Tuareg/northern Mali Islamist insurgency.
**Al-Murabitun** — Belmokhtar-linked network; hostage-taking and Saharan attack lineage.
**Macina Liberation Front / Katibat Macina** — Amadou Kouffa’s central Mali/Fulani mobilization structure.
**AQIM Sahara Emirate** — AQIM’s direct branch into JNIM.

JNIM is important because it translates Al-Qaeda’s older North African structure into a local Sahel insurgency coalition. NCTC says JNIM formed in 2017 by merging Ansar al-Din, al-Murabitun, Macina Liberation Front, and AQIM’s Sahara Emirate. ([Director of National Intelligence][27])

---

# 12. Al-Shabaab people roster — emir, finance, amniyat, foreign fighters

NCTC lists **Ahmed Diriye / Abu Ubaidah** as al-Shabaab’s amir since 2014, **Abukar Ali Adan** as deputy amir, and **Mahad Karate** as finance chief and commander of the amniyat intelligence/security wing. ([Counter Extremism Project][28])

## Al-Shabaab senior leadership

**Ahmed Abdi Godane / Mukhtar Abu Zubair** — former emir; killed in 2014; central to the formal Al-Qaeda pledge.
**Ahmed Diriye / Abu Ubaidah** — current emir since 2014; RFJ offers a reward for information on him. ([AFRICOM][29])
**Abukar Ali Adan** — deputy amir / former military chief.
**Mahad Karate / Abdirahman Mohamed Warsame** — finance chief and amniyat commander; State/RFJ reward increased to up to $10 million. ([Counter Extremism Project][28])

## Al-Shabaab foreign-fighter / U.S.-person node

**Jehad Serwan Mostafa**
**Aliases:** Ahmed Gurey, Anwar al-Amriki, Emir Anwar.
**Role:** U.S. citizen; senior al-Shabaab figure.
**FBI description:** Joined al-Shabaab around 2006; served as military instructor, foreign-fighter leader, media propagandist, intermediary with other terrorist organizations, and leader in explosives use. ([AFRICOM][29])

The TIC chart also lists Jehad Serwan Mostafa as “Emir Anwar / Ahmed Gurey / Anwar al-Amriki,” senior commander, and al-Shabaab member since 2006.

## Al-Shabaab organizational wings

**Amniyat:** Internal intelligence/security, assassinations, counterintelligence, infiltration, urban attack facilitation. Mahad Karate is publicly identified as commander.
**Finance wing:** Extortion, taxation, checkpoint revenue, business levies, local coercion.
**Military wing:** Rural insurgency, base raids, ambushes.
**Media wing:** al-Kataib Media Foundation.

---

# 13. Syria people roster — Nusra, HTS split, and Hurras al-Din

## Abu Muhammad al-Julani / Ahmed al-Sharaa

**Role:** Former emir of Jabhat al-Nusra; later leader of HTS.
**Al-Qaeda connection:** Former Al-Qaeda Syria affiliate leader who later broke away from Al-Qaeda.
**Chart note:** TIC says Julani led Tahrir al-Sham and previously served as emir of al-Nusra, with ties to both Al-Qaeda and IS; treat the “ties” phrasing as broad chart characterization.

## Abu Khalid al-Suri

**Role:** Al-Qaeda envoy in Syria; sent to mediate between Nusra and ISIS-related factions.
**Status:** Killed in 2014 by a rival rebel/jihadist attack.
**Network importance:** Direct emissary link between Zawahiri and Syria. ([Time][30])

## Abu al-Khayr al-Masri

**Role:** Al-Qaeda deputy / senior Syria-based figure.
**Status:** Killed in Syria in 2017.
**Family tie:** Public reporting describes him as Osama bin Laden’s son-in-law. ([The Guardian][11])

## Samir Hijazi / Faruq al-Suri

**Role:** Hurras al-Din amir in public profiles.
**Network importance:** Al-Qaeda loyalist after Nusra/HTS split.

## Sami al-Uraydi

**Role:** Hurras al-Din ideologue / religious figure.
**Network importance:** Theological legitimacy node for Al-Qaeda loyalists in Syria.

## Abu Hammam al-Shami

**Role:** Veteran Al-Qaeda/Hurras figure.
**Status:** Reported killed in 2020.
**Network importance:** Veteran operational bridge from older Al-Qaeda to Syria.

NCTC identifies Hurras al-Din as Al-Qaeda’s Syria affiliate from 2018 until it publicly announced dissolution in early 2025; former members remain relevant to the Al-Qaeda Syria file. ([Director of National Intelligence][13])
