# 15. Relationship clusters you should preserve

## Bin Laden family cluster

**Core:** Osama → wives → children.
**Operationally relevant:** Saad, Hamza, Khalid, Muhammad.
**Marriage links:** Hamza reportedly to Mohammed Atta’s daughter; one Bin Laden son to Mohammed Atef’s daughter; TIC chart also shows additional Hamza marriage links, but those should stay chart-claim unless verified. ([The Guardian][3])

## Zawahiri / Egyptian Islamic Jihad cluster

**Core:** Zawahiri → EIJ → Al-Qaeda merger.
**Family-command link:** Zawahiri → daughter Nabila → Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi.
**Military cadre:** Mohammed Atef, Sayf al-Adl, Abu Muhammad al-Masri, Abu al-Khayr al-Masri.
**Ideological dispute node:** Dr. Fadl / Sayyid Imam al-Sharif.

## Iran-based senior leadership cluster

**Core:** Sayf al-Adl, Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi, Yasin al-Suri.
**Older related nodes:** Atiyah Abd al-Rahman, Abu Muhammad al-Masri, Abu al-Khayr al-Masri, Muhsin al-Fadhli.
**Function:** leadership shelter, communications, funds/recruit movement, family movement, senior liaison.

## Haqqani family cluster

**Core:** Jalaluddin → Sirajuddin, Badruddin, Nasiruddin, Anas.
**Sibling/uncle layer:** Khalil Haqqani, Haji Mali Khan.
**Command/finance layer:** Yahya Haqqani, Aziz Haqqani, Abdul Rauf Zakir.
**Al-Qaeda link:** Jalaluddin as Bin Laden mentor; Haqqani Network as Taliban-Al-Qaeda sanctuary bridge.

## AQAP cluster

**Core leaders:** Wuhayshi → Raymi → Batarfi → Saad al-Awlaki.
**Security/media:** Ibrahim al-Banna, Ibrahim al-Qosi, Anwar al-Awlaki, Samir Khan, al-Asiri.
**Legacy Yemen nodes:** Fahd al-Quso, Jamal al-Badawi.

## AQIS cluster

**Core:** Asim Umar → Usama Mahmood.
**Deputy/recruitment:** Yahya Ghauri, Muhammad Maruf.
**Safe-haven relation:** Taliban allegiance / Afghanistan-Pakistan base.

## AQIM / JNIM cluster

**AQIM lineage:** GIA environment → GSPC → AQIM.
**AQIM leaders:** Hattab, Sahraoui, Droukdel, al-Anabi.
**Sahel commanders:** Belmokhtar, Abou Zeid, Yahya Abu al-Hammam.
**JNIM merger:** Iyad ag Ghali, Amadou Kouffa, Jafar Dicko, Ali Maychou.

## Al-Shabaab cluster

**Leadership:** Godane → Ahmed Diriye.
**Finance/security:** Mahad Karate.
**Deputy:** Abukar Ali Adan.
**Foreign fighter:** Jehad Mostafa.
**Wings:** amniyat, finance, media/al-Kataib, military.

PART 3 — AL-QAEDA PEOPLE, FAMILY NETWORKS, INNER CIRCLES, AND AFFILIATE ROSTERS
1. The human structure of Al-Qaeda

Al-Qaeda’s real network is not just “leader → soldiers.” It is built through overlapping layers:

Family lineage: Bin Laden family, Haqqani family, Zawahiri in-laws, marriage alliances, sons used as symbolic successors.

Veteran cadre: Afghan jihad veterans, Egyptian Islamic Jihad members, former trainers, security chiefs, military committee members, media leaders.

Affiliate emirs: AQAP, AQIS, AQIM, JNIM, al-Shabaab, Hurras al-Din / former Syria affiliate leadership.

Sanctuary relationships: Taliban, Haqqani Network, Afghanistan/Pakistan border networks, Iran-based senior leadership.

Media and communications: al-Sahab, al-Malahem, al-Kataib, AQIS media, Inspire/Resurgence-style propaganda.

Finance/facilitation: Iran-based facilitation pipeline, Gulf fundraising channels, kidnapping/extortion networks, affiliate tax systems.

The 9/11 Commission described pre-9/11 Al-Qaeda as an organization with intelligence, military, financial, political, media/propaganda arms, plus a Shura council, while also relying heavily on autonomous field commanders.

2. Bin Laden family tree and operationally relevant family members
Osama bin Laden

Full name: Osama bin Muhammad bin Awad bin Laden
Born: 1957, Riyadh, Saudi Arabia
Died: May 2, 2011, Abbottabad, Pakistan
Father: Muhammad Awad bin Laden
Mother: Hamida al-Attas
Role: Founder and first emir of Al-Qaeda
Leadership period: roughly 1988–2011
Family scale: Public profiles describe Osama as one of 52 children of Saudi construction magnate Muhammad Awad bin Laden.

Osama’s family matters because Al-Qaeda used bloodline legitimacy heavily after 2011. His sons Saad, Hamza, and Khalid became relevant either as reported militants, symbolic successors, or raid casualties, while other children and wives are mainly relevant for post-9/11 movement, Iran/Pakistan/Afghanistan family detention questions, and Abbottabad context. CNN’s public profile lists six marriages or marriage events and more than twenty publicly named children.

Osama’s wives and children
Najwa Ghanem

Relationship: First wife and first cousin.
Marriage: 1974; separated shortly before 9/11 according to public profiles.
Publicly listed children: Abdullah, Abdul Rahman, Saad, Omar, Osman, Muhammad, Fatima, Iman, Ladin “Bakir,” Rukhaiya, and Nour.

Operational relevance: Najwa’s children include Saad, one of the most operationally discussed Bin Laden sons, and Omar, who became publicly known for speaking about family life rather than for Al-Qaeda command. Most of Najwa’s children should be treated as family-context nodes, not operational Al-Qaeda nodes unless a specific public-source link exists.

Khadijah

Marriage: 1983–1995, divorced.
Publicly listed children: Ali, Amer, and Aisha.

Operational relevance: These children are not central Al-Qaeda leadership figures in public-source reporting.

Khairiah Sabar

Marriage: 1985 until Osama’s death.
Publicly listed child: Hamza bin Laden.

Operational relevance: Khairiah matters because Hamza bin Laden became Al-Qaeda’s most important bloodline-successor figure after Osama’s death.

Siham Sabar

Marriage: 1987 until Osama’s death.
Publicly listed children: Kadhija/Khadijah, Khalid, Miriam, and Sumaiya.

Operational relevance: Her son Khalid bin Laden was killed during the Abbottabad raid; he is relevant as a raid casualty and family-protection node, not as a major independent Al-Qaeda emir in most public-source accounts.

Unknown wife

Marriage: 1996; annulled within days.
Children: None listed in CNN’s public profile.

Amal Ahmed al-Sadah

Marriage: 2000 until Osama’s death.
Publicly listed children: Safiyah, Aasia, Ibrahim, Zainab, and Hussain.

Operational relevance: Amal is important because she was part of the Abbottabad household context. Her children are primarily family-context nodes.

3. Operationally relevant Bin Laden children
Saad bin Laden

Born: 1979
Mother: Najwa Ghanem
Status: Publicly reported dead, around 2009
Reported death: U.S. drone strike in Pakistan, though early public reporting carried uncertainty.
Role: Reported Al-Qaeda family/leadership figure.

Network importance: Saad appears in the “possible successor / senior family figure” category, but he did not become the durable post-Osama symbolic figure that Hamza became. The TIC chart labels Saad as “1st Deputy Emir Al-Qaeda,” but that title should be marked as a chart claim unless independently corroborated.

Hamza bin Laden

Born: 1989 or 1991, depending on public source.
Mother: Khairiah Sabar.
Status: Dead, according to U.S. government.
Death: The White House confirmed in 2019 that Hamza was killed in a U.S. counterterrorism operation in the Afghanistan/Pakistan region.
Role: High-ranking Al-Qaeda member and symbolic heir.

Marriage / family tie: The Guardian reported, based on interviews with Osama bin Laden’s half-brothers, that Hamza married a daughter of Mohammed Atta, the lead 9/11 hijacker. That should be treated as family-reported / media-reported, not a civil-record-confirmed fact.

Network importance: Hamza’s importance was symbolic first and operational second. He connected Al-Qaeda’s post-2011 messaging to Osama’s bloodline, and public reporting described him as having spent years in Iran, sometimes under restrictions or house arrest, before reappearing in Al-Qaeda messaging.

Khalid bin Laden

Born: 1989
Mother: Siham Sabar
Status: Dead
Death: Killed during the Abbottabad raid in 2011.
Network importance: Khalid is relevant to the Abbottabad household and raid chronology, not as a major public Al-Qaeda commander.

Omar bin Laden

Born: 1981
Mother: Najwa Ghanem
Status: Living public figure.
Role: Family witness/commentator, not an Al-Qaeda commander.
Network importance: Omar is useful for family-history context, especially because he publicly distanced himself from his father’s terrorism.

Muhammad bin Laden

Born: 1985
Mother: Najwa Ghanem
Possible marriage tie: Public reporting and biographies note that a daughter of Mohammed Atef / Abu Hafs al-Masri, Al-Qaeda’s military chief, married one of Bin Laden’s sons; the New Yorker specifically describes Atef as a military-wing leader whose daughter eventually married one of Bin Laden’s sons.

Network importance: This is a major family-command bridge: Bin Laden family ↔ Mohammed Atef family ↔ Al-Qaeda military wing.

4. Bin Laden extended family and non-operational relatives

Osama’s father, Muhammad Awad bin Laden, founded the Saudi Binladin Group and had a very large family. CNN describes Osama as the 17th of 52 children. The extended Bin Laden family is relevant because of social status, wealth, Saudi elite proximity, and the contrast between the mainstream Saudi business family and Osama’s militant breakaway path.

Important distinction for your OSINT platform: do not treat the Bin Laden family as Al-Qaeda by default. Most relatives were not Al-Qaeda members. Operational relevance should be attached only when a public source connects a relative to Al-Qaeda activity, detention, public messaging, marriage alliance, or movement through Iran/Afghanistan/Pakistan.

5. Zawahiri family, Egyptian Islamic Jihad, and the Egyptian cadre
Ayman al-Zawahiri

Full name: Ayman Mohammed Rabie al-Zawahiri
Born: June 19, 1951, Egypt
Died: July 31, 2022, Kabul, Afghanistan
Role: Egyptian physician, Egyptian Islamic Jihad leader, Al-Qaeda ideologue, second Al-Qaeda emir
Leadership period: 2011–2022
Death: Killed by U.S. drone strike in Kabul.

Zawahiri’s network matters because it fused Egyptian Islamic Jihad’s ideological and clandestine cadre into Al-Qaeda Core. Britannica says Egyptian Islamic Jihad coalesced in the late 1970s, was responsible for the assassination of Anwar Sadat, allied with Al-Qaeda in the late 1990s, and merged with Al-Qaeda in 2001.

Zawahiri family background

Public reporting describes Zawahiri as coming from a prominent Egyptian family. The New Yorker notes that he and his twin sister were born on June 19, 1951, and that his siblings included Heba, Mohammed, and Hussein, with several family members becoming doctors or professionals.

Operationally relevant family link: Zawahiri’s daughter Nabila al-Zawahiri is publicly identified as the wife of Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi / Muhammad Abbatay, a senior Al-Qaeda communications and media figure. Rewards for Justice states that al-Maghrebi is Zawahiri’s son-in-law and senior adviser.

This is one of the clearest documented family-command links in late Al-Qaeda Core:

Ayman al-Zawahiri → daughter Nabila al-Zawahiri → Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi → al-Sahab / External Communications Office / Iran-based leadership.

6. Egyptian Islamic Jihad network
Muhammad Abd al-Salam Faraj

Role: Early Egyptian Islamic Jihad ideologue.
Text: Author of The Neglected Duty.
Importance: EIJ ideological origin node. Britannica identifies Faraj’s treatise as the group’s ideological platform.

Abbud al-Zumar

Role: Egyptian Islamic Jihad figure connected to the Sadat assassination era.
Importance: Early EIJ leadership/legal-prisoner node.

Ayman al-Zawahiri

Role: Became EIJ’s dominant leader and later merged the network into Al-Qaeda.
Importance: Bridge from Egyptian clandestine militancy to Al-Qaeda global jihad.

Sayyid Imam al-Sharif / Dr. Fadl

Born: 1950
Role: Early jihadist ideologue and former associate of Zawahiri.
Importance: He later became one of Al-Qaeda’s most significant ideological critics from prison, creating an internal intellectual challenge to Zawahiri and Al-Qaeda’s violence. Public profiles describe him as one of Zawahiri’s oldest associates and a major figure in the global jihad movement.

Mohammed Atef / Abu Hafs al-Masri

Birth name: Sobhi Abd al-Aziz Mohamed El Gohary Abu Sitta
Born: 1944, Egypt
Died: November 2001, Afghanistan
Role: Al-Qaeda military chief, deputy/close aide to Bin Laden
Death: Killed in U.S. airstrike in Afghanistan.
Family tie: A daughter of Atef reportedly married one of Bin Laden’s sons.

Network importance: Atef is one of the most important nodes in the pre-9/11 Al-Qaeda command structure. The 9/11 Commission states that Bin Laden and Abu Hafs al-Masri / Mohammed Atef occupied undisputed leadership positions at the top of Al-Qaeda before 9/11.

Sayf al-Adl

Birth name / alias: Mohammed Salah al-Din al-Halim Zaydan
Birth dates used: FBI lists April 11, 1960 and April 11, 1963.
Nationality: Egyptian
Status: Alive / wanted
Current broad location: Iran-based, according to U.S. public sources.
Role: Senior Al-Qaeda leader, military committee head, Shura member, widely viewed as de facto leader after Zawahiri.

Network importance: Sayf al-Adl is the military-professional node of Al-Qaeda’s Egyptian cadre. Rewards for Justice describes him as a senior Al-Qaeda leader, member of the Majlis al-Shura, head of the military committee, and indicted figure in the 1998 East Africa Embassy bombings.

Abu Muhammad al-Masri / Abdullah Ahmed Abdullah

Nationality: Egyptian
Status: Publicly reported dead
Reported death: Killed in Tehran in 2020, according to public reporting.
Role: Senior Al-Qaeda figure associated with the East Africa Embassy bombings and Iran-based senior cadre.
Network importance: Old-guard Egyptian Al-Qaeda leadership, Iran-based after 9/11, bridge between original Al-Qaeda Core and later leadership succession.

Abu al-Khayr al-Masri

Name: Ahmad Hasan Abu al-Khayr al-Masri / Abdullah Abd al-Rahman Muhammad Rajab Abd al-Rahman
Born: 1957, Egypt
Died: February 26, 2017, Idlib Governorate, Syria
Role: Al-Qaeda deputy / senior Syria-based emissary
Family tie: Public reporting described him as a son-in-law of Osama bin Laden.
Death: Killed by U.S. drone strike in Syria.

Network importance: Abu al-Khayr links old Egyptian Al-Qaeda leadership, Bin Laden family marriage ties, the Iran detainee/release network, and Syria/Nusra-era Al-Qaeda strategy.

Thirwat Shehata

Born: 1960, Egypt
Role: Core Egyptian Islamic Jihad figure.
Network importance: Useful as an EIJ roster node, especially for Egyptian exile and legal/proxy networks. Public profiles identify him as a core EIJ member.

7. Iran-based Al-Qaeda senior leadership and facilitation network

This is one of the most important “hidden architecture” layers. NCTC says some senior Al-Qaeda leaders and lower-level members reside in Iran.

Sayf al-Adl

Role: De facto senior leader / military committee head.
Location: Iran-based in public U.S. descriptions.
Importance: Post-Zawahiri leadership problem. His Iran location complicates formal succession because it creates ideological and operational issues for Al-Qaeda’s global leadership claim.

Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi / Muhammad Abbatay

Born: Public sources vary, around 1970 or 1975.
Nationality: Moroccan.
Role: al-Sahab media director, External Communications Office leader, senior adviser to Zawahiri, son-in-law of Zawahiri.
Associated countries: FBI lists Morocco, Germany, Pakistan, Afghanistan, and Iran as countries associated with him.

Network importance: al-Maghrebi is a late-stage command-and-communications node. Rewards for Justice says he served as Al-Qaeda’s “general manager” in Afghanistan and Pakistan from 2012, later relocated to Iran, and coordinated with affiliates through Al-Qaeda’s External Communications Office.

Yasin al-Suri / Ezedin Abdel Aziz Khalil

Born: Public sources list 1982, Syria.
Role: Iran-based Al-Qaeda facilitator and financier.
Status: Wanted / public status uncertain.
Function: Money and recruit movement through Iran to support Al-Qaeda senior leadership. Rewards for Justice says al-Suri moved money and recruits from across the Middle East into Iran and then onward to Pakistan, and that Iranian authorities allowed him to operate inside Iran since 2005.

Network importance: Yasin al-Suri is one of the clearest public examples of the Iran facilitation pipeline: recruits, funds, family movement, and senior leadership support.

Muhsin al-Fadhli

Status: Dead; killed in Syria in 2015.
Role: Former leader of Al-Qaeda’s Iran-based facilitation network; later associated with the so-called Khorasan Group in Syria.
Network importance: Treasury and public reporting identify him as part of the Iran-based Al-Qaeda facilitation environment and later Syria external-operations concern.

Atiyah Abd al-Rahman

Nationality: Libyan
Died: 2011, Pakistan
Role: Senior Al-Qaeda manager / Bin Laden chief-of-staff type figure.
Iran connection: Treasury says Yasin al-Suri’s arrangement with Iran operated with the knowledge of Atiyah Abd al-Rahman.

8. Haqqani family structure and Al-Qaeda relevance

The Haqqani Network is not an Al-Qaeda branch, but it is one of the most important Al-Qaeda-connected family/militant networks. NCTC says Jalaluddin Haqqani was a known associate of Bin Laden and one of Bin Laden’s closest mentors during his formative Afghan-war years; NCTC also says Jalaluddin’s son Sirajuddin currently leads day-to-day activity with close relatives.

Jalaluddin Haqqani

Born: Public estimates vary, often around 1939.
Died: 2018.
Role: Founder of Haqqani Network; Afghan mujahideen commander; Taliban minister in the 1990s.
Al-Qaeda connection: Mentor/associate of Osama bin Laden during the 1980s Afghan jihad.

Network importance: Jalaluddin is a bridge between the anti-Soviet Afghan jihad, Arab foreign fighters, Bin Laden’s formation, Taliban governance, and the later Haqqani-Taliban insurgency.

Sirajuddin Haqqani

Relationship: Son of Jalaluddin Haqqani.
Role: Leader of Haqqani Network; Taliban acting interior minister after 2021.
Status: Alive.
Al-Qaeda relevance: Important as the modern Haqqani node inside Taliban governance. NCTC says Sirajuddin leads the group’s day-to-day activity with close relatives.

OSINT significance: Sirajuddin represents the post-2021 convergence of militant family network, Taliban state power, security ministry control, and historical Al-Qaeda sanctuary concerns.

Khalil al-Rahman Haqqani

Born: Public sources often list 1966.
Died: December 11, 2024, Kabul.
Death: Killed in an IS-KP-claimed suicide bombing.
Relationship: Brother of Jalaluddin Haqqani; uncle of Sirajuddin Haqqani.
Role: Senior Haqqani figure; Taliban minister for refugees and repatriation after 2021.

Al-Qaeda relevance: State’s RFJ notice described the Haqqani Network as allied with Al-Qaeda and the Afghan Taliban and offered rewards for Haqqani leaders including Khalil, Aziz, Yahya, Abdul Rauf Zakir, and Sirajuddin.

Nasiruddin Haqqani

Relationship: Son of Jalaluddin; brother of Sirajuddin.
Status: Dead; killed in Islamabad in 2013.
Role: Financier/emissary.
Network importance: Fundraising and Gulf/Arab-world linkage node. Public summaries describe him as a key financier and emissary of the network.

Badruddin Haqqani

Relationship: Son of Jalaluddin; brother of Sirajuddin.
Status: Dead; reportedly killed in 2012.
Role: Senior operational commander.
Network importance: One of the most important military-operational Haqqani sons during the insurgency era.

Anas Haqqani

Relationship: Son of Jalaluddin; brother of Sirajuddin.
Status: Alive.
Role: Public/political Haqqani figure.
Network importance: Useful for the post-2021 political face of the Haqqani family, but not an Al-Qaeda leadership node.

Aziz Haqqani / Abdul Aziz Abbasin

Role: Haqqani Network leader/commander.
Status: Public status unclear.
RFJ connection: State authorized reward offers for Aziz Haqqani as one of several key Haqqani Network leaders.

Yahya Haqqani

Role: Senior Haqqani Network member involved in military, financial, and propaganda activities according to public summaries.
Family link: Public profiles describe Yahya as Sirajuddin’s brother-in-law.
RFJ connection: State authorized a reward for Yahya Haqqani in 2014.

Haji Mali Khan

Role: Senior Haqqani commander; uncle to Sirajuddin and Badruddin according to public summaries.
Status: Captured by ISAF in 2011; reportedly released in a prisoner swap in 2019.
Network importance: Older-generation command/emissary node.

Abdul Rauf Zakir / Qari Zakir

Role: Haqqani Network / Taliban military figure; State included Abdul Rauf Zakir in RFJ reward offers for Haqqani leaders.
Chart claim: TIC labels Qari Zakir as “AQ Shura Council Member” and “Security Committee leader,” but that exact Al-Qaeda Shura title should be marked as chart claim unless corroborated independently.

9. AQAP people roster — leadership, media, bombmaking, detention, deaths

NCTC’s current AQAP profile identifies Sa’d Muhammad Atif / Saad bin Atef al-Awlaki as overall AQAP leader, Ibrahim al-Banna as a founding AQAP member and chief-of-security figure, and Ibrahim Ahmed Mahmoud al-Qosi as AQAP spokesperson and former Guantanamo detainee who worked directly for Bin Laden.

Core AQAP leaders

Nasir al-Wuhayshi / Nasir al-Wahishi — founder and emir; former Bin Laden aide; killed in 2015.
Sa’id al-Shihri — deputy; former Guantanamo detainee; killed/confirmed dead around 2013.
Qasim al-Raymi / Qassim al-Rimi — military commander, later emir; killed in 2020.
Khalid Batarfi — emir after al-Raymi; AQAP announced his death in 2024 and named Saad al-Awlaki successor.
Saad bin Atef al-Awlaki / Sa’d Muhammad Atif — current AQAP leader in public profiles; NCTC says he has publicly called for attacks against the United States and kidnapped Americans in Yemen.

AQAP media / propaganda people

Anwar al-Awlaki — AQAP ideologue and English-language propagandist; killed in 2011.
Samir Khan — AQAP media figure associated with English-language propaganda; killed in 2011.
Ibrahim al-Qosi — AQAP spokesperson; former Guantanamo detainee and former Bin Laden associate.
Nasser al-Ansi — AQAP propagandist/official involved in AQAP’s public messaging before being killed in 2015.

AQAP security / explosives / operations figures

Ibrahim al-Banna — founding AQAP member; served as chief of security and provided military/security guidance.
Ibrahim Hassan al-Asiri — AQAP bombmaker; publicly linked to aviation plots; reported killed.
Fahd al-Quso — Yemen Al-Qaeda/AQAP-linked operative tied to USS Cole case; killed in 2012.
Jamal al-Badawi — USS Cole participant; killed in 2019.

AQAP network character

AQAP is a hybrid of Al-Qaeda ideology, Yemeni local insurgency, former detainee networks, propaganda/media capability, tribal/criminal financing, and repeated leadership succession after strikes. The TIC chart places Ibrahim al-Banna and Saad bin Atef al-Awlaki inside the Al-Qaeda-centered framework, labeling them as senior AQAP / AQ Shura-linked figures; treat the AQAP roles as public-source supported and the exact Shura titles as chart claims.

10. AQIS people roster — South Asia, Taliban-safe-haven, and recruitment layer

NCTC lists AQIS as primarily based in Afghanistan, operating in Afghanistan, Pakistan, India, and Bangladesh, with fewer than 200 members as of May 2024; it says AQIS receives overarching guidance from Al-Qaeda senior leaders and has longstanding ties with the Taliban.

AQIS leaders

Asim Umar — founder; killed in a U.S.-Afghan raid in 2019.
Usama Mahmood / Abu Zar / Osama Mehmood — AQIS leader since 2019; formerly spokesperson.
Yahya Ghauri / Atif Yahya Ghouri — AQIS deputy amir.
Muhammad Maruf — recruitment head.

AQIS early cadre / associated figures

Ustad Ahmad Farooq — early AQIS deputy/media figure; killed in 2015.
Qari Imran — early AQIS Shura/commander figure; killed in 2015.
Bangladesh-linked attack cells — connected to AQIS claims against secular writers, publishers, activists, and LGBT rights figures, especially 2015–2016.

AQIS-Taliban connection

NCTC says AQIS pledged allegiance to Taliban leader Haybatullah Akhundzada partly to secure safe haven in Afghanistan. That makes AQIS a direct bridge between Al-Qaeda Core, Taliban Afghanistan, and South Asian jihadist networks.

11. AQIM / GSPC / JNIM people roster
AQIM / GSPC lineage figures

Hassan Hattab — founder of GSPC, direct predecessor to AQIM.
Nabil Sahraoui — GSPC leader after Hattab; killed in 2004.
Abdelmalek Droukdel / Abu Musab Abdel Wadoud — AQIM emir until killed by French forces in Mali in 2020.
Abu Ubaydah Yusuf al-Anabi / Yazid Mebarek — current AQIM emir; CEP says he previously led AQIM’s Council of Notables, served on AQIM’s Shura Council, and was AQIM media chief.

AQIM / Sahel commanders

Mokhtar Belmokhtar / Khalid Abu al-Abbas — al-Murabitun / AQIM-linked Saharan commander; status long contested. The TIC chart calls him “The One-Eyed,” “Nelson,” and “The Uncatchable,” and explicitly notes uncertainty over death claims.
Abdelhamid Abou Zeid — AQIM Sahara commander; killed in Mali in 2013.
Yahya Abu al-Hammam / Djamel Okacha — AQIM Sahara commander; killed in 2019.
Ali Maychou — JNIM religious/leadership figure; U.S. designated him as a JNIM leader in 2019.

JNIM leaders

Iyad ag Ghali — founder and emir of JNIM; prominent AQIM-linked Sahel figure.
Amadou Kouffa — JNIM deputy/founding member; founder of Katibat Macina.
Jafar Dicko — JNIM deputy leader in Burkina Faso according to NCTC.

Merger groups and their human significance

Ansar al-Din — Iyad ag Ghali’s vehicle; Tuareg/northern Mali Islamist insurgency.
Al-Murabitun — Belmokhtar-linked network; hostage-taking and Saharan attack lineage.
Macina Liberation Front / Katibat Macina — Amadou Kouffa’s central Mali/Fulani mobilization structure.
AQIM Sahara Emirate — AQIM’s direct branch into JNIM.

JNIM is important because it translates Al-Qaeda’s older North African structure into a local Sahel insurgency coalition. NCTC says JNIM formed in 2017 by merging Ansar al-Din, al-Murabitun, Macina Liberation Front, and AQIM’s Sahara Emirate.

12. Al-Shabaab people roster — emir, finance, amniyat, foreign fighters

NCTC lists Ahmed Diriye / Abu Ubaidah as al-Shabaab’s amir since 2014, Abukar Ali Adan as deputy amir, and Mahad Karate as finance chief and commander of the amniyat intelligence/security wing.

Al-Shabaab senior leadership

Ahmed Abdi Godane / Mukhtar Abu Zubair — former emir; killed in 2014; central to the formal Al-Qaeda pledge.
Ahmed Diriye / Abu Ubaidah — current emir since 2014; RFJ offers a reward for information on him.
Abukar Ali Adan — deputy amir / former military chief.
Mahad Karate / Abdirahman Mohamed Warsame — finance chief and amniyat commander; State/RFJ reward increased to up to $10 million.

Al-Shabaab foreign-fighter / U.S.-person node

Jehad Serwan Mostafa
Aliases: Ahmed Gurey, Anwar al-Amriki, Emir Anwar.
Role: U.S. citizen; senior al-Shabaab figure.
FBI description: Joined al-Shabaab around 2006; served as military instructor, foreign-fighter leader, media propagandist, intermediary with other terrorist organizations, and leader in explosives use.

The TIC chart also lists Jehad Serwan Mostafa as “Emir Anwar / Ahmed Gurey / Anwar al-Amriki,” senior commander, and al-Shabaab member since 2006.

Al-Shabaab organizational wings

Amniyat: Internal intelligence/security, assassinations, counterintelligence, infiltration, urban attack facilitation. Mahad Karate is publicly identified as commander.
Finance wing: Extortion, taxation, checkpoint revenue, business levies, local coercion.
Military wing: Rural insurgency, base raids, ambushes.
Media wing: al-Kataib Media Foundation.

13. Syria people roster — Nusra, HTS split, and Hurras al-Din
Abu Muhammad al-Julani / Ahmed al-Sharaa

Role: Former emir of Jabhat al-Nusra; later leader of HTS.
Al-Qaeda connection: Former Al-Qaeda Syria affiliate leader who later broke away from Al-Qaeda.
Chart note: TIC says Julani led Tahrir al-Sham and previously served as emir of al-Nusra, with ties to both Al-Qaeda and IS; treat the “ties” phrasing as broad chart characterization.

Abu Khalid al-Suri

Role: Al-Qaeda envoy in Syria; sent to mediate between Nusra and ISIS-related factions.
Status: Killed in 2014 by a rival rebel/jihadist attack.
Network importance: Direct emissary link between Zawahiri and Syria.

Abu al-Khayr al-Masri

Role: Al-Qaeda deputy / senior Syria-based figure.
Status: Killed in Syria in 2017.
Family tie: Public reporting describes him as Osama bin Laden’s son-in-law.

Samir Hijazi / Faruq al-Suri

Role: Hurras al-Din amir in public profiles.
Network importance: Al-Qaeda loyalist after Nusra/HTS split.

Sami al-Uraydi

Role: Hurras al-Din ideologue / religious figure.
Network importance: Theological legitimacy node for Al-Qaeda loyalists in Syria.

Abu Hammam al-Shami

Role: Veteran Al-Qaeda/Hurras figure.
Status: Reported killed in 2020.
Network importance: Veteran operational bridge from older Al-Qaeda to Syria.

NCTC identifies Hurras al-Din as Al-Qaeda’s Syria affiliate from 2018 until it publicly announced dissolution in early 2025; former members remain relevant to the Al-Qaeda Syria file.

15. Relationship clusters you should preserve
Bin Laden family cluster

Core: Osama → wives → children.
Operationally relevant: Saad, Hamza, Khalid, Muhammad.
Marriage links: Hamza reportedly to Mohammed Atta’s daughter; one Bin Laden son to Mohammed Atef’s daughter; TIC chart also shows additional Hamza marriage links, but those should stay chart-claim unless verified.

Zawahiri / Egyptian Islamic Jihad cluster

Core: Zawahiri → EIJ → Al-Qaeda merger.
Family-command link: Zawahiri → daughter Nabila → Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi.
Military cadre: Mohammed Atef, Sayf al-Adl, Abu Muhammad al-Masri, Abu al-Khayr al-Masri.
Ideological dispute node: Dr. Fadl / Sayyid Imam al-Sharif.

Iran-based senior leadership cluster

Core: Sayf al-Adl, Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi, Yasin al-Suri.
Older related nodes: Atiyah Abd al-Rahman, Abu Muhammad al-Masri, Abu al-Khayr al-Masri, Muhsin al-Fadhli.
Function: leadership shelter, communications, funds/recruit movement, family movement, senior liaison.

Haqqani family cluster

Core: Jalaluddin → Sirajuddin, Badruddin, Nasiruddin, Anas.
Sibling/uncle layer: Khalil Haqqani, Haji Mali Khan.
Command/finance layer: Yahya Haqqani, Aziz Haqqani, Abdul Rauf Zakir.
Al-Qaeda link: Jalaluddin as Bin Laden mentor; Haqqani Network as Taliban-Al-Qaeda sanctuary bridge.

AQAP cluster

Core leaders: Wuhayshi → Raymi → Batarfi → Saad al-Awlaki.
Security/media: Ibrahim al-Banna, Ibrahim al-Qosi, Anwar al-Awlaki, Samir Khan, al-Asiri.
Legacy Yemen nodes: Fahd al-Quso, Jamal al-Badawi.

AQIS cluster

Core: Asim Umar → Usama Mahmood.
Deputy/recruitment: Yahya Ghauri, Muhammad Maruf.
Safe-haven relation: Taliban allegiance / Afghanistan-Pakistan base.

AQIM / JNIM cluster

AQIM lineage: GIA environment → GSPC → AQIM.
AQIM leaders: Hattab, Sahraoui, Droukdel, al-Anabi.
Sahel commanders: Belmokhtar, Abou Zeid, Yahya Abu al-Hammam.
JNIM merger: Iyad ag Ghali, Amadou Kouffa, Jafar Dicko, Ali Maychou.

Al-Shabaab cluster

Leadership: Godane → Ahmed Diriye.
Finance/security: Mahad Karate.
Deputy: Abukar Ali Adan.
Foreign fighter: Jehad Mostafa.
Wings: amniyat, finance, media/al-Kataib, military.


Absolutely — Part 4 is the death / capture / arrest / indictment / designation / sanctions index. This is the legal-counterterrorism layer: who was killed, who was captured, who is detained, who was indicted, who is wanted, who is sanctioned, and which organizations were formally designated.
I’m keeping this public-source and broad. I am not including operational addresses, tactical procedures, or anything that would turn this into a live targeting guide. For the TIC chart, I’ll keep treating its disputed/extra network claims as chart-derived, because the chart itself says it is educational/public-source and not classified intelligence.
PART 4 — DEATH, CAPTURE, DETENTION, INDICTMENT, DESIGNATION, AND SANCTIONS INDEX
1. Why this layer matters
This is one of the most important layers for your OSINT platform because it lets you track network degradation: when a leader dies, who replaces him, which affiliate survives, which legal action exposes the network, and which individuals remain unresolved. NCTC’s current Al-Qaeda profile says affiliates now conduct most operational activity, with AQAP in Yemen, AQIM/JNIM in Mali/Sahel, al-Shabaab in Somalia, AQIS in Afghanistan/Pakistan, former Hurras al-Din elements in Syria, and some senior/lower-level members in Iran. (Director of National Intelligence)
For your platform, every person should eventually have one of these status labels: dead, captured, detained, convicted, indicted, wanted, sanctioned, released, defected, dissolved, unknown, contested, or chart-claim only.

2. Core Al-Qaeda chronology: deaths, captures, indictments, designations
1989 — Abdullah Azzam killed
Abdullah Azzam was killed by a car bomb in Peshawar in 1989 while traveling with two sons to Friday prayers. CTC West Point describes his assassination as unresolved and notes that speculation has ranged across multiple possible actors. He matters because he was a core Afghan-jihad mobilizer and ideological predecessor figure whose death left Bin Laden with more room to shape the Arab-Afghan network that became Al-Qaeda. (Combating Terrorism Center at West Point)
Status: dead. Category: assassination / car bombing. Network effect: removed an early ideological authority from the Afghan-Arab movement.

1993–1995 — First World Trade Center case and Ramzi Yousef capture
The 1993 World Trade Center bombing was not a mature Al-Qaeda Core operation in the same way 9/11 was, but the FBI explicitly frames it as a precursor to 9/11 because Ramzi Yousef’s uncle, Khalid Sheikh Mohammed, later helped Al-Qaeda carry out the 9/11 plot. Yousef was captured in Pakistan in 1995 and later convicted. (FBI)
Ramzi Yousef: captured / convicted. Khalid Sheikh Mohammed: family-linked and later operationally central to Al-Qaeda. Network effect: early preview of the KSM/Yousef transnational attack-planning lineage.

1998 — Embassy bombing indictments
After the August 7, 1998 U.S. Embassy bombings in Nairobi and Dar es Salaam, U.S. prosecutors indicted multiple Al-Qaeda figures. Rewards for Justice says Sayf al-Adl was indicted and charged by a U.S. federal grand jury in November 1998 for his role in the bombings, which killed 224 civilians and wounded more than 5,000. (Rewards For Justice)
Key indicted / charged or linked figures: Osama bin Laden, Ayman al-Zawahiri, Muhammad Atef / Abu Hafs al-Masri, Sayf al-Adl, Abdullah Ahmed Abdullah / Abu Muhammad al-Masri, Fazul Abdullah Mohammed, Khalfan Khamis Mohamed, Mohamed Rashed Daoud al-Owhali, Mohammed Saddiq Odeh, Wadih el-Hage, and others connected through the prosecution record.
Network effect: exposed Al-Qaeda’s East Africa attack network, Egyptian cadre, logistics cells, and Bin Laden’s global command structure.

1999 — Al-Qaeda designated Foreign Terrorist Organization
The U.S. State Department designated Al-Qaeda as a Foreign Terrorist Organization in October 1999, according to NCTC. This matters because it created the main U.S. legal designation framework for prosecution, immigration bars, sanctions, asset blocking, and support-material cases involving the organization. (Director of National Intelligence)
Status: organization designated. Category: FTO designation. Network effect: legal and financial pressure framework begins before 9/11.

2001 — Executive Order 13224
President George W. Bush signed Executive Order 13224 on September 23, 2001. The State Department describes it as a powerful tool to impede terrorist funding, and it underpins many later designations of Al-Qaeda leaders, financiers, facilitators, and associated groups. (state.gov)
Status: sanctions authority. Category: financial counterterrorism instrument. Network effect: enables blocking of assets and prohibition on transactions with designated terrorists and supporters.

November 2001 — Mohammed Atef / Abu Hafs al-Masri killed
Mohammed Atef, also known as Abu Hafs al-Masri, was one of Al-Qaeda’s top pre-9/11 military figures and was killed during the U.S. campaign in Afghanistan in late 2001. The 9/11 Commission described Bin Laden and Atef as occupying undisputed top leadership positions in the pre-9/11 structure. (Department of Justice)
Status: dead. Category: killed in U.S. military campaign. Network effect: removed Al-Qaeda’s pre-9/11 military chief.

2001 — Zacarias Moussaoui indictment
Zacarias Moussaoui was indicted in connection with the 9/11 conspiracy. The Justice Department indictment says that from about 1989 through the indictment date, Moussaoui and other Al-Qaeda members and associates conspired to kill and maim persons within the United States and destroy property, resulting in the deaths of thousands on September 11, 2001. (Department of Justice)
Status: convicted / imprisoned. Category: U.S. criminal prosecution. Network effect: major U.S. courtroom exposure of 9/11-linked conspiracy structure.

2002 — Abu Zubaydah captured
Abu Zubaydah was captured in Pakistan in 2002 and became one of the most controversial detainees of the post-9/11 period. He is historically important as a facilitator/training-camp figure, though his exact formal Al-Qaeda status has been disputed in later legal and intelligence discussions.
Status: captured / detained. Category: high-value detainee. Network effect: early capture in the post-9/11 detention system.

2002 — Abd al-Rahim al-Nashiri captured
Abd al-Rahim al-Nashiri was captured in 2002 and became a central detainee in the USS Cole case. He is associated with Al-Qaeda’s maritime attack planning and Yemen network.
Status: captured / detained. Category: USS Cole / maritime attack case. Network effect: key Yemen-linked detainee tied to one of Al-Qaeda’s major pre-9/11 attacks.

2003 — Khalid Sheikh Mohammed captured
Khalid Sheikh Mohammed was captured in Rawalpindi, Pakistan, in March 2003. The Justice Department later identified KSM among the five detainees accused in the 9/11 case, along with Walid bin Attash, Ramzi Binalshibh, Ali Abdul Aziz Ali / Ammar al-Baluchi, and Mustafa al-Hawsawi. (Department of Justice)
Status: captured / detained at Guantanamo. Category: 9/11 planner / high-value detainee. Network effect: removed Al-Qaeda’s most important publicly known external-operations planner.

2003 — USS Cole indictment
The Justice Department charged Al-Qaeda associates in the USS Cole case in May 2003. The indictment alleged that Jamal al-Badawi helped procure safehouses, the attack boat, and equipment used in the USS Cole bombing, while Fahd al-Quso facilitated the plot and prepared to film the attack from Aden. (Department of Justice)
Jamal al-Badawi: later killed in 2019. Fahd al-Quso: later killed in 2012. Network effect: exposed the Yemen Al-Qaeda infrastructure that later fed into AQAP.

2005 — Abu Faraj al-Libi captured
Abu Faraj al-Libi was captured in Pakistan in 2005. He was widely described in public reporting as a senior Al-Qaeda operations figure after KSM’s capture.
Status: captured / detained. Category: senior Al-Qaeda operative. Network effect: further weakened Al-Qaeda’s post-9/11 command and external-operations layer.

2006 — Abu Musab al-Zarqawi killed
Abu Musab al-Zarqawi, leader of Al-Qaeda in Iraq, was killed by U.S. forces in Iraq in 2006. He matters in an Al-Qaeda project because AQI became the pathway toward the Islamic State of Iraq and later ISIS. The TIC chart also frames Abu Muhammad al-Maqdisi as a mentor/spiritual guide to Zarqawi and links AQI as the branch from which ISIS emerged, though those diagrammed relationships should be marked as chart-derived when based only on the chart.
Status: dead. Category: killed in U.S. strike. Network effect: removed AQI’s founder, but the AQI-to-ISIS lineage survived.

2011 — Osama bin Laden killed
Osama bin Laden was killed by U.S. forces in Abbottabad, Pakistan, on May 2, 2011. The FBI describes Bin Laden as the founder of Al-Qaeda, and CIA’s public account describes the Abbottabad raid as the culmination of years of intelligence work. (FBI)
Status: dead. Category: U.S. special operations raid. Successor: Ayman al-Zawahiri. Network effect: removed Al-Qaeda’s founder and primary symbol, but did not destroy the affiliate network.

2011 — Atiyah Abd al-Rahman killed
Atiyah Abd al-Rahman, a Libyan senior Al-Qaeda manager and communications figure, was killed in Pakistan in 2011. Treasury’s Iran facilitation-network designation described Atiyah as a key Al-Qaeda leader based in Pakistan and tied him to the Iran-based pipeline through which Al-Qaeda moved money, facilitators, and operatives from the Middle East to South Asia. (U.S. Department of the Treasury)
Status: dead. Category: killed in strike. Network effect: damaged Al-Qaeda’s management layer and affiliate communications.

2012 — Abu Yahya al-Libi killed
Abu Yahya al-Libi was killed in 2012. He was a senior Al-Qaeda ideologue and public-facing figure after Bin Laden’s death and Atiyah’s death.
Status: dead. Category: killed in strike. Network effect: removed a major ideological/media figure during Al-Qaeda’s post-Bin Laden transition.

2015 — Sayf al-Adl released from Iranian custody
Rewards for Justice says Sayf al-Adl moved to southeastern Iran after the 1998 embassy bombings, was placed under house arrest by Iranian authorities in 2003, and was released in September 2015 with four other senior Al-Qaeda leaders in exchange for an Iranian diplomat kidnapped by Al-Qaeda in Yemen. (Rewards For Justice)
Status: alive / wanted / previously under Iranian custody or restriction. Category: release from Iranian custody. Network effect: restored one of Al-Qaeda’s most experienced military/security figures to the senior-leadership pool.

2019 — Hamza bin Laden killed
The White House confirmed in 2019 that Hamza bin Laden, son of Osama bin Laden and described as a high-ranking Al-Qaeda member, was killed in a U.S. counterterrorism operation in the Afghanistan/Pakistan region. (Trump White House Archives)
Status: dead. Category: U.S. counterterrorism operation. Network effect: removed Al-Qaeda’s most symbolically important Bin Laden-family successor.

2020 — Abu Muhammad al-Masri reportedly killed in Tehran
Abu Muhammad al-Masri / Abdullah Ahmed Abdullah, a senior Egyptian Al-Qaeda figure linked to the 1998 embassy bombings, was reportedly killed in Tehran in August 2020. FDD’s Long War Journal summarized public reporting that intelligence officials confirmed his killing in an Israeli operation. (FDD's Long War Journal)
Status: dead, publicly reported. Category: assassination / reported intelligence operation. Network effect: removed a senior old-guard Egyptian Al-Qaeda figure from the Iran-based leadership environment.

2022 — Ayman al-Zawahiri killed
Ayman al-Zawahiri was killed in a U.S. drone strike in Kabul on July 31, 2022. The U.S. Defense Department stated that Zawahiri had led Al-Qaeda after Bin Laden’s death in 2011. (U.S. Department of War)
Status: dead. Category: U.S. drone strike. Successor / de facto successor: Sayf al-Adl is widely viewed as de facto leader, though formal public succession remains complicated by his Iran-based status. Network effect: removed Al-Qaeda’s second emir and exposed continuing Taliban/Haqqani safe-haven controversy because he was killed in Kabul.

3. Current core Al-Qaeda wanted / sanctioned / unresolved figures
Sayf al-Adl
Sayf al-Adl is wanted by the FBI and Rewards for Justice. RFJ describes him as a senior Al-Qaeda leader, member of the Majlis al-Shura, head of Al-Qaeda’s military committee, and an indicted figure in the 1998 embassy bombings. RFJ also says he lived under IRGC protection in Iran, was placed under Iranian house arrest in 2003, and released in 2015. (Rewards For Justice)
Status: alive / wanted / sanctioned. Broad location: Iran-based in public U.S. descriptions. Importance: likely de facto emir or highest-profile senior military leader.

Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi / Muhammad Abbatay
The FBI lists Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi as a Moroccan-born Al-Qaeda member, with aliases including Muhammad Abbatay and Abd al Rahman al Maghribi, and says he studied software programming in Germany before traveling to Afghanistan, where he was selected to manage al-Sahab, Al-Qaeda’s primary media wing. The FBI says he fled to Iran after 9/11, may travel between Iran and Pakistan, and has been designated by the U.S. government as a senior Al-Qaeda leader. (FBI)
Status: alive / wanted / sanctioned. Broad locations: Morocco, Germany, Afghanistan, Pakistan, Iran. Importance: media, communications, external-affiliate coordination, Zawahiri family tie.

Hamza Salih bin Sa’id al-Ghamdi
Hamza al-Ghamdi is wanted by the FBI/RFJ. Public U.S. materials describe him as a Saudi national and senior Al-Qaeda figure; the TIC chart also places him inside the Al-Qaeda Shura environment and says he was one of Bin Laden’s trusted associates, but the chart-specific Shura title should be treated as chart-derived unless separately sourced.
Status: alive/wanted or unresolved public status. Importance: Bin Laden inner-circle/security layer.

Yasin al-Suri / Ezedin Abdel Aziz Khalil
Treasury identified Yasin al-Suri as part of a key Iran-based Al-Qaeda funding and support network, describing that network as a core pipeline used to move money, facilitators, and operatives from the Middle East to South Asia, including to Atiyah Abd al-Rahman. (U.S. Department of the Treasury)
Status: wanted / sanctioned. Broad location: Iran-based facilitation network. Importance: money, movement, facilitation, and Iran pipeline.

Muhsin al-Fadhli
Treasury said Muhsin al-Fadhli took over the Iran-based facilitation network from Yasin al-Suri in late 2011 and described related facilitation of extremist travel to Afghanistan or Iraq via Iran on behalf of Al-Qaeda. (U.S. Department of the Treasury)
Status: dead in public reporting. Importance: Iran facilitation network; later Syria/Khorasan Group concern.

4. AQAP death / capture / designation index
Organization designation
AQAP formed in 2009 in Yemen from Al-Qaeda members from Saudi Arabia and Yemeni extremists, according to NCTC. NCTC describes AQAP as one of Al-Qaeda’s most capable affiliates, based primarily in southern and central Yemen, with an estimated 2,000–3,000 fighters. (Director of National Intelligence)
The U.S. State Department designated AQAP as a Foreign Terrorist Organization in January 2010, and AQAP remains one of the most important Al-Qaeda affiliates for legal, sanctions, and counterterrorism tracking. (Director of National Intelligence)

Nasir al-Wuhayshi / Nasir al-Wahishi
Status: dead. Role: AQAP founder/emir; former Bin Laden aide. Death: killed in a U.S. strike in Yemen in 2015. Network effect: removed the strongest direct Bin Laden-to-AQAP leadership bridge.

Sa’id al-Shihri
Status: dead. Role: AQAP deputy emir; former Guantanamo detainee. Death: AQAP confirmed his death around 2013 after earlier reports. Network effect: weakened the Saudi/Yemeni founding generation of AQAP.

Qasim al-Raymi / Qassim al-Rimi
Status: dead. Role: AQAP military commander and later emir. Death: killed in a U.S. strike in 2020. Network effect: caused succession to Khalid Batarfi.

Khalid Batarfi
Status: dead. Role: AQAP emir after al-Raymi. Death: AQAP announced his death in March 2024; cause was not disclosed. Successor: Saad bin Atef al-Awlaki. Network effect: another major AQAP succession event.

Saad bin Atef al-Awlaki / Sa’d Atif al-Aulaqi
NCTC lists Sa’d Atif al-Aulaqi as AQAP’s overall leader as of May 2026. AQAP released a June 2025 video of him calling for attacks against senior U.S. officials, and State/RFJ has offered rewards for information on him, Ibrahim al-Banna, and Ibrahim al-Qosi. (Director of National Intelligence)
Status: alive / wanted. Role: current AQAP emir. Importance: current AQAP leadership node.

Ibrahim al-Banna
Status: alive / wanted. Role: founding AQAP member; security/military guidance figure. Importance: AQAP security and command layer. NCTC and State/RFJ identify him as one of the major current AQAP figures. (state.gov)

Ibrahim al-Qosi
Status: alive / wanted. Role: AQAP spokesperson; former Guantanamo detainee; former Bin Laden associate. Importance: propaganda, media, legacy Bin Laden link. (state.gov)

Anwar al-Awlaki and Samir Khan
Status: dead. Roles: AQAP English-language ideologue / propaganda figures. Deaths: killed in 2011. Network effect: damaged AQAP’s English-language outreach ecosystem, though AQAP media continued.

Ibrahim Hassan al-Asiri
Status: reported dead. Role: AQAP bombmaker publicly associated with aviation plots. Network effect: removed one of AQAP’s most notorious technical-operational figures. Keep descriptions nontechnical in your platform.

Fahd al-Quso and Jamal al-Badawi
The Justice Department’s USS Cole indictment named Fahd al-Quso and Jamal al-Badawi as key Al-Qaeda associates in the USS Cole plot. Quso later died in 2012 and Badawi in 2019. (Department of Justice)
Status: both dead. Importance: Yemen Al-Qaeda lineage before and into AQAP.

5. AQIS death / capture / designation index
Organization designation
NCTC says AQIS is based primarily in Afghanistan, with activity in Pakistan, India, and Bangladesh; it has fewer than 200 members and has used suicide bombings, small-arms attacks, ambushes, and assassinations against government/security personnel, civil society activists, and academics. NCTC says the U.S. State Department designated AQIS as a Foreign Terrorist Organization in 2016. (ODNI)

Asim Umar
Status: dead. Role: founding AQIS emir. Death: killed in a U.S.-Afghan raid in 2019. Network effect: removed AQIS’s founding leader and pushed succession to Usama Mahmood / Abu Zar.

Usama Mahmood / Osama Mahmood / Abu Zar
Status: alive / wanted. Role: AQIS leader after Asim Umar; formerly spokesperson. Chart note: TIC labels him AQIS emir and AQ Shura Council member; treat the AQIS-emir role as public-source supported, but the exact AQ Shura label as chart-derived unless independently verified.

Yahya Ghauri / Atif Yahya Ghouri
Status: alive / wanted or unresolved. Role: AQIS deputy amir in public profiles. Network effect: current AQIS deputy layer.

Muhammad Maruf
Status: unresolved / wanted in public profiles. Role: AQIS recruitment head. Network effect: recruitment layer.

Ustad Ahmad Farooq and Qari Imran
Status: dead. Roles: early AQIS deputy/media and shura/command figures. Deaths: reported killed in 2015. Network effect: early AQIS command degradation.

6. AQIM / GSPC death / capture / designation index
Organization designation and lineage
AQIM descends from Algeria’s GSPC and became Al-Qaeda’s North Africa affiliate after aligning with Al-Qaeda and adopting the AQIM name in 2007. The TIC chart places AQIM, JNIM, al-Murabitun, Ansar al-Din, Katibat Uqba ibn Nafi, and other Sahel/Maghreb actors inside its regional Al-Qaeda map.

Hassan Hattab
Status: surrendered / detained in Algerian context. Role: GSPC founder. Network effect: foundational but later sidelined.

Nabil Sahraoui
Status: dead. Role: GSPC leader after Hattab. Death: killed in 2004. Network effect: leadership moved toward Abdelmalek Droukdel.

Abdelmalek Droukdel / Abu Musab Abdel Wadoud
Status: dead. Role: AQIM emir. Death: killed by French forces in Mali in 2020. Network effect: removed AQIM’s long-running Algerian emir and elevated Abu Ubaydah Yusuf al-Anabi.

Abu Ubaydah Yusuf al-Anabi / Yazid Mebarek
Status: alive / wanted. Role: AQIM emir after Droukdel. Chart note: TIC labels him “Senior AQ Leader,” “AQ Shura Council Member,” and “Emir AQIM”; the AQIM emir role is publicly supported, while the exact Shura label should be treated as chart-derived unless separately confirmed.

Abdelhamid Abou Zeid
Status: dead. Role: AQIM Sahara commander. Death: killed in Mali in 2013. Network effect: degraded AQIM’s kidnapping/hostage commander layer.

Yahya Abu al-Hammam / Djamel Okacha
Status: dead. Role: AQIM Sahara / JNIM-linked commander. Death: killed in a French operation in 2019. Network effect: weakened the AQIM-to-JNIM Sahel command layer.

Mokhtar Belmokhtar
Status: contested / unknown. Role: AQIM / al-Murabitun commander. Death claims: repeatedly reported killed, but long contested. The TIC chart explicitly calls his status uncertain and says it presumes him alive for now, which should be recorded as a chart-derived uncertainty note.

7. JNIM death / capture / designation index
Organization designation
JNIM formed in March 2017 when Ansar al-Din, al-Murabitun, the Macina Liberation Front, and AQIM’s Sahara Emirate merged. NCTC says Iyad ag Ghali pledged allegiance to the emir of AQIM, the emir of Al-Qaeda, and the Taliban leader. JNIM is primarily based in Mali and Burkina Faso, active across much of West Africa, and has at least 6,000 members. (Director of National Intelligence)
The U.S. State Department designated JNIM as a Foreign Terrorist Organization and SDGT in September 2018. (United Nations)

Iyad ag Ghali
Status: alive / wanted. Role: JNIM founder and emir; formerly Ansar al-Din leader. Importance: central Al-Qaeda-aligned Sahel leader. Network effect: connects Tuareg insurgent politics, AQIM, JNIM, and Taliban/Al-Qaeda allegiance chains.

Amadou Kouffa
Status: alive / wanted; previously subject to contested death reports. Role: JNIM deputy / founder of Macina Liberation Front / Katibat Macina. Chart note: TIC notes that a French Special Forces assault was reported to have killed Kouffa in 2018, but that he later appeared in video, proving he remained alive.

Ali Maychou
Status: designated / wanted. Role: JNIM religious and leadership figure. Designation: State designated him in 2019 as a JNIM leader. Network effect: religious/legal authority layer.

Jafar Dicko
Status: alive / wanted. Role: JNIM deputy leader in Burkina Faso according to NCTC. Network effect: Burkina Faso expansion node.

8. Al-Shabaab death / capture / designation index
Organization designation
Al-Shabaab was designated by the U.S. as a Foreign Terrorist Organization in 2008 and is now one of Al-Qaeda’s most durable and dangerous affiliates. NCTC says al-Shabaab is based in Somalia and names Ahmed Diriye, Abukar Ali Adan, and Mahad Karate among its key leaders. (ODNI)

Aden Hashi Ayro
Status: dead. Role: early al-Shabaab military commander. Death: killed in a U.S. strike in 2008. Network effect: early leadership degradation before the Godane era.

Ahmed Abdi Godane / Mukhtar Abu Zubair
Status: dead. Role: al-Shabaab emir; central to the formal pledge to Al-Qaeda. Death: killed in U.S. strike in 2014. Successor: Ahmed Diriye / Abu Ubaidah. Network effect: Al-Shabaab remained operationally resilient after his death.

Ahmed Diriye / Abu Ubaidah
Status: alive / wanted. Role: al-Shabaab emir since 2014. Chart note: TIC labels him emir of al-Shabaab and also places him in an AQ Shura/Military Committee context; the al-Shabaab emir role is public-source supported, while the exact AQ Shura role should be treated as chart-derived unless corroborated.

Mahad Karate / Abdirahman Mohamed Warsame
Status: alive / wanted. Role: al-Shabaab finance chief and amniyat intelligence/security commander. Network effect: one of the most important current nodes because he links money, internal security, coercion, and attack capability. NCTC and public profiles identify al-Shabaab as one of the strongest current Al-Qaeda affiliates, while CTC notes that al-Shabaab and JNIM have become more lethal than Islamic State affiliates in Somalia/Sahel contexts. (Combating Terrorism Center at West Point)

Jehad Serwan Mostafa / Ahmed Gurey / Anwar al-Amriki
Status: alive / wanted. Role: U.S. citizen and senior al-Shabaab figure. Chart note: TIC lists him as a senior commander and al-Shabaab member since 2006.

9. Syria / Nusra / Hurras al-Din death / capture / designation index
Hurras al-Din designation and dissolution
The State Department designated Hurras al-Din as an SDGT in 2019 and described it as an Al-Qaeda-affiliated group in Syria. NCTC’s Al-Qaeda profile says Hurras al-Din publicly announced dissolution in early 2025, though former members still operate in Syria. (State Department)

Abu Muhammad al-Julani / Ahmed al-Sharaa
Status: alive. Role: former leader of Jabhat al-Nusra; later leader of HTS. Al-Qaeda relevance: former Al-Qaeda Syria affiliate leader who broke away from Al-Qaeda’s formal chain. Chart note: TIC labels him as Tahrir al-Sham leader and former al-Nusra emir with ties to Al-Qaeda and IS; record this as chart context unless separately sourced.

Abu Khalid al-Suri
Status: dead. Role: Al-Qaeda envoy in Syria. Death: killed in 2014. Network effect: removed one of Zawahiri’s key Syria mediators during the Al-Qaeda/ISIS rupture.

Abu al-Khayr al-Masri
Status: dead. Role: senior Al-Qaeda deputy / Syria-based figure. Death: killed in a U.S. strike in Syria in 2017. Network effect: removed a major old-guard Egyptian Al-Qaeda figure active in the Syria file.

Sami al-Uraydi
Status: alive / sanctioned. Role: Hurras al-Din religious/ideological leader. Designation: State designated Sami Mahmud Mohammed al-Uraydi as an SDGT, describing Hurras al-Din as an Al-Qaeda affiliate exploiting Syria’s conflict zone. (U.S. Department of State)

2025 Hurras al-Din strikes
CENTCOM said it killed a senior Hurras al-Din finance and logistics official in northwest Syria on February 15, 2025. CENTCOM described Hurras al-Din as an Al-Qaeda affiliate and said the strike was intended to disrupt the group’s ability to plan and conduct attacks. (U.S. Central Command)
Status: unnamed senior finance/logistics official killed. Category: U.S. strike. Network effect: post-dissolution pressure against residual Hurras/al-Qaeda Syria figures.

10. Haqqani / Taliban-linked status index relevant to Al-Qaeda
Jalaluddin Haqqani
Status: dead. Role: founder of Haqqani Network; Afghan jihad commander; Taliban-aligned figure. Death: Taliban announced his death after long illness in 2018. Al-Qaeda relevance: mentor/associate of Bin Laden in the Afghan jihad environment.

Sirajuddin Haqqani
Status: alive / sanctioned / wanted in U.S. public materials. Role: Haqqani Network leader; Taliban interior minister after 2021. Al-Qaeda relevance: key contemporary Haqqani/Taliban node because of the safe-haven and Afghan security-ministry context.

Khalil Haqqani
Status: dead. Role: senior Haqqani/Taliban figure; uncle of Sirajuddin. Death: killed in a Kabul bombing in December 2024, claimed by IS-KP. Chart note: TIC labels him Haqqani Network / Al-Qaeda member and head of security for Kabul; treat exact Al-Qaeda membership label as chart-derived unless separately verified.

Mullah Omar
Status: dead. Role: Taliban founder. Death: died in 2013; death publicly acknowledged later. Al-Qaeda relevance: Taliban emir who provided Bin Laden sanctuary before 9/11.

Haibatullah Akhundzada
Status: alive. Role: Taliban supreme leader. Al-Qaeda relevance: AQIS pledged allegiance to him, according to NCTC’s AQIS profile, which makes him an important sanctuary-legitimacy node rather than an Al-Qaeda commander. (ODNI)

Mullah Yaqoob Mujahid
Status: alive. Role: Taliban defense minister; son of Mullah Omar. Al-Qaeda relevance: Taliban command environment, not Al-Qaeda command.

11. Organization designation timeline
1999 — Al-Qaeda
Al-Qaeda was designated a Foreign Terrorist Organization by the U.S. State Department in October 1999. (Director of National Intelligence)
2001 — E.O. 13224 sanctions authority
Executive Order 13224 was signed September 23, 2001, creating a central U.S. terrorist-financing sanctions mechanism. (state.gov)
2008 — al-Shabaab
Al-Shabaab was designated as a Foreign Terrorist Organization in 2008 and remains one of Al-Qaeda’s most important active affiliates. (ODNI)
2010 — AQAP
AQAP was designated as a Foreign Terrorist Organization in January 2010; NCTC describes it as one of Al-Qaeda’s most capable affiliates. (Director of National Intelligence)
2016 — AQIS
AQIS was designated as a Foreign Terrorist Organization in 2016. NCTC lists AQIS as based primarily in Afghanistan, with activity in Pakistan, India, and Bangladesh. (ODNI)
2018 — JNIM
JNIM was designated as a Foreign Terrorist Organization and SDGT in 2018. The group was formed in 2017 by the merger of Ansar al-Din, al-Murabitun, Macina Liberation Front, and AQIM’s Sahara Emirate. (Director of National Intelligence)
2019 — Hurras al-Din
Hurras al-Din was designated as an SDGT in 2019; State described it as an Al-Qaeda-affiliated group in Syria. (State Department)
Continuing UN sanctions-monitoring framework
The UN Security Council’s ISIL/Da’esh and Al-Qaida Monitoring Team maintains a continuing report series under the 1267/1989/2253 sanctions framework, with reports listed through February 2026. This is one of your strongest sources for updating sanctions, listings, affiliate trends, and regional developments over time. (United Nations)

12. “Dead / captured / wanted” master index by network
Al-Qaeda Core
Dead: Abdullah Azzam; Mohammed Atef / Abu Hafs al-Masri; Osama bin Laden; Ayman al-Zawahiri; Atiyah Abd al-Rahman; Abu Yahya al-Libi; Hamza bin Laden; Abu Muhammad al-Masri; Abu al-Khayr al-Masri.
Captured / detained / convicted: Khalid Sheikh Mohammed; Ramzi Binalshibh; Walid bin Attash; Ali Abdul Aziz Ali / Ammar al-Baluchi; Mustafa al-Hawsawi; Abd al-Rahim al-Nashiri; Abu Zubaydah; Abu Faraj al-Libi; Zacarias Moussaoui; Khalid al-Fawwaz.
Wanted / sanctioned / unresolved: Sayf al-Adl; Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi; Hamza al-Ghamdi; Yasin al-Suri; other Iran-based facilitation figures.
AQAP
Dead: Nasir al-Wuhayshi; Sa’id al-Shihri; Qasim al-Raymi; Khalid Batarfi; Anwar al-Awlaki; Samir Khan; Ibrahim al-Asiri; Fahd al-Quso; Jamal al-Badawi; Nasser al-Ansi.
Wanted / alive: Saad bin Atef al-Awlaki; Ibrahim al-Banna; Ibrahim al-Qosi.
Captured / detained history: Sa’id al-Shihri and Ibrahim al-Qosi were former Guantanamo detainees; Khalid Batarfi had a public prison/jailbreak history before later AQAP leadership.
AQIS
Dead: Asim Umar; Ustad Ahmad Farooq; Qari Imran.
Wanted / alive: Usama Mahmood / Abu Zar; Yahya Ghauri; Muhammad Maruf.
Designation: AQIS designated FTO in 2016.
AQIM / GSPC
Dead: Nabil Sahraoui; Abdelmalek Droukdel; Abdelhamid Abou Zeid; Yahya Abu al-Hammam.
Captured / surrendered: Hassan Hattab.
Wanted / alive: Abu Ubaydah Yusuf al-Anabi.
Contested / unresolved: Mokhtar Belmokhtar.
JNIM
Wanted / alive: Iyad ag Ghali; Amadou Kouffa; Jafar Dicko; Ali Maychou.
Designation: JNIM designated FTO/SDGT in 2018.
al-Shabaab
Dead: Aden Hashi Ayro; Ahmed Abdi Godane.
Wanted / alive: Ahmed Diriye; Mahad Karate; Abukar Ali Adan; Jehad Serwan Mostafa.
Defected / surrendered / political transition: Mukhtar Robow / Abu Mansur.
Syria / Nusra / Hurras al-Din
Dead: Abu Khalid al-Suri; Abu al-Khayr al-Masri; Abu Hammam al-Shami; multiple unnamed or lesser-known Hurras figures killed in U.S. strikes.
Alive / sanctioned: Sami al-Uraydi; Faruq al-Suri / Samir Hijazi status should be tracked carefully depending on latest reporting.
Former Al-Qaeda / separate path: Abu Muhammad al-Julani / Ahmed al-Sharaa.
Haqqani / Taliban context
Dead: Jalaluddin Haqqani; Khalil Haqqani; Mullah Omar; Badruddin Haqqani; Nasiruddin Haqqani.
Alive / important: Sirajuddin Haqqani; Haibatullah Akhundzada; Mullah Yaqoob Mujahid; Abdul Aziz Haqqani; Yahya Haqqani; Qari Zakir / Abdul Rauf Zakir.

13. What Part 4 adds
This part gives you the legal-status and attrition skeleton of the Al-Qaeda network:
* who died,
* who was captured,
* who remains detained,
* who was indicted,
* who is wanted,
* who is sanctioned,
* which organizations were designated,
* which leadership successions followed each death,
* and which statuses remain contested.
For your OSINT platform, this is the layer that should drive status badges: dead, detained, wanted, sanctioned, designated, contested, chart-claim, and unresolved.
Part 5 should be financing, facilitation, courier networks, media infrastructure, and communications architecture — al-Sahab, al-Malahem, al-Kataib, Inspire, Resurgence, Yasin al-Suri, Iran pipeline, Gulf fundraising, kidnapping-for-ransom, hawala-style networks, AQAP/AQIM/JNIM/al-Shabaab revenue systems, and safe-haven logistics.


PART 5 IS ONLY FOR NOTES NOT FOR GRAPH OR MAPS BUT NOTES ONLY UNLESS LOCATIONS AND EVENTS ARE INCLUDED: Absolutely — Part 5 is the infrastructure layer: money, facilitators, couriers, communications, media organs, propaganda ecosystems, fundraising, kidnapping/extortion economies, prison networks, broad safe-haven logistics, and how Al-Qaeda’s affiliates sustain themselves after repeated leadership losses.
I’ll keep this public-source and non-operational. I am not going to provide live tactical procedures, technical instructions, safehouse-level locations, or anything that would function as a logistics manual. This is for OSINT mapping and historical/network analysis.
PART 5 — FINANCING, FACILITATION, COURIERS, MEDIA, AND COMMUNICATIONS INFRASTRUCTURE
1. The infrastructure layer: why Al-Qaeda survives leadership losses
Al-Qaeda’s durability comes from its infrastructure, not just from individual leaders. Its senior leaders can be killed, captured, or displaced, but the network survives through five overlapping systems: money movement, facilitation pipelines, communications/media, sanctuary relationships, and affiliate self-financing. NCTC’s current profile states that Al-Qaeda affiliates carry out most of the network’s operational activity, with AQAP in Yemen, AQIM/JNIM in Mali and the Sahel, al-Shabaab in Somalia, AQIS in Afghanistan/Pakistan, former Hurras al-Din elements in Syria, and some senior/lower-level members residing in Iran. (Director of National Intelligence)
The TIC chart you uploaded captures this same logic visually: it places Al-Qaeda at the center of a wider ecosystem involving AQAP, AQIS, AQIM, JNIM, al-Shabaab, Haqqani/Taliban nodes, media/propaganda roles, covert operations roles, shura labels, regional boxes, and Iran-linked actors. Treat the chart’s internal labels as chart claims unless corroborated by official or independent sources.

2. Al-Qaeda Core financing before and after 9/11
Before 9/11, Al-Qaeda’s financial system was less a single bank account than a dispersed ecosystem of donors, facilitators, charities, couriers, informal transfer networks, and cash movement. The 9/11 Commission’s terrorist-financing staff report says Al-Qaeda relied primarily on hawala and couriers to move substantial funds before 9/11, and that charities were also used as conduits from donors to Al-Qaeda leaders. It also estimates that pre-9/11 Al-Qaeda cost about $30 million per year to sustain and that funds came largely through donations rather than Bin Laden’s personal fortune. (9/11 Commission)
That is important because it corrects a common misconception: Al-Qaeda did not run solely on Bin Laden’s inherited wealth. The system depended on financial facilitators who raised money from donors, religious/social networks, sympathetic businessmen, and charitable fronts, sometimes with donors unaware of the final destination. After 9/11, Al-Qaeda Core lost major training infrastructure and Taliban-backed territorial depth, so the network’s costs changed and affiliates increasingly developed their own local revenue bases. (9/11 Commission)
For your OSINT platform, the pre-9/11 finance layer should have these categories: donors, charity conduits, hawala/informal transfer facilitators, cash couriers, training-camp expenditures, Taliban-support costs, safe travel, documents/logistics, media/communications, and attack-cell support. Do not model this as a modern banking ledger; model it as a human-facilitator network.

3. Couriers and human communications
Al-Qaeda has repeatedly used trusted couriers because electronic communications are vulnerable to surveillance. In the pre-9/11 and immediate post-9/11 period, couriers moved money, messages, documents, and personal instructions between senior leaders and field operatives. The 9/11 Commission’s terrorist-financing staff report specifically notes the role of couriers in moving funds before 9/11. (9/11 Commission)
The post-2011 Abbottabad material reinforced how important physical and human communications were to the network. CIA released nearly 470,000 additional files from the May 2011 Abbottabad raid, explicitly saying the release was intended to enhance public understanding of Al-Qaeda and Bin Laden. Those files matter because they show Al-Qaeda’s command culture: letters, drafts, media guidance, affiliate correspondence, internal advice, family material, and concerns about surveillance. (CIA)
For your project, “courier” should be a relationship category, not just a job title. Some people were literal message-carriers; others were money movers, media intermediaries, family facilitators, or emissaries. A useful OSINT distinction is:
Courier: physically carries messages, money, or materials. Facilitator: arranges movement, shelter, documents, finance, or contacts. Liaison: connects leadership to an affiliate, state actor, tribal network, or regional commander. Media intermediary: moves statements, recordings, approvals, edits, or releases. Family facilitator: helps relocate or shelter family members of senior leaders.

4. Iran-based facilitation network
The Iran-based Al-Qaeda file is one of the most important infrastructure layers after 9/11. NCTC’s current Al-Qaeda profile states that some senior leaders and lower-level members reside in Iran. (Director of National Intelligence) This does not mean Iran is “Al-Qaeda” or that every relationship is straightforward; rather, open-source U.S. government reporting repeatedly identifies Iran-based facilitation networks used by Al-Qaeda figures.
Yasin al-Suri / Ezedin Abdel Aziz Khalil
Yasin al-Suri is one of the clearest public-source examples of the Iran facilitation pipeline. Treasury identified him in July 2011 as part of an Iran-based Al-Qaeda network that moved money, facilitators, and operatives from the Middle East to South Asia. Treasury said the network served as a “core pipeline” through which Al-Qaeda moved funds and personnel to support senior leaders in Pakistan. (9/11 Commission)
In network terms, Yasin al-Suri should be modeled as a facilitator-financier node connecting donors, recruits, Iran-based transit, Pakistan/Afghanistan senior leadership, and Al-Qaeda’s older command layer. The TIC chart also names Yasin al-Suri as an “Iranian AQ operative,” but the chart’s more specific wording about Gaza operations should be treated as chart-derived unless corroborated.
Muhsin al-Fadhli
Treasury stated that Muhsin al-Fadhli worked with Al-Qaeda’s Iran-based facilitation network beginning in 2009, was arrested by Iranian authorities, released in 2011, and then assumed leadership of the facilitation network from Yasin al-Suri later that year. Treasury also identified Adel Radi Saqr al-Wahabi al-Harbi as a key member and deputy within al-Fadhli’s Iran-based network. (U.S. Department of the Treasury)
Al-Fadhli is useful in your platform because he links three layers: Iran-based facilitation, Gulf donor/facilitator channels, and later Syria/Khorasan Group concerns. His role was not simply “fighter” or “commander”; he was part of the connective tissue that allowed people, funds, and instructions to cross regional boundaries. (U.S. Department of the Treasury)
Sayf al-Adl
Rewards for Justice describes Sayf al-Adl as an Iran-based senior Al-Qaeda leader, a member of Al-Qaeda’s Majlis al-Shura, and head of its military committee. RFJ says he moved to southeastern Iran after the 1998 embassy bombings, was placed under Iranian house arrest in 2003, and was released in 2015 in a prisoner exchange. (U.S. Department of the Treasury)
That creates one of the biggest modern leadership problems: if Sayf al-Adl is de facto leader, Al-Qaeda’s top command is politically and geographically constrained by an Iran-based status. For OSINT mapping, he should connect to military leadership, Shura Council, 1998 embassy bombing indictment, Iran-based senior leadership, and post-Zawahiri succession ambiguity. (U.S. Department of the Treasury)
Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi
Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi is the key bridge between media, external communications, Zawahiri’s family, and Iran-based leadership. Rewards for Justice says he heads Al-Qaeda’s External Communications Office and coordinates activities with Al-Qaeda affiliates. RFJ also states that he was designated as an SDGT in January 2021. (Rewards For Justice)
The FBI says al-Maghrebi reportedly studied software programming in Germany before traveling to Afghanistan, where he was selected to manage Al-Sahab, Al-Qaeda’s primary media wing; after 9/11, he fled to Iran and may travel between Iran and Pakistan. (FBI)
For your platform, al-Maghrebi deserves a very high importance score because he touches four major systems: Zawahiri family tie, al-Sahab media, external affiliate communications, and Iran-based leadership. The TIC chart also labels him head of AQ propaganda / As-Sahab Media, which aligns with FBI/RFJ descriptions, though any extra Shura-specific chart label should still be tagged by source-confidence.

5. Al-Qaeda Core media infrastructure
Al-Sahab Media
Al-Sahab is Al-Qaeda Core’s primary media arm. It historically produced leadership statements, ideological lectures, martyrdom-style messaging, strategic guidance, and affiliate-facing propaganda. The FBI identifies al-Sahab as Al-Qaeda’s primary media wing and states that Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi was selected to manage it after traveling to Afghanistan. (FBI)
Al-Sahab matters because media was not just propaganda; it was also command signaling. When Bin Laden, Zawahiri, or senior figures released messages, those messages could set priorities, mediate disputes, endorse affiliates, respond to global events, or give ideological framing. In a decentralized network, media becomes a quasi-command tool.
Your platform should treat al-Sahab as:
* Media wing of Al-Qaeda Core
* Leadership statement channel
* Affiliate legitimacy channel
* Ideological training/dissemination arm
* External communications bridge
* Archive source for leadership priorities
* Indicator of succession, disputes, and affiliate recognition
Bin Laden / Zawahiri communications style
Bin Laden’s late-period communications, especially those reflected in Abbottabad materials, show a leader worried about surveillance, affiliate discipline, public image, and the strategic costs of indiscriminate violence. CIA’s Abbottabad release is valuable because it contains files recovered from Bin Laden’s compound and was released to improve public understanding of Al-Qaeda and its former leader. (CIA)
Zawahiri’s communications often functioned as ideological arbitration: he addressed unity, affiliate conduct, the Islamic State split, South Asia expansion, Syria disputes, and loyalty questions. In your system, Zawahiri should connect to EIJ, Al-Qaeda Core, AQIS announcement, Nusra/ISIS dispute, al-Maghrebi, al-Sahab, and post-2011 continuity messaging.

6. AQAP finance, media, and facilitation infrastructure
AQAP is one of the best examples of a mature Al-Qaeda affiliate because it combined insurgency, territorial control, external plotting, kidnapping, media production, and local financing. NCTC describes AQAP as one of Al-Qaeda’s most capable affiliates, formed in Yemen in 2009 by Saudi and Yemeni extremists, and says it attacks Yemeni government officials, security forces, Huthis, ISIS-Yemen, infrastructure, and Western targets while operating mostly in southern and central Yemen. (Director of National Intelligence)
AQAP revenue system
AQAP’s public-source revenue categories include:
Kidnapping and ransom AQAP has used hostage-taking both as leverage and as revenue, especially in Yemen’s fragmented tribal/security environment.
Bank robbery / looting / wartime asset seizure During periods of territorial control, especially around Mukalla, AQAP reportedly gained access to state and local revenue sources.
Taxation and extortion during territorial control AQAP’s Ansar al-Sharia governance attempts allowed it to extract funds from ports, businesses, transport, and local populations.
Tribal and local patronage relationships AQAP survived partly by embedding into local grievances, tribal arrangements, and anti-Huthi or anti-government conflict dynamics.
External donations and facilitation AQAP inherited part of Al-Qaeda’s broader donor/facilitator ecosystem, but its Yemen base also forced it to become locally self-sustaining.
AQAP media: Al-Malahem, Inspire, and English-language outreach
AQAP’s media wing is commonly associated with Al-Malahem Media. AQAP also became globally notorious for Inspire, an English-language online magazine. Inspire is widely described as an AQAP publication, with Samir Khan as its former editor and Anwar al-Awlaki as its most famous English-language ideological figure. (Wikipedia)
Do not reproduce operationally useful content from Inspire in your platform. For OSINT purposes, document it as a propaganda artifact, not as content to quote extensively. The important analytical points are:
* It targeted English-speaking audiences.
* It helped AQAP project influence beyond Yemen.
* It amplified Anwar al-Awlaki’s ideological brand.
* It linked lone-actor messaging to Al-Qaeda’s global narrative.
* Its production showed AQAP’s unusual media sophistication compared with many local insurgent groups.
AQAP media figures
Anwar al-Awlaki should be tagged as: AQAP ideologue, English-language preacher, external-influence figure, killed 2011. Public reporting at the time described him as a major AQAP-linked propagandist whose sermons influenced several Western plots. (The Guardian)
Samir Khan should be tagged as: AQAP media producer, Inspire editor, English-language propaganda figure, killed 2011. Public reporting describes Khan as associated with Inspire and killed alongside Awlaki. (WIRED)
Ibrahim al-Qosi should be tagged as: former Bin Laden associate, courier/accountant/treasurer history, former Guantanamo detainee, later AQAP spokesperson. The Counter Extremism Project states that Qosi served as a courier, accountant, and treasurer for Al-Qaeda until his arrest in 2001, was detained at Guantanamo from 2002 to 2012, and later rejoined Al-Qaeda in 2014. (Counter Extremism Project)
AQAP facilitation and prison networks
AQAP’s leadership history is full of former detainees, jailbreaks, and prison-network effects. Sa’id al-Shihri was a former Guantanamo detainee; Ibrahim al-Qosi was a former Guantanamo detainee; Khalid Batarfi had a prison/jailbreak history before later AQAP leadership. This matters because prisons served as recruitment environments, credentialing systems, and reconstitution points for the network.
Your platform should tag these people with “former detainee,” “prison network,” “AQAP reconstitution,” and “media/leadership transition” where appropriate.

7. AQIM / JNIM finance and facilitation infrastructure
AQIM and JNIM are built around a different financing ecology than Al-Qaeda Core or AQAP. They operate through Sahel mobility, kidnapping-for-ransom, smuggling-route taxation, local extortion, protection payments, rural coercion, and siege/blockade economics. NCTC says AQIM revenue includes extortion, kidnapping for ransom, and donations. (Director of National Intelligence)
AQIM financial model
AQIM grew out of the Algerian GSPC, but its Sahel expansion gave it access to a wider desert economy. Its revenue base included:
* Kidnapping foreign nationals.
* Ransom bargaining through intermediaries.
* Smuggling-route taxation.
* Protection payments from traffickers and local actors.
* Arms and vehicle acquisition through regional conflict spillover.
