* Alliances with local commanders and mobile desert units.
AQIM’s financial model was not simply “terrorist fundraising”; it increasingly resembled a hybrid of insurgency, criminal brokerage, local governance, and trans-Saharan mobility.
JNIM financial model
JNIM inherited AQIM’s Sahel financing model but localized it further through Mali and Burkina Faso. The State Department describes JNIM as Al-Qaeda’s largest and most lethal affiliate in West Africa and the Sahel and says it receives funding through kidnapping for ransom, extortion, and from smugglers and traffickers. (U.S. Department of State)
JNIM’s most important financial mechanisms are:
Kidnapping-for-ransom Targets can include foreign nationals, officials, local elites, aid workers, and security-linked figures. Ransom is both a revenue source and political leverage.
Extortion and forced taxation Local communities, transporters, traders, herders, and businesses may be coerced into payment.
Smuggling/trafficking taxation JNIM can tax or regulate movement across routes without necessarily owning every commodity flow.
Siege and blockade economics The 2025 Mali fuel blockade is important because it shows JNIM using economic disruption as strategic pressure, not just violence. NCTC lists JNIM attacks on Malian fuel import convoys as causing widespread fuel shortages in Bamako and across Mali. (Director of National Intelligence)
Local conflict monetization JNIM benefits from weak governance, ethnic/community disputes, retaliation cycles, and competition between state forces, militias, and rival jihadist groups.
Al-Murabitun and Belmokhtar’s financing role
Al-Murabitun and Mokhtar Belmokhtar’s network are central to the kidnapping/smuggling/hostage side of the Sahel file. The TIC chart identifies Belmokhtar as leader of al-Murabitun and notes uncertainty around death claims.
For your OSINT platform, Belmokhtar should connect to:
* AQIM
* Al-Murabitun
* In Amenas
* Sahara kidnapping economy
* Smuggling-route ecosystem
* JNIM merger lineage
* Status: contested/uncertain

8. Al-Shabaab finance, taxation, amniyat, and media
Al-Shabaab has one of the most sophisticated affiliate revenue systems in the Al-Qaeda ecosystem. NCTC identifies Mahad Karate / Abdirahman Mohammed Warsame as both finance chief and commander of al-Shabaab’s amniyat, its intelligence and security wing. (Director of National Intelligence)
Al-Shabaab revenue model
Al-Shabaab’s financing is heavily local and coercive. Public country-of-origin reporting by the EUAA describes al-Shabaab’s system as an extensive and diversified revenue system based on coercive taxation, with revenue from checkpoints, forced zakat/alms, business extortion, import taxation, kidnapping for ransom, taxation of farm produce, livestock sales, water and irrigation resources, vehicle registration fees, operating-license fees, ad hoc local taxes, property, and business ventures. (European Union Agency for Asylum)
That makes al-Shabaab different from classic donor-driven Al-Qaeda Core. It behaves like a shadow state: taxing movement, trade, agriculture, business, and social life. Its finance architecture is embedded into Somalia’s economy and enforced through coercion, courts, threats, and intelligence.
Treasury’s al-Shabaab finance designations
Treasury and the Terrorist Financing Targeting Center designated al-Shabaab networks in 2025, describing targets involved in fundraising and other support activities for al-Shabaab. Treasury also said the group abuses the regional financial system and has designated financial facilitators serving as interlocutors between al-Shabaab and local businesses. (U.S. Department of the Treasury)
That matters for your platform because al-Shabaab finance should not be modeled as one “treasurer.” It is a distributed taxation bureaucracy involving commanders, collectors, business intermediaries, coercive courts, checkpoints, and money-laundering/facilitation networks.
Amniyat: intelligence and coercive enforcement
The UN sanctions profile for Mahad Karate describes al-Shabaab’s Amniyat as the group’s intelligence wing and states that it plays a key role in suicide attacks, assassinations in Somalia, Kenya, and the region, and logistics/support for al-Shabaab terrorist activities. (United Nations)
The Amniyat should be mapped as both a security wing and a financial-enforcement mechanism. It identifies dissent, enforces payments, intimidates businesses, executes assassinations, monitors internal loyalty, and supports complex attacks. In a graph, Mahad Karate should connect to:
* al-Shabaab
* Amniyat
* Finance chief role
* Assassination/intelligence capability
* Garissa University attack context
* UN sanctions
* RFJ/U.S. designations
* Somalia/Kenya regional operations
Al-Kataib Media Foundation
Al-Shabaab’s media arm, commonly known as Al-Kataib, handles official messaging, attack claims, battlefield media, ideological releases, and recruitment/legitimacy material. In your system, treat al-Kataib as the East Africa equivalent of a media organ like al-Sahab or al-Malahem, but tied more directly to insurgent territorial and military messaging.

9. AQIS financing, recruitment, and communications
AQIS is smaller and less financially self-sufficient than al-Shabaab or JNIM, but it is strategically important because of its Taliban safe-haven relationship, South Asian recruitment, Bangladesh/Pakistan/India targeting narrative, and connection to Al-Qaeda Core. NCTC says AQIS is primarily based in Afghanistan, operates in Afghanistan, Pakistan, India, and Bangladesh, has fewer than 200 members, receives overarching guidance from Al-Qaeda senior leaders, and maintains longstanding Taliban ties. (Director of National Intelligence)
AQIS finance and support structure
AQIS appears to rely on:
* Taliban-linked sanctuary and permissive environments.
* South Asian militant recruitment pools.
* Ideological/media outreach in Urdu, Bengali, and regional languages.
* Small-cell attacks rather than large territorial taxation.
* Relationship with Al-Qaeda senior leadership.
* Cross-border militant ecosystems involving Pakistan, Afghanistan, Bangladesh, India, and Kashmir narratives.
NCTC says AQIS pledged allegiance to Taliban leader Haybatullah Akhundzada partly to secure safe haven in Afghanistan. This means AQIS’s “infrastructure” is less about controlling a revenue empire and more about embedding into the Afghan/Taliban sanctuary ecosystem. (Director of National Intelligence)
AQIS media
AQIS media should be treated as a regional propaganda system designed to speak to South Asian audiences. Its propaganda has historically emphasized India, Bangladesh, Pakistan, Kashmir, blasphemy narratives, secular writers, and Taliban legitimacy. Do not reproduce propaganda; for OSINT, track outlets, claims, dates, and languages only.
AQIS recruitment nodes
NCTC names Muhammad Maruf as AQIS recruitment head and Usama Mahmood / Abu Zar as AQIS leader after the death of Asim Umar. These people should connect to recruitment/media/leadership rather than large-scale territorial finance. (Director of National Intelligence)

10. Syria / Hurras al-Din facilitation and media ecosystem
Syria’s Al-Qaeda infrastructure is messy because it includes Jabhat al-Nusra, the Nusra-to-HTS break, and the later Al-Qaeda-loyalist Hurras al-Din structure. NCTC says Hurras al-Din was Al-Qaeda’s Syria affiliate before it publicly announced dissolution in early 2025, though former members remain in Syria. (Director of National Intelligence)
Syria’s role as a facilitation hub
From 2011 onward, Syria became a magnet for foreign fighters, financiers, ideological entrepreneurs, and Al-Qaeda veterans. The core Al-Qaeda problem in Syria was not only money; it was legitimacy and chain of command. Zawahiri had to manage the rivalry between Nusra and ISIS, then the split between Nusra/HTS and Al-Qaeda loyalists.
Hurras al-Din infrastructure
Hurras al-Din’s infrastructure was more fragile than AQAP, JNIM, or al-Shabaab. It depended on:
* Former Nusra loyalists.
* Veteran Al-Qaeda-linked figures.
* Syria-based safe zones and factional alliances.
* Religious legitimacy from figures like Sami al-Uraydi.
* Connections to older Al-Qaeda leadership.
* Limited finance/logistics networks under heavy pressure.
State designated Hurras al-Din in 2019 as an Al-Qaeda-affiliated group in Syria, and CENTCOM stated in 2025 that it killed a senior Hurras al-Din finance and logistics official in northwest Syria. (state.gov)
Syria media layer
Syria’s Al-Qaeda media ecosystem was fragmented: Nusra, then Jabhat Fatah al-Sham, then HTS-controlled media and Al-Qaeda-loyalist outlets around Hurras and allied factions. For your Al-Qaeda-centric project, the important point is not to track all HTS media as Al-Qaeda; instead, split it into:
* Former Al-Qaeda affiliate media: Jabhat al-Nusra period.
* Breakaway / independent HTS media: post-Al-Qaeda split.
* Al-Qaeda-loyalist media: Hurras al-Din and allied loyalists.
* Veteran ideological statements: al-Uraydi, Abu al-Khayr, Abu Khalid al-Suri-style figures.

11. Training-camp and safe-haven geography: broad public layer
Training geography should be mapped historically and broadly. Avoid precise current facilities or addresses. The useful OSINT categories are historic camp zone, safe-haven region, affiliate base area, cross-border corridor, and post-strike dispersal area.
Afghanistan
Afghanistan is the original safe-haven geography of Al-Qaeda’s mature form. It hosted training camps, leadership sanctuary, Taliban protection, and the pre-9/11 infrastructure. The 9/11 Commission report sections on Al-Qaeda’s rise and renewal in Afghanistan are the foundation source for this layer. (9/11 Commission)
Key broad zones: Kandahar, Khost, Nangarhar, Kabul, Tora Bora/eastern Afghanistan. Key relationships: Taliban, Haqqani Network, Al-Qaeda Core, AQIS. Platform label: historic sanctuary / training / leadership base.
Pakistan
Pakistan is essential because of post-2001 dispersal, captures, courier networks, Abbottabad, FATA/Waziristan militant space, and Al-Qaeda/Taliban/AQIS overlap. The Abbottabad files released by CIA/ODNI should be treated as primary material for studying late Bin Laden communications and network management. (CIA)
Key broad zones: Abbottabad, Karachi, Rawalpindi, Peshawar, North/South Waziristan, FATA borderlands. Platform label: post-9/11 refuge / capture zone / courier and leadership communications zone.
Iran
Iran’s significance is the senior-leadership and facilitation layer, not a classic training-camp geography. NCTC says some Al-Qaeda senior and lower-level members reside in Iran; Treasury identifies Iran-based facilitation networks involving Yasin al-Suri and Muhsin al-Fadhli; RFJ/FBI identify Sayf al-Adl and al-Maghrebi as Iran-based. (Director of National Intelligence)
Platform label: facilitation / restricted sanctuary / senior-leadership ambiguity / financial pipeline.
Yemen
Yemen is AQAP’s operational base. NCTC says AQAP operates mainly in southern and central Yemen and remains one of Al-Qaeda’s most capable affiliates. (Director of National Intelligence)
Key broad zones: Abyan, Shabwah, Hadramawt, al-Bayda, Marib, Sana’a, Aden/Mukalla as historical event cities. Platform label: AQAP base / prison-break network / territorial control / external plotting.
Somalia and Kenya
Somalia is al-Shabaab’s base; Kenya is a major external attack theater. NCTC’s al-Shabaab profile identifies Somalia as its base and lists notable attacks in Mogadishu, Nairobi, and Manda Bay. (Director of National Intelligence)
Key broad zones: southern/central Somalia, Mogadishu, Lower Shabelle, Jubaland, Kenya borderlands, Nairobi/Manda Bay as event locations. Platform label: affiliate base / taxation system / amniyat operations / regional attack projection.
Mali / Burkina Faso / Sahel
JNIM and AQIM operate across the Sahel. NCTC describes JNIM as primarily based in Mali and Burkina Faso, active across West Africa, and one of the deadliest current terrorist organizations. (Director of National Intelligence)
Key broad zones: northern Mali, central Mali, Kidal, Gao, Timbuktu, Mopti, Burkina Faso north/east, Niger borderlands. Platform label: JNIM/AQIM insurgency zone / kidnapping economy / smuggling taxation / siege/blockade strategy.
Syria
Syria is the former Nusra and Hurras al-Din theater. NCTC says Hurras al-Din was based in Syria before dissolution and former members remain. (Director of National Intelligence)
Key broad zones: Idlib, northwest Syria, Aleppo/Latakia/Raqqah/Damascus as event-linked areas. Platform label: former affiliate theater / split zone / veteran Al-Qaeda relocation.

12. Affiliate-by-affiliate infrastructure summary
Al-Qaeda Core
Money: donations, facilitators, historic charity conduits, couriers, Iran-based pipeline. Media: al-Sahab. Communications: human couriers, letters, media statements, external communications office. Sanctuary: Afghanistan/Taliban pre-2001; Pakistan post-2001; Iran-based senior figures; Taliban Afghanistan after 2021 as a concern. Key people: Bin Laden, Zawahiri, Sayf al-Adl, al-Maghrebi, Yasin al-Suri, al-Fadhli, Atiyah.
AQAP
Money: kidnapping, local extortion, looting, territorial taxation, criminal activity, tribal/local arrangements. Media: al-Malahem, Inspire, English-language propaganda. Communications: Yemen-based leadership releases, external claims, media statements. Sanctuary: southern/central Yemen. Key people: Wuhayshi, Raymi, Batarfi, Saad al-Awlaki, Ibrahim al-Banna, Ibrahim al-Qosi, Awlaki, Samir Khan.
AQIM / JNIM
Money: kidnapping-for-ransom, extortion, smuggling/trafficking taxes, route control, protection payments. Media: AQIM/JNIM releases and claims infrastructure. Communications: Sahel commander networks, AQIM/JNIM pledge hierarchy. Sanctuary: Mali, Burkina Faso, Sahel borderlands. Key people: Droukdel, al-Anabi, Belmokhtar, Iyad ag Ghali, Amadou Kouffa, Ali Maychou.
Al-Shabaab
Money: coercive taxation, checkpoints, business extortion, zakat, agriculture/livestock/water fees, imports/ports, licensing, local courts. Media: al-Kataib. Communications: local governance/intimidation systems, amniyat, clan/community pressure. Sanctuary: Somalia; regional projection into Kenya. Key people: Ahmed Diriye, Mahad Karate, Abukar Ali Adan, Jehad Mostafa.
AQIS
Money: smaller-scale support, Taliban-linked sanctuary, recruitment networks, regional facilitation. Media: AQIS regional propaganda. Communications: Al-Qaeda senior guidance; Taliban-aligned safe-haven relationship. Sanctuary: Afghanistan/Pakistan. Key people: Asim Umar, Usama Mahmood, Yahya Ghauri, Muhammad Maruf.
Hurras al-Din / Syria
Money: local facilitation, veteran networks, factional alliances, limited fundraising/logistics under pressure. Media: Al-Qaeda-loyalist Syria messaging. Communications: veteran emissaries, ideological statements, Syria-based channels. Sanctuary: northwest Syria, before public dissolution. Key people: Faruq al-Suri, Sami al-Uraydi, Abu al-Khayr al-Masri, Abu Khalid al-Suri.

13. The media organs: clean reference index
Al-Sahab Media
Network: Al-Qaeda Core. Function: central leadership messaging, ideological statements, affiliate legitimacy, succession signaling. Key person: Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi. FBI identifies him as selected to manage al-Sahab and later designated as a senior Al-Qaeda leader. (FBI)
Al-Malahem Media
Network: AQAP. Function: AQAP statements, video releases, attack claims, ideological messaging, Inspire ecosystem. Key associated figures: Anwar al-Awlaki, Samir Khan, Nasser al-Ansi, Ibrahim al-Qosi. Importance: gave AQAP an unusually strong international propaganda footprint.
Inspire
Network: AQAP. Function: English-language propaganda magazine. Former editor: Samir Khan. Importance: global outreach to English-speaking audiences. Public sources describe Inspire as AQAP’s English-language online magazine. (Wikipedia)
Resurgence
Network: Al-Qaeda / AQIS-associated English-language propaganda. Function: strategic/ideological magazine-style outreach aimed at South Asia and English-language audiences. Importance: should be catalogued as propaganda artifact, not reproduced.
Al-Kataib Media Foundation
Network: al-Shabaab. Function: attack claims, videos, battlefield messaging, recruitment, legitimacy. Importance: East Africa affiliate media organ.
AQIM / JNIM media
Network: AQIM/JNIM. Function: Sahel attack claims, communiqués, kidnapping statements, allegiance messaging, threats to regional states and foreign forces. Importance: shows JNIM’s political strategy and coercive messaging, especially during blockades and sieges.
Hurras al-Din / Syria media
Network: former Al-Qaeda Syria affiliate. Function: loyalty statements, ideological releases, Syria-specific messaging. Importance: useful for distinguishing Al-Qaeda-loyalist Syria nodes from HTS breakaway media.

14. Financing / facilitation nodes to add as named entities
Core / Iran-based
Yasin al-Suri / Ezedin Abdel Aziz Khalil — Iran-based facilitator; Treasury-identified pipeline node. (9/11 Commission) Muhsin al-Fadhli — took over Iran-based network from al-Suri, according to Treasury. (U.S. Department of the Treasury) Adel Radi Saqr al-Wahabi al-Harbi — key member/deputy in al-Fadhli’s Iran-based network, according to Treasury reporting. (FDD's Long War Journal) Sayf al-Adl — Iran-based senior leader / military committee. (U.S. Department of the Treasury) Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi — al-Sahab / External Communications Office. (Rewards For Justice) Atiyah Abd al-Rahman — senior manager tied to communications and facilitation; Treasury says al-Suri’s arrangement operated with Atiyah’s knowledge. (9/11 Commission)
AQAP
Ibrahim al-Banna — security/military guidance. Ibrahim al-Qosi — courier/accountant/treasurer history; later AQAP spokesperson. (Counter Extremism Project) Anwar al-Awlaki — ideological/media influence. Samir Khan — Inspire editor/media. Ibrahim al-Asiri — technical operations figure; keep nontechnical. Nasser al-Ansi — senior propagandist/public claimant.
AQIM / JNIM
Mokhtar Belmokhtar — Sahara smuggling/kidnap lineage, al-Murabitun. Abdelhamid Abou Zeid — kidnapping/hostage commander lineage. Iyad ag Ghali — JNIM emir; political/military coalition manager. Amadou Kouffa — Macina/Katibat Macina mobilization. Ali Maychou — JNIM religious/leadership figure. Jafar Dicko — Burkina Faso expansion node.
Al-Shabaab
Mahad Karate — finance chief and amniyat commander. (Director of National Intelligence) Ahmed Diriye — emir. Abukar Ali Adan — deputy/military leadership. Jehad Mostafa — foreign fighter/media/training-related role in public profiles. Aadan Jiss / related TFTC-designated facilitators — al-Shabaab finance/enforcement layer, per Treasury/TFTC designations. (U.S. Department of the Treasury)
AQIS
Usama Mahmood / Abu Zar — leader/media background. Muhammad Maruf — recruitment head. Yahya Ghauri — deputy. Asim Umar — founding emir, killed 2019.

15. How the infrastructure actually fits together
At the strategic level, Al-Qaeda’s infrastructure can be visualized as a hub-and-spoke system, but that misses the real nuance. The better model is federated survival.
Al-Qaeda Core provides ideological legitimacy, senior leadership continuity, media framing, brand identity, and strategic arbitration. It does not need to directly fund every affiliate.
Affiliates provide local revenue, manpower, attacks, governance attempts, propaganda output, and battlefield relevance. AQAP, JNIM, al-Shabaab, and AQIS all support Al-Qaeda’s global brand in different ways.
Facilitators connect otherwise separate theaters: Iran to Pakistan/Afghanistan, Gulf donors to South Asia, Yemen to external propaganda, Somalia taxation to regional finance, Sahel kidnappings to ransom economies.
Media organs convert violence, leadership statements, and ideological claims into legitimacy. Al-Sahab, al-Malahem, al-Kataib, and affiliate media are therefore strategic infrastructure, not just publicity.
Safe havens reduce pressure on leadership and allow continuity. Afghanistan, Pakistan, Iran, Yemen, Somalia, Mali/Sahel, and Syria each served different roles at different times.
Prisons and detainee networks became reconstitution points. AQAP especially shows how former detainees and prison breaks fed leadership and recruitment.
Family and trust networks helped solve the problem of infiltration. Bin Laden family ties, Zawahiri’s son-in-law al-Maghrebi, Haqqani family structures, and veteran Afghan-Arab relationships all mattered because trust is hard to manufacture in clandestine networks.

16. Source-confidence guide for this part
For your platform, tag infrastructure claims like this:
Official high-confidence: NCTC affiliate profiles, FBI wanted pages, RFJ profiles, Treasury sanctions notices, State designations, UN sanctions-monitoring reports. Primary historical: 9/11 Commission, CIA/ODNI Abbottabad releases. Strong analytical: CTC West Point, CSIS, FDD Long War Journal, CEP, major wire services. Use carefully: TIC chart, user screenshots, older media, Wikipedia/secondary biographies. Do not overstate: exact Shura membership, current travel routes, private family details, disputed deaths, and current physical locations.
The UN Security Council’s ISIL/Da’esh and Al-Qaida Monitoring Team report series should be one of the recurring update anchors for sanctions, affiliate trends, financing, and regional threat developments because the UN maintains reports through 2026. (United Nations)

17. What Part 5 adds
This part gives you the infrastructure skeleton:
* how Al-Qaeda moved money before 9/11;
* how couriers and human communications mattered;
* how Iran-based facilitation worked as a public-source network;
* why al-Maghrebi is critical to media and external communications;
* how AQAP funded itself and projected English-language media;
* how AQIM/JNIM monetized kidnapping, extortion, smuggling routes, and blockades;
* how al-Shabaab built a shadow taxation state in Somalia;
* how AQIS depends more on sanctuary/recruitment than large territorial finance;
* how Syria/Hurras operated as a fragile veteran-loyalist network;
* and how media organs function as command, legitimacy, and recruitment infrastructure.
   Absolutely — Part 6 is the location and training-camp geography layer. This is where the network becomes spatial: where Al-Qaeda formed, where it found sanctuary, where affiliates embedded, where leaders moved, where attacks happened, and why each geography mattered.
I’ll keep this broad and historical. I will not give live operational addresses, precise current camp coordinates, or anything that could function as a tactical guide.
PART 6 — LOCATION, SANCTUARY, TRAINING-CAMP, AND REGIONAL GEOGRAPHY
1. The global geography of Al-Qaeda
Al-Qaeda’s geography has never been static. It began in the Afghan jihad / Pakistan support environment, relocated and professionalized during the Sudan period, renewed itself under Taliban protection in Afghanistan, dispersed after 9/11 into Pakistan, Iran, Yemen, Syria, Somalia, and the Sahel, and now functions through a network of affiliates. NCTC’s 2026 profile says affiliates carry out most of Al-Qaeda’s activity: AQAP is based in Yemen; AQIM and JNIM in Mali/Sahel; al-Shabaab in Somalia; AQIS in Afghanistan and Pakistan; Hurras al-Din was based in Syria before its public dissolution; and some senior/lower-level Al-Qaeda members reside in Iran. (Director of National Intelligence)
For your OSINT platform, each location should be classified by function:
Sanctuary: where leaders or families could hide, recover, communicate, or rebuild. Training geography: where recruits historically trained or where public sources report training activity. Transit corridor: movement route between theaters. Affiliate base: where an Al-Qaeda affiliate is primarily based. Attack theater: where Al-Qaeda or an affiliate conducted attacks. Finance geography: where money, ransom, taxation, smuggling, or donor networks mattered. Media/communications node: where propaganda, leadership statements, or courier systems moved through. Prison/detention geography: where arrests, prison breaks, or detainee reconstitution affected the network. Symbolic geography: places important to Al-Qaeda’s narrative even if not a primary base.
The TIC chart you uploaded organizes the same broad geography visually: West Africa, Islamic Maghreb, Arabian Peninsula, Middle East, Khorasan, East Africa, Indian Subcontinent, South-East Asia, Europe/US, Islamic State, and IRGC/Iran-related nodes. Treat its specific relationship lines as chart claims unless corroborated, but the broad regional structure is useful for your platform.

2. Afghanistan — original sanctuary, training base, and post-2021 safe-haven concern
Why Afghanistan matters
Afghanistan is the single most important geography in Al-Qaeda’s history. It was the battlefield that created the Arab-Afghan network, the place where Al-Qaeda matured under Taliban protection, the site of pre-9/11 training camps, the place where Al-Qaeda lost its main sanctuary after the U.S. invasion, and again a central concern after the Taliban returned to power in 2021.
The 9/11 Commission’s report explicitly breaks Al-Qaeda’s early geographic development into phases: the rise of Bin Laden and Al-Qaeda from 1988–1992, building an organization and declaring war from 1992–1996, and Al-Qaeda’s renewal in Afghanistan from 1996–1998. (9/11 Commission)
Key Afghan functions
1980s: Afghan jihad theater. Foreign fighters, Arab financiers, clerics, commanders, and support networks converged around the anti-Soviet war.
1984–1988: Maktab al-Khidamat / MAK era. The TIC chart dates MAK to 1984–1988 and places it as the direct predecessor environment to Al-Qaeda.
1988 onward: Al-Qaeda formed from this Arab-Afghan support and fighter network.
1996–2001: Taliban-controlled Afghanistan gave Bin Laden and Al-Qaeda territorial sanctuary. This allowed training camps, leadership meetings, propaganda production, and external attack planning.
2001: U.S.-led invasion destroyed much of the open training-camp infrastructure and forced leadership dispersal.
2021 onward: Taliban return to power revived concerns about Al-Qaeda and AQIS safe haven.
Important Afghan regions and cities
Kandahar
Function: Taliban political center during the pre-9/11 period; Bin Laden’s relationship with Taliban leadership developed around the Taliban’s southern power base.
Why it matters: Kandahar symbolizes Taliban protection of Bin Laden and Al-Qaeda before 9/11. It should be classified as a historic sanctuary / leadership-proximity zone, not a modern operational location.
Khost
Function: Historic training-camp and militant geography.
Why it matters: Khost was part of the Afghan war and later Al-Qaeda training geography. It should be tagged as historic camp zone / Afghan jihad legacy geography.
Nangarhar / Jalalabad
Function: Eastern Afghan corridor, Afghan-Pakistan movement zone, historic militant geography.
Why it matters: Eastern Afghanistan matters because of cross-border mobility with Pakistan and proximity to routes used by fighters and facilitators.
Tora Bora / eastern Afghanistan
Function: Post-9/11 escape and battle geography.
Why it matters: Tora Bora is central to the 2001 Al-Qaeda escape/dispersal story. It belongs in the platform as battle/dispersal geography, not as a current camp note.
Kabul
Function: Political capital; also the place where Ayman al-Zawahiri was killed in 2022.
Why it matters: Zawahiri’s death in Kabul became a major post-2021 safe-haven controversy. It showed that Al-Qaeda’s senior-leadership geography still intersected with Taliban-controlled Afghanistan after the Taliban’s return to power. NCTC separately says some senior Al-Qaeda leaders reside in Iran, while AQIS is based in Afghanistan and Pakistan; the Kabul strike still matters as a confirmed senior-leader presence event. (Director of National Intelligence)
Helmand / Kandahar / southern Afghanistan
Function: Taliban heartland and insurgency geography.
Why it matters: More relevant to Taliban than Al-Qaeda directly, but critical because Al-Qaeda’s Afghan presence depends heavily on the Taliban security environment.
Post-2021 Afghanistan and AQIS
AQIS is the main modern Al-Qaeda structure tied to Afghanistan. NCTC says AQIS is based in Afghanistan and Pakistan, receives overarching guidance from Al-Qaeda senior leaders, has longstanding ties with the Taliban, pledged allegiance to Taliban leader Haybatullah Akhundzada, and sought to defend the Taliban and help establish an Islamic emirate. (ODNI)
State’s 2024 Afghanistan terrorism report says the Taliban failed to deny safe haven to Al-Qaeda and AQIS, and cites a January 2024 UN report saying Al-Qaeda conducted recruitment from and established training camps in Afghanistan. For your platform, this should be stored as current public-source safe-haven concern, but still kept broad and non-tactical. (state.gov)
Afghanistan platform labels
* Original jihad theater
* MAK / Arab-Afghan incubator
* Pre-9/11 Al-Qaeda sanctuary
* Historic training-camp geography
* Taliban protection environment
* Post-2001 dispersal origin
* Post-2021 safe-haven concern
* AQIS base / Taliban-aligned sanctuary
* Zawahiri death location

3. Pakistan — rear base, capture geography, courier network, and Abbottabad
Why Pakistan matters
Pakistan is the second-most important Al-Qaeda geography after Afghanistan. It was the Afghan jihad rear base, the location of Peshawar’s foreign-fighter infrastructure, the place where Abdullah Azzam was assassinated, a major post-9/11 refuge and capture theater, and the site of Osama bin Laden’s death in Abbottabad.
CIA’s Abbottabad archive says the agency released additional materials recovered in the May 2, 2011 raid on Bin Laden’s Abbottabad compound to enhance public understanding of Al-Qaeda. That makes Abbottabad not only a death location, but also a documentary archive point for studying late-stage Bin Laden command, communications, family life, and affiliate concerns. (CIA)
Important Pakistani locations
Peshawar
Function: Afghan jihad support hub.
Why it matters: Peshawar connected Arab volunteers, Pakistani religious networks, Afghan commanders, fundraisers, clerics, and the MAK infrastructure. Abdullah Azzam was killed in Peshawar in 1989, making it a central symbolic and operational origin point.
Platform label: Afghan jihad rear-base / MAK-era hub / Azzam assassination city.
Abbottabad
Function: Bin Laden’s final hideout and death site.
Why it matters: Abbottabad is the most important late-stage Bin Laden geography. The compound produced a massive public-document archive and confirmed that Al-Qaeda’s founder survived for years in Pakistan after 9/11.
Platform label: Bin Laden final sanctuary / raid site / archive source.
Rawalpindi
Function: Capture geography.
Why it matters: Khalid Sheikh Mohammed was captured in Rawalpindi in 2003, making the city a key node in the post-9/11 capture/detention map.
Platform label: KSM capture geography / post-9/11 counterterrorism location.
Karachi
Function: Urban transit, finance, and militant ecosystem.
Why it matters: Karachi appears repeatedly in South Asian militant networks, including Al-Qaeda-linked travel, logistics, and AQIS-related plots. It should be mapped as a large urban facilitation environment, not a single Al-Qaeda base.
North Waziristan / South Waziristan / former FATA
Function: Post-9/11 militant refuge and drone-war geography.
Why it matters: These areas became heavily associated with Al-Qaeda, Taliban, TTP, foreign fighters, drone strikes, training, and leadership attrition after 2001.
Platform label: post-9/11 refuge / cross-border militant sanctuary / strike geography.
Quetta
Function: Taliban leadership/sanctuary geography.
Why it matters: More Taliban-centered than Al-Qaeda-centered, but important in a Taliban/Al-Qaeda safe-haven map.
Pakistan platform labels
* Afghan jihad rear area
* MAK support geography
* Al-Qaeda post-9/11 refuge
* High-value capture geography
* Abbottabad archive geography
* AQIS/Pakistan operational environment
* Taliban/Haqqani cross-border sanctuary context

4. Iran — senior-leadership ambiguity and facilitation geography
Why Iran matters
Iran is not an Al-Qaeda affiliate base like Yemen, Somalia, or Mali. Its role is more complicated: public U.S. sources describe it as a geography where some senior Al-Qaeda leaders and facilitators have resided, often under restrictions, ambiguous protection, or contested arrangements.
NCTC says some senior Al-Qaeda leaders and lower-level members reside in Iran. Rewards for Justice identifies Sayf al-Adl as an Iran-based senior Al-Qaeda leader, Majlis al-Shura member, and head of Al-Qaeda’s military committee. (Director of National Intelligence) (Rewards For Justice)
Key Iran-based nodes
Sayf al-Adl
Location function: senior-leadership / command ambiguity.
RFJ says Sayf al-Adl is Iran-based and heads Al-Qaeda’s military committee. The FBI also describes him as Iran-based and a leader of the Hittin Committee, which coordinates the group’s transnational activities. (Rewards For Justice) (FBI)
Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi
Location function: external communications / media / affiliate coordination.
Rewards for Justice says al-Maghrebi served as Al-Qaeda’s general manager in Afghanistan and Pakistan from 2012, later relocated to Iran, and as head of the External Communications Office coordinates activities with Al-Qaeda affiliates. (Rewards For Justice)
Yasin al-Suri / Ezedin Abdel Aziz Khalil
Location function: facilitation / movement / finance.
Treasury reporting on the Iran-based Al-Qaeda facilitation network described Yasin al-Suri as part of a pipeline that moved funds and personnel between the Middle East and South Asia. Treasury later said Muhsin al-Fadhli assumed leadership of the network from Yasin al-Suri. (U.S. Department of the Treasury)
Muhsin al-Fadhli
Location function: Iran facilitation → Syria connection.
Treasury said al-Fadhli began working with Al-Qaeda’s Iran-based facilitation network in 2009, was later arrested and released by Iran, and assumed leadership from Yasin al-Suri in 2011. (U.S. Department of the Treasury)
Iran platform labels
* Senior-leadership residence / restriction geography
* Facilitation pipeline
* Al-Qaeda external communications geography
* Post-9/11 family/member movement geography
* Iran-based succession ambiguity
* Not a classic training-camp zone
* Not an Al-Qaeda affiliate base

5. Yemen — AQAP base, prison-break geography, external-plot platform
Why Yemen matters
Yemen is the center of AQAP. NCTC describes AQAP as one of Al-Qaeda’s most capable affiliates, formed in Yemen in 2009 by Al-Qaeda members from Saudi Arabia and Yemeni violent extremists. It is based primarily in southern and central Yemen and has 2,000–3,000 fighters. AQAP has attacked Yemeni government officials, security forces, infrastructure, Huthis, ISIS-Yemen, and Western targets, and its last U.S. attack was the 2019 Pensacola attack by an associate. (Director of National Intelligence)
Important Yemeni locations
Sana’a
Function: prison-break, capital, government/diplomatic attack geography.
Why it matters: Sana’a is tied to AQY/AQAP prison escapes, embassy/diplomatic threats, and attacks on Yemeni government infrastructure. The 2006 Sana’a prison break helped seed the AQAP predecessor environment.
Platform label: prison-break network / capital attack geography / AQAP reconstitution node.
Aden
Function: maritime attack geography.
Why it matters: Aden is central to the USS Cole bombing and the pre-AQAP Yemen Al-Qaeda lineage. It should connect to Jamal al-Badawi, Fahd al-Quso, Abd al-Rahim al-Nashiri, and the broader maritime plot file.
Platform label: USS Cole / maritime attack geography.
Abyan
Function: AQAP/Ansar al-Sharia insurgent base and territorial control area.
Why it matters: AQAP exploited instability to seize or influence parts of Abyan, including Zinjibar, during the Arab Spring / Yemeni state-fragmentation period.
Platform label: AQAP insurgent base / Ansar al-Sharia governance attempt.
Shabwah
Function: AQAP operating area and leadership/strike geography.
Why it matters: Public reporting repeatedly links AQAP to Shabwah as one of its southern operating areas. Use broad regional labels only.
Platform label: AQAP southern Yemen operating area.
Hadramawt / Mukalla
Function: AQAP territorial seizure and revenue geography.
Why it matters: AQAP seized Mukalla in 2015 and used the city/port environment as a major revenue and governance experiment before being pushed out in 2016.
Platform label: territorial control / taxation / port revenue / prison-break geography.
al-Bayda
Function: AQAP conflict geography.
Why it matters: al-Bayda has appeared in AQAP/U.S./Yemeni counterterrorism contexts, including the 2017 Yakla raid.
Platform label: AQAP rural operating area / counterterrorism raid geography.
Marib
Function: tribal, energy, war-economy, and militant geography.
Why it matters: Marib is more broadly tied to Yemen’s civil war, tribal security, and AQAP movement pressures.
Yemen platform labels
* AQAP base
* AQY predecessor geography
* Prison-break reconstitution
* Maritime attack geography
* External-plot platform
* Territorial-control experiment
* Local extortion / taxation geography
* Saudi-Yemeni militant merger zone

6. Saudi Arabia and the Gulf — origin, donor, target, and AQAP merger geography
Saudi Arabia
Saudi Arabia matters in four ways: Bin Laden’s origin, early donor networks, Al-Qaeda’s anti-Saudi messaging, and AQAP’s Saudi-Yemeni merger. AQAP’s formation involved Al-Qaeda members from Saudi Arabia and Yemeni extremists, according to NCTC. (Director of National Intelligence)
Important functions:
* Bin Laden family origin and Saudi elite contrast.
* Early jihad donor networks and clerical/social channels.
* AQAP anti-Saudi objective.
* Saudi militants joining Yemen-based AQAP.
* Attempted assassination of Saudi counterterrorism official Mohammed bin Nayef in 2009.
Platform label: origin / donor geography / AQAP merger input / target state.
Kuwait, Qatar, UAE, Gulf donor environment
This geography is relevant mainly for finance and facilitation, not as a classic Al-Qaeda base. Treasury’s reporting on Iran-based networks references Kuwaiti donor networks in relation to Muhsin al-Fadhli and support to Al-Qaeda-affiliated elements in Syria via Turkey. (FRASER)
Platform label: donor/facilitation environment / finance scrutiny geography.

7. Sudan — Bin Laden’s 1990s base and organizational-building geography
Why Sudan matters
Sudan was the major bridge between the Afghan jihad origins and the renewed Afghanistan/Taliban sanctuary period. Bin Laden lived in Sudan in the early-to-mid 1990s, built business and logistical networks, and expanded Al-Qaeda’s organization before returning to Afghanistan.
The 9/11 Commission’s report identifies 1992–1996 as the period when Bin Laden built an organization and declared war on the United States, followed by Al-Qaeda’s renewal in Afghanistan from 1996–1998. (9/11 Commission)
Key Sudan functions
Business and infrastructure development: Bin Laden used businesses, construction, agriculture, and logistics to build networks.
Militant networking: Sudan hosted a range of Islamist and militant actors during this period.
Transition geography: Sudan was not the final sanctuary; it was the staging period before Bin Laden’s 1996 return to Afghanistan.
Political pressure: Sudan expelled or pressured Bin Laden out in 1996, after which Afghanistan became central again.
Sudan platform labels
* 1990s Al-Qaeda organizational-building geography
* Bin Laden business/logistics environment
* Transitional sanctuary
* Pre-Afghanistan-renewal location

8. Somalia and Kenya — al-Shabaab base and East Africa attack projection
Somalia
Somalia is al-Shabaab’s core base. NCTC describes al-Shabaab as a Sunni terrorist group that publicly pledged loyalty to Al-Qaeda in 2012 and seeks to overthrow the Somali Federal Government, expel foreign forces, and establish an Islamist state. (Director of National Intelligence)
Mogadishu
Function: capital attack theater.
Why it matters: Mogadishu has been the site of repeated al-Shabaab bombings, assassinations, hotel attacks, ministry attacks, and political violence. It is not simply “where al-Shabaab operates”; it is the symbolic and political contest point.
Platform label: capital attack theater / government target geography.
Lower Shabelle / Middle Shabelle
Function: rural insurgent geography, attack corridor, taxation zone.
Why it matters: These areas are often tied to al-Shabaab movement, military pressure, and attacks on Somali/African Union forces.
Platform label: rural insurgency / taxation / base geography.
Jubaland / Kismayo / Juba Valley
Function: southern Somalia and Kenya-border geography.
Why it matters: This is important for cross-border Kenya operations, al-Shabaab retreat and attack corridors, and older port/charcoal-economy issues.
Platform label: cross-border attack corridor / revenue geography.
Bay / Bakool / Gedo
Function: rural influence, checkpoints, taxation, and clan-conflict geography.
Why it matters: Al-Shabaab’s strength depends on its ability to tax, coerce, adjudicate, and move across rural districts.
Kenya
Kenya is the major external attack theater for al-Shabaab.
Nairobi
Function: external urban attack theater.
Why it matters: Westgate Mall and DusitD2 made Nairobi central to al-Shabaab’s regional attack map.
Platform label: external attack city / Kenya target geography.
Garissa
Function: university massacre / border-proximate attack geography.
Why it matters: The 2015 Garissa University attack was one of al-Shabaab’s deadliest Kenya attacks.
Platform label: education-target massacre / Kenya borderland vulnerability.
Lamu / Manda Bay
Function: coastal and military attack geography.
Why it matters: The 2020 Camp Simba / Manda Bay attack killed one U.S. soldier and two U.S. contractors and demonstrated al-Shabaab’s reach against U.S.-linked targets. NCTC lists it as a notable al-Shabaab attack. (Director of National Intelligence)
East Africa older Al-Qaeda geography: Kenya and Tanzania
Kenya and Tanzania matter beyond al-Shabaab because the 1998 U.S. Embassy bombings in Nairobi and Dar es Salaam were Al-Qaeda Core’s first world-shaking external attacks. These cities should be tagged separately from al-Shabaab’s later Kenya operations.
Somalia/Kenya platform labels
* Al-Shabaab base
* East Africa affiliate sanctuary
* Taxation and shadow-governance geography
* Amniyat/internal-security geography
* Regional attack projection
* Kenya external attack theater
* 1998 Al-Qaeda Core embassy-bombing geography

9. Mali, Burkina Faso, Niger, and the Sahel — AQIM/JNIM’s modern center of gravity
Why the Sahel matters
The Sahel is one of the most important modern Al-Qaeda geographies. AQIM moved southward from Algeria into Mali/Sahel networks, while JNIM became the major Al-Qaeda-aligned coalition. NCTC says JNIM is primarily based in Mali and Burkina Faso, active across West Africa including parts of Niger, Nigeria, and coastal West Africa, and has at least 6,000 members. (Director of National Intelligence)
JNIM’s geography is not only “where attacks happen.” It is a route-control, taxation, kidnapping, siege, and rural-governance geography.
Mali
Northern Mali: Kidal, Gao, Timbuktu
Function: historic northern Mali insurgency and jihadist-control geography.
Why it matters: In 2012, AQIM and allied groups exploited Mali’s collapse and helped seize major northern towns. Later, Kidal remained symbolically and strategically central to Mali’s state-collapse and insurgent-separatist dynamics.
Platform label: northern Mali insurgent geography / Tuareg-jihadist convergence / AQIM-JNIM lineage.
Central Mali: Mopti, Macina, Ségou areas
Function: JNIM/Katibat Macina mobilization geography.
Why it matters: Amadou Kouffa’s Macina/Katibat Macina network made central Mali one of JNIM’s most important recruitment, coercion, and violence zones.
Platform label: Katibat Macina / Fulani mobilization / village attack and coercion geography.
Bamako
Function: capital pressure and strategic-symbolic geography.
Why it matters: JNIM attacks and blockades that affect Bamako show strategic reach. NCTC lists JNIM attacks on fuel import convoys in 2025 as causing widespread shortages in Bamako and throughout Mali. (Director of National Intelligence)
Platform label: national-pressure node / strategic-economic target geography.
Kidal
Function: northern strategic node.
Why it matters: NCTC lists coordinated April 2026 attacks by JNIM and the Front for the Liberation of Azawad that included capture of Kidal and attacks on military locations across Mali. (Director of National Intelligence)
Platform label: northern strategic hub / 2026 coordinated offensive geography.
Burkina Faso
Sahel Region / Soum Province / Djibo
Function: JNIM siege and blockade geography.
Why it matters: Djibo and northern Burkina Faso are central to JNIM’s coercive siege/blockade pattern.
Platform label: siege geography / northern Burkina expansion.
Centre-Nord and Est regions
Function: cross-border JNIM expansion geography.
Why it matters: JNIM uses rural insecurity, weak state presence, and cross-border movement to expand across Burkina Faso.
Platform label: cross-border expansion / rural insurgency geography.
Niger
Function: Sahel borderland and cross-pressure geography.
Why it matters: Niger sits between Mali, Burkina Faso, Nigeria, Libya, and Algeria. JNIM activity and broader jihadist activity use borderlands and weak-governance areas.
Platform label: Sahel corridor / borderland operating environment.
Coastal West Africa
Function: JNIM pressure zone.
Why it matters: NCTC says JNIM is active across much of West Africa, including coastal West Africa. This matters because Al-Qaeda’s Sahel strategy increasingly pressures states south of Mali/Burkina. (Director of National Intelligence)
Sahel platform labels
* AQIM southward expansion
* JNIM base
* Kidnapping-for-ransom geography
* Smuggling/trafficking taxation routes
* Rural shadow-governance geography
* Siege/blockade geography
* Cross-border expansion
* Tuareg/Fulani/local-conflict exploitation
* Post-French-withdrawal security vacuum
* Russian/Wagner/Africa Corps conflict environment, where relevant

10. Algeria, Libya, Tunisia, and the Islamic Maghreb — AQIM roots and North African corridor
Algeria
Algeria is AQIM’s origin geography. The GSPC emerged from the Algerian Civil War environment and renamed itself AQIM after officially joining Al-Qaeda in 2006/2007. NCTC says GSPC became AQIM in January 2007 after joining Al-Qaeda in September 2006 and that AQIM historically operated in northern coastal Algeria and desert areas of southern Algeria and the Sahel. (Director of National Intelligence)
Algiers
Function: major bombing geography.
Why it matters: AQIM’s 2007 bombings in Algiers signaled its Al-Qaeda-branded attack strategy.
Platform label: AQIM urban attack geography.
Southern Algeria / Sahara
Function: kidnapping, transit, desert mobility.
Why it matters: AQIM’s Sahara commanders used desert geography for hostages, mobility, and cross-border activity.
Platform label: Sahara mobility / kidnapping corridor.
Libya
Libya matters as a post-2011 arms-flow, militant-mobility, and jihadist-network environment. The TIC chart includes Libyan Islamic Fighting Group, Ansar al-Sharia in Libya, Islamic State Libya, and al-Mourabitoun in the North Africa/Maghreb cluster.
Platform labels:
* post-2011 arms dispersion
* North Africa facilitation environment
* Benghazi case-file geography
* LIFG legacy geography
* AQIM/JNIM/Maghreb overlap geography
Tunisia
Tunisia matters in AQIM/Maghreb mapping because of Katibat Uqba ibn Nafi, border/mountain militancy, and the broader post-Arab Spring militant environment. The TIC chart lists Katibat Uqba ibn Nafi under the Maghreb cluster.
Platform label: Maghreb affiliate/splinter geography.
Mauritania, Morocco, Western Sahara
These geographies matter in the AQIM/Sahel map because of recruitment, kidnapping, smuggling, and ideological networks, but they are secondary to Algeria/Mali in Al-Qaeda’s core regional geography.

11. Syria and Iraq — former branch, split, and Hurras geography
Syria
Syria became a major Al-Qaeda theater after 2011. NCTC identifies Hurras al-Din as Al-Qaeda’s Syria affiliate, publicly dissolved in early 2025, with former members still considered a threat. (Director of National Intelligence)
Idlib / northwest Syria
Function: Nusra/HTS/Hurras geography.
Why it matters: Idlib became the core terrain for the Nusra-to-HTS transition and later Hurras al-Din’s Al-Qaeda-loyalist presence.
Platform label: former Al-Qaeda Syria affiliate geography / split geography.
Aleppo, Latakia, Raqqah, Damascus
Function: broader Syria conflict and attack geography.
Why it matters: These locations are relevant as conflict theaters and Hurras/Nusra attack or presence zones, but your Al-Qaeda map should not treat all Syrian jihadist activity as Al-Qaeda.
Iraq
Iraq matters because of AQI and the Al-Qaeda-to-ISIS split, not because Iraq is a current Al-Qaeda Core base.
Baghdad / Anbar / Diyala / Mosul
Function: AQI insurgency geography.
Why it matters: Zarqawi’s network built the Iraq branch, which later became Islamic State of Iraq and then ISIS. This geography should be treated as Al-Qaeda legacy geography.
Platform labels:
* AQI legacy
* Zarqawi network
* Al-Qaeda-to-ISIS split
* Former branch, not current Al-Qaeda Core

12. India, Bangladesh, Kashmir, and South Asia — AQIS target and recruitment geography
Why South Asia matters
AQIS is Al-Qaeda’s South Asia branch. NCTC says AQIS operates in Afghanistan, Pakistan, India, and Bangladesh, has fewer than 200 members, receives guidance from Al-Qaeda senior leaders, has Taliban ties, and pledged allegiance to Haybatullah Akhundzada partly to secure safe haven in Afghanistan. (ODNI)
Bangladesh
Function: AQIS-linked assassination and civil-society target geography.
Why it matters: AQIS claimed attacks against activists and writers, including the 2016 murders of Xulhaz Mannan and Mahbub Tonoy. Bangladesh belongs in the platform as low-volume, high-symbolism assassination geography.
India
Function: AQIS ideological target geography.
Why it matters: AQIS propaganda has emphasized India, Kashmir, and South Asian Muslim grievance narratives, even though AQIS’s physical base is primarily Afghanistan/Pakistan.
Kashmir
Function: symbolic recruitment and propaganda geography.
Why it matters: Kashmir is central to South Asian jihadist narratives. It should be tagged carefully as ideological/propaganda geography unless a specific Al-Qaeda/AQIS event is sourced.
Pakistan
Already covered above, but for AQIS specifically, Pakistan is both a recruitment and operational environment.
South Asia platform labels
* AQIS target geography
* Taliban safe-haven dependency
* Bangladesh assassination campaign
* India/Kashmir propaganda geography
* Pakistan-Afghanistan command corridor

13. Europe and the United States — attack target, recruitment, propaganda, and prosecution geography
United States
The U.S. is primarily an attack target and prosecution geography, not a base. Relevant nodes include:
* 1993 World Trade Center bombing precursor network.
* 9/11 attacks.
* Pensacola 2019 AQAP-claimed attack.
* U.S. criminal prosecutions and detentions.
* FBI/RFJ wanted listings.
* State/Treasury designations.
NCTC’s AQAP profile identifies Pensacola as AQAP’s last U.S. attack, when an associate killed three U.S. servicemembers and injured eight others in December 2019. (Director of National Intelligence)
Europe
Europe matters through:
* Hamburg cell / 9/11 planning environment.
* Madrid 2004 and London 2005 Al-Qaeda-inspired or linked attack geography.
* France / Charlie Hebdo AQAP claim.
* Germany as al-Maghrebi’s education/residence history before Afghanistan, according to FBI. (FBI)
* Fundraising, logistics, radicalization, and prosecution cases.
Platform label: external target / diaspora facilitation / prosecution geography / propaganda audience.

14. South-East Asia — Jemaah Islamiyah and Al-Qaeda-linked regional ecosystem
South-East Asia is not one of Al-Qaeda’s current strongest official affiliate bases, but it is historically important because of Jemaah Islamiyah, the Bali bombings, Hambali, Abu Bakar Bashir, and Al-Qaeda’s Southeast Asian training/funding relationship.
The TIC chart places Jemaah Islamiyah, Abu Sayyaf Group, Bangsamoro Islamic Freedom Fighters, and Islamic State Philippines in the South-East Asia regional cluster. For your Al-Qaeda-centric platform, include only those links that are historically tied to Al-Qaeda, mainly Jemaah Islamiyah and Hambali-era networks.
Indonesia
Function: Jemaah Islamiyah base geography.
Why it matters: Bali bombings and JI’s historical Al-Qaeda linkage.
Malaysia
Function: meetings, travel, and JI/Hambali regional geography.
Why it matters: Southeast Asian militant networking and 9/11-related meeting history.
Philippines
Function: Abu Sayyaf/JI overlap geography.
Why it matters: Important to broader jihadist geography, but not a core Al-Qaeda affiliate in the same way AQAP/AQIS/AQIM/JNIM/al-Shabaab are.

15. Turkey and the transit geography problem
Turkey matters in several periods as a transit and Syria-access geography, especially during the Syrian civil war. Treasury’s older reporting on Iran-based Al-Qaeda networks described money and fighters moving through Turkey to support Al-Qaeda-affiliated elements in Syria. (FRASER)
For your platform, Turkey should be a transit/facilitation geography, not an Al-Qaeda base.
Platform labels:
* Syria transit route
* donor/facilitator movement geography
* foreign-fighter transit
* logistical node, not affiliate base

16. Location-by-location platform index
Core-origin and sanctuary locations
Afghanistan: original sanctuary, training, Taliban protection, AQIS safe haven, Zawahiri death. Pakistan: rear base, captures, Abbottabad, Waziristan, AQIS corridor. Sudan: 1990s Bin Laden organizational-building period. Iran: senior leadership/facilitation/communications ambiguity.
Affiliate base locations
Yemen: AQAP base, prison breaks, Mukalla, external plots. Somalia: al-Shabaab base, taxation, amniyat, insurgency. Mali: AQIM/JNIM center, northern/central insurgency, blockade/siege. Burkina Faso: JNIM expansion, siege/blockade geography. Afghanistan/Pakistan: AQIS base. Syria: former Hurras/Nusra geography.
Attack-symbolic locations
Nairobi / Dar es Salaam: 1998 embassy bombings. New York / Washington / Pennsylvania: 9/11. Aden: USS Cole. Paris: Charlie Hebdo / AQAP claim. Pensacola: AQAP-claimed U.S. attack. Bamako / Ouagadougou / Grand-Bassam: AQIM/JNIM-linked urban attacks. Nairobi / Garissa / Manda Bay: al-Shabaab external attacks.
Finance/facilitation locations
Gulf: donor/facilitation environment. Iran: Al-Qaeda facilitation pipeline. Turkey: Syria transit. Sahel: kidnapping/smuggling/extortion routes. Somalia: taxation/shadow-state revenue. Yemen: AQAP kidnapping/extortion/port revenue.
Legacy/split locations
Iraq: AQI legacy and ISIS split. Syria: Nusra/HTS/Hurras split. Algeria: GSPC → AQIM. Mali: AQIM → JNIM. Bangladesh: AQIS assassination campaign. Indonesia/Malaysia/Philippines: JI/Al-Qaeda-linked Southeast Asia history.
