18. How to connect locations to people
Osama bin Laden
Saudi Arabia: origin and family. Afghanistan: Afghan jihad, training, Taliban sanctuary. Sudan: 1990s organizational-building. Afghanistan again: 1996–2001 renewal. Pakistan: post-9/11 hiding and Abbottabad death.
Ayman al-Zawahiri
Egypt: origin, EIJ. Afghanistan/Pakistan: Al-Qaeda Core leadership. Iran-linked network: indirect through in-laws and senior cadre. Kabul: death in 2022.
Sayf al-Adl
Egypt: origin. Afghanistan/Pakistan: Al-Qaeda military leadership history. Iran: current public-source senior leadership geography.
Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi
Morocco: origin. Germany: education/software background in FBI profile. Afghanistan/Pakistan: al-Sahab and general-manager role. Iran: external communications role.
Jalaluddin Haqqani
Afghanistan/Pakistan borderlands: mujahideen, Haqqani base, Bin Laden mentorship. Taliban Afghanistan: ministry and sanctuary relationship.
Sirajuddin Haqqani
Afghanistan/Pakistan borderlands: Haqqani leadership. Kabul: post-2021 Taliban security power.
Nasir al-Wuhayshi
Yemen: AQAP leadership. Afghanistan: Bin Laden aide legacy.
Saad bin Atef al-Awlaki
Yemen: current AQAP leadership.
Iyad ag Ghali
Mali: JNIM, northern Mali, Tuareg jihadist geography. Sahel: regional expansion.
Ahmed Diriye
Somalia: al-Shabaab leadership. Kenya: attack-projection geography through al-Shabaab.
Usama Mahmood
Afghanistan/Pakistan: AQIS leadership. South Asia: propaganda/target geography.

19. What Part 6 adds
This part gives you the geographic skeleton of the Al-Qaeda project:
* Afghanistan as origin/sanctuary/training/AQIS concern.
* Pakistan as rear-base, capture, courier, Abbottabad, and AQIS corridor.
* Iran as senior-leadership and facilitation geography.
* Yemen as AQAP’s base and prison-break/territorial-control platform.
* Saudi/Gulf as origin/donor/target geography.
* Sudan as Bin Laden’s 1990s organizational-building base.
* Somalia/Kenya as al-Shabaab base and external-attack geography.
* Mali/Burkina/Sahel as AQIM/JNIM’s modern center of gravity.
* Algeria/Libya/Tunisia as AQIM/Maghreb roots and corridors.
* Syria/Iraq as former affiliate, split, and legacy geography.
* India/Bangladesh/Kashmir as AQIS ideological and attack geography.
* Europe/US as target/prosecution/propaganda-audience geography.
* South-East Asia as Jemaah Islamiyah/Al-Qaeda-linked historical geography.



PART 7 — MASTER AL-QAEDA EVENT TIMELINE, 1984–2026
1984–1988 — Maktab al-Khidamat / MAK phase
1984 — Maktab al-Khidamat emerges MAK forms around the Afghan jihad support environment. It connects Arab volunteers, fundraising, travel, logistics, recruitment, and clerical authority around the anti-Soviet war in Afghanistan. The TIC chart dates MAK to 1984–1988 and places it as the predecessor infrastructure above Al-Qaeda.
1984–1988 — Peshawar / Afghanistan foreign-fighter pipeline grows Peshawar, Pakistan, becomes the external support hub for Arab-Afghan volunteers going into Afghanistan. Abdullah Azzam and Osama bin Laden become central to this mobilization environment. This is the first location layer that matters: Afghanistan is the battlefield; Pakistan is the rear-base; Peshawar is the logistics/intellectual hub.
1988 — Al-Qaeda founded Al-Qaeda emerges from the Afghan jihad support environment. The 9/11 Commission breaks the origin period into “The Rise of Bin Ladin and al Qaeda (1988–1992),” followed by the Sudan organizational-building period and then Afghanistan renewal after 1996.
1988 — early organizational structure develops Al-Qaeda begins to take shape as a more formal structure with a leader, advisory council, military functions, finance, propaganda/media, and external networks. This is the beginning of Al-Qaeda as an organization rather than just a support network.

1989–1992 — transition from Afghan jihad to global network
November 24, 1989 — Abdullah Azzam assassinated Azzam is killed by a car bomb in Peshawar along with two sons. His death removes one of the most important ideological authorities in the Arab-Afghan environment and leaves Bin Laden with greater room to shape the emerging Al-Qaeda network.
1989 — Soviet withdrawal from Afghanistan The Soviet withdrawal changes the purpose of the Arab-Afghan network. Fighters, donors, and clerics who had mobilized around Afghanistan begin dispersing into new conflicts and ideological campaigns.
1990–1991 — Gulf War political rupture The U.S. military presence in Saudi Arabia during and after the Gulf War becomes a major ideological grievance for Bin Laden. This is one of the political triggers that shifts Al-Qaeda from Afghan war logistics toward a broader anti-U.S. campaign.
1991–1992 — Bin Laden relocates toward Sudan period Bin Laden leaves Saudi Arabia and eventually builds his base in Sudan. This begins the second major Al-Qaeda geography after Afghanistan/Pakistan.

1992–1996 — Sudan organizational-building phase
1992 — Aden hotel bombings / early anti-U.S. attack environment Al-Qaeda-linked militants attempted to target U.S. personnel in Yemen. This is one of the earliest events in the Yemen-Al-Qaeda lineage that later leads to the USS Cole attack and then AQAP.
1992–1996 — Sudan becomes Al-Qaeda’s organizational base Bin Laden uses Sudan as a place to build businesses, logistics, contacts, and militant relationships. The 9/11 Commission frames this as the phase of “Building an Organization, Declaring War on the United States.”
1993 — First World Trade Center bombing This attack is best treated as an Al-Qaeda-adjacent precursor rather than a direct mature Al-Qaeda Core operation. The key connection is the Ramzi Yousef / Khalid Sheikh Mohammed family-planning network that later intersects with Al-Qaeda’s 9/11 architecture.
1993 — Battle of Mogadishu and Somalia memory enters Al-Qaeda narrative Somalia becomes important in Al-Qaeda’s propaganda and strategic learning. Later, Somalia becomes the core geography of al-Shabaab, Al-Qaeda’s East Africa affiliate.
1995 — Riyadh bombing / Saudi militancy escalation Militant violence in Saudi Arabia increases in the mid-1990s. This contributes to the anti-Saudi / anti-U.S. jihadist environment that later feeds into AQAP’s Saudi-Yemeni structure.
1995 — Ramzi Yousef captured in Pakistan Yousef’s arrest matters because it exposes the KSM/Yousef transnational attack-planning lineage. This is a precursor to KSM’s later role in 9/11.
1996 — Bin Laden expelled from Sudan / returns to Afghanistan Bin Laden leaves Sudan and returns to Taliban-controlled Afghanistan. This is one of the most important geographic transitions in the entire timeline. Al-Qaeda’s renewed sanctuary in Afghanistan sets the conditions for the 1998 embassy bombings, the USS Cole attack, and 9/11.
1996 — Bin Laden issues declaration of war against the United States This marks the ideological transition from regional jihad support to direct anti-U.S. global jihad.

1996–1998 — Afghanistan renewal and global escalation
1996–1998 — Al-Qaeda renews under Taliban protection The 9/11 Commission explicitly treats this as “Al Qaeda’s Renewal in Afghanistan.” Al-Qaeda gains training geography, leadership security, propaganda space, and the ability to plan external operations under Taliban protection.
1997–1998 — Al-Qaeda external operations mature Al-Qaeda develops the capacity for synchronized international attacks. East Africa becomes the first major proof of concept.
February 1998 — World Islamic Front declaration / anti-U.S. escalation Bin Laden, Zawahiri, and allied figures issue a public call framing attacks on U.S. targets as a religious duty. This becomes a key ideological milestone before the embassy bombings.
August 7, 1998 — U.S. Embassy bombings in Kenya and Tanzania Al-Qaeda operatives carry out near-simultaneous truck bombings against the U.S. embassies in Nairobi and Dar es Salaam. NCTC lists 224 killed and more than 5,000 injured; the FBI identifies the bombings as Al-Qaeda attacks.
August 1998 — U.S. cruise missile strikes The United States launches retaliatory strikes against targets in Afghanistan and Sudan. This is a major pre-9/11 U.S.-Al-Qaeda confrontation.
1998–1999 — Embassy bombing indictments and wanted listings The embassy bombings expose Al-Qaeda’s Egyptian cadre, East Africa cells, document networks, and global logistics.

1999–2001 — designation, USS Cole, Massoud assassination, 9/11
October 1999 — Al-Qaeda designated U.S. Foreign Terrorist Organization The U.S. State Department designates Al-Qaeda as an FTO. NCTC records the October 1999 designation.
Late 1999 — Millennium Plot disruption Plots linked to Al-Qaeda-aligned networks are disrupted around the millennium. This period reveals the breadth of Al-Qaeda-associated travel, documents, and border-crossing networks.
January 2000 — USS The Sullivans failed attack attempt Al-Qaeda-linked attackers attempt a maritime attack in Yemen, but the boat sinks. This becomes the dry run before the USS Cole attack.
October 12, 2000 — USS Cole bombing, Aden, Yemen Al-Qaeda operatives use an explosives-laden boat to attack the USS Cole in Aden harbor, killing 17 U.S. sailors and injuring 39. The FBI identifies the event as an Al-Qaeda case, and NCTC lists it in both the Al-Qaeda and AQAP-predecessor timelines.
2000–2001 — Hamburg cell / 9/11 planning matures The 9/11 plot develops through Al-Qaeda Core, Khalid Sheikh Mohammed, Bin Laden, Mohammed Atef, and the Hamburg cell. Europe becomes a planning/recruitment geography, while Afghanistan remains the training/sanctuary geography.
September 9, 2001 — Ahmad Shah Massoud assassinated Al-Qaeda-linked operatives assassinate Northern Alliance leader Ahmad Shah Massoud in Afghanistan two days before 9/11. This weakens a key Taliban opponent immediately before the U.S. invasion.
September 11, 2001 — 9/11 attacks Nineteen Al-Qaeda hijackers attack New York, Washington, and Pennsylvania, killing nearly 3,000 people. NCTC lists the attacks as Al-Qaeda’s most consequential operation.
September 23, 2001 — Executive Order 13224 The United States creates a major terrorism-financing sanctions tool. This becomes central to later Al-Qaeda, affiliate, and facilitator designations.
October 2001 — U.S.-led invasion of Afghanistan The Taliban regime is overthrown, and Al-Qaeda loses its main open sanctuary. Senior leaders disperse into Pakistan, Iran, Yemen, and other theaters.
December 2001 — Tora Bora and dispersal Al-Qaeda fighters and senior personnel flee eastern Afghanistan. This is a major dispersal event and marks the transition from open sanctuary to clandestine network survival.
November 2001 — Mohammed Atef killed Mohammed Atef / Abu Hafs al-Masri, Al-Qaeda’s military chief, is killed in Afghanistan. This removes one of Bin Laden’s most important pre-9/11 deputies.

2002–2004 — dispersal, arrests, regionalization, Iraq branch
2002 — Abu Zubaydah captured in Pakistan His capture becomes one of the earliest high-value detainee events of the post-9/11 period.
2002 — Abd al-Rahim al-Nashiri captured Al-Nashiri becomes central to the USS Cole detention/prosecution file.
April 2002 — Djerba synagogue bombing, Tunisia An Al-Qaeda-linked attack targets a synagogue in Tunisia. This belongs to the early post-9/11 global Al-Qaeda attack environment.
June 2002 — Karachi U.S. Consulate bombing Al-Qaeda-linked networks target U.S. interests in Pakistan. Karachi becomes an important urban facilitation and attack geography.
October 2002 — Limburg tanker attack, Yemen Al-Qaeda-linked militants attack the French oil tanker Limburg off Yemen. This continues the maritime attack lineage from USS Cole into the Yemen/AQAP predecessor file.
October 2002 — Bali bombings, Indonesia Jemaah Islamiyah carries out the Bali bombings. This is not an Al-Qaeda Core attack in the same direct sense as 9/11, but it belongs in the Al-Qaeda-linked ecosystem because of JI’s historical Al-Qaeda connections.
November 2002 — Mombasa attacks, Kenya Al-Qaeda-linked attackers strike an Israeli-owned hotel and attempt to shoot down an Israeli airliner. This keeps East Africa in the Al-Qaeda external-operations map after the 1998 embassy bombings.
March 2003 — Khalid Sheikh Mohammed captured in Rawalpindi KSM, principal architect of 9/11, is captured in Pakistan. This is one of the most important Al-Qaeda arrest/capture events.
May 2003 — Riyadh compound bombings Al-Qaeda-linked militants attack residential compounds in Saudi Arabia. This begins a major Saudi domestic counterterrorism phase.
May 2003 — Casablanca bombings, Morocco Al-Qaeda-inspired/local jihadist networks attack targets in Casablanca, showing North Africa’s integration into the wider post-9/11 jihadist wave.
November 2003 — Istanbul bombings Al-Qaeda-linked attackers strike synagogues, the British consulate, and HSBC. Turkey enters the Al-Qaeda attack geography.
2003–2004 — Zarqawi’s Iraq network rises Abu Musab al-Zarqawi’s network becomes the central Iraq jihadist force. Iraq becomes the major new post-9/11 jihadist battlefield.
March 2004 — Madrid train bombings The Madrid attack becomes Europe’s most consequential Al-Qaeda-linked / Al-Qaeda-inspired mass-casualty attack after 9/11.
October 2004 — Zarqawi pledges allegiance to Bin Laden Zarqawi’s group becomes Al-Qaeda in Iraq / AQI. This is a critical branch event because AQI later evolves into the Islamic State of Iraq and then ISIS.

2005–2007 — London, AQI, GSPC-to-AQIM, al-Shabaab emergence
July 7, 2005 — London bombings Four suicide attackers bomb London’s transport system, killing 52 and injuring nearly 800. NCTC lists the London bombings as a notable Al-Qaeda attack.
July 2005 — Sharm el-Sheikh bombings, Egypt Al-Qaeda-inspired/local jihadist networks attack Egyptian tourist areas. This belongs in the wider Al-Qaeda ideological influence environment.
November 2005 — Amman hotel bombings, Jordan AQI-linked attackers bomb hotels in Amman. This demonstrates AQI’s regional attack capability beyond Iraq.
February 2006 — Sana’a prison escape Al-Qaeda prisoners escape from a Yemeni prison. This is crucial for the later AQAP story because veterans from this environment help rebuild Yemen’s Al-Qaeda network.
June 7, 2006 — Abu Musab al-Zarqawi killed Zarqawi is killed in Iraq. AQI survives and later becomes the Islamic State of Iraq pathway.
2006 — Islamic Courts Union collapse / al-Shabaab remains active Al-Shabaab emerges from the Islamic Courts Union military wing environment in Somalia. NCTC says al-Shabaab formed as the ICU’s military wing and remained active after the ICU was removed from power in December 2006.
September 2006 — GSPC aligns with Al-Qaeda The Algerian GSPC aligns with Al-Qaeda. NCTC identifies GSPC as AQIM’s predecessor and says it aligned with Al-Qaeda in September 2006.
January 2007 — AQIM name adopted GSPC becomes Al-Qaeda in the Lands of the Islamic Maghreb / AQIM. NCTC says AQIM assumed its name in January 2007 and retained a largely autonomous command structure and targeting strategy.
April 2007 — Algiers bombings AQIM conducts major bombings in Algeria. This signals the Algerian insurgent network’s rebranding into an Al-Qaeda affiliate.
December 11, 2007 — Algiers UN / Constitutional Court bombings AQIM attacks UN offices and Algeria’s Constitutional Court with car bombs, killing 60 people, according to NCTC.

2008–2010 — al-Shabaab designation, AQAP formation, aviation plots
March 2008 — al-Shabaab designated U.S. Foreign Terrorist Organization NCTC records al-Shabaab’s FTO designation in March 2008.
2008 — U.S. Embassy attack, Sana’a Militants attack the U.S. Embassy in Yemen. This is part of the immediate pre-AQAP escalation environment.
January 2009 — AQAP formally emerges AQAP is formed in Yemen by Al-Qaeda members from Saudi Arabia and Yemeni violent extremists. NCTC identifies AQAP as one of Al-Qaeda’s most capable affiliates.
August 2009 — attempted assassination of Saudi Prince Mohammed bin Nayef AQAP attempts to assassinate Saudi Arabia’s counterterrorism chief. This is AQAP’s early proof of regional reach.
December 25, 2009 — Northwest Flight 253 attempted bombing An AQAP operative attempts to detonate a bomb on a flight from Amsterdam to Detroit. NCTC lists the attempted attack as a major AQAP event.
January 2010 — AQAP designated FTO and SDGT entity The State Department designates AQAP as a Foreign Terrorist Organization and Specially Designated Global Terrorist entity.
July 2010 — Kampala World Cup bombings, Uganda Al-Shabaab carries out bombings in Uganda during the World Cup final, demonstrating external regional attack capability.
2010 — Inspire begins AQAP begins publishing Inspire, an English-language propaganda outlet. NCTC notes AQAP’s English-language outreach through Inspire, which began in 2010.
October 29, 2010 — AQAP cargo bomb plot AQAP attempts to send two explosive packages hidden as printer cartridges on U.S.-bound cargo planes; they are intercepted in the UK and UAE. NCTC lists this as a major AQAP attack attempt.

2011 — Bin Laden death, Arab Spring, AQAP territorial turn
January–March 2011 — Arab Spring destabilization begins Political upheaval across Yemen, Libya, Syria, Egypt, and Tunisia creates openings for Al-Qaeda affiliates and aligned groups.
May 2, 2011 — Osama bin Laden killed in Abbottabad A U.S. raid kills Bin Laden in Abbottabad, Pakistan. CIA describes the operation as the culmination of years of intelligence work.
June 2011 — Ayman al-Zawahiri becomes Al-Qaeda emir Zawahiri succeeds Bin Laden. NCTC records that Zawahiri replaced Bin Laden in June 2011 and served until his death in July 2022.
2011 — AQAP / Ansar al-Sharia seizes territory in Abyan, Yemen AQAP uses the Yemeni state crisis to seize or influence territory, including Zinjibar. This begins AQAP’s governance/territorial phase.
August 2011 — Atiyah Abd al-Rahman killed Al-Qaeda loses a major post-9/11 manager and communications figure.
September 2011 — Anwar al-Awlaki and Samir Khan killed AQAP loses its most famous English-language propagandist and a major media figure.
2011 — Syrian civil war begins Syria becomes the next major jihadist theater and later the site of Jabhat al-Nusra, the Al-Qaeda/ISIS split, HTS, and Hurras al-Din.

2012 — al-Shabaab pledge, AQAP attacks, Benghazi case layer, Syria jihadist growth
February 2012 — al-Shabaab pledges loyalty to Al-Qaeda NCTC says al-Shabaab publicly pledged loyalty to Al-Qaeda in 2012. This formally integrates Somalia/East Africa into Al-Qaeda’s affiliate structure.
May 2012 — Sana’a military parade rehearsal bombing AQAP-linked militants kill scores of Yemeni soldiers. This is one of AQAP’s major attacks against Yemeni military forces.
June 2012 — Abu Yahya al-Libi killed Al-Qaeda loses one of its most important public-facing ideologues and senior figures.
September 2012 — Benghazi attack case layer The Benghazi attack becomes a disputed and complex case involving Libyan jihadist networks, AQIM/AQI-linked claims, and local militants. For your platform, keep this as a separate case-file layer using the screenshots and independent legal/source corroboration. Your screenshots list AQSL, AQI, and AQIM Mali Group individuals, but those should remain screenshot-derived until matched to court records, official reports, or sanctions listings.
2012 — Jabhat al-Nusra emerges in Syria Al-Nusra becomes the Al-Qaeda-aligned Syrian jihadist organization.
2012 — Northern Mali jihadist takeover AQIM, Ansar al-Din, MUJAO, and Tuareg rebel dynamics produce a major takeover of northern Mali. This creates the conditions for AQIM’s Sahel transformation and later JNIM formation.

2013 — Mali intervention, In Amenas, Westgate, ISIS split begins
January 2013 — In Amenas hostage crisis, Algeria AQIM splinter group al-Mulathamun Battalion seizes the In Amenas gas plant; 37 hostages die during the Algerian rescue attempt, according to NCTC.
January 2013 — French intervention in Mali Operation Serval pushes AQIM and allied groups out of overt urban control in northern Mali but disperses them into rural/desert insurgency.
April 2013 — ISIS / Nusra split begins Baghdadi claims a merger between Islamic State of Iraq and Jabhat al-Nusra; Julani rejects it and pledges to Zawahiri. This is one of the most important split events in jihadist history.
September 2013 — Westgate Mall attack, Nairobi Al-Shabaab carries out a four-day siege at Westgate Mall, killing 67 and injuring about 200. NCTC says al-Shabaab claimed it in retaliation for Kenya’s intervention in Somalia.
December 2013 — Yemen Ministry of Defense attack AQAP attacks Yemen’s Ministry of Defense complex. This is one of the major Yemeni government-target attacks in the AQAP timeline.

2014 — AQIS announced, ISIS split irreversible, Godane killed
February 2014 — Sana’a prison break AQAP frees prisoners from Sana’a Central Prison. This continues the AQAP prison-reconstitution pattern.
February 2014 — Al-Qaeda disavows ISIS The Al-Qaeda/ISIS split becomes irreversible. AQI’s legacy has now fully broken from Al-Qaeda command.
September 6, 2014 — AQIS Karachi Naval Dockyard attack attempt AQIS-linked militants try to seize a Pakistani Navy frigate at Karachi dockyard, aiming ultimately to target a U.S. Navy vessel. NCTC lists one Pakistani officer killed.
September 2014 — AQIS formally announced Ayman al-Zawahiri announces Al-Qaeda in the Indian Subcontinent as an umbrella for South Asian jihadist groups. NCTC states AQIS was formally announced in 2014 and based mainly in Afghanistan.
September 2014 — Ahmed Abdi Godane killed Al-Shabaab’s emir is killed by a U.S. strike. Ahmed Diriye / Abu Ubaidah becomes leader.
2014 — Houthi rise in Yemen / AQAP conflict environment changes Yemen’s war environment intensifies. AQAP increasingly operates inside a multi-sided civil war involving Yemeni government forces, Huthis, tribal forces, southern actors, and later coalition-backed forces.

2015 — Charlie Hebdo, AQAP Mukalla, Garissa, Wuhayshi killed, Bamako
January 7, 2015 — Charlie Hebdo attack, Paris Two attackers kill 12 and wound 11 at the offices of Charlie Hebdo. AQAP claims responsibility two days later, according to NCTC.
April 2015 — AQAP seizes Mukalla, Yemen AQAP captures Mukalla and frees prisoners. This is the peak of AQAP’s territorial-governance phase and a major revenue event.
April 2, 2015 — Garissa University attack, Kenya Al-Shabaab gunmen attack Garissa University, killing 148 and injuring 100. NCTC describes it as al-Shabaab’s deadliest attack in Kenya.
June 2015 — Nasir al-Wuhayshi killed AQAP loses its founder and most important Bin Laden-linked leader.
2015 — Hamza bin Laden begins emerging in Al-Qaeda media Hamza’s symbolic role grows as Al-Qaeda tries to preserve the Bin Laden bloodline legitimacy.
November 20, 2015 — Radisson Blu Hotel attack, Bamako AQIM and al-Murabitun attack the Radisson Blu Hotel, take at least 100 hostages, and leave 27 dead, including a U.S. international development worker, according to NCTC.
2015 — Sayf al-Adl released from Iranian custody/restriction Rewards for Justice has stated that Sayf al-Adl was released in 2015 in a prisoner exchange after years under Iranian house arrest/restriction. This matters because he later becomes the most important post-Zawahiri leadership figure.

2016 — AQIM coastal attacks, AQIS Bangladesh killing, Nusra break
January 15, 2016 — Ouagadougou attack, Burkina Faso AQIM militants attack a luxury hotel and police station, killing 30 and taking roughly 100 hostages, according to NCTC.
March 13, 2016 — Grand-Bassam attack, Côte d’Ivoire AQIM gunmen attack a coastal resort, killing at least 16 civilians and three special forces soldiers, according to NCTC.
April 25, 2016 — Xulhaz Mannan and Mahbub Tonoy murdered, Bangladesh AQIS claims responsibility for the murders of LGBTQIA+ activists Xulhaz Mannan and Mahbub Tonoy in Kalabagan, Bangladesh. NCTC lists the attack as AQIS’s last claimed attack as of its May 2024 profile.
June 2016 — AQIS designated U.S. Foreign Terrorist Organization NCTC records AQIS’s 2016 FTO designation.
July 2016 — Jabhat al-Nusra breaks publicly from Al-Qaeda and rebrands Nusra becomes Jabhat Fatah al-Sham, beginning the formal split that later produces HTS and Al-Qaeda-loyalist Hurras al-Din.
April 2016 — AQAP pushed from Mukalla Yemeni and coalition-backed forces expel AQAP from Mukalla. AQAP loses a major territorial and revenue base but remains active.

2017 — JNIM formation, Abu al-Khayr death, Mogadishu bombing, HTS
January 2017 — HTS forms Hay’at Tahrir al-Sham forms from Jabhat Fatah al-Sham and other factions. This marks the Syria branch’s move away from formal Al-Qaeda command.
February 2017 — Abu al-Khayr al-Masri killed in Syria Al-Qaeda loses a senior Egyptian deputy and Syria-linked figure.
March 2017 — JNIM forms JNIM forms when Ansar al-Din, al-Murabitun, Macina Liberation Front, and AQIM’s Sahara Emirate merge. Iyad ag Ghali pledges allegiance to AQIM’s emir, Al-Qaeda’s emir, and the Taliban leader, according to NCTC.
October 14, 2017 — Mogadishu truck bombing Al-Shabaab detonates a truck bomb in downtown Mogadishu, killing more than 500 people and injuring almost 300. NCTC identifies it as al-Shabaab’s deadliest attack in Somalia.

2018 — Hurras al-Din, JNIM designation, Timbuktu UN attack
February 2018 — Hurras al-Din becomes Al-Qaeda’s formal Syria presence NCTC says Hurras al-Din became Al-Qaeda’s formal presence in Syria in February 2018, formed by Nusra Front members loyal to Al-Qaeda after Nusra broke from Al-Qaeda and joined other groups to form HTS.
April 14, 2018 — JNIM attacks UN base near Timbuktu Airport JNIM conducts a complex attack near Timbuktu Airport using rockets, mortars, VBIEDs, and attackers disguised as UN peacekeepers, killing one peacekeeper and wounding 17, according to NCTC.
September 2018 — JNIM designated U.S. Foreign Terrorist Organization NCTC records JNIM’s 2018 FTO designation.
2018 — Jalaluddin Haqqani death announced The Taliban announces the death of Haqqani Network founder Jalaluddin Haqqani. This is a major Haqqani/Taliban/Al-Qaeda sanctuary-network generational shift.

2019 — al-Shabaab Nairobi, AQIS leadership loss, Hamza killed, Pensacola
January 15, 2019 — DusitD2 Hotel attack, Nairobi Al-Shabaab attacks the DusitD2 Hotel complex using an IED and small-arms fire, killing 21, including one U.S. citizen, according to NCTC.
2019 — Yahya Abu al-Hammam killed AQIM/JNIM loses a major Sahel commander.
2019 — Asim Umar killed AQIS founder Asim Umar is killed in a U.S.-Afghan raid. NCTC lists him as AQIS founder and notes his death in 2019.
2019 — Hamza bin Laden killed The U.S. announces Hamza bin Laden was killed in a counterterrorism operation in the Afghanistan/Pakistan region. This removes Al-Qaeda’s most important Bin Laden-family successor symbol.
September 2019 — Hurras al-Din designated NCTC says the U.S. State Department designated Hurras al-Din as a Foreign Terrorist Organization and designated Faruq al-Suri as an SDGT in September 2019.
December 6, 2019 — Pensacola attack A Saudi military officer kills three U.S. servicemembers and wounds eight at Naval Air Station Pensacola. AQAP claims responsibility in February 2020; NCTC says the attacker had been in contact with AQAP for years.

2020 — AQAP, AQIM, al-Shabaab, Iran-based leadership attrition
January 5, 2020 — Camp Simba / Manda Bay attack, Kenya Al-Shabaab attacks the joint U.S.-Kenyan military facility at Manda Bay, killing one U.S. soldier and two U.S. contractors, according to NCTC.
2020 — Qasim al-Raymi killed AQAP emir Qasim al-Raymi is killed by U.S. strike. Khalid Batarfi later succeeds him.
June 2020 — Abdelmalek Droukdel killed AQIM emir Droukdel is killed by French forces in Mali. Abu Ubaydah Yusuf al-Anabi later becomes AQIM emir. NCTC lists al-Anabi as AQIM amir from 2020 onward and Droukdel as deceased former amir.
August 2020 — Abu Muhammad al-Masri reportedly killed in Tehran A senior old-guard Al-Qaeda figure linked to the 1998 embassy bombing case is reportedly killed in Iran. This weakens the Iran-based Egyptian cadre.
2020 — AQAP under Khalid Batarfi AQAP enters a weakened but still active phase under Batarfi, facing pressure from U.S. strikes, Yemen’s civil war fragmentation, Huthi conflict dynamics, and local tribal/security forces.

2021 — Taliban takeover, Hurras attacks, AQIS safe-haven concern
January 2021 — Hurras al-Din attack in Ar Raqqah Province NCTC says Hurras al-Din detonated an explosives-laden vehicle outside a Russian military base in Ar Raqqah Province, causing an unspecified number of casualties.
August 2021 — Taliban returns to power in Afghanistan The Taliban takeover changes Al-Qaeda’s strategic geography. AQIS’s Taliban relationship becomes more significant because NCTC says AQIS pledged allegiance to Haybatullah Akhundzada partly to secure safe haven in Afghanistan.
August 2021 — Hurras al-Din attack in Damascus NCTC says Hurras al-Din attacked a Syrian Republican Guard bus in Damascus, killing one and injuring three, marking the group’s first known attack inside Syria’s capital.
2021 — AQIS congratulates Taliban NCTC says AQIS congratulated the Taliban after the August 2021 takeover of Afghanistan.

2022 — Zawahiri killed, Somalia hotel siege, post-Bin Laden/Zawahiri era
July 31, 2022 — Ayman al-Zawahiri killed in Kabul Zawahiri is killed by a U.S. drone strike in Kabul. NCTC records that he served as Al-Qaeda emir from June 2011 until his death in July 2022.
August 2022 — Hayat Hotel attack, Mogadishu Al-Shabaab conducts a prolonged hotel siege in Mogadishu. This shows the persistence of al-Shabaab’s urban attack capability.
2022 — Sayf al-Adl succession ambiguity intensifies After Zawahiri’s death, Sayf al-Adl becomes widely viewed as the de facto senior figure, but his Iran-based status complicates public formal succession. NCTC lists him as an Iran-based senior leader.

2023 — Sahel expansion, Hurras activity, affiliate-first Al-Qaeda
April 2023 — Hurras al-Din participates in Latakia attack NCTC says Hurras al-Din participated in an attack led and claimed by Ansar al-Islam against an Iranian outpost in Latakia.
2023 — JNIM siege/blockade strategy expands JNIM increasingly uses rural coercion, route interdiction, town sieges, taxation, kidnapping, and local agreements across Mali and Burkina Faso.
2023 — al-Shabaab remains Al-Qaeda’s richest component NCTC’s 2026 profile later states that as of 2025 al-Shabaab was Al-Qaeda’s wealthiest component, reflecting its deep taxation/extortion system.
2023 — UN monitoring continues to track Al-Qaeda affiliates The UN Security Council’s ISIL/Al-Qaeda Monitoring Team continues producing semiannual reports on Al-Qaeda, ISIS, affiliates, sanctions, and regional developments. The UN report archive lists reports through February 2026.

2024 — AQAP succession, JNIM massacres and Bamako attacks, Khalil Haqqani killed
March 2024 — Khalid Batarfi dies; Saad bin Atef al-Awlaki succeeds him AQAP announces Batarfi’s death and names Saad bin Atef al-Awlaki as successor. NCTC’s 2026 AQAP profile lists Sa’d Atif al-Aulaqi as overall AQAP leader.
May 2024 — AQIS public profile: small but strategically important NCTC’s May 2024 AQIS profile says AQIS is based primarily in Afghanistan, has fewer than 200 members, has activity in Pakistan, India, and Bangladesh, and has longstanding Taliban ties.
August 2, 2024 — Lido Beach attack, Mogadishu Al-Shabaab conducts a suicide and small-arms attack at Lido Beach, killing 37 and injuring more than 210, according to NCTC.
August 24, 2024 — Barsalogho massacre, Burkina Faso NCTC lists a JNIM attack in Barsalogho town, Sanmatenga Province, killing at least 130 civilians and wounding 200 more. The NCTC page appears to contain a line typo saying “Mali,” but the event is Barsalogho, Burkina Faso.
September 17, 2024 — JNIM attacks military facilities in Bamako JNIM attacks several military facilities around Bamako and at Modibo Keita International Airport, according to NCTC.
December 2024 — Khalil Haqqani killed in Kabul Khalil Haqqani, senior Haqqani/Taliban figure and uncle of Sirajuddin Haqqani, is killed in Kabul by an IS-KP-claimed bombing. This is primarily a Taliban/Haqqani event but matters to the Al-Qaeda safe-haven network layer.

2025 — Hurras dissolution, JNIM escalation, AQAP threat messaging
January 2025 — Hurras al-Din publicly announces dissolution NCTC says Hurras al-Din, Al-Qaeda’s Syria affiliate, publicly announced dissolution in early 2025, though former members remain a threat. Before dissolution, NCTC estimated 2,000–2,500 fighters.
March 2025 — Hurras al-Din members participate in Latakia/Tartus violence NCTC says Hurras al-Din members participated in widespread violence against religious minorities in Latakia and Tartus, resulting in more than 1,000 casualties, mostly among Alawite civilians.
March 2025 — attempted assassination of Somali President Hassan Sheikh Mohamud NCTC says al-Shabaab attempted to assassinate the Somali president in Mogadishu.
May 11, 2025 — JNIM seizes Djibo, Burkina Faso, for hours NCTC says JNIM operatives seized control of Djibo, a regional capital in northern Burkina Faso, for about nine hours and killed dozens of soldiers and civilians.
June 2025 — AQAP leader Saad al-Awlaki releases threat video NCTC says AQAP released a video of Sa’d Atif al-Aulaqi calling for attacks against senior U.S. officials, including the president and members of Congress.
September 26, 2025 — JNIM kidnaps UAE royal family member near Bamako NCTC says JNIM kidnapped a member of the UAE royal family near Bamako and later released him for ransom.
September–November 2025 — JNIM fuel import blockade in Mali JNIM repeatedly attacks Malian fuel import convoys, causing widespread shortages in Bamako and throughout Mali, according to NCTC. This is one of the most important modern JNIM events because it shows economic warfare, not just tactical attacks.
2025 — JNIM becomes one of the world’s deadliest terrorist groups NCTC says JNIM was the world’s second-deadliest terrorist group in 2025, accounting for more than 1,200 deaths across four countries.
2025 — al-Shabaab described as Al-Qaeda’s wealthiest component NCTC says that as of 2025, al-Shabaab was Al-Qaeda’s wealthiest component and had killed more U.S. citizens than any other Al-Qaeda affiliate since 2014.

2026 — current status and latest major JNIM activity
January 2026 — NCTC Hurras profile updated NCTC’s January 2026 profile states that Hurras al-Din dissolved publicly in early 2025, but former members remain a threat and may continue terrorist activity in the Middle East.
February 2026 — UN Monitoring Team thirty-seventh report listed The UN Security Council’s ISIL/Al-Qaeda Monitoring Team report archive lists S/2026/44, the thirty-seventh report, dated February 4, 2026. This should be one of your recurring update anchors for sanctions and global network status.
April 2026 — NCTC al-Shabaab profile updated NCTC’s April 2026 profile lists al-Shabaab’s operating area as southern Somalia with operations in Somalia, Kenya, and Ethiopia; it estimates membership at 7,000–12,000; and identifies Ahmed Diriye, Abukar Ali Adan, and Mahad Karate as key leaders.
April 25, 2026 — coordinated JNIM / Front for the Liberation of Azawad attacks across Mali NCTC says JNIM and the Front for the Liberation of Azawad conducted coordinated attacks across Mali, captured Kidal, and targeted military locations in and around Bamako and several cities in northern and central Mali.
May 2026 — NCTC Al-Qaeda profile: decentralized affiliate network NCTC’s May 2026 Al-Qaeda profile describes Al-Qaeda as a dispersed global network whose central leadership has been weakened, with affiliates conducting most operational activity: AQAP in Yemen, AQIM/JNIM in Mali, al-Shabaab in Somalia, AQIS in Afghanistan/Pakistan, former Hurras members in Syria, and some senior/lower-level members in Iran.
May 2026 — NCTC AQAP profile: Saad al-Awlaki era NCTC’s May 2026 AQAP profile lists Sa’d Atif al-Aulaqi as overall AQAP leader, Ibrahim al-Banna as a founding/security figure, and Ibrahim al-Qosi as AQAP spokesperson and former Guantanamo detainee.
May 2026 — NCTC JNIM profile: Sahel center of gravity NCTC’s May 2026 JNIM profile identifies the group as primarily based in Mali and Burkina Faso, active across West Africa, with at least 6,000 members, and funded through ransom, taxation, smuggling weapons, and extortion.

Condensed affiliate timeline by region
Al-Qaeda Core
1988: founded. 1996: returns to Afghanistan. 1998: embassy bombings. 1999: FTO designation. 2000: USS Cole. 2001: 9/11; Afghanistan invasion; leadership dispersal. 2011: Bin Laden killed; Zawahiri succeeds. 2022: Zawahiri killed. 2023–2026: Sayf al-Adl widely treated as de facto senior leader, while NCTC lists him as Iran-based senior leader.
AQAP
2000: USS Cole predecessor cell. 2006: Sana’a prison escape. 2009: AQAP formed; Bin Nayef assassination attempt; Flight 253 attempt. 2010: FTO designation; cargo bomb plot; Inspire era. 2011–2012: territorial seizure in Abyan; Yemeni military/government attacks. 2013: Yemen Ministry of Defense attack. 2014: Sana’a prison break. 2015: Charlie Hebdo claim; Mukalla seizure; Wuhayshi killed. 2020: Raymi killed; Batarfi succeeds. 2024: Batarfi dies; Saad al-Awlaki succeeds. 2025–2026: AQAP remains active in southern/central Yemen under Saad al-Awlaki.
AQIM / JNIM
1998: GSPC formed. 2006: GSPC aligns with Al-Qaeda. 2007: AQIM name adopted; Algiers bombings. 2012: northern Mali takeover. 2013: In Amenas. 2015: Radisson Blu Bamako. 2016: Ouagadougou; Grand-Bassam. 2017: JNIM formed. 2018: JNIM FTO designation; Timbuktu UN base attack. 2020: Droukdel killed; al-Anabi succeeds AQIM. 2024: Bamako and Barsalogho attacks. 2025: Djibo seizure, UAE hostage, fuel blockade. 2026: coordinated Mali attacks and Kidal capture.
al-Shabaab
2006: emerges from ICU environment. 2008: FTO designation. 2010: Kampala bombings. 2012: pledges allegiance to Al-Qaeda. 2013: Westgate Mall. 2014: Godane killed; Ahmed Diriye succeeds. 2015: Garissa University. 2017: Mogadishu truck bombing. 2019: DusitD2. 2020: Manda Bay / Camp Simba. 2024: Lido Beach. 2025: attempted assassination of Somali president. 2026: remains Al-Qaeda’s wealthiest component by NCTC profile.
AQIS
2014: announced by Zawahiri; Karachi dockyard attack attempt. 2016: Bangladesh activist murders; FTO designation. 2019: Asim Umar killed; Usama Mahmood becomes leader. 2021: Taliban takeover; AQIS congratulates Taliban. 2024: NCTC profile says AQIS remains based mainly in Afghanistan, with some activity in Pakistan, India, and Bangladesh, and fewer than 200 members.
Syria / Nusra / Hurras
2012: Jabhat al-Nusra emerges. 2013: Nusra/ISIS split. 2016: Nusra breaks from Al-Qaeda and rebrands. 2017: HTS forms. 2018: Hurras al-Din becomes Al-Qaeda’s formal Syria presence. 2019: Hurras designated. 2021: Ar Raqqah and Damascus attacks. 2023: Latakia attack participation. 2025: Hurras announces dissolution; members participate in Latakia/Tartus violence. 2026: former members remain a threat according to NCTC.

What Part 7 gives you
This part gives you the master chronological skeleton of the entire Al-Qaeda project:
* origin infrastructure,
* founding,
* Sudan phase,
* Afghanistan renewal,
* global attacks,
* 9/11,
* dispersal,
* AQI legacy,
* AQAP rise,
* AQIM/JNIM Sahel expansion,
* al-Shabaab East Africa campaign,
* AQIS South Asia campaign,
* Syria/Nusra/Hurras split,
* leadership deaths,
* prison breaks,
* designations,
* territorial seizures,
* and current 2026 affiliate status.



PART 8 — ORGANIZATION-BY-ORGANIZATION RELATIONSHIP MAP
1. Relationship categories
For your OSINT platform, each organization should be classified by relationship type, not just by “connected / not connected.”
Use these conceptual labels:
Direct core organization Part of Al-Qaeda itself: Al-Qaeda Core, Shura Council, al-Sahab, military/security/media structures.
Predecessor Organizations or networks that helped form Al-Qaeda: MAK, Afghan-Arab networks, Egyptian Islamic Jihad.
Formal affiliate Groups publicly recognized as Al-Qaeda branches or affiliates: AQAP, AQIM, AQIS, al-Shabaab, Hurras al-Din before dissolution.
Sahel merger component Groups that merged into JNIM: Ansar al-Din, al-Murabitun, Macina Liberation Front, AQIM Sahara Emirate.
Former branch / breakaway Groups that were once Al-Qaeda-aligned but later separated: Jabhat al-Nusra / HTS.
Legacy branch / rival successor path AQI, which began as Al-Qaeda’s Iraq branch but evolved into the Islamic State pathway and then became a rival.
Sanctuary / alliance / protection network Taliban and Haqqani Network: not Al-Qaeda branches, but historically central to Al-Qaeda’s safe haven and survival.
Operational ecosystem / overlap group Groups with documented overlap, personnel contact, theater overlap, or ideological alignment but not clean Al-Qaeda command status.
Chart-claim only / needs corroboration Groups or relationships shown on the TIC chart or screenshots but not yet independently verified.

2. Al-Qaeda Core / AQC
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: central organization. Status: active, weakened, dispersed. Function: ideological center, symbolic brand, senior leadership, affiliate-recognition authority, strategic messaging, historical command network.
Al-Qaeda Core is the hub from which most other relationships radiate. NCTC’s 2026 profile describes Al-Qaeda as a dispersed global network whose central leadership has been weakened, while affiliates conduct most operational activity. NCTC identifies AQAP, AQIM/JNIM, al-Shabaab, AQIS, former Hurras al-Din members, and some Iran-based members as the key current network components.
Internal structures
Al-Qaeda Core historically included:
* Emir / overall leader
* Majlis al-Shura / consultative council
* Military committee
* Security committee
* Finance structure
* Media and propaganda structure
* External communications
* Liaison channels to affiliates
* Trusted couriers and facilitators
The 9/11 Commission describes Al-Qaeda’s early development through the rise of Bin Laden and Al-Qaeda from 1988–1992, the Sudan organizational-building phase from 1992–1996, and renewed sanctuary in Afghanistan from 1996–1998.
Key relationship lines
Al-Qaeda Core → AQAP: formal affiliate in Yemen. Al-Qaeda Core → AQIM: formal affiliate in North Africa/Sahel. Al-Qaeda Core → AQIS: formal affiliate in South Asia. Al-Qaeda Core → al-Shabaab: formal affiliate after public pledge. Al-Qaeda Core → Hurras al-Din: formal Syria affiliate from 2018 until public dissolution in 2025. Al-Qaeda Core → Taliban: sanctuary/protection relationship, not branch relationship. Al-Qaeda Core → Haqqani Network: allied/sanctuary/operational environment relationship, not branch relationship. Al-Qaeda Core → AQI: former branch / legacy branch / later rival successor path. Al-Qaeda Core → Jabhat al-Nusra: former Syria affiliate / later breakaway lineage.

3. Maktab al-Khidamat / MAK
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: predecessor network. Status: inactive. Core period: 1980s Afghan jihad period. Main figures: Abdullah Azzam, Osama bin Laden. Relationship label: predecessor-of / incubator-of / foreign-fighter support network.
MAK should be mapped as the pre-Al-Qaeda logistical and mobilization infrastructure. It connected Arab volunteers, donations, travel, clerical authority, guesthouses, and Afghan battlefield participation. It is not exactly the same thing as Al-Qaeda, but it is the closest organizational predecessor.
How it connects
MAK → Al-Qaeda Core: predecessor and incubator. Abdullah Azzam → MAK: co-founder / ideological mobilizer. Osama bin Laden → MAK: financier, organizer, co-founder or central patron. MAK → Afghanistan/Pakistan: geographic operating environment. MAK → Arab-Afghan veterans: recruitment and social network.
Importance
MAK created the human pool and logistics culture that Al-Qaeda inherited. The organization’s significance is less about direct continuity of name and more about continuity of personnel, trust, battlefield experience, and transnational recruitment.

4. Egyptian Islamic Jihad / EIJ
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: predecessor / merger partner / cadre source. Status: merged into Al-Qaeda. Key figure: Ayman al-Zawahiri. Relationship label: merged-into / personnel-source / ideological-cadre.
Egyptian Islamic Jihad coalesced in the late 1970s, allied with Al-Qaeda in the late 1990s, and merged with Al-Qaeda in 2001. Britannica identifies the group as responsible for the assassination of Anwar Sadat and states that it merged with Al-Qaeda in 2001.
How it connects
EIJ → Al-Qaeda Core: merger and cadre infusion. Ayman al-Zawahiri → EIJ: leader / ideological strategist. EIJ → Egyptian cadre: source network for Zawahiri, Sayf al-Adl, Mohammed Atef, Abu Muhammad al-Masri, Abu al-Khayr al-Masri, and other Egyptian-linked Al-Qaeda figures. EIJ → Al-Qaeda ideology: helped sharpen Al-Qaeda’s clandestine, anti-regime, and anti-U.S. strategic outlook.
Importance
EIJ is one of the most important non-Afghan roots of Al-Qaeda. MAK gave Al-Qaeda the Afghan-Arab battlefield network; EIJ gave Al-Qaeda an experienced Egyptian clandestine cadre, ideological vocabulary, assassination tradition, and organizational discipline.

5. Taliban
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: sanctuary provider / political protector / ideological ally / separate organization. Status: active governing power in Afghanistan. Relationship label: safe-haven-provider / host / ally-of-convenience / not affiliate.
The Taliban should not be categorized as an Al-Qaeda branch. It is a separate Afghan movement and governing actor. But it is one of the most important organizations in Al-Qaeda history because it gave Al-Qaeda pre-9/11 sanctuary. NCTC states that in the years before September 11, 2001, the Taliban provided safe haven to Al-Qaeda, giving it a base to recruit, train, and deploy terrorists abroad.
How it connects
Taliban → Al-Qaeda Core: safe haven and protection before 9/11. Taliban → AQIS: modern safe-haven/allegiance relationship; AQIS pledged allegiance to Taliban leader Haybatullah Akhundzada partly to secure safe haven in Afghanistan.  Taliban → Haqqani Network: integrated Afghan militant/political ecosystem. Taliban → JNIM: symbolic allegiance chain; JNIM’s leader pledged allegiance to the Taliban leader as part of JNIM’s formation declaration.  Taliban → Mullah Omar / Haibatullah Akhundzada: religious-political legitimacy nodes for Al-Qaeda-affiliated pledges.
Importance
The Taliban is the sanctuary axis. Al-Qaeda’s ability to mature before 9/11 depended heavily on Taliban-controlled Afghanistan. After 2021, the Taliban’s return to power renewed concerns about safe haven, especially through AQIS and Afghanistan-based Al-Qaeda remnants.
OSINT classification
* Not Al-Qaeda.
* Not an affiliate.
* Separate movement.
* Critical sanctuary provider.
* Critical legitimacy node.
* Critical AQIS safe-haven relationship.

6. Haqqani Network
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: allied/sanctuary network / Taliban-integrated militant family network. Status: active within the Taliban ecosystem. Relationship label: longstanding-ties / sanctuary-bridge / family-militant network / not formal affiliate.
NCTC says the Haqqani Network has longstanding ties to Al-Qaeda and other militant Islamist groups operating in Afghanistan and the region. It also states that the group is closely tied to the Taliban and was designated by the U.S. in 2012 partly because of its ties to the Taliban and Al-Qaeda.
How it connects
Haqqani Network → Taliban: allied/integrated Afghan militant ecosystem. Haqqani Network → Al-Qaeda Core: longstanding ties and sanctuary environment. Jalaluddin Haqqani → Osama bin Laden: mentor/associate relationship during the Afghan jihad environment, according to public profiles discussed in earlier parts. Sirajuddin Haqqani → Taliban security power: modern Taliban/Haqqani command node. Haqqani Network → AQIS: indirect safe-haven ecosystem through Taliban Afghanistan. Haqqani Network → Zawahiri Kabul controversy: relevant context because Zawahiri’s presence in Kabul after 2021 raised questions about Taliban/Haqqani protection networks.
Importance
The Haqqani Network is the family-network version of safe haven. Where the Taliban is the political sanctuary frame, the Haqqanis are the personal, tribal, militant, and security-network frame connecting Afghanistan/Pakistan borderlands to Al-Qaeda’s survival.
OSINT classification
* Not Al-Qaeda.
* Not a formal affiliate.
* Taliban-linked militant network.
* Al-Qaeda safe-haven bridge.
* Family/tribal command structure.
* High relevance to Afghanistan/Pakistan geography.

7. AQAP — Al-Qaeda in the Arabian Peninsula
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: formal affiliate. Status: active. Base: Yemen. Relationship label: formal-affiliate-of / regional-branch-of.
NCTC describes AQAP as one of Al-Qaeda’s most capable affiliates, formed in 2009 in Yemen by Al-Qaeda members from Saudi Arabia and Yemeni violent extremists. It supports Al-Qaeda’s overall goals and has called for attacks against the United States and allies through media releases.
How it connects
AQAP → Al-Qaeda Core: formal affiliate and ideological alignment. AQAP → Saudi/Yemeni Al-Qaeda elements: merger origin. AQAP → AQY / Yemen Al-Qaeda predecessor: successor relationship. AQAP → Ansar al-Sharia in Yemen: local governance/branding relationship. AQAP → al-Malahem Media / Inspire: media and external propaganda infrastructure. AQAP → Bin Laden inner circle: Nasir al-Wuhayshi was a former Bin Laden aide, making AQAP unusually close to Al-Qaeda Core legitimacy. AQAP → U.S./Western targets: external attack intent, especially aviation plots and Pensacola claim. AQAP → Yemeni civil war: local insurgency and survival environment.
Importance
AQAP is the external-operations and propaganda affiliate. It became famous not just for Yemen insurgency, but for attempted aviation attacks, English-language propaganda, and its ability to inspire or direct Western-facing plots.
Relationship cautions
AQAP is definitely Al-Qaeda. But not every group fighting in Yemen, not every anti-Houthi militia, and not every Ansar al-Sharia local formation should automatically be treated as AQAP without source support.

8. AQIS — Al-Qaeda in the Indian Subcontinent
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: formal affiliate. Status: active but small. Base: Afghanistan/Pakistan with activity in South Asia. Relationship label: formal-affiliate-of / regional-branch-of / Taliban-safe-haven-dependent.
NCTC says AQIS receives overarching guidance from Al-Qaeda senior leaders, has longstanding ties with the Taliban, and pledged allegiance to Taliban leader Haybatullah Akhundzada partly to secure safe haven in Afghanistan. NCTC also states AQIS congratulated the Taliban after the August 2021 takeover of Afghanistan.
How it connects
AQIS → Al-Qaeda Core: formal affiliate receiving senior guidance. AQIS → Taliban: allegiance and safe-haven relationship. AQIS → Afghanistan: primary base. AQIS → Pakistan, India, Bangladesh: operational/target geography. AQIS → Bangladesh assassination campaign: localized attack activity. AQIS → Kashmir/India narratives: propaganda and recruitment frame. AQIS → Asim Umar / Usama Mahmood: leadership lineage.
Importance
AQIS is the Taliban-safe-haven affiliate. Its importance is not its size; it is the way it ties Al-Qaeda Core back into Afghanistan after the Taliban’s return and projects Al-Qaeda’s brand into South Asia.
Relationship cautions
Do not classify every South Asian jihadist group as AQIS. AQIS overlaps with regional grievances and other militant networks, but formal relationship should be sourced case-by-case.

9. AQIM — Al-Qaeda in the Lands of the Islamic Maghreb
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: formal affiliate. Status: active but partly overshadowed by JNIM in the Sahel. Base: North Africa / Sahel. Relationship label: formal-affiliate-of / successor-to-GSPC / parent-lineage-of-JNIM.
NCTC says AQIM’s predecessor was the Algeria-based Salafist Group for Preaching and Combat / GSPC, which formed in 1998 as a faction of the Armed Islamic Group. GSPC aligned with Al-Qaeda in September 2006 and adopted the AQIM name in January 2007. NCTC notes that although AQIM is an Al-Qaeda affiliate, it maintains a largely autonomous command structure and targeting strategy.
How it connects
GSPC → AQIM: predecessor/successor relationship. AQIM → Al-Qaeda Core: formal affiliate. AQIM → JNIM: parent lineage through AQIM Sahara Emirate and allegiance chain. AQIM → al-Murabitun: splinter/merger ecosystem. AQIM → Ansar al-Din: alliance/merger environment through JNIM. AQIM → Macina Liberation Front: indirect merger relationship through JNIM. AQIM → Algeria/Mali/Sahel: operating geography. AQIM → kidnapping/smuggling networks: financing and mobility ecosystem.
Importance
AQIM is the North Africa-to-Sahel bridge. It transformed an Algerian insurgent lineage into an Al-Qaeda-branded regional network and then fed the rise of JNIM.
Relationship cautions
AQIM is formal Al-Qaeda, but many Sahel actors are local, ethnic, separatist, criminal, or opportunistic. Only classify them as AQIM/JNIM when a public source shows organizational connection.

10. GSPC — Salafist Group for Preaching and Combat
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: predecessor to AQIM. Status: inactive under original name. Base: Algeria. Relationship label: predecessor-of-AQIM / Al-Qaeda-aligned-by-2006.
The GSPC emerged from Algeria’s Armed Islamic Group environment, aligned itself with Al-Qaeda in September 2006, and became AQIM in January 2007. The UN sanctions summary similarly states that GSPC was renamed AQIM in January 2007 after its union with Al-Qaeda was announced by Ayman al-Zawahiri in September 2006.
How it connects
GIA → GSPC: splinter/predecessor environment. GSPC → Al-Qaeda Core: allegiance/alignment. GSPC → AQIM: direct rename/successor. GSPC → Algerian Civil War: origin environment. GSPC → AQIM Sahel expansion: organizational seed.
Importance
GSPC is the Algerian root of Al-Qaeda’s Maghreb/Sahel architecture. Without GSPC, there is no AQIM; without AQIM, JNIM’s formal Al-Qaeda lineage is weaker.

11. JNIM — Jama’at Nusrat al-Islam wal-Muslimin
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: Al-Qaeda-aligned Sahel coalition; AQIM-linked affiliate structure. Status: active and highly significant. Base: Mali, Burkina Faso, wider Sahel/West Africa. Relationship label: AQIM-linked coalition / affiliate-of-Al-Qaeda-through-allegiance-chain / Sahel merger organization.
NCTC says JNIM formed in March 2017 when four Mali-based groups — Ansar al-Din, al-Murabitun, Macina Liberation Front, and AQIM’s Sahara Emirate subgroup — merged. NCTC also says Iyad ag Ghali pledged JNIM’s allegiance to the emir of AQIM, the emir of Al-Qaeda, and the leader of the Taliban.
How it connects
JNIM → AQIM: merger lineage and allegiance to AQIM’s emir. JNIM → Al-Qaeda Core: allegiance to Al-Qaeda emir. JNIM → Taliban: symbolic allegiance to Taliban leader. Ansar al-Din → JNIM: merger component. Al-Murabitun → JNIM: merger component. Macina Liberation Front → JNIM: merger component. AQIM Sahara Emirate → JNIM: merger component. JNIM → Mali/Burkina Faso/Sahel: primary operating geography. JNIM → hostage, extortion, blockade economy: financing and coercion system.
Importance
JNIM is the modern Sahel center of gravity for Al-Qaeda. In current operational relevance, JNIM may matter more than AQIM Core because it is the active coalition conducting large-scale insurgent pressure across Mali and Burkina Faso.
Relationship cautions
JNIM is Al-Qaeda-aligned, but it is also deeply local. Its power comes from Al-Qaeda branding plus local conflict systems: Tuareg politics, Fulani mobilization, rural grievances, state absence, smuggling corridors, and anti-foreign-force narratives.

12. Ansar al-Din
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: JNIM merger component / AQIM-aligned local movement. Status: largely absorbed into JNIM structure. Base: Mali. Relationship label: merged-into-JNIM / AQIM-aligned.
NCTC describes Ansar al-Din as an Al-Qaeda-aligned armed group formed in November 2011 under Iyad ag Ghali. It began an association with AQIM in mid-2012, seized territory in northern Mali after the 2012 coup, and later became one of the components that merged into JNIM.
How it connects
Ansar al-Din → Iyad ag Ghali: founded/led by. Ansar al-Din → AQIM: association/alignment. Ansar al-Din → JNIM: merger component. Ansar al-Din → northern Mali: territorial-control geography. Ansar al-Din → Taliban/Al-Qaeda chain: through JNIM’s later pledge structure.
Importance
Ansar al-Din is the Tuareg-Mali bridge into JNIM. It gives the Al-Qaeda Sahel network local political and ethnic grounding beyond a purely foreign jihadist structure.

13. Al-Murabitun
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: AQIM splinter / JNIM merger component. Status: largely absorbed into JNIM structure. Base: Mali/Sahel/Northwest Africa. Relationship label: AQIM-splinter / merged-into-JNIM.
NCTC says al-Murabitun formed in 2013 from the merger of al-Mulathamun Battalion and another AQIM splinter group, and that it was one of the four groups that merged into JNIM in 2017.
How it connects
AQIM → al-Murabitun: splinter lineage. Mokhtar Belmokhtar → al-Murabitun: commander/founder figure. Al-Murabitun → JNIM: merger component. Al-Murabitun → In Amenas / hotel attack lineage: high-profile Sahel attack ecosystem. Al-Murabitun → kidnapping/smuggling: financing/mobility link.
Importance
Al-Murabitun is the Saharan attack-and-hostage lineage inside JNIM. It connects Belmokhtar’s network, AQIM splinters, hostage-taking, smuggling, and urban hotel attacks to the broader Al-Qaeda Sahel coalition.

14. Macina Liberation Front / Katibat Macina
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: JNIM merger component. Status: active within JNIM ecosystem. Base: central Mali. Relationship label: merged-into-JNIM / local-mobilization-wing.
NCTC lists the Macina Liberation Front as one of the four groups that merged to form JNIM in March 2017.
How it connects
Macina Liberation Front → JNIM: merger component. Amadou Kouffa → Macina Liberation Front: founder/leader figure. Macina → central Mali: primary geography. Macina → Fulani/community mobilization: local recruitment and coercion frame. Macina → JNIM rural expansion: central Mali engine.
Importance
Macina is the central Mali social-mobilization engine within JNIM. It matters because it turns Al-Qaeda’s global brand into local insurgent governance, coercion, and recruitment in rural Mali.

15. AQIM Sahara Emirate
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: AQIM subgroup / JNIM merger component. Status: absorbed into JNIM. Base: Sahara/Sahel. Relationship label: AQIM-subgroup / merged-into-JNIM.
NCTC identifies the Sahara Emirate subgroup of AQIM as one of the four groups that merged into JNIM.
How it connects
AQIM → Sahara Emirate: regional subgroup. Sahara Emirate → JNIM: merger component. Sahara Emirate → AQIM commanders: links to Droukdel, Yahya Abu al-Hammam, Abou Zeid, and Sahel field commanders. Sahara Emirate → desert mobility: kidnapping, smuggling, ambush, route-control environment.
Importance
The Sahara Emirate is the formal AQIM branch inside the JNIM merger. It gives JNIM its cleanest direct organizational connection back to AQIM and therefore to Al-Qaeda Core.

16. Al-Shabaab
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: formal affiliate. Status: active. Base: Somalia, with operations in East Africa. Relationship label: pledged-affiliate-of-Al-Qaeda.
NCTC describes al-Shabaab as a Sunni terrorist group that publicly pledged loyalty to Al-Qaeda in 2012 and seeks to overthrow Somalia’s federal government, expel foreign forces, and establish a fundamentalist Islamic state.
How it connects
Al-Shabaab → Al-Qaeda Core: formal pledge/allegiance relationship. Al-Shabaab → Somalia: primary base. Al-Shabaab → Kenya/Uganda/Ethiopia/Djibouti: regional attack projection. Al-Shabaab → al-Kataib Media: media wing. Al-Shabaab → Amniyat: intelligence/security structure. Al-Shabaab → Mahad Karate: finance/amniyat command node. Al-Shabaab → Bin Laden correspondence: older evidence suggests loyalty and strategic advice before public pledge; still, the formal public pledge date is 2012. Al-Shabaab → East Africa Al-Qaeda history: not the same as the 1998 embassy bombing cells, but part of the same broader East Africa Al-Qaeda geography.
Importance
Al-Shabaab is the East Africa affiliate and one of Al-Qaeda’s strongest modern branches. It is especially important because of its shadow taxation system, amniyat wing, Somalia territorial influence, and repeated attacks in Kenya and Somalia.
Relationship cautions
Al-Shabaab has its own Somali political and clan dynamics. Do not reduce it to “Al-Qaeda’s Somalia office.” It is both a local insurgency and an Al-Qaeda affiliate.

17. Hurras al-Din
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: former formal Syria affiliate / Al-Qaeda-loyalist splinter. Status: publicly dissolved in early 2025, former members remain a threat. Base: Syria. Relationship label: former-affiliate / Al-Qaeda-loyalist-Syria-branch / post-Nusra splinter.
NCTC says Hurras al-Din became Al-Qaeda’s formal presence in Syria in February 2018. It was formed by Nusrah Front members who remained loyal to Al-Qaeda after Nusrah broke from Al-Qaeda and joined other groups to form Hay’at Tahrir al-Sham. NCTC says Hurras publicly announced dissolution in early 2025, but former members remain a threat.
How it connects
Jabhat al-Nusra → Hurras al-Din: loyalist splinter after Nusra/HTS break. Hurras al-Din → Al-Qaeda Core: formal Syria affiliate from 2018. Hurras al-Din → HTS: rival/suppressed/marginalized relationship after HTS consolidated power. Hurras al-Din → Syria veteran cadre: older Al-Qaeda-linked figures and loyalists. Hurras al-Din → post-2025 remnants: former-member threat, not a robust formal branch.
Importance
Hurras al-Din is the Al-Qaeda-loyalist residue of the Syria project. Its rise and dissolution show Al-Qaeda’s failure to retain dominance in the Syrian jihadist theater after Nusra/HTS broke away. CTC West Point describes Hurras al-Din’s history as a rise, marginalization by HTS, and dissolution, calling it a major setback for Al-Qaeda in Syria.

18. Jabhat al-Nusra / Nusrah Front
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: former formal Syria affiliate. Status: no longer Al-Qaeda; rebranded and later became part of HTS. Base: Syria. Relationship label: former-affiliate / broke-from-Al-Qaeda / predecessor-to-HTS.
Jabhat al-Nusra was Al-Qaeda’s Syria affiliate before it broke from Al-Qaeda and later joined other factions to form Hay’at Tahrir al-Sham. NCTC’s Hurras al-Din profile explicitly states that Hurras was formed by Nusrah Front members who remained loyal to Al-Qaeda after Nusrah broke away and joined other groups to form HTS.
How it connects
Al-Qaeda Core → Jabhat al-Nusra: former Syria affiliate. Jabhat al-Nusra → Jabhat Fatah al-Sham: rebrand after public break. Jabhat Fatah al-Sham → HTS: coalition/reorganization. Jabhat al-Nusra loyalists → Hurras al-Din: Al-Qaeda-loyalist splinter. Jabhat al-Nusra → ISIS split: rejected Baghdadi’s attempted merger and pledged to Zawahiri.
Importance
Nusra is the successful-but-lost Syria affiliate. It was one of Al-Qaeda’s most powerful local branches, but its breakaway was a strategic defeat for Al-Qaeda Core.
Relationship cautions
HTS should not be treated as current Al-Qaeda. It belongs in the map as a former affiliate lineage and rival/breakaway successor, not as a formal Al-Qaeda branch.

19. Hay’at Tahrir al-Sham / HTS
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: former branch lineage / breakaway / rival local authority. Status: separate from Al-Qaeda. Base: Syria. Relationship label: breakaway-successor / former-affiliate-lineage / not-current-Al-Qaeda.
HTS matters because it inherited much of the Nusra-era Syrian jihadist infrastructure but moved away from Al-Qaeda’s formal command. Hurras al-Din formed specifically from Nusra members who rejected that break and stayed loyal to Al-Qaeda.
How it connects
HTS → Jabhat Fatah al-Sham / Nusra: successor lineage. HTS → Al-Qaeda Core: former relationship, later break. HTS → Hurras al-Din: rivalry/marginalization relationship. HTS → Syria local governance: local insurgent authority rather than global Al-Qaeda affiliate.
Importance
HTS is critical for explaining why Al-Qaeda lost Syria. It represents localization, rebranding, and institutional breakaway from Al-Qaeda central authority.

20. AQI — Al-Qaeda in Iraq
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: former branch / legacy branch / rival-successor pathway. Status: inactive as Al-Qaeda; evolved into Islamic State pathway. Base: Iraq. Relationship label: former-affiliate / legacy-branch / evolved-into-rival.
AQI became Al-Qaeda’s Iraq branch after Abu Musab al-Zarqawi pledged allegiance to Bin Laden. It is essential to Al-Qaeda history because it later became Islamic State of Iraq and then ISIS, which ultimately broke with and rivaled Al-Qaeda.
How it connects
Zarqawi network → Al-Qaeda Core: pledge to Bin Laden. Zarqawi network → AQI: formal Iraq branch. AQI → Islamic State of Iraq: successor path. Islamic State of Iraq → ISIS: successor path. ISIS → Al-Qaeda Core: rival / breakaway enemy. AQI → al-Maqdisi: ideological mentor context, not command relationship. AQI → Syria/Nusra dispute: ISIS/Nusra split began from Iraq-Syria merger dispute.
Importance
AQI is the branch that became Al-Qaeda’s greatest rival. It belongs in your Al-Qaeda project because it explains the ISIS split, not because ISIS is Al-Qaeda.
Relationship cautions
Do not collapse AQI, ISIS, and Al-Qaeda into one organization. AQI is a former Al-Qaeda branch; ISIS is a rival successor movement after the split.

21. Islamic State / ISIS
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: rival successor from AQI lineage. Status: separate terrorist organization. Relationship label: splinter-successor / rival-of-Al-Qaeda / not-Al-Qaeda.
ISIS should appear in an Al-Qaeda project only as a rival and successor from AQI. It should not be treated as an Al-Qaeda affiliate.
How it connects
AQI → Islamic State of Iraq → ISIS: lineage. ISIS → Jabhat al-Nusra: attempted merger/conflict. ISIS → Al-Qaeda Core: split and rivalry. ISIS → Hurras / HTS / AQ affiliates: competitor for recruits, legitimacy, and theater dominance.
Importance
ISIS changed Al-Qaeda’s strategic environment by challenging its claim to global jihadist leadership, stealing recruits and affiliates, and forcing Al-Qaeda to reposition itself as more patient, decentralized, and locally embedded.

22. Ansar al-Sharia variants
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: local brand / local affiliate-adjacent label, varies by country. Status: country-specific. Relationship label: local-brand / affiliate-adjacent / needs-country-specific verification.
“Ansar al-Sharia” appears in multiple countries and should not automatically be treated as one unified organization. In Yemen, Ansar al-Sharia was heavily associated with AQAP’s local governance and insurgency branding. In Libya and Tunisia, Ansar al-Sharia groups belonged to the broader jihadist ecosystem and had varied relationships to AQIM, local networks, and ISIS-era currents.
How it connects
Ansar al-Sharia Yemen → AQAP: local brand / governance face. Ansar al-Sharia Libya → Al-Qaeda/AQIM ecosystem: contextual, case-by-case. Ansar al-Sharia Tunisia → AQIM/Maghreb ecosystem: contextual, case-by-case. Ansar al-Sharia variants → local post-Arab Spring instability: recruitment and governance opportunity.
Importance
Ansar al-Sharia labels show how Al-Qaeda-linked networks sometimes use local branding to avoid the baggage of the Al-Qaeda name while pursuing aligned aims.

23. Libyan Islamic Fighting Group / LIFG
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: historical overlap / veteran network / not clean affiliate. Status: largely inactive as original group. Base: Libya. Relationship label: veteran-overlap / ideological-network / not-formal-affiliate unless specifically sourced.
LIFG is relevant because Libyan veteran networks fed into Al-Qaeda, AQIM, Syria, and post-2011 Libya environments. But LIFG itself should not be simplistically classified as an Al-Qaeda branch.
How it connects
LIFG → Al-Qaeda Core: some personnel overlap and Afghanistan-era links. LIFG → Libya post-2011: militant/political veteran environment. LIFG → Syria networks: some Libyan foreign-fighter pathways. LIFG → AQIM/Maghreb: contextual ecosystem overlap.
Importance
LIFG is a veteran-network node, useful for mapping Libyan militants and post-2011 North Africa/Syria movements.

24. East Turkestan Islamic Movement / Turkistan Islamic Party
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: operational ecosystem / Afghan-Pakistan/Syria overlap. Status: active/varies by designation and source. Relationship label: aligned-ecosystem / theater-overlap / not-Al-Qaeda-branch.
The TIC chart places East Turkestan Islamic Movement and Turkistan Islamic Party in the Khorasan/Indian Subcontinent sphere and links some individuals to Al-Qaeda Shura claims. Treat those chart-specific leadership claims as chart claims unless separately verified.
How it connects
TIP/ETIM → Afghanistan/Pakistan: historical training and sanctuary overlap. TIP/ETIM → Syria: foreign-fighter theater overlap. TIP/ETIM → Al-Qaeda ecosystem: ideological and theater overlap. TIP/ETIM → AQIS/Khorasan: regional proximity.
Importance
These groups matter as foreign-fighter and sanctuary-overlap actors in Afghanistan/Pakistan and Syria, not as formal Al-Qaeda branches.

25. Islamic Movement of Uzbekistan / IMU and Islamic Jihad Union / IJU
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: Central Asian militant ecosystem overlap. Status: changed over time; many factions splintered or shifted. Relationship label: allied-ecosystem / training-overlap / not-formal-Al-Qaeda-branch unless sourced.
IMU and IJU appear in many Al-Qaeda/Afghanistan/Pakistan network maps because of training-camp, Taliban, and foreign-fighter overlap.
How it connects
IMU/IJU → Taliban/Afghanistan/Pakistan: sanctuary and battlefield overlap. IMU/IJU → Al-Qaeda Core: historical training and alliance environment. IMU/IJU → AQIS/Khorasan: regional ecosystem overlap. IMU/IJU → ISIS-K: some later splinter/defection dynamics, depending on faction and period.
Importance
These groups help explain why the Afghanistan/Pakistan zone became a multinational militant ecosystem.

26. Tehrik-e-Taliban Pakistan / TTP
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: allied ecosystem / Pakistani Taliban network / not Al-Qaeda branch. Status: active. Base: Pakistan/Afghanistan borderlands. Relationship label: allied-network / sanctuary-overlap / operational-cooperation-case-by-case.
TTP has had extensive overlap with Al-Qaeda and other militant groups in Pakistan’s tribal/border regions, but it is not the same organization as Al-Qaeda.
How it connects
TTP → Al-Qaeda Core: sanctuary and operational ecosystem overlap. TTP → AQIS: regional overlap and possible cooperation depending on case. TTP → Afghan Taliban: ideological and historical relationship, though organizationally distinct. TTP → Pakistan: primary target geography.
Importance
TTP is a Pakistan borderland ecosystem node. It helps explain how Al-Qaeda survived in Pakistan/Afghanistan after 2001.

27. Lashkar-e-Taiba / LeT
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: South Asian overlap group / separate organization. Status: active/proscribed. Relationship label: ecosystem-overlap / not-Al-Qaeda-affiliate.
LeT appears in some Al-Qaeda network diagrams because of South Asian militant overlap, Afghanistan/Pakistan linkages, and shared anti-Western/anti-India narratives. But it should not be treated as an Al-Qaeda affiliate.
How it connects
LeT → Al-Qaeda ecosystem: limited overlap and shared theater. LeT → Pakistan/India/Kashmir: primary geography. LeT → AQIS: ideological/target overlap, not command hierarchy. LeT → Hafiz Saeed: leadership node separate from Al-Qaeda Core.
Importance
LeT is relevant for South Asian jihadist ecosystem mapping, but keep its relationship separate and carefully sourced.

28. Jaish-e-Mohammed / JeM
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: South Asian overlap group / separate organization. Status: active/proscribed. Relationship label: ecosystem-overlap / not-formal-Al-Qaeda-affiliate.
JeM appears in Al-Qaeda/Khorasan/Indian Subcontinent diagrams because it shares Afghanistan/Pakistan/Kashmir geography and militant networks.
How it connects
JeM → AQIS/Khorasan environment: theater and ideological overlap. JeM → Taliban/Afghanistan: historical training and movement overlap. JeM → Al-Qaeda Core: not a formal affiliate unless a specific sourced relationship exists. JeM → Masood Azhar: separate leadership node.
Importance
JeM is a Kashmir/Pakistan militant ecosystem node, relevant to AQIS mapping but not equivalent to AQIS.

29. Jemaah Islamiyah / JI
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: Southeast Asian allied network / historical partner. Status: changed over time; original militant network degraded. Base: Indonesia/Southeast Asia. Relationship label: allied-network / Al-Qaeda-linked / not core affiliate.
JI is historically important because of the Bali bombings, Hambali, and Southeast Asian Al-Qaeda connections. It belongs in an Al-Qaeda project because it shows Al-Qaeda’s Southeast Asian reach, not because JI was simply “Al-Qaeda Southeast Asia.”
How it connects
JI → Al-Qaeda Core: historical training, funding, and operational links. JI → Hambali: liaison/operations bridge. JI → Bali bombing: major Al-Qaeda-linked Southeast Asia attack context. JI → Indonesia/Malaysia/Philippines: regional network geography.
Importance
JI is the Southeast Asia Al-Qaeda-linked network. It demonstrates that Al-Qaeda influence extended beyond Arab/Afghan theaters into regional partner organizations.

30. Abu Sayyaf Group
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: Southeast Asian overlap group / historically linked in parts. Status: fragmented; many later ISIS-linked factions. Relationship label: historical-overlap / not-core-Al-Qaeda-affiliate.
The Abu Sayyaf Group appears in many jihadist network maps because of links to JI, foreign fighters, kidnappings, and later ISIS-linked shifts. For an Al-Qaeda project, it should be included only where specific Al-Qaeda/JI connections are sourced.
How it connects
Abu Sayyaf → JI: regional overlap. Abu Sayyaf → Al-Qaeda ecosystem: historical/financial/training claims in parts. Abu Sayyaf → Philippines: local insurgency/kidnapping geography. Abu Sayyaf → ISIS later: many factions shifted toward ISIS, so do not overstate current Al-Qaeda relevance.

31. Boko Haram / ISWAP / Ansaru
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: West Africa overlap / mixed lineage. Status: varies by group. Relationship label: ecosystem-overlap / some AQ-aligned elements / not automatically Al-Qaeda.
For an Al-Qaeda-centered map, Ansaru is more relevant than Boko Haram proper because Ansaru had clearer Al-Qaeda/AQIM-aligned tendencies. Boko Haram and ISWAP are mostly better handled in an ISIS/West Africa project, unless explaining competition or network overlap.
How it connects
Ansaru → AQIM/JNIM ecosystem: ideological and regional overlap. Boko Haram → Al-Qaeda ecosystem: limited historical contact/possible training claims; later ISIS alignment dominates. ISWAP → ISIS: not Al-Qaeda. JNIM → West Africa expansion: separate Al-Qaeda-aligned Sahel track.
Importance
Use this cluster to explain West Africa competition and overlap, not to expand Al-Qaeda beyond sourced relationships.

32. Hamas, Palestinian Islamic Jihad, Hezbollah, IRGC/Quds Force
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: contextual / rival / occasional theater-overlap / not Al-Qaeda. Status: separate organizations. Relationship label: non-Al-Qaeda contextual actor.
The TIC chart includes Middle East actors such as Hamas, Palestinian Islamic Jihad, Hezbollah, Quds Force, Houthis, and IRGC Iran in a broader regional box. In an Al-Qaeda-centered project, these should not be treated as Al-Qaeda affiliates. They are relevant only where public sources show specific contact, sanctuary, conflict, rivalry, or regional theater interaction.
How they connect
Hezbollah / IRGC / Quds Force → Al-Qaeda: not ideological allies; Iran-based Al-Qaeda leadership/facilitation claims make Iran relevant, but this does not mean Hezbollah or IRGC are Al-Qaeda branches. Hamas / PIJ → Al-Qaeda: different ideological and organizational tradition; occasional shared anti-Israel rhetoric does not equal command relationship. Houthis → AQAP: enemy/target relationship inside Yemen’s war. IRGC Iran → Sayf al-Adl / Iran-based Al-Qaeda file: state/facilitation ambiguity, not organizational unity.
Importance
These groups are best used as regional context and contrast. Keep them separate to avoid analytical contamination.

33. Relationship matrix
Direct Al-Qaeda organizations

Al-Sahab	Al-Qaeda Core media arm	high
AQAP	formal affiliate	high
AQIM	formal affiliate	high
AQIS	formal affiliate	high
al-Shabaab	formal affiliate after 2012 pledge	high
Hurras al-Din	former formal Syria affiliate, dissolved 2025	high
Jabhat al-Nusra	former Syria affiliate	high
AQI	former Iraq branch, later rival lineage	high
Predecessors and merger sources
Organization	Relationship	Confidence
MAK	Al-Qaeda predecessor	high

GSPC	became AQIM	high

AQIM Sahara Emirate	merged into JNIM	high
