34. The clean Al-Qaeda relationship tree
Here is the cleanest text version of the full map:
Afghan jihad / MAK / Arab-Afghan network → produces Al-Qaeda Core.
Egyptian Islamic Jihad → merges into Al-Qaeda Core and strengthens the Egyptian senior cadre.
Taliban → provides sanctuary to Al-Qaeda Core before 9/11; later becomes safe-haven context for AQIS after 2021.
Haqqani Network → provides Afghan/Pakistan militant-family sanctuary ecosystem tied to both Taliban and Al-Qaeda.
AQAP → formal Al-Qaeda affiliate in Yemen, formed from Saudi and Yemeni Al-Qaeda elements.
GSPC → aligns with Al-Qaeda → becomes AQIM.
AQIM Sahara Emirate + Ansar al-Din + al-Murabitun + Macina Liberation Front → merge into JNIM, which pledges allegiance to AQIM, Al-Qaeda, and Taliban leadership.
al-Shabaab → pledges loyalty to Al-Qaeda in 2012 → becomes East Africa affiliate.
AQIS → formal South Asia affiliate → tied to Taliban safe-haven relationship and Afghanistan/Pakistan geography.
Jabhat al-Nusra → Al-Qaeda’s Syria affiliate → breaks from Al-Qaeda → becomes JFS/HTS path.
Nusra loyalists → form Hurras al-Din as Al-Qaeda’s formal Syria presence in 2018 → Hurras dissolves publicly in 2025, leaving remnants.
Zarqawi network → becomes AQI → evolves into Islamic State of Iraq / ISIS path → becomes Al-Qaeda’s rival, not affiliate.

35. What Part 8 adds
This part gives you the relationship grammar of the entire Al-Qaeda system:
* which groups are truly Al-Qaeda,
* which groups are predecessors,
* which groups merged into affiliates,
* which groups broke away,
* which groups became rivals,
* which groups provided sanctuary,
* which groups only overlap by theater,
* and which groups should be kept out of the Al-Qaeda category unless directly sourced.
The most important analytical distinction is this:
Al-Qaeda is not one giant command tree. It is a core brand, old veteran cadre, formal affiliate system, sanctuary network, and local-insurgency federation






PART NINE IS ALSO NOTES JUST LIKE PART 5 ONLY NOTES PART 8 — ORGANIZATION-BY-ORGANIZATION RELATIONSHIP MAP
1. Relationship categories
For your OSINT platform, each organization should be classified by relationship type, not just by “connected / not connected.”
Use these conceptual labels:
Direct core organization Part of Al-Qaeda itself: Al-Qaeda Core, Shura Council, al-Sahab, military/security/media structures.
Predecessor Organizations or networks that helped form Al-Qaeda: MAK, Afghan-Arab networks, Egyptian Islamic Jihad.
Formal affiliate Groups publicly recognized as Al-Qaeda branches or affiliates: AQAP, AQIM, AQIS, al-Shabaab, Hurras al-Din before dissolution.
Sahel merger component Groups that merged into JNIM: Ansar al-Din, al-Murabitun, Macina Liberation Front, AQIM Sahara Emirate.
Former branch / breakaway Groups that were once Al-Qaeda-aligned but later separated: Jabhat al-Nusra / HTS.
Legacy branch / rival successor path AQI, which began as Al-Qaeda’s Iraq branch but evolved into the Islamic State pathway and then became a rival.
Sanctuary / alliance / protection network Taliban and Haqqani Network: not Al-Qaeda branches, but historically central to Al-Qaeda’s safe haven and survival.
Operational ecosystem / overlap group Groups with documented overlap, personnel contact, theater overlap, or ideological alignment but not clean Al-Qaeda command status.
Chart-claim only / needs corroboration Groups or relationships shown on the TIC chart or screenshots but not yet independently verified.

2. Al-Qaeda Core / AQC
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: central organization. Status: active, weakened, dispersed. Function: ideological center, symbolic brand, senior leadership, affiliate-recognition authority, strategic messaging, historical command network.
Al-Qaeda Core is the hub from which most other relationships radiate. NCTC’s 2026 profile describes Al-Qaeda as a dispersed global network whose central leadership has been weakened, while affiliates conduct most operational activity. NCTC identifies AQAP, AQIM/JNIM, al-Shabaab, AQIS, former Hurras al-Din members, and some Iran-based members as the key current network components.
Internal structures
Al-Qaeda Core historically included:
* Emir / overall leader
* Majlis al-Shura / consultative council
* Military committee
* Security committee
* Finance structure
* Media and propaganda structure
* External communications
* Liaison channels to affiliates
* Trusted couriers and facilitators
The 9/11 Commission describes Al-Qaeda’s early development through the rise of Bin Laden and Al-Qaeda from 1988–1992, the Sudan organizational-building phase from 1992–1996, and renewed sanctuary in Afghanistan from 1996–1998.
Key relationship lines
Al-Qaeda Core → AQAP: formal affiliate in Yemen. Al-Qaeda Core → AQIM: formal affiliate in North Africa/Sahel. Al-Qaeda Core → AQIS: formal affiliate in South Asia. Al-Qaeda Core → al-Shabaab: formal affiliate after public pledge. Al-Qaeda Core → Hurras al-Din: formal Syria affiliate from 2018 until public dissolution in 2025. Al-Qaeda Core → Taliban: sanctuary/protection relationship, not branch relationship. Al-Qaeda Core → Haqqani Network: allied/sanctuary/operational environment relationship, not branch relationship. Al-Qaeda Core → AQI: former branch / legacy branch / later rival successor path. Al-Qaeda Core → Jabhat al-Nusra: former Syria affiliate / later breakaway lineage.

3. Maktab al-Khidamat / MAK
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: predecessor network. Status: inactive. Core period: 1980s Afghan jihad period. Main figures: Abdullah Azzam, Osama bin Laden. Relationship label: predecessor-of / incubator-of / foreign-fighter support network.
MAK should be mapped as the pre-Al-Qaeda logistical and mobilization infrastructure. It connected Arab volunteers, donations, travel, clerical authority, guesthouses, and Afghan battlefield participation. It is not exactly the same thing as Al-Qaeda, but it is the closest organizational predecessor.
How it connects
MAK → Al-Qaeda Core: predecessor and incubator. Abdullah Azzam → MAK: co-founder / ideological mobilizer. Osama bin Laden → MAK: financier, organizer, co-founder or central patron. MAK → Afghanistan/Pakistan: geographic operating environment. MAK → Arab-Afghan veterans: recruitment and social network.
Importance
MAK created the human pool and logistics culture that Al-Qaeda inherited. The organization’s significance is less about direct continuity of name and more about continuity of personnel, trust, battlefield experience, and transnational recruitment.

4. Egyptian Islamic Jihad / EIJ
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: predecessor / merger partner / cadre source. Status: merged into Al-Qaeda. Key figure: Ayman al-Zawahiri. Relationship label: merged-into / personnel-source / ideological-cadre.
Egyptian Islamic Jihad coalesced in the late 1970s, allied with Al-Qaeda in the late 1990s, and merged with Al-Qaeda in 2001. Britannica identifies the group as responsible for the assassination of Anwar Sadat and states that it merged with Al-Qaeda in 2001.
How it connects
EIJ → Al-Qaeda Core: merger and cadre infusion. Ayman al-Zawahiri → EIJ: leader / ideological strategist. EIJ → Egyptian cadre: source network for Zawahiri, Sayf al-Adl, Mohammed Atef, Abu Muhammad al-Masri, Abu al-Khayr al-Masri, and other Egyptian-linked Al-Qaeda figures. EIJ → Al-Qaeda ideology: helped sharpen Al-Qaeda’s clandestine, anti-regime, and anti-U.S. strategic outlook.
Importance
EIJ is one of the most important non-Afghan roots of Al-Qaeda. MAK gave Al-Qaeda the Afghan-Arab battlefield network; EIJ gave Al-Qaeda an experienced Egyptian clandestine cadre, ideological vocabulary, assassination tradition, and organizational discipline.

5. Taliban
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: sanctuary provider / political protector / ideological ally / separate organization. Status: active governing power in Afghanistan. Relationship label: safe-haven-provider / host / ally-of-convenience / not affiliate.
The Taliban should not be categorized as an Al-Qaeda branch. It is a separate Afghan movement and governing actor. But it is one of the most important organizations in Al-Qaeda history because it gave Al-Qaeda pre-9/11 sanctuary. NCTC states that in the years before September 11, 2001, the Taliban provided safe haven to Al-Qaeda, giving it a base to recruit, train, and deploy terrorists abroad.
How it connects
Taliban → Al-Qaeda Core: safe haven and protection before 9/11. Taliban → AQIS: modern safe-haven/allegiance relationship; AQIS pledged allegiance to Taliban leader Haybatullah Akhundzada partly to secure safe haven in Afghanistan.  Taliban → Haqqani Network: integrated Afghan militant/political ecosystem. Taliban → JNIM: symbolic allegiance chain; JNIM’s leader pledged allegiance to the Taliban leader as part of JNIM’s formation declaration.  Taliban → Mullah Omar / Haibatullah Akhundzada: religious-political legitimacy nodes for Al-Qaeda-affiliated pledges.
Importance
The Taliban is the sanctuary axis. Al-Qaeda’s ability to mature before 9/11 depended heavily on Taliban-controlled Afghanistan. After 2021, the Taliban’s return to power renewed concerns about safe haven, especially through AQIS and Afghanistan-based Al-Qaeda remnants.
OSINT classification
* Not Al-Qaeda.
* Not an affiliate.
* Separate movement.
* Critical sanctuary provider.
* Critical legitimacy node.
* Critical AQIS safe-haven relationship.

6. Haqqani Network
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: allied/sanctuary network / Taliban-integrated militant family network. Status: active within the Taliban ecosystem. Relationship label: longstanding-ties / sanctuary-bridge / family-militant network / not formal affiliate.
NCTC says the Haqqani Network has longstanding ties to Al-Qaeda and other militant Islamist groups operating in Afghanistan and the region. It also states that the group is closely tied to the Taliban and was designated by the U.S. in 2012 partly because of its ties to the Taliban and Al-Qaeda.
How it connects
Haqqani Network → Taliban: allied/integrated Afghan militant ecosystem. Haqqani Network → Al-Qaeda Core: longstanding ties and sanctuary environment. Jalaluddin Haqqani → Osama bin Laden: mentor/associate relationship during the Afghan jihad environment, according to public profiles discussed in earlier parts. Sirajuddin Haqqani → Taliban security power: modern Taliban/Haqqani command node. Haqqani Network → AQIS: indirect safe-haven ecosystem through Taliban Afghanistan. Haqqani Network → Zawahiri Kabul controversy: relevant context because Zawahiri’s presence in Kabul after 2021 raised questions about Taliban/Haqqani protection networks.
Importance
The Haqqani Network is the family-network version of safe haven. Where the Taliban is the political sanctuary frame, the Haqqanis are the personal, tribal, militant, and security-network frame connecting Afghanistan/Pakistan borderlands to Al-Qaeda’s survival.
OSINT classification
* Not Al-Qaeda.
* Not a formal affiliate.
* Taliban-linked militant network.
* Al-Qaeda safe-haven bridge.
* Family/tribal command structure.
* High relevance to Afghanistan/Pakistan geography.

7. AQAP — Al-Qaeda in the Arabian Peninsula
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: formal affiliate. Status: active. Base: Yemen. Relationship label: formal-affiliate-of / regional-branch-of.
NCTC describes AQAP as one of Al-Qaeda’s most capable affiliates, formed in 2009 in Yemen by Al-Qaeda members from Saudi Arabia and Yemeni violent extremists. It supports Al-Qaeda’s overall goals and has called for attacks against the United States and allies through media releases.
How it connects
AQAP → Al-Qaeda Core: formal affiliate and ideological alignment. AQAP → Saudi/Yemeni Al-Qaeda elements: merger origin. AQAP → AQY / Yemen Al-Qaeda predecessor: successor relationship. AQAP → Ansar al-Sharia in Yemen: local governance/branding relationship. AQAP → al-Malahem Media / Inspire: media and external propaganda infrastructure. AQAP → Bin Laden inner circle: Nasir al-Wuhayshi was a former Bin Laden aide, making AQAP unusually close to Al-Qaeda Core legitimacy. AQAP → U.S./Western targets: external attack intent, especially aviation plots and Pensacola claim. AQAP → Yemeni civil war: local insurgency and survival environment.
Importance
AQAP is the external-operations and propaganda affiliate. It became famous not just for Yemen insurgency, but for attempted aviation attacks, English-language propaganda, and its ability to inspire or direct Western-facing plots.
Relationship cautions
AQAP is definitely Al-Qaeda. But not every group fighting in Yemen, not every anti-Houthi militia, and not every Ansar al-Sharia local formation should automatically be treated as AQAP without source support.

8. AQIS — Al-Qaeda in the Indian Subcontinent
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: formal affiliate. Status: active but small. Base: Afghanistan/Pakistan with activity in South Asia. Relationship label: formal-affiliate-of / regional-branch-of / Taliban-safe-haven-dependent.
NCTC says AQIS receives overarching guidance from Al-Qaeda senior leaders, has longstanding ties with the Taliban, and pledged allegiance to Taliban leader Haybatullah Akhundzada partly to secure safe haven in Afghanistan. NCTC also states AQIS congratulated the Taliban after the August 2021 takeover of Afghanistan.
How it connects
AQIS → Al-Qaeda Core: formal affiliate receiving senior guidance. AQIS → Taliban: allegiance and safe-haven relationship. AQIS → Afghanistan: primary base. AQIS → Pakistan, India, Bangladesh: operational/target geography. AQIS → Bangladesh assassination campaign: localized attack activity. AQIS → Kashmir/India narratives: propaganda and recruitment frame. AQIS → Asim Umar / Usama Mahmood: leadership lineage.
Importance
AQIS is the Taliban-safe-haven affiliate. Its importance is not its size; it is the way it ties Al-Qaeda Core back into Afghanistan after the Taliban’s return and projects Al-Qaeda’s brand into South Asia.
Relationship cautions
Do not classify every South Asian jihadist group as AQIS. AQIS overlaps with regional grievances and other militant networks, but formal relationship should be sourced case-by-case.

9. AQIM — Al-Qaeda in the Lands of the Islamic Maghreb
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: formal affiliate. Status: active but partly overshadowed by JNIM in the Sahel. Base: North Africa / Sahel. Relationship label: formal-affiliate-of / successor-to-GSPC / parent-lineage-of-JNIM.
NCTC says AQIM’s predecessor was the Algeria-based Salafist Group for Preaching and Combat / GSPC, which formed in 1998 as a faction of the Armed Islamic Group. GSPC aligned with Al-Qaeda in September 2006 and adopted the AQIM name in January 2007. NCTC notes that although AQIM is an Al-Qaeda affiliate, it maintains a largely autonomous command structure and targeting strategy.
How it connects
GSPC → AQIM: predecessor/successor relationship. AQIM → Al-Qaeda Core: formal affiliate. AQIM → JNIM: parent lineage through AQIM Sahara Emirate and allegiance chain. AQIM → al-Murabitun: splinter/merger ecosystem. AQIM → Ansar al-Din: alliance/merger environment through JNIM. AQIM → Macina Liberation Front: indirect merger relationship through JNIM. AQIM → Algeria/Mali/Sahel: operating geography. AQIM → kidnapping/smuggling networks: financing and mobility ecosystem.
Importance
AQIM is the North Africa-to-Sahel bridge. It transformed an Algerian insurgent lineage into an Al-Qaeda-branded regional network and then fed the rise of JNIM.
Relationship cautions
AQIM is formal Al-Qaeda, but many Sahel actors are local, ethnic, separatist, criminal, or opportunistic. Only classify them as AQIM/JNIM when a public source shows organizational connection.

10. GSPC — Salafist Group for Preaching and Combat
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: predecessor to AQIM. Status: inactive under original name. Base: Algeria. Relationship label: predecessor-of-AQIM / Al-Qaeda-aligned-by-2006.
The GSPC emerged from Algeria’s Armed Islamic Group environment, aligned itself with Al-Qaeda in September 2006, and became AQIM in January 2007. The UN sanctions summary similarly states that GSPC was renamed AQIM in January 2007 after its union with Al-Qaeda was announced by Ayman al-Zawahiri in September 2006.
How it connects
GIA → GSPC: splinter/predecessor environment. GSPC → Al-Qaeda Core: allegiance/alignment. GSPC → AQIM: direct rename/successor. GSPC → Algerian Civil War: origin environment. GSPC → AQIM Sahel expansion: organizational seed.
Importance
GSPC is the Algerian root of Al-Qaeda’s Maghreb/Sahel architecture. Without GSPC, there is no AQIM; without AQIM, JNIM’s formal Al-Qaeda lineage is weaker.

11. JNIM — Jama’at Nusrat al-Islam wal-Muslimin
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: Al-Qaeda-aligned Sahel coalition; AQIM-linked affiliate structure. Status: active and highly significant. Base: Mali, Burkina Faso, wider Sahel/West Africa. Relationship label: AQIM-linked coalition / affiliate-of-Al-Qaeda-through-allegiance-chain / Sahel merger organization.
NCTC says JNIM formed in March 2017 when four Mali-based groups — Ansar al-Din, al-Murabitun, Macina Liberation Front, and AQIM’s Sahara Emirate subgroup — merged. NCTC also says Iyad ag Ghali pledged JNIM’s allegiance to the emir of AQIM, the emir of Al-Qaeda, and the leader of the Taliban.
How it connects
JNIM → AQIM: merger lineage and allegiance to AQIM’s emir. JNIM → Al-Qaeda Core: allegiance to Al-Qaeda emir. JNIM → Taliban: symbolic allegiance to Taliban leader. Ansar al-Din → JNIM: merger component. Al-Murabitun → JNIM: merger component. Macina Liberation Front → JNIM: merger component. AQIM Sahara Emirate → JNIM: merger component. JNIM → Mali/Burkina Faso/Sahel: primary operating geography. JNIM → hostage, extortion, blockade economy: financing and coercion system.
Importance
JNIM is the modern Sahel center of gravity for Al-Qaeda. In current operational relevance, JNIM may matter more than AQIM Core because it is the active coalition conducting large-scale insurgent pressure across Mali and Burkina Faso.
Relationship cautions
JNIM is Al-Qaeda-aligned, but it is also deeply local. Its power comes from Al-Qaeda branding plus local conflict systems: Tuareg politics, Fulani mobilization, rural grievances, state absence, smuggling corridors, and anti-foreign-force narratives.

12. Ansar al-Din
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: JNIM merger component / AQIM-aligned local movement. Status: largely absorbed into JNIM structure. Base: Mali. Relationship label: merged-into-JNIM / AQIM-aligned.
NCTC describes Ansar al-Din as an Al-Qaeda-aligned armed group formed in November 2011 under Iyad ag Ghali. It began an association with AQIM in mid-2012, seized territory in northern Mali after the 2012 coup, and later became one of the components that merged into JNIM.
How it connects
Ansar al-Din → Iyad ag Ghali: founded/led by. Ansar al-Din → AQIM: association/alignment. Ansar al-Din → JNIM: merger component. Ansar al-Din → northern Mali: territorial-control geography. Ansar al-Din → Taliban/Al-Qaeda chain: through JNIM’s later pledge structure.
Importance
Ansar al-Din is the Tuareg-Mali bridge into JNIM. It gives the Al-Qaeda Sahel network local political and ethnic grounding beyond a purely foreign jihadist structure.

13. Al-Murabitun
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: AQIM splinter / JNIM merger component. Status: largely absorbed into JNIM structure. Base: Mali/Sahel/Northwest Africa. Relationship label: AQIM-splinter / merged-into-JNIM.
NCTC says al-Murabitun formed in 2013 from the merger of al-Mulathamun Battalion and another AQIM splinter group, and that it was one of the four groups that merged into JNIM in 2017.
How it connects
AQIM → al-Murabitun: splinter lineage. Mokhtar Belmokhtar → al-Murabitun: commander/founder figure. Al-Murabitun → JNIM: merger component. Al-Murabitun → In Amenas / hotel attack lineage: high-profile Sahel attack ecosystem. Al-Murabitun → kidnapping/smuggling: financing/mobility link.
Importance
Al-Murabitun is the Saharan attack-and-hostage lineage inside JNIM. It connects Belmokhtar’s network, AQIM splinters, hostage-taking, smuggling, and urban hotel attacks to the broader Al-Qaeda Sahel coalition.

14. Macina Liberation Front / Katibat Macina
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: JNIM merger component. Status: active within JNIM ecosystem. Base: central Mali. Relationship label: merged-into-JNIM / local-mobilization-wing.
NCTC lists the Macina Liberation Front as one of the four groups that merged to form JNIM in March 2017.
How it connects
Macina Liberation Front → JNIM: merger component. Amadou Kouffa → Macina Liberation Front: founder/leader figure. Macina → central Mali: primary geography. Macina → Fulani/community mobilization: local recruitment and coercion frame. Macina → JNIM rural expansion: central Mali engine.
Importance
Macina is the central Mali social-mobilization engine within JNIM. It matters because it turns Al-Qaeda’s global brand into local insurgent governance, coercion, and recruitment in rural Mali.

15. AQIM Sahara Emirate
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: AQIM subgroup / JNIM merger component. Status: absorbed into JNIM. Base: Sahara/Sahel. Relationship label: AQIM-subgroup / merged-into-JNIM.
NCTC identifies the Sahara Emirate subgroup of AQIM as one of the four groups that merged into JNIM.
How it connects
AQIM → Sahara Emirate: regional subgroup. Sahara Emirate → JNIM: merger component. Sahara Emirate → AQIM commanders: links to Droukdel, Yahya Abu al-Hammam, Abou Zeid, and Sahel field commanders. Sahara Emirate → desert mobility: kidnapping, smuggling, ambush, route-control environment.
Importance
The Sahara Emirate is the formal AQIM branch inside the JNIM merger. It gives JNIM its cleanest direct organizational connection back to AQIM and therefore to Al-Qaeda Core.

16. Al-Shabaab
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: formal affiliate. Status: active. Base: Somalia, with operations in East Africa. Relationship label: pledged-affiliate-of-Al-Qaeda.
NCTC describes al-Shabaab as a Sunni terrorist group that publicly pledged loyalty to Al-Qaeda in 2012 and seeks to overthrow Somalia’s federal government, expel foreign forces, and establish a fundamentalist Islamic state.
How it connects
Al-Shabaab → Al-Qaeda Core: formal pledge/allegiance relationship. Al-Shabaab → Somalia: primary base. Al-Shabaab → Kenya/Uganda/Ethiopia/Djibouti: regional attack projection. Al-Shabaab → al-Kataib Media: media wing. Al-Shabaab → Amniyat: intelligence/security structure. Al-Shabaab → Mahad Karate: finance/amniyat command node. Al-Shabaab → Bin Laden correspondence: older evidence suggests loyalty and strategic advice before public pledge; still, the formal public pledge date is 2012. Al-Shabaab → East Africa Al-Qaeda history: not the same as the 1998 embassy bombing cells, but part of the same broader East Africa Al-Qaeda geography.
Importance
Al-Shabaab is the East Africa affiliate and one of Al-Qaeda’s strongest modern branches. It is especially important because of its shadow taxation system, amniyat wing, Somalia territorial influence, and repeated attacks in Kenya and Somalia.
Relationship cautions
Al-Shabaab has its own Somali political and clan dynamics. Do not reduce it to “Al-Qaeda’s Somalia office.” It is both a local insurgency and an Al-Qaeda affiliate.

17. Hurras al-Din
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: former formal Syria affiliate / Al-Qaeda-loyalist splinter. Status: publicly dissolved in early 2025, former members remain a threat. Base: Syria. Relationship label: former-affiliate / Al-Qaeda-loyalist-Syria-branch / post-Nusra splinter.
NCTC says Hurras al-Din became Al-Qaeda’s formal presence in Syria in February 2018. It was formed by Nusrah Front members who remained loyal to Al-Qaeda after Nusrah broke from Al-Qaeda and joined other groups to form Hay’at Tahrir al-Sham. NCTC says Hurras publicly announced dissolution in early 2025, but former members remain a threat.
How it connects
Jabhat al-Nusra → Hurras al-Din: loyalist splinter after Nusra/HTS break. Hurras al-Din → Al-Qaeda Core: formal Syria affiliate from 2018. Hurras al-Din → HTS: rival/suppressed/marginalized relationship after HTS consolidated power. Hurras al-Din → Syria veteran cadre: older Al-Qaeda-linked figures and loyalists. Hurras al-Din → post-2025 remnants: former-member threat, not a robust formal branch.
Importance
Hurras al-Din is the Al-Qaeda-loyalist residue of the Syria project. Its rise and dissolution show Al-Qaeda’s failure to retain dominance in the Syrian jihadist theater after Nusra/HTS broke away. CTC West Point describes Hurras al-Din’s history as a rise, marginalization by HTS, and dissolution, calling it a major setback for Al-Qaeda in Syria.

18. Jabhat al-Nusra / Nusrah Front
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: former formal Syria affiliate. Status: no longer Al-Qaeda; rebranded and later became part of HTS. Base: Syria. Relationship label: former-affiliate / broke-from-Al-Qaeda / predecessor-to-HTS.
Jabhat al-Nusra was Al-Qaeda’s Syria affiliate before it broke from Al-Qaeda and later joined other factions to form Hay’at Tahrir al-Sham. NCTC’s Hurras al-Din profile explicitly states that Hurras was formed by Nusrah Front members who remained loyal to Al-Qaeda after Nusrah broke away and joined other groups to form HTS.
How it connects
Al-Qaeda Core → Jabhat al-Nusra: former Syria affiliate. Jabhat al-Nusra → Jabhat Fatah al-Sham: rebrand after public break. Jabhat Fatah al-Sham → HTS: coalition/reorganization. Jabhat al-Nusra loyalists → Hurras al-Din: Al-Qaeda-loyalist splinter. Jabhat al-Nusra → ISIS split: rejected Baghdadi’s attempted merger and pledged to Zawahiri.
Importance
Nusra is the successful-but-lost Syria affiliate. It was one of Al-Qaeda’s most powerful local branches, but its breakaway was a strategic defeat for Al-Qaeda Core.
Relationship cautions
HTS should not be treated as current Al-Qaeda. It belongs in the map as a former affiliate lineage and rival/breakaway successor, not as a formal Al-Qaeda branch.

19. Hay’at Tahrir al-Sham / HTS
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: former branch lineage / breakaway / rival local authority. Status: separate from Al-Qaeda. Base: Syria. Relationship label: breakaway-successor / former-affiliate-lineage / not-current-Al-Qaeda.
HTS matters because it inherited much of the Nusra-era Syrian jihadist infrastructure but moved away from Al-Qaeda’s formal command. Hurras al-Din formed specifically from Nusra members who rejected that break and stayed loyal to Al-Qaeda.
How it connects
HTS → Jabhat Fatah al-Sham / Nusra: successor lineage. HTS → Al-Qaeda Core: former relationship, later break. HTS → Hurras al-Din: rivalry/marginalization relationship. HTS → Syria local governance: local insurgent authority rather than global Al-Qaeda affiliate.
Importance
HTS is critical for explaining why Al-Qaeda lost Syria. It represents localization, rebranding, and institutional breakaway from Al-Qaeda central authority.

20. AQI — Al-Qaeda in Iraq
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: former branch / legacy branch / rival-successor pathway. Status: inactive as Al-Qaeda; evolved into Islamic State pathway. Base: Iraq. Relationship label: former-affiliate / legacy-branch / evolved-into-rival.
AQI became Al-Qaeda’s Iraq branch after Abu Musab al-Zarqawi pledged allegiance to Bin Laden. It is essential to Al-Qaeda history because it later became Islamic State of Iraq and then ISIS, which ultimately broke with and rivaled Al-Qaeda.
How it connects
Zarqawi network → Al-Qaeda Core: pledge to Bin Laden. Zarqawi network → AQI: formal Iraq branch. AQI → Islamic State of Iraq: successor path. Islamic State of Iraq → ISIS: successor path. ISIS → Al-Qaeda Core: rival / breakaway enemy. AQI → al-Maqdisi: ideological mentor context, not command relationship. AQI → Syria/Nusra dispute: ISIS/Nusra split began from Iraq-Syria merger dispute.
Importance
AQI is the branch that became Al-Qaeda’s greatest rival. It belongs in your Al-Qaeda project because it explains the ISIS split, not because ISIS is Al-Qaeda.
Relationship cautions
Do not collapse AQI, ISIS, and Al-Qaeda into one organization. AQI is a former Al-Qaeda branch; ISIS is a rival successor movement after the split.

21. Islamic State / ISIS
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: rival successor from AQI lineage. Status: separate terrorist organization. Relationship label: splinter-successor / rival-of-Al-Qaeda / not-Al-Qaeda.
ISIS should appear in an Al-Qaeda project only as a rival and successor from AQI. It should not be treated as an Al-Qaeda affiliate.
How it connects
AQI → Islamic State of Iraq → ISIS: lineage. ISIS → Jabhat al-Nusra: attempted merger/conflict. ISIS → Al-Qaeda Core: split and rivalry. ISIS → Hurras / HTS / AQ affiliates: competitor for recruits, legitimacy, and theater dominance.
Importance
ISIS changed Al-Qaeda’s strategic environment by challenging its claim to global jihadist leadership, stealing recruits and affiliates, and forcing Al-Qaeda to reposition itself as more patient, decentralized, and locally embedded.

22. Ansar al-Sharia variants
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: local brand / local affiliate-adjacent label, varies by country. Status: country-specific. Relationship label: local-brand / affiliate-adjacent / needs-country-specific verification.
“Ansar al-Sharia” appears in multiple countries and should not automatically be treated as one unified organization. In Yemen, Ansar al-Sharia was heavily associated with AQAP’s local governance and insurgency branding. In Libya and Tunisia, Ansar al-Sharia groups belonged to the broader jihadist ecosystem and had varied relationships to AQIM, local networks, and ISIS-era currents.
How it connects
Ansar al-Sharia Yemen → AQAP: local brand / governance face. Ansar al-Sharia Libya → Al-Qaeda/AQIM ecosystem: contextual, case-by-case. Ansar al-Sharia Tunisia → AQIM/Maghreb ecosystem: contextual, case-by-case. Ansar al-Sharia variants → local post-Arab Spring instability: recruitment and governance opportunity.
Importance
Ansar al-Sharia labels show how Al-Qaeda-linked networks sometimes use local branding to avoid the baggage of the Al-Qaeda name while pursuing aligned aims.

23. Libyan Islamic Fighting Group / LIFG
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: historical overlap / veteran network / not clean affiliate. Status: largely inactive as original group. Base: Libya. Relationship label: veteran-overlap / ideological-network / not-formal-affiliate unless specifically sourced.
LIFG is relevant because Libyan veteran networks fed into Al-Qaeda, AQIM, Syria, and post-2011 Libya environments. But LIFG itself should not be simplistically classified as an Al-Qaeda branch.
How it connects
LIFG → Al-Qaeda Core: some personnel overlap and Afghanistan-era links. LIFG → Libya post-2011: militant/political veteran environment. LIFG → Syria networks: some Libyan foreign-fighter pathways. LIFG → AQIM/Maghreb: contextual ecosystem overlap.
Importance
LIFG is a veteran-network node, useful for mapping Libyan militants and post-2011 North Africa/Syria movements.

24. East Turkestan Islamic Movement / Turkistan Islamic Party
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: operational ecosystem / Afghan-Pakistan/Syria overlap. Status: active/varies by designation and source. Relationship label: aligned-ecosystem / theater-overlap / not-Al-Qaeda-branch.
The TIC chart places East Turkestan Islamic Movement and Turkistan Islamic Party in the Khorasan/Indian Subcontinent sphere and links some individuals to Al-Qaeda Shura claims. Treat those chart-specific leadership claims as chart claims unless separately verified.
How it connects
TIP/ETIM → Afghanistan/Pakistan: historical training and sanctuary overlap. TIP/ETIM → Syria: foreign-fighter theater overlap. TIP/ETIM → Al-Qaeda ecosystem: ideological and theater overlap. TIP/ETIM → AQIS/Khorasan: regional proximity.
Importance
These groups matter as foreign-fighter and sanctuary-overlap actors in Afghanistan/Pakistan and Syria, not as formal Al-Qaeda branches.

25. Islamic Movement of Uzbekistan / IMU and Islamic Jihad Union / IJU
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: Central Asian militant ecosystem overlap. Status: changed over time; many factions splintered or shifted. Relationship label: allied-ecosystem / training-overlap / not-formal-Al-Qaeda-branch unless sourced.
IMU and IJU appear in many Al-Qaeda/Afghanistan/Pakistan network maps because of training-camp, Taliban, and foreign-fighter overlap.
How it connects
IMU/IJU → Taliban/Afghanistan/Pakistan: sanctuary and battlefield overlap. IMU/IJU → Al-Qaeda Core: historical training and alliance environment. IMU/IJU → AQIS/Khorasan: regional ecosystem overlap. IMU/IJU → ISIS-K: some later splinter/defection dynamics, depending on faction and period.
Importance
These groups help explain why the Afghanistan/Pakistan zone became a multinational militant ecosystem.

26. Tehrik-e-Taliban Pakistan / TTP
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: allied ecosystem / Pakistani Taliban network / not Al-Qaeda branch. Status: active. Base: Pakistan/Afghanistan borderlands. Relationship label: allied-network / sanctuary-overlap / operational-cooperation-case-by-case.
TTP has had extensive overlap with Al-Qaeda and other militant groups in Pakistan’s tribal/border regions, but it is not the same organization as Al-Qaeda.
How it connects
TTP → Al-Qaeda Core: sanctuary and operational ecosystem overlap. TTP → AQIS: regional overlap and possible cooperation depending on case. TTP → Afghan Taliban: ideological and historical relationship, though organizationally distinct. TTP → Pakistan: primary target geography.
Importance
TTP is a Pakistan borderland ecosystem node. It helps explain how Al-Qaeda survived in Pakistan/Afghanistan after 2001.

27. Lashkar-e-Taiba / LeT
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: South Asian overlap group / separate organization. Status: active/proscribed. Relationship label: ecosystem-overlap / not-Al-Qaeda-affiliate.
LeT appears in some Al-Qaeda network diagrams because of South Asian militant overlap, Afghanistan/Pakistan linkages, and shared anti-Western/anti-India narratives. But it should not be treated as an Al-Qaeda affiliate.
How it connects
LeT → Al-Qaeda ecosystem: limited overlap and shared theater. LeT → Pakistan/India/Kashmir: primary geography. LeT → AQIS: ideological/target overlap, not command hierarchy. LeT → Hafiz Saeed: leadership node separate from Al-Qaeda Core.
Importance
LeT is relevant for South Asian jihadist ecosystem mapping, but keep its relationship separate and carefully sourced.

28. Jaish-e-Mohammed / JeM
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: South Asian overlap group / separate organization. Status: active/proscribed. Relationship label: ecosystem-overlap / not-formal-Al-Qaeda-affiliate.
JeM appears in Al-Qaeda/Khorasan/Indian Subcontinent diagrams because it shares Afghanistan/Pakistan/Kashmir geography and militant networks.
How it connects
JeM → AQIS/Khorasan environment: theater and ideological overlap. JeM → Taliban/Afghanistan: historical training and movement overlap. JeM → Al-Qaeda Core: not a formal affiliate unless a specific sourced relationship exists. JeM → Masood Azhar: separate leadership node.
Importance
JeM is a Kashmir/Pakistan militant ecosystem node, relevant to AQIS mapping but not equivalent to AQIS.

29. Jemaah Islamiyah / JI
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: Southeast Asian allied network / historical partner. Status: changed over time; original militant network degraded. Base: Indonesia/Southeast Asia. Relationship label: allied-network / Al-Qaeda-linked / not core affiliate.
JI is historically important because of the Bali bombings, Hambali, and Southeast Asian Al-Qaeda connections. It belongs in an Al-Qaeda project because it shows Al-Qaeda’s Southeast Asian reach, not because JI was simply “Al-Qaeda Southeast Asia.”
How it connects
JI → Al-Qaeda Core: historical training, funding, and operational links. JI → Hambali: liaison/operations bridge. JI → Bali bombing: major Al-Qaeda-linked Southeast Asia attack context. JI → Indonesia/Malaysia/Philippines: regional network geography.
Importance
JI is the Southeast Asia Al-Qaeda-linked network. It demonstrates that Al-Qaeda influence extended beyond Arab/Afghan theaters into regional partner organizations.

30. Abu Sayyaf Group
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: Southeast Asian overlap group / historically linked in parts. Status: fragmented; many later ISIS-linked factions. Relationship label: historical-overlap / not-core-Al-Qaeda-affiliate.
The Abu Sayyaf Group appears in many jihadist network maps because of links to JI, foreign fighters, kidnappings, and later ISIS-linked shifts. For an Al-Qaeda project, it should be included only where specific Al-Qaeda/JI connections are sourced.
How it connects
Abu Sayyaf → JI: regional overlap. Abu Sayyaf → Al-Qaeda ecosystem: historical/financial/training claims in parts. Abu Sayyaf → Philippines: local insurgency/kidnapping geography. Abu Sayyaf → ISIS later: many factions shifted toward ISIS, so do not overstate current Al-Qaeda relevance.

31. Boko Haram / ISWAP / Ansaru
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: West Africa overlap / mixed lineage. Status: varies by group. Relationship label: ecosystem-overlap / some AQ-aligned elements / not automatically Al-Qaeda.
For an Al-Qaeda-centered map, Ansaru is more relevant than Boko Haram proper because Ansaru had clearer Al-Qaeda/AQIM-aligned tendencies. Boko Haram and ISWAP are mostly better handled in an ISIS/West Africa project, unless explaining competition or network overlap.
How it connects
Ansaru → AQIM/JNIM ecosystem: ideological and regional overlap. Boko Haram → Al-Qaeda ecosystem: limited historical contact/possible training claims; later ISIS alignment dominates. ISWAP → ISIS: not Al-Qaeda. JNIM → West Africa expansion: separate Al-Qaeda-aligned Sahel track.
Importance
Use this cluster to explain West Africa competition and overlap, not to expand Al-Qaeda beyond sourced relationships.

32. Hamas, Palestinian Islamic Jihad, Hezbollah, IRGC/Quds Force
Relationship to Al-Qaeda
Type: contextual / rival / occasional theater-overlap / not Al-Qaeda. Status: separate organizations. Relationship label: non-Al-Qaeda contextual actor.
The TIC chart includes Middle East actors such as Hamas, Palestinian Islamic Jihad, Hezbollah, Quds Force, Houthis, and IRGC Iran in a broader regional box. In an Al-Qaeda-centered project, these should not be treated as Al-Qaeda affiliates. They are relevant only where public sources show specific contact, sanctuary, conflict, rivalry, or regional theater interaction.
How they connect
Hezbollah / IRGC / Quds Force → Al-Qaeda: not ideological allies; Iran-based Al-Qaeda leadership/facilitation claims make Iran relevant, but this does not mean Hezbollah or IRGC are Al-Qaeda branches. Hamas / PIJ → Al-Qaeda: different ideological and organizational tradition; occasional shared anti-Israel rhetoric does not equal command relationship. Houthis → AQAP: enemy/target relationship inside Yemen’s war. IRGC Iran → Sayf al-Adl / Iran-based Al-Qaeda file: state/facilitation ambiguity, not organizational unity.
Importance
These groups are best used as regional context and contrast. Keep them separate to avoid analytical contamination.

33. Relationship matrix

Al-Sahab	Al-Qaeda Core media arm	high
AQAP	formal affiliate	high

Hurras al-Din	former formal Syria affiliate, dissolved 2025	high
Jabhat al-Nusra	former Syria affiliate	high
AQI	former Iraq branch, later rival lineage	high

AQIM Sahara Emirate	merged into JNIM	high

Organization	Relationship	Confidence

Houthis	AQAP enemy/context in Yemen, not Al-Qaeda	high
ISWAP	ISIS-aligned West Africa actor, not Al-Qaeda	high
34. The clean Al-Qaeda relationship tree
Here is the cleanest text version of the full map:
Afghan jihad / MAK / Arab-Afghan network → produces Al-Qaeda Core.
Egyptian Islamic Jihad → merges into Al-Qaeda Core and strengthens the Egyptian senior cadre.
Taliban → provides sanctuary to Al-Qaeda Core before 9/11; later becomes safe-haven context for AQIS after 2021.
Haqqani Network → provides Afghan/Pakistan militant-family sanctuary ecosystem tied to both Taliban and Al-Qaeda.
AQAP → formal Al-Qaeda affiliate in Yemen, formed from Saudi and Yemeni Al-Qaeda elements.
GSPC → aligns with Al-Qaeda → becomes AQIM.
AQIM Sahara Emirate + Ansar al-Din + al-Murabitun + Macina Liberation Front → merge into JNIM, which pledges allegiance to AQIM, Al-Qaeda, and Taliban leadership.
al-Shabaab → pledges loyalty to Al-Qaeda in 2012 → becomes East Africa affiliate.
AQIS → formal South Asia affiliate → tied to Taliban safe-haven relationship and Afghanistan/Pakistan geography.
Jabhat al-Nusra → Al-Qaeda’s Syria affiliate → breaks from Al-Qaeda → becomes JFS/HTS path.
Nusra loyalists → form Hurras al-Din as Al-Qaeda’s formal Syria presence in 2018 → Hurras dissolves publicly in 2025, leaving remnants.
Zarqawi network → becomes AQI → evolves into Islamic State of Iraq / ISIS path → becomes Al-Qaeda’s rival, not affiliate.

35. What Part 8 adds
This part gives you the relationship grammar of the entire Al-Qaeda system:
* which groups are truly Al-Qaeda,
* which groups are predecessors,
* which groups merged into affiliates,
* which groups broke away,
* which groups became rivals,
* which groups provided sanctuary,
* which groups only overlap by theater,
* and which groups should be kept out of the Al-Qaeda category unless directly sourced.
The most important analytical distinction is this:
Al-Qaeda is not one giant command tree. It is a core brand, old veteran cadre, formal affiliate system, sanctuary network, and local-insurgency federation
     PART 10 — MASTER ENTITY LIST FOR THE AL-QAEDA OSINT PLATFORM
1. Source-confidence system
Use these labels on every profile:
OFFICIAL-HIGH — NCTC, FBI, DOJ, Treasury, State, RFJ, UN sanctions/monitoring, 9/11 Commission, CIA/ODNI releases. RESEARCH-HIGH — CTC West Point, major academic work, major wire services, reputable investigative reporting. PUBLIC-REPORTED — widely reported but not always officially confirmed. CONTESTED — status, death, rank, or relationship disputed. CHART-DERIVED — from the TIC chart/PDF; useful but needs corroboration. SCREENSHOT-DERIVED — from your Benghazi/AQI/AQIM screenshots; preserve but verify before presenting as confirmed. CONTEXTUAL — relevant to the ecosystem but not a formal Al-Qaeda entity.
NCTC’s current public profile is the best anchor for the modern network: Al-Qaeda affiliates conduct most operational activity; AQAP is based in Yemen, AQIM/JNIM in Mali/Sahel, al-Shabaab in Somalia, AQIS in Afghanistan/Pakistan, former Hurras al-Din elements remain in Syria after public dissolution, and some senior/lower-level Al-Qaeda members reside in Iran.

2. Master organization index
Al-Qaeda Core / AQC
Type: central organization. Status: active, weakened, dispersed. Primary period: 1988–present. Primary geographies: Afghanistan, Pakistan, Sudan, Iran, Yemen, Syria, Somalia, Mali/Sahel through affiliates. Key leaders: Osama bin Laden, Ayman al-Zawahiri, Sayf al-Adl. Relationship role: parent organization / ideological center / affiliate-recognition authority. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH. Notes: Al-Qaeda Core is now best understood as a senior-leadership and brand/ideology hub whose affiliates conduct most operational activity.
Maktab al-Khidamat / MAK
Type: predecessor network. Status: inactive. Primary period: 1980s Afghan jihad. Key figures: Abdullah Azzam, Osama bin Laden. Relationship role: precursor / incubator / Arab-Afghan support network. Source confidence: RESEARCH-HIGH / CHART-DERIVED for chart-specific dates. Notes: This is the pre-Al-Qaeda support infrastructure: recruitment, travel, guesthouses, fundraising, and Afghan jihad mobilization.
Egyptian Islamic Jihad / EIJ
Type: predecessor / merger cadre. Status: merged into Al-Qaeda. Key figures: Ayman al-Zawahiri, Sayf al-Adl, Mohammed Atef, Abu Muhammad al-Masri, Abu al-Khayr al-Masri. Relationship role: merged-into Al-Qaeda / Egyptian cadre source. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH / RESEARCH-HIGH. Notes: EIJ gave Al-Qaeda much of its Egyptian senior cadre and clandestine organizational discipline.
Taliban
Type: separate Afghan movement / sanctuary provider. Status: active governing power in Afghanistan. Relationship to Al-Qaeda: sanctuary provider, political protector, symbolic allegiance target for some affiliates. Key figures: Mullah Omar, Haibatullah Akhundzada, Mullah Yaqoob. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH. Notes: Not an Al-Qaeda branch. It is a separate organization, but historically central because it provided pre-9/11 sanctuary and remains relevant to AQIS and post-2021 safe-haven concerns. NCTC states AQIS has longstanding Taliban ties and pledged allegiance to Taliban leader Haybatullah Akhundzada partly to secure safe haven.
Haqqani Network
Type: Taliban-linked militant-family network. Status: active within Taliban ecosystem. Relationship to Al-Qaeda: sanctuary bridge / longstanding ties / contextual ally. Key figures: Jalaluddin Haqqani, Sirajuddin Haqqani, Khalil Haqqani, Nasiruddin Haqqani, Badruddin Haqqani, Yahya Haqqani, Aziz Haqqani. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH / RESEARCH-HIGH. Notes: Not an Al-Qaeda branch. It is a family-based Afghan/Pakistan militant network that intersects with Al-Qaeda through sanctuary, Taliban relations, and historic Bin Laden-era ties.
AQAP — Al-Qaeda in the Arabian Peninsula
Type: formal Al-Qaeda affiliate. Status: active. Primary geography: Yemen. Founded: 2009. Key figures: Nasir al-Wuhayshi, Sa’id al-Shihri, Qasim al-Raymi, Khalid Batarfi, Saad bin Atef al-Awlaki, Ibrahim al-Banna, Ibrahim al-Qosi, Anwar al-Awlaki, Samir Khan. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH. Notes: NCTC describes AQAP as one of Al-Qaeda’s most capable affiliates, formed in Yemen by Saudi and Yemeni extremist elements, with attacks and propaganda directed against the United States and its allies.
AQIM — Al-Qaeda in the Islamic Maghreb
Type: formal Al-Qaeda affiliate. Status: active but partly eclipsed by JNIM in Sahel operational importance. Predecessor: GSPC. Primary geographies: Algeria, Mali, Sahel, North Africa. Key figures: Hassan Hattab, Nabil Sahraoui, Abdelmalek Droukdel, Abu Ubaydah Yusuf al-Anabi, Mokhtar Belmokhtar, Abdelhamid Abou Zeid. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH. Notes: NCTC identifies AQIM as originally formed in 1998 as GSPC, a faction of Algeria’s Armed Islamic Group, before becoming an Al-Qaeda affiliate.
JNIM — Jama’at Nusrat al-Islam wal-Muslimin
Type: Al-Qaeda-aligned Sahel coalition / AQIM-linked affiliate structure. Status: active and highly significant. Founded: March 2017. Primary geographies: Mali, Burkina Faso, Sahel, wider West Africa. Merger components: Ansar al-Din, al-Murabitun, Macina Liberation Front, AQIM Sahara Emirate. Key figures: Iyad ag Ghali, Amadou Kouffa, Ali Maychou, Jafar Dicko. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH. Notes: NCTC states JNIM formed from four Mali-based extremist groups and that Iyad ag Ghali pledged allegiance to AQIM’s emir, Al-Qaeda’s emir, and the Taliban leader.
Al-Shabaab
Type: formal Al-Qaeda affiliate. Status: active. Primary geography: Somalia; regional attack activity in Kenya and elsewhere. Pledge: publicly pledged loyalty to Al-Qaeda in 2012. Key figures: Ahmed Abdi Godane, Ahmed Diriye, Mahad Karate, Abukar Ali Adan, Jehad Serwan Mostafa. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH. Notes: NCTC describes al-Shabaab as an Al-Qaeda-loyal Somali terrorist group that seeks to overthrow Somalia’s federal government and expel foreign forces.
AQIS — Al-Qaeda in the Indian Subcontinent
Type: formal Al-Qaeda affiliate. Status: active but smaller than AQAP, JNIM, or al-Shabaab. Founded: 2014. Primary geography: Afghanistan, Pakistan, India, Bangladesh. Key figures: Asim Umar, Usama Mahmood, Yahya Ghauri / Atif Yahya Ghouri, Muhammad Maruf. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH. Notes: NCTC describes AQIS as an Al-Qaeda affiliate based mainly in Afghanistan, formally announced in 2014 as an umbrella movement for South Asian jihadist groups.
Jabhat al-Nusra / Nusrah Front
Type: former Al-Qaeda Syria affiliate. Status: no longer Al-Qaeda. Primary geography: Syria. Key figure: Abu Muhammad al-Julani / Ahmed al-Sharaa. Relationship role: former affiliate / predecessor to Jabhat Fatah al-Sham and HTS. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH. Notes: Important as Al-Qaeda’s former Syria branch before the breakaway and HTS transition.
Hurras al-Din
Type: former formal Al-Qaeda Syria affiliate. Status: publicly announced dissolution in early 2025; former members remain a threat. Primary geography: Syria. Key figures: Faruq al-Suri / Samir Hijazi, Sami al-Uraydi, Abu Hammam al-Shami. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH. Notes: NCTC describes Hurras al-Din as Al-Qaeda’s Syria affiliate and says it dissolved publicly in early 2025, though former members likely remain active threats.
AQI — Al-Qaeda in Iraq
Type: former Al-Qaeda branch / legacy branch. Status: inactive as Al-Qaeda; evolved into Islamic State path. Primary geography: Iraq. Key figure: Abu Musab al-Zarqawi. Relationship role: former branch that became Al-Qaeda’s greatest rival lineage. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH / RESEARCH-HIGH. Notes: AQI is essential because it explains ISIS’s origin, but ISIS is not Al-Qaeda.
Islamic State / ISIS
Type: rival successor movement from AQI lineage. Status: separate organization. Relationship to Al-Qaeda: rival / splinter-successor, not affiliate. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH. Notes: Include in your platform only as a former-branch-to-rival lineage, not as current Al-Qaeda.

3. Al-Qaeda Core people index
Osama bin Laden
Aliases: Usama bin Ladin, UBL. Status: dead. Born: 1957, Saudi Arabia. Died: May 2, 2011, Abbottabad, Pakistan. Organizations: MAK, Al-Qaeda Core. Roles: founder, emir, symbolic leader. Family ties: son of Muhammad Awad bin Laden; father of Saad, Hamza, Khalid, Omar, Muhammad, and other publicly named children. Locations: Saudi Arabia, Afghanistan, Sudan, Pakistan. Connected events: MAK era, Al-Qaeda founding, 1998 embassy bombings, USS Cole, 9/11, Abbottabad raid. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH.
Ayman al-Zawahiri
Status: dead. Born: 1951, Egypt. Died: July 31, 2022, Kabul, Afghanistan. Organizations: Egyptian Islamic Jihad, Al-Qaeda Core. Roles: EIJ leader, Al-Qaeda deputy, Al-Qaeda emir after Bin Laden. Family tie: father-in-law of Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi. Locations: Egypt, Afghanistan, Pakistan, Kabul. Connected events: EIJ merger, post-2011 succession, AQIS announcement, Syria/Nusra dispute, death in Kabul. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH.
Sayf al-Adl
Aliases: Saif al-Adel; Muhammad Salah al-Din Zaydan. Status: alive / wanted / Iran-based according to U.S. public sources. Organizations: EIJ-linked Egyptian cadre, Al-Qaeda Core. Roles: senior leader, military committee figure, de facto post-Zawahiri leadership candidate. Locations: Egypt, Afghanistan, Pakistan, Sudan, Iran. Connected events: 1998 embassy bombing indictment, Iran-based leadership, post-Zawahiri succession. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH. Notes: Rewards for Justice says al-Adl was released from Iranian custody in 2015 and identifies him as a senior Al-Qaeda leader.
Mohammed Atef / Abu Hafs al-Masri
Status: dead. Organizations: Egyptian cadre, Al-Qaeda Core. Roles: military chief, senior deputy. Locations: Egypt, Afghanistan. Connected events: 9/11 planning context, killed in Afghanistan in 2001. Family tie: a daughter reportedly married into Bin Laden’s family. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH / RESEARCH-HIGH.
Abu Muhammad al-Masri / Abdullah Ahmed Abdullah
Status: dead, publicly reported. Organizations: Al-Qaeda Core, Egyptian cadre. Roles: senior leader, East Africa embassy bombing case figure. Locations: Egypt, Afghanistan, Iran. Connected events: 1998 embassy bombings, Iran-based senior cadre, reported 2020 death in Tehran. Source confidence: PUBLIC-REPORTED / OFFICIAL-HIGH for indictment-linked role.
Atiyah Abd al-Rahman
Status: dead. Nationality: Libyan. Organizations: Al-Qaeda Core. Roles: senior manager, communications/facilitation node, Bin Laden correspondence figure. Locations: Libya, Afghanistan, Pakistan. Connected events: Abbottabad files, Iran facilitation context, killed 2011. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH / RESEARCH-HIGH.
Abu Yahya al-Libi
Status: dead. Nationality: Libyan. Organizations: Al-Qaeda Core. Roles: senior ideologue, public media figure. Locations: Libya, Afghanistan, Pakistan. Connected events: post-Bin Laden media/ideology period, killed 2012. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH / RESEARCH-HIGH.
Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi / Muhammad Abbatay
Aliases: Abd al Rahman al Maghribi, Muhammad Abbatay, Muhammed Abaytah. Status: alive / wanted / Iran-based according to RFJ. Nationality: Moroccan. Organizations: Al-Qaeda Core, al-Sahab, External Communications Office. Roles: al-Sahab director, external communications leader, senior adviser. Family tie: son-in-law of Ayman al-Zawahiri. Locations: Morocco, Germany, Afghanistan, Pakistan, Iran. Connected events: Abbottabad documents, post-2011 external communications, Iran-based senior network. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH. Notes: RFJ identifies him as an Iran-based key Al-Qaeda leader, longtime al-Sahab director, and Zawahiri’s son-in-law/senior adviser.
Yasin al-Suri / Ezedin Abdel Aziz Khalil
Status: alive / wanted / sanctioned. Nationality: Syrian. Organizations: Al-Qaeda facilitation network. Roles: Iran-based facilitator, money/recruit movement node. Locations: Syria, Iran, Pakistan. Connected events: Iran-based facilitation pipeline. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH. Notes: RFJ identifies him as an Iran-based Al-Qaeda facilitator.
Muhsin al-Fadhli
Status: dead. Nationality: Kuwaiti. Organizations: Al-Qaeda facilitation network; Syria/Khorasan Group context. Roles: Iran-based facilitation successor to Yasin al-Suri, later Syria external-operations concern. Locations: Kuwait, Iran, Syria. Connected events: Iran facilitation network, Syria Al-Qaeda-linked external operations concern. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH / PUBLIC-REPORTED.
Abu al-Khayr al-Masri
Status: dead. Organizations: Al-Qaeda Core, Egyptian cadre, Syria file. Roles: deputy/senior leader, Syria-based emissary. Family tie: publicly reported Bin Laden son-in-law. Locations: Egypt, Iran, Syria. Connected events: Syria/Nusra/Hurras file, killed 2017. Source confidence: RESEARCH-HIGH / PUBLIC-REPORTED.

4. Bin Laden family index
Muhammad Awad bin Laden

Hamida al-Attas
Status: living/deceased status not central here. Relationship: mother of Osama bin Laden. Role: family-origin context. Source confidence: PUBLIC-HIGH. Notes: Family-context node only.
Najwa Ghanem
Relationship: first wife and cousin of Osama bin Laden. Children publicly associated: Abdullah, Abdul Rahman, Saad, Omar, Osman, Muhammad, Fatima, Iman, Ladin/Bakir, Rukhaiya, Nour. Source confidence: PUBLIC-HIGH. Notes: Family-context node; important because Saad and Omar are among her publicly known children.
Khairiah Sabar
Relationship: wife of Osama bin Laden. Child: Hamza bin Laden. Source confidence: PUBLIC-HIGH. Notes: Relevant because Hamza became the major post-2011 Bin Laden-family symbolic successor.
Siham Sabar
Relationship: wife of Osama bin Laden. Children publicly associated: Khalid, Miriam, Sumaiya, Khadijah/Kadhija. Source confidence: PUBLIC-HIGH. Notes: Relevant because Khalid was killed during the Abbottabad raid.
Amal Ahmed al-Sadah
Relationship: wife of Osama bin Laden. Children publicly associated: Safiyah, Aasia, Ibrahim, Zainab, Hussain. Source confidence: PUBLIC-HIGH. Notes: Abbottabad household context.
Saad bin Laden
Status: dead, publicly reported. Relationship: son of Osama bin Laden and Najwa Ghanem. Role: Al-Qaeda family/senior-symbolic node. Source confidence: PUBLIC-REPORTED / CHART-DERIVED for any “deputy emir” title. Notes: TIC chart-style titles should not be treated as confirmed without corroboration.
Hamza bin Laden
Status: dead. Relationship: son of Osama bin Laden and Khairiah Sabar. Role: symbolic heir / high-profile Al-Qaeda figure. Connected events: post-Bin Laden messaging, killed in U.S. counterterrorism operation. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH for death; PUBLIC-REPORTED for marriage links.
Khalid bin Laden
Status: dead. Relationship: son of Osama bin Laden and Siham Sabar. Connected event: killed during Abbottabad raid. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH. Notes: Primarily Abbottabad/family context.
Omar bin Laden
Status: living public figure. Relationship: son of Osama bin Laden and Najwa Ghanem. Role: family witness/commentator, not Al-Qaeda command. Source confidence: PUBLIC-HIGH. Notes: Do not classify as Al-Qaeda operative.
Muhammad bin Laden
Relationship: son of Osama bin Laden. Role: family/marriage-network node. Connected tie: reported marriage link to Mohammed Atef family. Source confidence: PUBLIC-REPORTED. Notes: Relevant for family-command marriage mapping only.

10. AQIS people index
Source anchor: NCTC describes AQIS as an Al-Qaeda affiliate based mainly in Afghanistan and formally announced in 2014.

14. Event entity index
Core Al-Qaeda events
MAK formation / Afghan jihad mobilization — origin event. Al-Qaeda founding, 1988 — core organizational birth. Azzam assassination, 1989 — ideological predecessor death. Sudan period, 1992–1996 — organizational-building phase. Bin Laden return to Afghanistan, 1996 — sanctuary renewal. World Islamic Front declaration, 1998 — ideological escalation. 1998 U.S. Embassy bombings — first major global Al-Qaeda attack. Al-Qaeda FTO designation, 1999 — legal designation milestone. USS Cole bombing, 2000 — Yemen/maritime attack milestone. 9/11 attacks, 2001 — global turning point. Afghanistan invasion, 2001 — sanctuary destruction/dispersal. Abbottabad raid, 2011 — Bin Laden death/archive event. Zawahiri death in Kabul, 2022 — post-2021 safe-haven controversy.
AQAP events
Sana’a prison escape, 2006 — AQY/AQAP reconstitution. AQAP formation, 2009 — formal affiliate birth. Bin Nayef assassination attempt, 2009 — Saudi target. Flight 253 attempted bombing, 2009 — aviation plot. Cargo bomb plot, 2010 — aviation/cargo plot. Abyan/Zinjibar seizure, 2011 — territorial phase. Sana’a military parade bombing, 2012 — Yemeni military attack. Yemen Ministry of Defense attack, 2013 — government target. Sana’a prison break, 2014 — reconstitution event. Charlie Hebdo claim, 2015 — external propaganda/claim event. Mukalla seizure, 2015 — territorial/revenue peak. Mukalla loss, 2016 — territorial setback. Pensacola attack claim, 2019/2020 — U.S. attack claim. Batarfi death / Saad al-Awlaki succession, 2024 — leadership transition.
AQIM / JNIM events
GSPC formation, 1998 — AQIM predecessor. GSPC aligns with Al-Qaeda, 2006 — allegiance transition. AQIM name adopted, 2007 — formal affiliate identity. Algiers bombings, 2007 — AQIM urban attacks. Northern Mali takeover, 2012 — Sahel transformation. In Amenas, 2013 — hostage crisis. Radisson Blu Bamako, 2015 — hotel attack. Ouagadougou, 2016 — hotel/café attack. Grand-Bassam, 2016 — coastal expansion attack. JNIM formation, 2017 — merger event. JNIM FTO designation, 2018 — legal milestone. Droukdel killed, 2020 — AQIM leadership transition. Barsalogho, 2024 — mass-casualty village attack. Bamako military facility attacks, 2024 — capital-pressure event. Djibo seizure, 2025 — JNIM town seizure. Mali fuel blockade, 2025 — economic warfare/blockade. Coordinated Mali attacks / Kidal, 2026 — strategic escalation. Source anchor: NCTC and UN monitoring are the recurring authoritative anchors for JNIM/AQIM current trends.
Al-Shabaab events
Al-Shabaab emergence, 2006 — post-ICU origin. FTO designation, 2008 — legal milestone. Kampala bombings, 2010 — Uganda regional attack. Pledge to Al-Qaeda, 2012 — formal affiliation. Westgate Mall, 2013 — Kenya urban attack. Godane killed, 2014 — leadership transition. Garissa University, 2015 — Kenya massacre. Mogadishu truck bombing, 2017 — Somalia mass-casualty event. DusitD2, 2019 — Nairobi attack. Manda Bay / Camp Simba, 2020 — U.S./Kenya military target. Lido Beach, 2024 — Mogadishu attack. Attempted assassination of Somali president, 2025 — leadership target event. Source anchor: NCTC al-Shabaab profile.
AQIS events
AQIS announced, 2014 — formal affiliate birth. Karachi Naval Dockyard attack attempt, 2014 — early major operation. Bangladesh secular writer/activist attacks, 2015–2016 — assassination campaign. Xulhaz Mannan / Mahbub Tonoy murders, 2016 — AQIS-claimed attack. AQIS FTO designation, 2016 — legal milestone. Asim Umar killed, 2019 — leadership transition. Taliban takeover, 2021 — safe-haven context shift. Source anchor: NCTC AQIS profile.
Syria / Hurras events
Jabhat al-Nusra emergence, 2012 — Syria affiliate birth. ISIS/Nusra split, 2013 — jihadist rupture. Al-Qaeda disavows ISIS, 2014 — formal split. Nusra breaks/rebrands, 2016 — Al-Qaeda loses Syria branch. HTS forms, 2017 — breakaway successor. Hurras al-Din forms, 2018 — Al-Qaeda loyalist Syria affiliate. Hurras designated, 2019 — legal milestone. Hurras dissolution announcement, 2025 — Syria affiliate collapse/remnant phase. Source anchor: NCTC Hurras profile.

15. Location entity index
Core sanctuary and origin locations
Afghanistan — Afghan jihad, Al-Qaeda founding environment, Taliban sanctuary, pre-9/11 camps, AQIS safe-haven concern. Pakistan — Peshawar MAK hub, post-9/11 refuge, captures, Abbottabad, AQIS corridor. Sudan — Bin Laden organizational-building base, 1992–1996. Iran — senior leadership/facilitation geography, not a formal Al-Qaeda affiliate base. NCTC notes that some senior and lower-level Al-Qaeda members reside in Iran.
Affiliate base locations
Yemen — AQAP base; Sana’a, Aden, Abyan, Shabwah, Hadramawt/Mukalla, al-Bayda, Marib. Somalia — al-Shabaab base; Mogadishu, Lower Shabelle, Jubaland, Bay/Bakool, Gedo. Mali — AQIM/JNIM base; Kidal, Gao, Timbuktu, Mopti, Bamako. Burkina Faso — JNIM expansion; Djibo, Barsalogho, northern/eastern regions. Syria — Nusra/HTS/Hurras geography; Idlib, northwest Syria, Aleppo, Latakia, Raqqah, Damascus. Afghanistan/Pakistan — AQIS base and Taliban-aligned sanctuary.
Attack-symbolic locations
Nairobi — 1998 embassy bombing, Westgate, DusitD2. Dar es Salaam — 1998 embassy bombing. Aden — USS Cole. New York / Washington / Pennsylvania — 9/11. Paris — Charlie Hebdo. Pensacola — AQAP-claimed attack. Bamako — Radisson Blu, JNIM capital-pressure attacks. Ouagadougou — AQIM/JNIM-linked attack geography. Grand-Bassam — AQIM coastal expansion. Garissa — al-Shabaab Kenya attack. Manda Bay — al-Shabaab U.S./Kenya military attack. Kabul — Zawahiri death; Taliban/AQ safe-haven controversy.

16. Media / propaganda entity index
Al-Sahab Media
Organization: Al-Qaeda Core. Role: central media arm, leadership messaging, ideological releases, affiliate legitimacy. Key person: Abd al-Rahman al-Maghrebi. Source confidence: OFFICIAL-HIGH. Notes: RFJ and FBI link al-Maghrebi to al-Sahab leadership.
Al-Malahem Media
Organization: AQAP. Role: AQAP media wing, claims, propaganda, video releases. Key figures: Anwar al-Awlaki, Samir Khan, Nasser al-Ansi, Ibrahim al-Qosi. Source confidence: PUBLIC-HIGH / OFFICIAL-HIGH.
Inspire
Organization: AQAP media ecosystem. Role: English-language propaganda magazine. Key figures: Samir Khan, Anwar al-Awlaki. Source confidence: PUBLIC-HIGH. Safety note: Catalog as propaganda artifact only; do not reproduce operational content.
Resurgence
Organization: Al-Qaeda / AQIS-associated propaganda environment. Role: English-language/South Asia-oriented propaganda magazine. Source confidence: PUBLIC-HIGH. Safety note: Catalog without reproducing operational content.
Al-Kataib Media
Organization: al-Shabaab. Role: official media wing, attack claims, recruitment, battlefield propaganda. Source confidence: PUBLIC-HIGH.
AQIM / JNIM media channels
Organization: AQIM/JNIM. Role: attack claims, hostage statements, Sahel propaganda, allegiance messaging. Source confidence: PUBLIC-HIGH / RESEARCH-HIGH.
Hurras al-Din media
Organization: Hurras al-Din. Role: Al-Qaeda-loyalist Syria statements, ideological messaging. Source confidence: PUBLIC-HIGH.

17. Relationship type index
Use these relationship labels across your platform:
FOUNDED_BY — person founded organization. LED_BY — person led organization. SUCCEEDED_BY — leadership transition. DEPUTY_OF — deputy relationship. MEMBER_OF — membership. FORMAL_AFFILIATE_OF — official Al-Qaeda branch relationship. PREDECESSOR_OF — earlier organization became later organization. MERGED_INTO — group merged into coalition. SPLINTER_OF — group split from another group. BREAKAWAY_FROM — former affiliate separated from parent. RIVAL_OF — ISIS/Al-Qaeda relationship. SAFE_HAVEN_PROVIDER_FOR — Taliban/Haqqani sanctuary relationship. FACILITATOR_FOR — money, movement, personnel, communications. MEDIA_ARM_OF — al-Sahab, al-Malahem, al-Kataib. FAMILY_OF — kinship relationship. MARRIED_TO / IN_LAW_OF — marriage/in-law ties. KILLED_IN — death event. CAPTURED_IN — arrest/capture event. INDICTED_FOR — legal case. DESIGNATED_AS — sanctions/designation. CLAIMED_BY — attack claim. ATTRIBUTED_TO — attribution by official/credible sources. SCREENSHOT_CLAIMED_RELATIONSHIP — unverified screenshot-derived link. CHART_CLAIMED_RELATIONSHIP — unverified chart-derived link.

18. Status badge index
Use these profile badges:
Alive Dead Captured Detained Convicted Indicted Wanted Sanctioned Designated Former member Defected / surrendered Released Unknown Contested death Reported dead Chart-derived only Screenshot-derived only Contextual, not Al-Qaeda Former affiliate, not current Al-Qaeda Rival successor, not Al-Qaeda

19. Clean relationship tree for the whole platform
MAK / Arab-Afghan network → produces Al-Qaeda Core.
Egyptian Islamic Jihad → merges into Al-Qaeda Core.
Taliban → provides sanctuary to Al-Qaeda Core before 9/11 → becomes post-2021 safe-haven context for AQIS.
Haqqani Network → provides Afghanistan/Pakistan sanctuary ecosystem → connects Taliban security/family network to Al-Qaeda history.
AQAP → formal Al-Qaeda affiliate in Yemen.
GSPC → becomes AQIM.
AQIM Sahara Emirate + Ansar al-Din + al-Murabitun + Macina Liberation Front → merge into JNIM.
Al-Shabaab → pledges to Al-Qaeda in 2012 → becomes East Africa affiliate.
AQIS → formal South Asia affiliate → tied to Taliban safe-haven relationship.
Jabhat al-Nusra → former Al-Qaeda Syria affiliate → breaks away into JFS/HTS path.
Nusra loyalists → form Hurras al-Din → Hurras publicly dissolves in 2025; remnants remain.
Zarqawi network → becomes AQI → evolves into Islamic State path → becomes ISIS, Al-Qaeda’s rival, not its affiliate.

20. Final platform section structure
For your site/app, the research can be organized as:
1. Core Network Al-Qaeda Core, Bin Laden, Zawahiri, Sayf al-Adl, Egyptian cadre, al-Sahab.
2. Family Trees Bin Laden family, Zawahiri family, Haqqani family.
3. Affiliates AQAP, AQIM, JNIM, al-Shabaab, AQIS, Hurras al-Din.
4. Former Branches and Splinters AQI, Jabhat al-Nusra, HTS, ISIS split path.
5. Sanctuary Networks Taliban, Haqqani Network, Iran-based facilitation, Pakistan/Afghanistan geography.
6. Case Files Embassy bombings, USS Cole, 9/11, Abbottabad, AQAP aviation plots, Benghazi, AQI-to-ISIS, JNIM Sahel, al-Shabaab, AQIS Bangladesh, Syria split.
7. Locations Afghanistan, Pakistan, Iran, Yemen, Somalia, Mali, Burkina Faso, Algeria, Libya, Syria, Iraq, Bangladesh, India, Kenya, Tanzania, Saudi Arabia, Sudan, Gulf, Europe, United States.
8. Media and Propaganda al-Sahab, al-Malahem, Inspire, Resurgence, al-Kataib, AQIM/JNIM media, Hurras media.
9. Legal and Sanctions Layer FTO designations, SDGT designations, UN sanctions, FBI wanted, RFJ rewards, DOJ prosecutions.
10. Source-Confidence Layer Official, research-high, public-reported, contested, chart-derived, screenshot-derived.

21. What Part 10 gives you
This gives you the final master index of the project:
* people,
* families,
* organizations,
* locations,
* events,
* media organs,
* statuses,
* relationship types,
* source-confidence labels,
* and the clean hierarchy of how everything connects.
The strongest recurring sources for keeping this project current are NCTC terrorist group profiles, FBI wanted pages, Rewards for Justice profiles, State/Treasury designations, and the UN ISIL/Al-Qaeda Monitoring Team reports, which list reports through February 2026
